HEADLINE: Union Leader Urges Companies to Forge Alliance With Labor

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE 

 BODY:
John J. Sweeney used his first anniversary as president of the A.F.L.-C.I.O. to tell business leaders that the labor movement wants to help American businesses compete but will also continue pressuring companies to give workers more money, training and power.
Speaking on Friday in New York to a group called Business for Social Responsibility, Mr. Sweeney urged companies to promote both competitiveness and social equity by taking the high road -- by treating workers as assets, by training them and cooperating with them -- instead of taking the low road of squeezing down wages and casting off workers. 
 Attacked by conservative critics for being anti-business and giving short shrift to the importance of economic growth, Mr. Sweeney expounded more than ever before about labor's desire to cooperate to increase corporate competitiveness.
"We want to help American business compete in the world and create new wealth for your shareholders and your employees," Mr. Sweeney said. "We want to work with you to bake a larger pie which all Americans can share and not just argue with you about how to divide the existing pie. It is time for business and labor to see each other as natural allies, not natural enemies."
In his speech, to several hundred executives, Mr. Sweeney added: "American labor no longer takes the position that quality, productivity and profits are not our business. They are our business. They are our jobs. And, indeed, they are our livelihoods."
If corporations are intent on improving quality and service, he said, they should give workers a greater say in decisions making because that will foster a more dedicated, cooperative work force.
Soon after Mr. Sweeney won the first contested race for the A.F.L.-C.I.O.'s presidency, he told corporate executives that he wanted to build bridges to business but was also ready to battle with them if they treated workers shabbily.
In his speech, he took a hard line against companies that he said took the low road. He urged executives in the audience, from companies, like Levi Strauss and Stride Rite, that are known for treating their workers well, to pressure other companies to join them in providing health insurance and extensive training.
"The standards of corporate behavior are much too low because corporate America has too much power, and the rest of us have too little," he said. "A stronger movement of working Americans can help to correct the imbalance."
He pointed to results of an A.F.L.-C.I.O.-sponsored poll indicating that 83 percent of Americans, union and nonunion, say corporations care more about profits than about being fair and loyal to their workers.
Mr. Sweeney said it would be smart business for companies that are already unionized to criticize competitors that dismiss workers who back unionization drives. He noted that a study by a Harvard Law School professor, Paul Weiler, found that 10,000 workers lost their jobs each year for trying to organize unions.
He also told the executives that if they were serious about being socially responsible, they should make sure the contractors that clean their buildings pay workers a living wage and provide health insurance and good working conditions.
Seeking to forge an alliance with companies in Business for Social Responsibility, he cautioned them that competitors who did not provide health insurance or pensions were making it hard for responsible companies to compete. "Together," he said, "we can raise the standards of competition, so that you can take the high road with confidence."

SECTION: Section 4; Page 14; Column 1; Editorial Desk 

LENGTH: 1765 words

HEADLINE: BIll Clinton for President

 BODY:
Today we endorse the re-election of President Bill Clinton. Readers of this page will know that we share many of the public's concerns about Mr. Clinton's resoluteness and sensitivity to ethical standards in government. But our endorsement is delivered in the unequivocal confidence that he is the best candidate in the field and in the belief that because he has grown in the job he can build on the successes of his first term while correcting its defects. Toward that end, our endorsement comes with a set of recommendations for how Mr. Clinton can, before Election Day, address voters' concerns about his personality and character.
First, however, we want to outline the case for Mr. Clinton's re-election based on policy, performance and growth in office. Our view of Mr. Clinton mirrors that of millions of swing voters who are choosing him over Bob Dole and Ross Perot. He is clearly the candidate of hope and progress in this race. No one can doubt his commitment to using government to spur the economy, protect the environment, defend the cities, promote racial justice and combine compassion with fiscal prudence. 
 The choice of Mr. Clinton is easier because of Mr. Dole's halting campaign. Neither his 15 percent tax cut nor his wild charge that newspapers have pulled their punches on Whitewater stands the test of logic or represents Mr. Dole at his best. He is a good man whose service on behalf of the nation, in the Army and the Senate, will be well remembered. Indeed, many voters are dismayed that Mr. Dole has strayed from his moderate record and look to Mr. Clinton as a protection from Republican excess.
 
A Revived Party and Presidency
But a vote for Mr. Clinton is more than a defensive measure. He is clearly the most skilled navigator of today's contrary political seas. Even his most notable defeat, on health care, arose from his correct judgment that Americans want universal, affordable coverage. Mr. Clinton understands that the electorate makes contradictory demands. Voters are sullen and suspicious about government, yet anxious that it serve them. Americans have grown conservative, yet they want their interests and values protected. Mr. Clinton's Presidency has tacked this way and that, in part because it had to. He always calculates how far he can go and at what cost. We have disagreed with some of his calculations, but over the past two years he has not only revived his Presidency, he has also refashioned the Democratic Party's approach to government.
Some argue wrongly that Mr. Clinton has had no sense of direction. In several areas, though, he has picked his destinations and risked his political interests to get there. The pattern of the last two years provides a template for success in a second term.
 
The Economy
The campaign has produced no more fallacious statement than Mr. Dole's assertion that the nation has the worst economy in 100 years. The real situation is that Mr. Clinton's drive toward a balanced budget has helped keep interest rates low and promote an economic expansion now in its fifth year. Mr. Clinton stood up to the spendthrifts in his own party at the start of his term. He curbed the Federal deficits that had piled up over years of Republican Presidents proclaiming devotion to fiscal conservatism. Yet he wisely opposed a balanced-budget amendment that would tie a President's hands in a military or fiscal emergency.
Mr. Clinton raised taxes primarily on those most able to pay while pushing through one of the most important initiatives of his Presidency, the earned-income tax credit, which channeled billions of dollars into the poorest segment of the work force and lifted more than three million people out of poverty.
 
International Trade
Today both parties are driven by differences over trade, which accounts for a third of the economy. The temptation to demagogue about job flight is ever present. Yet Mr. Clinton has performed with a tough sense of purpose, helping to educate the public that foreign competition cannot be wished away. Although accused of unwillingness to take on his own party's interest groups, Mr. Clinton bucked the Democratic leadership to secure the free-trade agreement with Canada and Mexico. He then went on to support the global trade agreement that created the World Trade Organization. At the same time, he has been more aggressive in pressing Japan and China to open their markets.
 
Foreign Policy
In 1993, Mr. Clinton lacked experience in foreign affairs, and he stumbled early by confusing consultation with leadership when it came to Bosnia. Now he is regarded internationally as a leader with a sophisticated grasp of a superpower's obligation to help the world manage its conflicts and economic contests.
The hallmark of this new sophistication is Mr. Clinton's timing of those moments when American prestige and resources can be decisive. His decision to throw political and financial support behind the election of President Boris Yeltsin in Russia, then mired at below 10 percent in the polls, was a successful, high-risk intervention.
In applying American prestige to the Middle East process of reconciliation, Mr. Clinton was building on a bipartisan tradition. But without his intense efforts, the process would have foundered. In Bosnia, Mr. Clinton ignored persistent bad advice about how to use force, invented a peace process from the most unpromising situation and finally stabilized a war that posed a security threat to Europe and endangered NATO.
 
Health Care
 In the field of health care, the Clinton Administration failed through a toxic combination of hubris and secrecy. But Mr. Clinton was headed in the right direction. Americans need and want a health care system that covers everyone and keeps costs down through competition. If Mr. Clinton is elected, the journey toward this valuable goal will continue. If Mr. Dole is elected, that journey will end, and the assault on Medicaid and Medicare will continue. Instead of quality care for all, the country will move toward making Medicare a second-class program for the elderly poor and toward a health-insurance system favoring the affluent and the healthy.
 
Political Values
The last few years have seen an ugliness of tone in American politics toward the poor, minorities and immigrants. Mr. Clinton has been the most important voice for conciliation, but even he has bowed to expediency. We opposed his signing of the welfare bill, but he has promised to ease its unfair attacks on the poor and legal immigrants. His lack of backbone on this issue was at least balanced by a courageous stand in favor of affirmative action.
In sharp contrast to the two previous Administrations, Mr. Clinton has used common sense on guns. By supporting local police, he erased the Republicans' unearned copyright on the crime issue. He has defended choice on abortion, and his re-election will help produce a Supreme Court that protects this and other freedoms.
Another value asserted by Mr. Clinton is reverence for the earth. Electing the Democratic ticket will return to office Vice President Al Gore, the most knowledgeable and consistent defender of the environment in Washington. He converted Mr. Clinton from a relaxed to a muscular guardian of clean air and water. In a second term, they can generate a new wave of sensible environmental laws.
 
Ethics
Obviously, we could not ask our readers to vote for Mr. Clinton without addressing his most significant leadership problem. Many Americans do not trust him or believe him to be a person of character. We do not dodge that issue, nor should Mr. Clinton. Indeed, he must view it as a prime opportunity of his second term. A fraction of the electorate, of course, will never forgive his reputation for philandering. But he can reclaim the trust of the great majority by demonstrating a zeal for financial integrity and for protecting the machinery of justice from politics. Toward that end, we urge Mr. Clinton to close the campaign with a series of dramatic gestures.
First, he should accept the Republican dare and pledge not to pardon anyone convicted in prosecutions arising from Whitewater, the White House travel office firings, the mishandling of F.B.I. files, or the raising of funds for the 1996 campaign. He should promise that he, the First Lady and every member of the executive branch will cooperate with all investigations, whether they are from the Justice Department, special prosecutors or Congressional committees.
Next, Mr. Clinton should deal with his party's Indonesian fund-raising scandal by acknowledging that both parties' financial practices are wrong even if not illegal. He can then credibly pledge to recapture one of the main themes of his 1992 campaign. We saluted then and we still believe in the stirring call in his inaugural address "to reform our politics so that power and privilege no longer shout down the voice of the people."
The Democratic Congressional leadership talked him into shelving campaign finance legislation because their members wanted to keep lapping up contributions from political-action committees. Now is the moment for Mr. Clinton to renew his promise by sponsoring campaign laws that end foreign donations and "soft money" dodges and that give all credible candidates a level playing field when it comes to mail and advertising.
Such dramatic pledges would do more than defuse the criticisms of Mr. Perot and Mr. Dole in the closing days of this election. They would also enlist public opinion on Mr. Clinton's side as a protection against Republican excesses in the Congressional investigations that are coming whether Mr. Clinton opts for openness or sticks to the hunker-down strategy that has done his Administration such damage.
More important, Mr. Clinton would be demonstrating that he regards winning on Nov. 5 as a necessary prelude to the important work that lies ahead. Mr. Clinton's original vision of a country where no one waits for health care, social justice and economic opportunity to trickle down is still valid. His education in the leadership burden that rests on the world's strongest nation and its President has proceeded more rapidly and successfully than anyone could have dared hope. The Presidency he once dreamed is still within his reach if he brings the requisite integrity to the next four years. By adding self discipline to vision, he can build on the achievements he has already made and make a fair bid to leave Washington in 2001 as one of the notable Presidents of the 20th century.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: A G.O.P. Stronghold Starts to Lean Toward the Democrats

SERIES: AMERICAN PROFILES -- New Hampshire

BYLINE: By SARA RIMER 

DATELINE: DERRY, N.H.

 BODY:
The sugar maples that line the roads here are a blaze of gold and red. The farmhouse where Robert Frost once lived and wrote poems has closed to visitors for the season. So has Clam Haven, the seafood shack down the road.
Still, this fall is not like all the others in Derry. For the first time in 20 years, the Democrats have set up a campaign office in what has been a staunchly Republican town in this staunchly Republican state. 
 "Derry has been a black hole for the Democrats," Michael King, the chairman of the New Hampshire Democratic Party, said at a recent celebration marking the opening of the new headquarters. "Democrats here have been afraid to come out of their houses."
Not this year.
Dozens of Democrats, and even some Republicans reveled on Main Street at the balloon-festooned campaign office in what used to be the Woman's Shop, next to J. J. Pizza. They wore buttons proclaiming their allegiance to Bill Clinton -- and Jeanne Shaheen.
Mrs. Shaheen, a three-term State Senator with a reputation as a pragmatist, is running for Governor.
The latest poll by the University of New Hampshire Survey Center for WMUR-TV in Manchester, released earlier this month, gave her a 19-point lead over her conservative Republican opponent, Ovide Lamontagne, the former chairman of the State Board of Education. The same poll showed that 30 percent of Republicans support Mrs. Shaheen. If she wins, she will be the first Democratic Governor in New Hampshire since 1980 -- and the first elected female chief executive in the state's history.
Rick and Susan Katzenberg, who are Democrats, recently gave a party for Mrs. Shaheen at their house in Amherst and most of the 80 guests were Republican. The Katzenbergs were delighted.
"We've never had this many Republicans in the house before," Mrs. Katzenberg, an antiques dealer, said as neighbors chatted over coffee in her living room.
Mrs. Shaheen's standing just over a week from Election Day parallels what is happening in national races in New Hampshire. Bill Clinton has a substantial lead here over his Republican challenger, Bob Dole, and Democrats are in competitive races for the Senate and the House.
This is New Hampshire? Some things used to be certain here. The leaves would change in fall. There would be no sales or income tax. Republicans, for the most part, would dominate the political scene.
"For the first time in history, this is a two-party state," said Dayton Duncan, who was the chief of staff to the last Democratic governor, Hugh Gallen, elected in 1978. "It used to be that the most Democrats could hope for was one or two races near the top that might be competitive. This is the first time Democrats can compete everywhere."
The turn of events for Democrats here has stunned John Ellis, a columnist for The Boston Globe, who has followed New Hampshire politics for 25 years. "It runs counter to everything I know," he said. "It's as if you told me Fidelity is in the business of making cars. I'd say, 'No, they're in the business of selling mutual funds.' I can't believe any of them can win in New Hampshire. It would be like Utah electing Kennedy and Dukakis."
To be sure, no one is ready to predict fundamental, or long-term change in New Hampshire.
"I don't think this is a repudiation of the Republican Party," said R. Kelly Myers, director of the University of New Hampshire Survey Center. "I don't think we're in the midst of a Democratic tide here. What we have is a unique set of circumstances befitting the Democratic Party. The economy has turned around sharply since Clinton took office. It's like night and day here. The President can take the credit for that. And Dole is not an attractive alternative."
As for the Governor's race, Mr. Kelly said, Mrs. Shaheen is a moderate at a time when moderate candidates are succeeding.
"She's running a good campaign," he said. "If you look at previous elections, the Democratic candidates in New Hampshire were not particularly attractive.
"Come back in two years. The Republicans could own everything again. And don't count them out yet -- even this year."
Tom Rath, a lawyer from Concord and longtime Republican political analyst, explained the developments this way: "Republicans here and all around the country are suffering an enthusiasm deficit. I don't think New Hampshire is changing. We have always been a centrist state. I don't think this means that the ideology or the political leanings of the state have actually changed. It's more a commentary that party identification is less important than it was."
He said that the bitter Republican Presidential primary turned off a lot of moderate Republicans. And the Granite State simply has never warmed up to Bob Dole.
Tom Marquis, a book distributor from Nashua, and a Republican, said that he will vote for Mr. Dole. But his support is hardly enthusiastic.
"I'd rather vote for three million people other than Bob Dole," he said, adding that he has ruled out Bill Clinton as untrustworthy.
In a state that fiercely proclaims its independence with the motto "Live Free or Die" on its license plates, one of the keys to Mrs. Shaheen's success was her decision to take "the pledge," as it is known, to oppose any income or sales tax. In doing so, she may have learned from the experience of a predecessor, Deborah Arnesen, a Democrat, who campaigned as the "tax lady" in the 1992 gubernatorial race and advocated a broad-based tax. She lost to Stephen Merrill, a Republican who is not seeking re-election. Ms. Arnesen is now in a close House race.
There is another explanation: The inclination of women to support Democrats nationally this year extends to New Hampshire. The University of New Hampshire poll shows Mrs. Shaheen, 49 and the mother of three, leading among women by 30 percentage points, though by only 8 points among men.
"She's bringing New Hampshire into the 21st century," said Maureen Barrows, an administrator at a mental hospital who lives in Exeter. "She's practical. She's someone who pays attention to detail. She's not someone who will waste our money."
Most of the guests at the Amherst house party were not only Republicans, they were women. They listened admiringly as Mrs. Shaheen talked about financing statewide kindergarten and lowering electric rates -- the state has the highest in the country.
Although she is not only trying to capitalize on her sex, women voters clearly relate. "She has that mom quality," saud Kitten Stearns, who intends to vote for Mrs. Shaheen.
Mrs. Stearns is a moderate Republican. So is Mrs. Barrows.
"Not all Republicans are right-wing extremists," Mrs. Barrows said.. "There's a group of us who are very, very moderate."
Mrs. Barrows, who is 60 and ran unsuccessfully for the House in 1992, said she was supporting Mr. Dole. Mr. Lamontagne is another story. Mrs. Barrows, who favors a woman's right to abortion, said some of his positions are extreme.
The Republican gubernatorial candidate has said that he opposes abortion except in cases where the mother's life is in danger. As chairman of the state board of education, he blocked the state from applying for Federal education money last year because, he said, there were too many strings attached. He also supported the teaching of creationism in public schools in the town of Merrimack, which was battling over the issue a year ago.
A lawyer, he has never held elective office.
A month ago, Mrs. Barrows confided her doubts about Mr. Lamontagne to another Republican in town. "I told him, 'I'd love to be brave enough to vote for Jeanne Shaheen,' " she said. "He said, 'Here's her home number. Call Billy.' "
Billy, as he is known in New Hampshire, is Bill Shaheen, a lawyer, who gave up his job as district judge to campaign for his wife. Mrs. Barrows told him she wanted to help -- without openly declaring her support. Then, two weeks ago, she decided to go public. "I said, 'What am I pussyfooting around for? This is nuts.' "
Susan McLane, a Republican who served 25 years as a member of the State Legislature, is also supporting Jeanne Shaheen -- and the Democratic candidates for Congress, and Bill Clinton. Last spring Mrs. McLane switched her political registration to Independent. Last month she became a Democrat.
"I was a liberal Republican," Mrs. McLane said. "The Republican Party changed. I decided it was dishonest to stay Republican."
It is the talk of the state. Mrs. McLane, whose husband is the grandson of a former Governor of New Hampshire, was an icon of Republican politics in New Hampshire. She was a state legislator for 25 years. She gave the first party for George Bush in New Hampshire, at her house in Concord, in 1978. Barbara Bush stayed at her lake house.
"I lent her my bathing suit," Mrs. McLane said. "She forgot hers."
Mrs. Shaheen was introduced at the Katzenbergs' party by Pixie Lown, a former Republican state legislator. This is the first time Mrs. Lown is supporting a Democratic candidate. She said she was driven to it by the candidacy of Mr. Lamontagne, whom she regards as extreme.
But Mr. Lamontagne, who is 39 and has three children, has appealed to the state's conservative residents.
At a recent forum sponsored by the Merrimack Taxpayers Association, the room was filled with his supporters, including April Sarasin, a 37-year-old school bus driver and mother of three. Describing herself as "pro-life," she said she was concerned that there was too much government and feared that tough economic times would return. "I'm concerned we're not going to be able to keep living in New Hampshire," Mrs. Sarasin said. "We're going to have to move. There are a lot of very vocal liberals moving into town."
Even so, the difficulty facing Republicans here was summed up by Duane Martin, who was taking a break between games at the Merrimack Ten Pin Bowling Center.
A self-described diehard Republican and a carpenter, Mr. Martin discounted the talk about an improving economy in New Hampshire.
"I'm not making any more money than I was 10 years ago," he said. "The rich are getting richer the poor are getting poorer. The Democrats are giving too much away; the government is out of control."
Nonetheless, he said: "I'm thinking of voting for Clinton."

SECTION: Section 1; Page 30; Column 1; National Desk

HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE SENATE;
The Race to Fill Dole's Senate Seat Is Testing the Appeal of Conservatives

BYLINE: By ERIC SCHMITT 

DATELINE: WICHITA, Kan., Oct. 23

 BODY:
It has been 64 years since Kansans elected a Democrat to the United States Senate.
But Patricia Bennett, a lifelong Republican, says she is voting for a Democrat, Jill Docking, to fill the last two years of the seat that Bob Dole quit in his quest for the Presidency. The Republican candidate, Representative Sam Brownback, is too conservative for her, Ms. Bennett said, especially on issues like gun control, education and abortion. 
 "Although Docking is a Democrat, she is a moderate with what I consider traditional Republican views on economic policy and balancing the budget, but she'd do it in a humane way," said Ms. Bennett, 36, of Kansas City, a lawyer whose father, Robert F. Bennett, was a Republican Governor of Kansas in the 1970's.
The big question in several Senate races, including those in Colorado, Georgia, Illinois and Wyoming, is whether conservative Republicans, often closely allied with the Christian right, are alienating moderates in their own party and driving them to vote Democratic this year.
Nowhere is this struggle thrown into sharper relief than in the cliffhanger between Mr. Brownback and Mrs. Docking, two affable, articulate 40-year-olds who both lay claim to the tradition of prairie pragmatism.
"Kansans have to figure out how far conservatives in the Republican Party here can go and still represent their traditional Republican values," said Burdett A. Loomis, a professor of political science at the University of Kansas.
Mr. Brownback, a lawyer from Topeka and a top lieutenant in Speaker Newt Gingrich's Republican revolution, said he had picked up the endorsements of many conservative Democrats.
But Republicans outnumber Democrats 3 to 2 in Kansas. So moderate Republicans, especially women, and a large group of independents are likely to decide a race that most analysts say is a dead heat.
Mrs. Docking faces an uphill battle, but the Docking name carries weight. A Massachusetts native, Jill Sadowsky married Tom Docking 19 years ago, and in the mid-1980's he became Lieutenant Governor. Both Mr. Docking's father and grandfather were popular conservative Democratic Governors of Kansas.
Mrs. Docking, a stockbroker from Wichita, is promising to carry on the family tradition in her first race, and she is hoping that the name recognition helps offset Mr. Brownback's 2-to-1 edge in campaign spending.
"I am a centrist," Mrs. Docking said at a candidates forum on Tuesday in Topeka, adding that she supports term limits, a balanced budget and cuts in capital-gains taxes.
While embracing Mr. Clinton's support of abortion rights and a ban on assault weapons, Mrs. Docking criticized the Administration for its ill-fated effort to overhaul health care. "They tried to turn it upside-down in an hour and a half, and it didn't work," she said.
Mrs. Docking even praises some goals of the Republican-led Congress, "like focusing on balancing the budget," before hammering home her main point: "But they were too extremist. They were ideological and arrogant, and they shut down the Government."
Mr. Brownback, who opposes most abortions and favors a moment of silence in schools, is backed by the powerful religious-right community, including the Christian Coalition, and benefits from the coalition's well-organized telephone banks and letter-writing campaigns.
"He's become a poster child for the conservatives," said Joseph K. Unekis, a professor of political science at Kansas State University.
Mr. Brownback warns that electing Mrs. Docking would shift control of the Senate to liberal Democrats. "Ted Kennedy will be chairing the Labor Committee," Mr. Brownback said at the forum. "Joe Biden will chair the Judiciary Committee."
In a blizzard of television commercials, Mr. Brownback accuses Mrs. Docking of being "very liberal" and of supporting tax increases that "even liberals like Bill Clinton do not support." Mr. Brownback supports a balanced-budget amendment, tort reform and a 10 percent pay cut for all members of Congress.
Unlike some Republican candidates, Mr. Brownback is not afraid to run on Congress's record. He lists the accomplishments -- new welfare and health-insurance laws, new farming programs and streamlining Congress -- and plays down Democratic assertions that Republicans tried to cut money for student loans, environmental protection and Medicare.
But like many other Republicans, Mr. Brownback has distanced himself from the unpopular Mr. Gingrich.
"The Speaker has been a strong political leader, but I've had my differences with him," Mr. Brownback said in an interview.
The race between Mrs. Docking and Mr. Brownback has overshadowed a second race, to fill the seat of 18-year Republican Senator Nancy Landon Kassebaum, who is retiring.
Pat Roberts, 60, a Republican in the House for eight terms who headed the House Agriculture Committee, holds a double-digit lead in local polls over the Democrat, State Treasurer Sally Thompson.
Ms. Thompson, 56, a former president of a savings and loan association, has attacked Mr. Roberts's future Congressional pension as excessive and his living in Washington for 30 years as evidence that he is out of touch with Kansans.
Mr. Roberts, whose dry wit rivals Mr. Dole's, boasts of going "toe to toe" with Mr. Gingrich to preserve the Food Stamp program, and has accused Ms. Thompson of mismanaging state money.
The campaign received scant notice outside Kansas until a bizarre incident two weeks ago. After a meeting with editors of The Kansas City Star, Mr. Roberts lingered in a third-floor corridor as Ms. Thompson walked ahead.
"I'm not getting into the elevator with the bitch," Mr. Roberts told his son David, 23, who was upset with Ms. Thompson's remarks about his father at the meeting.
Mr. Roberts later apologized publicly to Ms. Thompson. "It shows he doesn't have respect for women," Ms. Thompson said this week.
"I really regret it," Mr. Roberts said in an interview this week.
But he added, "I don't think it'll have much effect on the campaign."

HEADLINE: Tides of Change in Troubled St. Petersburg

BYLINE: By MIREYA NAVARRO 

DATELINE: ST. PETERSBURG, Fla., Oct. 26

 BODY:
After the arson and violence in this city's streets two nights ago, residents and officials here are just coming to grips with the unrest, which some say underscores the changing character of an area many still regard as a placid, picturesque town.
"This came as a complete surprise," said an 86-year-old woman who has lived here for 33 years and wanted to be identified only as "just a person who loves St. Petersburg." 
 "This is considered a little town," she added. Everybody is so family-oriented."
Ronald Akers, director of the Center for Studies in Criminology and Law at the University of Florida, agreed that the city, on the Gulf of Mexico, was considered an unlikely spot for a disturbance. "St. Petersburg is perceived as the place where old people live, a tranquil, relatively peaceful place with some good beaches," he said. "It has never been perceived as an urban hot spot."
But St. Petersburg's population has grown by about one-fifth since 1970, to 245,000, and city officials say the average age has dropped to 38 from 48 in the same period. Retirees account for barely a quarter of the residents, and they are increasingly home-grown. Retirees moving in from out of the state look away from urban centers like St. Petersburg to find a low cost of living and the amenities of retirement communities, city officials said.
While the rejuvenated city has been shedding the image of a retirement mecca for one of vibrancy -- the Tampa Bay Devil Rays will bring major league baseball to its domed stadium in 1998 -- it is also hobbled by some big-city problems, including higher unemployment and lower education levels among its black residents, who account for 19 percent of its population.
Urban problems, along with charges of heavy-handedness by the police, seem to be at the root of the disturbance on Thursday night, Mayor David J. Fischer said. "I think the failing is in the young adult group, the 18-to-28-year-old who feels disenfranchised and disillusioned," he said today. "We're going to do a lot of work evaluating what happened. Are we on the wrong path? Are we different than other cities? We're going to work real hard on that."
The site of the disturbances was calm today, and the police said Friday night had been quieter than usual. Except for a couple of arrests and several isolated incidents of arson and bottle-throwing, the night was uneventful for the 200 police officers on patrol, four times the usual number on a weekend.
A state of emergency, which bans any sales of gasoline in containers or guns, remained in force until Monday, however, and about 200 National Guardsmen were standing by.
A dozen people were injured and 28 fires were set after a crowd estimated in the hundreds reacted violently to the fatal shooting of a black teen-ager in his car by a white police officer on Thursday night in a predominantly black neighborhood in the southern part of the city. The police said the teen-ager, Tyron Lewis, 18, had been stopped for speeding but had refused to roll down his window and had been shot through the windshield when his car lurched forward toward the officer.
The police shooting was the second in the area within a few days -- the first involved a black man who the police said was killed by officers as he held a knife to his estranged wife. It was also the fifth this year involving moving vehicles and similar circumstances, and, in most cases, a black driver, police said.
But city officials expressed surprise at the violent reaction because they said racial tensions between the police and black residents had eased under Police Chief Darrel W. Stephens, who stepped up community policing efforts. He was hired in 1993 to deal with the worsening racial discord inside and outside the Police Department under his predecessor.
Residents of the area of the disturbance, however, said a policy of bringing police officers closer to the community through volunteer centers and more time spent in neighborhoods had not reduced police harassment. Black teen-agers complained of attracting police attention whenever they gathered in small groups, while a white resident, Don Mosley, 49, said he had often been stopped in his car for driving in his own neighborhood.
"They see a white person in a black community, and they think you're buying drugs," Mr. Mosley said.
Sevell C. Brown 3d, president of the St. Petersburg chapter of the Southern Christian Leadership Conference, said there was disappointment in Mayor Fischer, who was elected with the overwhelming support of black voters. He said the city had not made black residents' concerns about police harassment and other matters a priority, and he called the riot what happens "when you refuse to listen to the rumblings" of dissatisfaction.
"Look around and try to find a black club," he said. "Most blacks have to go to Tampa to entertain themselves."
Mayor Fischer, however, has focused on neighborhood revitalization projects, including in the area where the disturbance occurred.
Police Chief Stephens said that although there might be some racism among his 500 officers, 17 percent of them black, he did not believe that most of his officers singled out black residents for harassment.
"Clearly there's racism in our society, and we have those kinds of problems in our department," he said, "but our officers' behavior, by and large, is professional."
To insure peace, however, the city has invited the Federal Commission on Civil Rights, which had found an improvement in racial tensions between the police and the black community here in recent years, to come back again to evaluate the shooting incident and its aftermath. Community meetings are also scheduled.

HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE THIRD PARTY;
Where Perot Ran Strong, Loyalties Persist

SERIES: AN AMERICAN PLACE -- Looking for a Leader

BYLINE: By MICHAEL WINERIP 

DATELINE: CANTON, Ohio, Oct. 26

 BODY:
Stark is a political swing county where a lot of people -- 25 percent of the voters -- supported Ross Perot for President in 1992. As a result, it is an easy place to find a lot of people who are deserting Mr. Perot in 1996. Their reasons break down into four basic categories.
First, there is the "goofy" problem. "He had an original story that appealed to me last time," said Frederick Mottice, a lineman for a power company. "But now he might be a little goofy. He thinks people are out to get him." Jim Lenart, a program analyst, mentioned this factor, too. "I still like what he stands for," Mr. Lenart said. "But a lot of people are saying maybe he's a little goofy." 
 There is also the vanishing allure problem, similar to what middle-aged couples experience before a divorce. "I thought Perot in '92 was something different, something special," said Ron Patterson, a computer technician. "But now he just seems like a rich guy."
Then there is the tough-talking, plain-spoken quitter issue. "He sounded like a guy who would really make something happen," said Mike Kelly, a union auto worker. "But then he changed his mind too many times, being in, being out, being in, being out. I don't like quitters."
And finally, there is the wasted vote syndrome. Keith George, an engineering administrator, still likes Mr. Perot's economic policies and his character, but will not vote for him because he does not appear to be electable. "I certainly don't want to waste my vote again, and that's what I did," said Mr. George, a Republican. "After the '92 election, a good friend said, 'If all of you who'd voted for Perot supported Bush, he'd have won.' It was a bad feeling."
And yet, even with all these problems, and seemingly insurmountable odds, it is striking from interviews with several dozen people who voted for Mr. Perot in 1992 that many of them here -- perhaps half -- are sticking by him. They fear for the local manufacturing economy, they like his economic ideas, they hate politicians, they think it is important to vote their conscience, they think that even if he seems a little paranoid it does not mean people are not out to get him ("Look at the debates," one said). And several said that they simply did not believe the polls, that the polls were wrong or that the polls were made up, and that Mr. Perot could win.
They think Mr. Perot is having an effect on the national political dialogue on issues like the deficit, and they got a chuckle out of Bob Dole's overture to him this week. "Mr. Dole is sounding a little desperate to me," said Al Coppock, a retired truck salesman and a Perot supporter.
(Not that the President impresses them. "Clinton's moral character? A shambles," said David Bibby, a technical director for a maker of hand-held computers, who is sticking with Mr. Perot.)
In 1992, Mr. Perot received 25 percent of the vote in Stark County and 21 percent in Ohio, compared with 19 percent nationally. Ohio is a leading manufacturing state, and Stark's four biggest employers make steel, roller bearings, vacuum cleaners and automated teller machines. The sucking sound of jobs heading south is a worry here, even in these relatively good economic times.
Every Tuesday morning at the Denny's on West Tuscarawas Avenue, about a dozen Perot sympathizers, mostly blue collar, middle class and union members, meet for breakfast. This week they griped about taxes ("too high"), government ("too much") and media conspiracies ("very suspicious"). Their talk is filled with "Gatts" and "Naftas," the free trade agreements, which they think will be their downfall.
Richard Cordes, a union steelworker at the Timken Company, has 10 copies of a video called "Vanishing Jobs," which chronicles the movement of American factories to Mexico and which he lends to co-workers. "They're all loaned out," he said. He keeps Perot literature in his toolbox at the steel mill.
Since the end of Patrick J. Buchanan's Presidential campaign, the Perot supporters are about the only ones here talking passionately about the problems with the economy. The Democrats have no incentive to discuss it: good economic news is Mr. Clinton's ticket to re-election. Mr. Dole's supporters say the economy could be better and talk about his 15 percent cut proposal, but they are not about to attack their fellow Republicans in the boardrooms who are making the decisions to downsize and outsource and send jobs south.
The economy here is a lot better than it was four years ago, but it is still a concern for the average worker. On the plus side, unemployment is about half the 1992 rate. Diebold and the Timken Company are posting record profits, and analysts say the Hoover Company, a division of Maytag, is also performing strongly. In the last several years, Diebold has added white-collar jobs, and Timken has made an unprecedented number of foreign and national purchases this year. It has also expanded existing plants.
On the minus side, Hoover decided this summer to build its newest line of upright vacuums at a nonunion plant in El Paso. It said it was trying to stay competitive with Eureka, which long ago moved much of its vacuum cleaner manufacturing to El Paso and Juarez, Mexico. Over the last dozen years, hundreds of union manufacturing jobs at Diebold have been lost to nonunion plants in the South and to outsourcing, and the plant workers who remain here make less than they did in 1983.
A unionized Ford plant closed here in the late 1980's, while nonunion companies like DLH Industries, which supplies parts to General Motors and pays a few dollars an hour above minimum wage, have grown. The county's second biggest steel company, Republic Engineered Steels, is having its worst year in history as it tries to stay competitive with small, nonunion mills in the South.
The good and bad are epitomized by the Strip, a huge new suburban shopping complex in Jackson. When finished, it will have more than a dozen anchor stores and generate thousands of new jobs. But most of those jobs will pay little above minimum wage.
"No matter how Ross does, these problems are not going away," Mr. Coppock said. "We're getting jobs we don't need in this community."
The Denny's group is not about to get discouraged, even if Mr. Perot appears far behind. Many, like Tom Heatherly, are used to being far behind. In a 1994 Republican primary race for county commission, Mr. Heatherly ran fourth out of four, collecting 12 percent of the vote.
But they have also had their victories. Mr. Coppock, Mr. Heatherly and Mr. Cordes were part of a small group that had practically no money yet led successful campaigns to defeat seven proposed sales tax increases in recent years, nearly paralyzing Stark County government more than once.
Today at midday, they were to meet at the county's main library and caravan to the Youngstown area, where Mr. Perot was scheduled to speak. As might be expected with Perot supporters, before agreeing to caravan they discussed whether it would be better to go their separate ways and meet at the speech. In contrast, when the local Democrats went to hear Mr. Clinton speak in Cleveland on Monday, they rented two buses.
Although several of the Denny's regulars have been on the edge of local politics for years, the typical Perot supporters interviewed do not want to be that close, which is precisely why they are sticking with their man.
Richard Heasley, a union steelworker who calls the Reform Party candidate "a breath of fresh air," was not bothered that Mr. Perot was excluded from the debates. "Nah," he said, "more people are interested in the baseball."
Asked whether he worried about wasting his vote, Billy Roseberry, a retired ironworker, said: "I think you should vote for who you want to win or lose. You should vote for who you support. If everyone figured they wouldn't vote for a guy who doesn't have a chance, we'd never have anything."
As for the national polls showing Mr. Perot in single digits, why should they trust polls any more than politicians? they ask. "The polls may say 5 percent," said Tony DeStephen, a draftsman, "but maybe things will change. You never know. It's early."
And Dee Clapsaddle, a medical bookkeeper, wonders whether these pollsters really even talk to anyone. "I'm very skeptical," she said. "I've never met anyone who was polled."
Living midway between Democratic Cleveland and Republican Columbus, the citizens of Canton, Ohio, and surrounding Stark County have a history of voting for the winning Presidential candidate. This is the 32d article in a yearlong series that explores the forces shaping the political debate at the grass roots. (Previous articles are available to computer users on The New York Times Web, at http://www.nytimes.com/specials/canton.)

HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE RACE FACTOR;
Incumbency Helps Black Candidate in Redrawn District

BYLINE: By STEVEN A. HOLMES 

DATELINE: ALBANY, Ga., Oct. 23

 BODY:
Shaking hands with elderly residents at a candidates forum today, Representative Sanford D. Bishop Jr., a black Democrat, was approached by a white man. "I've followed you for some time," the man said, extending his hand. "Your record speaks for itself."
As he seeks re-election in the newly redrawn Second Congressional District, Mr. Bishop is hoping that many white voters will assess him in the same way, by record and not skin color. Elected in a district drawn specifically to have a majority of black voters, he is now running in a district that was redrawn after the Supreme Court had disallowed what it said was blatant racial gerrymandering. 
 Mr. Bishop is one of five black incumbent Democrats in the South in that position. In his case, the proportion of the black population was reduced, to 35 percent from 52.
Yet Mr. Bishop is considered the favorite, as are the four others. Victories by most or all of them may provide provocative lessons about the power of incumbency, the success of race-based affirmative-action programs that give blacks the chance to be judged ultimately on their performance and ideology and not their race, and, perhaps, the lessening of race as a factor in Southern politics.
"I would like to think that with a win that we have matured in the South and that we are saying to the nation that the old stigma that has been attached to the South is no longer applicable," Mr. Bishop said in an interview.
As state legislatures, prodded by recent Supreme Court decisions, drastically redrew maps and reduced the numbers of blacks in several districts in Georgia, Louisiana, Florida and Texas, many civil rights leaders grimly predicted a reduction in the number of black members in the House.
But the black incumbents in those districts appear to be running well. Mr. Bishop and another black incumbent, Cynthia A. McKinney, easily won their primaries in Georgia and are leading their Republican opponents, though Ms. McKinney's race has tightened considerably in recent weeks after accusations that she had been a defender of the Nation of Islam minister, Louis Farrakhan.
In Florida, another incumbent, Corrine D. Brown, is well ahead of her Republican rivals, and Eddie Bernice Johnson in Dallas and Sheila Jackson Lee in Houston are leading in their districts.
"It's better than we would have expected, given the blood letting that went on in the Supreme Court," said Penda Hair, a lawyer for the NAACP Legal Defense and Education Fund.
There are some exceptions. In Louisiana, Representative Cleo Fields, whose district dropped to less than 30 percent black registered voters from 64 percent as a result of redistricting, said his chances of winning in the new district were slim and declined to run again.
Political scientists, experts in redistricting and the candidates themselves attribute the black candidates' strong showing to the power of incumbency, a status that many say the black candidates would have never achieved had the majority black districts not been created.
Having been in Congress the lawmakers have compiled records, achieved some name recognition and gained access to major campaign donors, achievements that might otherwise have been out of reach to black challengers.
"The central question, I believe, is not what happens to the first generation after redistricting, but what happens to the next generation," said Ms. McKinney, who is running in a district that is 32 percent black after having first been elected in a district that was more than 60 percent black.
Some who have studied the records of the black incumbents say what the next generation of black politicians will do is what black politicians like Mr. Bishop have been doing all along, position themselves in the center or the right of the political spectrum and try to increase their appeal to whites.
Since his election in 1992, Mr. Bishop has assiduously tried remain in step with his overwhelmingly rural, intensely conservative district in southwestern Georgia. He became the only black lawmaker to join the blue dogs, a group of fiscally conservative Democrats, mainly from the South. He was given a seat on the Agriculture Committee, where he has been zealous in protecting subsidies for peanut and cotton farmers. And he has looked out for the three military bases in his district.
"You got a lot of people who want to send messages, angry white males and whatnot," said James Lee Adams Jr., a white farmer who supports Mr. Bishop. "But Sanford brings home the bacon and makes sure it's in all the right smokehouses."
In his latest race, Mr. Bishop is taking no chances. Before the district was redrawn, he voted to ban the sale and manufacture of some semiautomatic weapons. After redistricting, he voted to repeal the ban.
To counter charges from his Republican opponent that he is a liberal, Mr. Bishop promotes his positions in favor of the death penalty, for the welfare bill that President Clinton signed and for an end to a Federal AIDS prevention program for homosexual men.
"For any politician, he or she has got to be responsible to the needs of their constituents, whoever those constituents are," Mr. Bishop said. "I've tried to be the kind of Congressman, no matter how they draw the lines, people will say he's done a good job."
The question then becomes will it be enough for whites to overcome the racially polarized voting patterns that have been the norm here.
"If it turns out, after all, that people are going to vote against him simply because he's black, then that says something pretty bad about the continuing problems in race relations in the United States," said David Bositis, a senior fellow at the Joint Center for Political and Economic Studies in Washington.

HEADLINE: Candidates Both Try to Appear Hawkish on Tax Cuts

BYLINE: By ALAN FINDER 

 BODY:
When it comes to taxes and the Federal budget, Representative Robert G. Torricelli says, there is really little separating him from Representative Richard A. Zimmer, his opponent for the United States Senate seat from New Jersey.
"I voted for a tax cut last year, so did Dick Zimmer," Mr. Torricelli said in a recent debate. "I voted for the balanced budget amendment, so did Dick Zimmer." 
 But Mr. Zimmer, a Republican with a long record as a fiscal conservative, contends that there are sharp distinctions between himself and Mr. Torricelli, a Democrat. He views Mr. Torricelli as a late convert to tax and spending reductions.
As is often the case with the positioning that goes on in election campaigns, both candidates are essentially correct. Mr. Torricelli did vote last year for tax cuts proposed by Republican members of Congress, and he has voted in recent years for the balanced budget amendment, after initially opposing it. The measure was passed by the House but failed last year to win approval by the Senate.
But there are also significant differences in emphasis between the two men when they talk, and vote, on Federal spending and taxing. Mr. Zimmer is right when he asserts that he has been, in his words, "a hawk" on cutting spending and taxes and eliminating the Federal deficit, and that he has been one longer and more passionately than Mr. Torricelli.
The candidates' views on a host of other major issues -- education, health care, the environment and social programs, among others -- are shaped by their fundamental priorities on Federal spending. Mr. Zimmer places greater importance on shrinking the size and scope of Government, while Mr. Torricelli has a somewhat more activist view of Government.
Their disagreement is not over whether the Federal budget should be balanced, but how it should be done.
Mr. Zimmer places a priority on cutting spending and taxes, arguing that reducing taxes would give Americans more of their own income to invest or spend. That, in turn, would cause the economy to grow much more quickly than it has in recent years, he contends, so all Americans would be better off.
"You'll be making more money," he told workers at an eyeglass factory last week. "You can save what you earn, and we in the Government will take away less."
Mr. Zimmer, who says he would never vote for a measure that resulted in a net increase in taxes, has tried to differentiate himself from Mr. Torricelli by highlighting the Democrat's votes in favor of tax increases during his seven terms in Congress. Mr. Zimmer particularly points to President Clinton's five-year economic plan, which was passed by Congress, with Mr. Torricelli's support, in 1993. The plan significantly cut the size of the Federal deficit, by reducing spending and raising taxes.
"I've come to understand that when you raise taxes, elected legislators spend every dollar of the new revenue, and then some," Mr. Zimmer said during a recent interview. "And that's why I've become such a hawk on cutting spending."
Mr. Zimmer has voted with Republican Congressional leaders over the last two years to trim the budgets of some Federal agencies and to cut the rate of growth in other Federal programs. He says he would cut not only social programs favored by liberals but also military and space programs that have been supported by conservatives.
"The only way we are going to balance the budget is by putting the broad range of spending programs on the table," Mr. Zimmer said.
Mr. Torricelli says he, too, wants to eliminate the Federal deficit. But like the President and countless other Democrats around the country this fall, he has been emphasizing the preservation of certain programs -- particularly those favored by the middle class, like Medicare, environmental protection and educational aid -- while also bringing Federal spending in line with Federal revenues.
Mr. Torricelli favors a cut in the capital gains tax -- as does Mr. Zimmer -- which Mr. Torricelli says would spur economic growth by increasing investments. He also supports the targeted tax reductions proposed by the President for families with children.
But Mr. Torricelli spends more time talking about Mr. Zimmer's fiscal ideas than his own, in the apparent belief that voters are more worried this year about Republican-sponsored cuts in popular Government programs than they are about reducing taxes.
Mr. Torricelli's television advertisements have concentrated on services Mr. Zimmer has voted to cut, either by reducing actual spending or, in the case of Medicare, by cutting a program's rate of growth. Mr. Zimmer insists that he has never voted to reduce Medicare. But Mr. Torricelli contends that cutting the program's rate of growth while the number of elderly people is growing guarantees that there is less Federal money available for each individual's care.
"With baby boomers retiring and a proposed $270 billion reduction in Medicare spending, there would be $1,700 less available per person on Medicare and a nearly $1,000 increase in their premiums," Mr. Torricelli said in a recent interview.
"Mr. Zimmer can say that's not a cut," he said. "But in the real world in which a senior citizen lives, they're both paying more and having less money available for their services."
Mr. Torricelli has attacked Mr. Zimmer for supporting a Republican bill that would have cut by 25 percent the budget of several Federal agencies, including the Environmental Protection Agency. Mr. Zimmer responded that, because he thought many of the agencies should have been cut, he reluctantly supported the proposed reduction to the E.P.A.
In the end, Mr. Torricelli is presenting himself to voters as someone who will protect Federal services that they care about, while simultaneously balancing the budget. Mr. Zimmer offers himself as someone who is much more determined to cut spending and taxes, which would make Americans better off.

HEADLINE: Cuban Artists Abroad Straddle Two Worlds;
Ideological Pressure Intrudes on Acclaim

BYLINE: By MIRTA OJITO 

 BODY:
They dance on New York's most famed stages and organize the latest downtown art exhibitions. They perform in respected tiny theaters and play their music in big outdoor spaces. They are young and talented and they are Cuban. Unfailingly, they draw big crowds.
Yet when the curtain comes down and the show is over, these Cubans go home. 
 "Some of the people I know think I'm crazy for wanting to return," said Alfredo Alonso, 29, the lead actor in "Vagos Rumores" ("Vague Rumors"), a Cuban play at the Gramercy Arts Theater. "They are betting I won't return, but I will."
Mr. Alonso, who plays a 19th-century Cuban poet who was critical of slavery and Spain's domination but who refused to go into exile as many of his compatriots did, says he would not have come to the United States if it had meant leaving Cuba for good.
The artists' desire to go home, and the fact that the Cuban Government allows them to, sets them apart from hundreds of thousands of Cubans who in the last 37 years have had to renounce country and family to work in the United States.
But the privilege comes at a price: they must straddle an ideological fence in a politically charged situation that for almost four decades has demanded clear definitions.
"The first thing people want to know when they hear I come from Cuba is am I or am I not against the Cuban Government," said Juan Carlos Cremata, 34, a film maker who lives in Manhattan and plans to return soon to the island after five years of world travel. "All these questions are unnecessary."
And the answers are seldom clear.
At a time of increasing tension between the two countries, brought about by Cuba's downing of two United States planes in February and Washington's swift reaction to tighten travel and trade with the island, the artists stand on uncharted terrain. Neither government spokesmen nor government critics, they walk an ideological fine line.
"If you have been here too long and return to Cuba, people think you are C.I.A.," Mr. Cremata said. "And if you've been in Cuba too long and then come here, people think you work for the state security apparatus. There is paranoia everywhere."
Adding to the artists' balancing act is a troublesome fact: rejection by members of the Cuban exile community, who assume that a decision to return to Cuba is an implicit statement of support for Fidel Castro.
For Cuban artists working in the United States, openness about their intentions could cost them performance contracts and clients. A Miami concert by the Cuban singer Rosita Fornes was recently canceled after exiles threatened mass protests outside the theater. In New York, where the singer performed for four nights in July at the Gramercy Arts Theater, some 60 exiles staged demonstrations the first two nights outside the theater and local Spanish-language papers carried scathing editorials. But the show went on.
Protests have not held back New York audiences, who flock to see these artists precisely because they live in Cuba and, some believe, bring with them the more authentic sounds and rhythms of the island.
But there are political reasons as well that draw certain audiences to Cuban art, particularly those who advocate closer relations between the two countries. After decades of cold war tension, these artists are the only bridge over the chasm that divides the two countries.
Foundations and nonprofit organizations that bring many of the artists here say they relish their groundbreaking role. "We see this as a very positive experience," said Holly Block, executive director of Art in General, an alternative nonprofit art organization in TriBeCa that has brought four artists from the island in less than a year and plans to bring five more soon. "It foments dialogue, it creates discussion and it helps to clear some of the stereotypes about what's going on in Cuba."
In the last few months alone, New Yorkers have been exposed to an onslaught of Cuban artists.
Jose Manuel Carreno, a principal dancer with American Ballet Theater, danced to rave reviews in the spring and will tour with the company early next year. The musician and composer Chucho Valdes packed the Village Vanguard every night a few weeks ago. Gerardo Mosquera, an art critic, is a curator at the New Museum in SoHo. Two members of a trio called Los Carpinteros (the Carpenters) participated in a recent TriBeCa art exhibition.
Earlier this year, Alexis (Kcho) Leyva Machado had a well-received one-man show at the Barbara Gladstone Gallery in SoHo. And Grupo AfroCuba de Matanzas, a music and folkloric dance group, gave summer concerts at Lincoln Center, at the Apollo Theater in Harlem and at the Metropolitan Museum of Art.
The concept of artists living and working abroad, particularly in the United States, after 35 years of embargo against the island, is relatively new in Cuba. Before 1990, the Cuban Government allowed few artists or performers to visit here.
Observers of Cuban affairs say that things began to change rapidly as the country's economic crisis deepened after the loss of cash subsidies from the former Soviet Union. No longer able to support its artists -- or, some say, looking to squelch growing dissent among the intellectual and artistic elite who expected Cuba to democratize as Eastern bloc countries did -- the Government began to relax the exit-permit rules.
"If we can no longer provide materials and perks for them, it's unfair for us to ask them not to seek them elsewhere," said Roberto Robaina, Cuba's Foreign Minister, during a recent visit here. "So we have to let them go and do their own thing, wherever their work takes them."
Their work takes them throughout the world, from Buenos Aires to Caracas, Madrid to Istanbul, but the United States is their country of choice. And New York, with its thirst for art and new talent, is the city of their dreams.
"This is where I always wanted to dance," said Mr. Carreno, 28. "I love New York."
In 1995, the State Department issued travel visas to 84 Cuban artists, entertainers and professionals of "extraordinary ability"; 23 of them came as temporary workers, State Department officials say. In 1989, only 4 Cubans received the same type of visa.
"They are coming in droves," said Manuel Gonzalez, a corporate curator in Manhattan. "It's a free-for-all."
Yet it is never an easy journey. Because of embargo restrictions, Cuban professionals who wish to enter the United States must seek special visas and their sponsors must secure a license from the Treasury Department to pay for everything from plane tickets to salaries.
Most of the artists receive just a modest per diem. Each of the six members of the theater company that brought "Vagos Rumores" to Manhattan, for example, receives $56 a day for food and transportation from Repertorio Espanol. If they eat frugally, they have money left over to please their whims.
"I may not be able to eat for the rest of the month, but I'm going to see a Broadway show," said Adria Santana, 48, the female lead in "Vagos Rumores."
These much-sought-after trips offer a rare chance to earn dollars and buy scarce products, from soap to leather shoes, and they also provide the artists with an opportunity to expand their knowledge and create new markets for their art. The artists, who say they shun politics and are here for professional reasons only, are careful not to jeopardize their travel privileges by insulting the political sensitivities of their hosts and audiences and of the Cuban Government, which controls exit visas. They measure every word, especially to strangers.
"You never know if the person next to you likes the Cuban Government or not," said a painter who recently came to New York and flew briefly to Miami to seek new clients. Speaking on condition of anonymity, he insisted that he was "a free man" who refused to be manipulated by other people's ideologies. But remember, he said before he left, "I don't want this story to ruin the Miami market for me."
Mr. Cremata, saying he is tired of demagogy on both sides, declines to discuss politics. His father was killed when anti-Castro terrorists blew up a plane over the Caribbean in 1976.
"I saw the beginning of the movie and I want to see how it ends," he said when asked why he wants to return to Cuba, using an apt metaphor for a film maker but saying little about his feelings toward the Cuban Government. "And there's also my family; I don't want to leave them behind."
Aside from ties to family and friends at home, the artists working here also say they have sound business reasons not to put roots down in New York. Without support from the sponsors who brought them here, they would have to compete for jobs on the open market, a difficult skill to acquire for artists who have been government trained and supported.
"I know actresses who are working as clerks during the day in some stores and don't get a chance to perform," Ms. Santana said. "I don't want that kind of life. In Cuba, no matter how difficult things are, I can do what I love to do."
Arturo Cuenca, a conceptual artist who defected in 1991 after two years of playing the role his colleagues are now assuming, is critical of their on-the-fence position, calling it comfortable but dishonest.
"It's very easy to let the Cuban Government back your career. It's a lot more difficult to go at it alone," said Mr. Cuenca, 40, adding that his career suffered after he defected, because he lost the cachet of being a Cuban living in Cuba. "Once you become an exile and make statements against the Cuban Government, the doors of the art community, for the most part, are closed."
The artists, who know they lead a privileged life, are taking advantage of their newly found quasi-freedom.
Ms. Santana has traveled to three countries with "Vagos Rumores" and to 10 more with a monologue she will also perform here after the play closes in November. Mr. Carreno has danced in Europe, Asia and the United States for six years. Mr. Cremata has traveled through Europe and Latin America thanks to a 16-minute black-and-white experimental film he made in 1990.
"Every time my film gets selected for a festival anywhere, I have to go with it," he said. "I will not send the film alone. That little film has allowed me to see the world. People ask us to choose, but why should we?
"We are opening a door," Mr. Cremata said. "And my job as an artist is to open doors, not to close them."

HEADLINE: Mayor Seeks to End Link Of Mob to Food Markets

BYLINE: By SELWYN RAAB 

 BODY:
In a new tactic to combat organized crime's influence at New York City's five major wholesale food markets, the Giuliani administration wants the authority to investigate and ban officials of unions who represent market employees and are suspected of having ties to the mob.
The administration's request is contained in the draft of a proposed law before the City Council that for the first time would empower city officials to expel union officers if they conclude they are connected to Mafia figures or to corruption. 
 "We can't clean up the markets without addressing the traditional role organized crime has played in these unions," said Councilman Kenneth K. Fisher of Brooklyn, one of the chief sponsors of the bill.
A draft of the legislation was criticized by some labor leaders as an improper intrusion into union activities that would give City Hall unusual authority to scrutinize the backgrounds of officers, require them to be fingerprinted and disqualify them for acts of misconduct related to "the orderly operation of the market."
A major union leader in the city, Assemblyman Brian M. McLaughlin of Queens, who is the president of the New York City Central Labor Council, said that the group did not contest the city's right to conduct background investigations or the need to rid the markets of mobsters and their allies.
But Mr. McLaughlin, in an interview, described the proposed version of the law as too vague in specifying the reasons the city could use to bar officials from union posts and employees from holding market jobs.
"This bill, as it is presently written, puts into the hands of a single city official, the Commissioner of Business Services, unfettered discretion to decide what is a reasonable cause to remove someone," Mr. McLaughlin added. "That is our main objection, plus the fact that the bill is way too vague and way too broad."
The council is the coordinating and lobbying body for almost 500 A.F.L.-C.I.O. locals representing 1.5 million members.
Spokesmen for the three main unions in the markets denied that their units were under mob control and questioned the constitutionality of the bill.
"This is going to be fought to the Supreme Court," said Anthony Cirillo, the president of a Fulton Fish Market union that is one of the targets of the bill.
Aides to Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani and some City Council members say the law is needed to invigorate a campaign begun last year to reform the markets through stricter city regulations and oversight. The law would cover the Fulton Fish Market in lower Manhattan, the Hunts Point Produce Market in the Bronx, the Gansevoort Meat Market in Greenwich Village, the Brooklyn Terminal Market in Sunset Park and the Brooklyn Wholesale Meat Market in Canarsie.
Randy M. Mastro, the Deputy Mayor for Operations and one of the chief designers of the bill, said the legislation would not infringe on traditional union rights to organize members, handle grievances, organize boycotts and negotiate contracts with employers. He said that the bill was modeled on laws in New Jersey and in Nevada that allowed state agencies that regulated casinos to eject union officials for Mafia links or misconduct. Those laws have been upheld by the Federal courts.
The five markets covered by the bill are important assets of the city economy. They handle most of the fresh fish and unpackaged fruit, vegetables and meat shipped into the New York region and then sold to retail stores and restaurants. More than 200 businesses, mainly wholesale dealers, in the markets generated more than $3 billion in sales last year and employed 8,000 people.
City and law-enforcement officials assert that for decades mobsters, by handpicking union officials at the markets, extorted money from merchants in return for labor peace, demanded no-show jobs for mobsters and their relatives and got sweetheart union contracts for mob-run companies. The Mafia, the officials say, also used the markets as bases for gambling, loan sharking and money laundering operations as well as for narcotics trafficking.
Mr. Mastro said the proposed law was mostly directed at three union locals in the markets whose officials in the last two decades were linked through convictions and indictments to the Gambino, Genovese and Lucchese crime families.
The unions are the United Seafood Workers Local 359 of the United Food and Commercial Workers, which represents more than 300 employees at the Fulton Fish Market; Local 174 of the United Food and Commercial Workers, which represents 800 employees at the Hunts Point and the Gansevoort Markets, and Local 202 of the International Brotherhood of Teamsters, with 1,400 members at Hunts Point.
Mr. Cirillo, Local 359's president, said the administration had singled out his union for retaliation because a Federal judge in 1989 rejected a racketeering suit brought by Mr. Giuliani when he was a Federal prosecutor that asserted the local was a tool of the Genovese family.
Daniel J. Kane of the teamsters' Local 202 said the bill "is overkill" because the local had ousted all mob-tainted officials and was being monitored by both court-appointed and independent investigators.
A spokesman for Local 174 and the national Food and Commercial Workers Union, Richard Lipsky, said that the union would lobby to "remove onerous clauses from the bill that abrogate basic union prerogatives and interfere with existing jurisdiction by the National Labor Relations Board."
Last year, the City Council approved a bill proposed by Mayor Giuliani that granted the city the right to determine who could work and operate businesses at the fish market. That law, however, did not apply to unions or their officials at the fish market.
Hearings on the new bill will be held on Thursday, Friday and on Nov. 7 at City Hall before the City Council's Economic Development Committee.
