HEADLINE: Hank Williams. Garth Brooks. BR5-49?

BYLINE: By Peter Applebome; Peter Applebome is chief national education correspondent for The New York Times. This is adapted from "Dixie Rising: How the South Is Shaping American Values, Politics and Culture," to be published in November by Times Books/Random House.

 BODY:
LATE ONE AFTERNOON IN AUGUST 1995, TIM DUBOIS AND THE FOLKS AT THE Arista/Nashville record company began trickling into Robert's Western World, a combination honky tonk and seedy boot store with ridiculous, turquoise-colored booths and ancient bar stools in sunflower patterns. DuBois, a former songwriter ("She Got the Gold Mine -- I Got the Shaft"), senior financial analyst at the Federal Reserve Bank of Dallas and accounting professor, sat at the first table sipping a Miller Lite and staring at the ratty blue stage where five young musicians in Salvation Army Western wear were ripping through a manic set of primordial rockabilly and country music from the 40's, 50's and 60's. The music was so antediluvian that today's slick country radio would find most of it as alien as a Kurdish folk song.
For a while, DuBois watched with a half smile, sometimes stroking his chin as if pondering a really enticing riddle. But as the show went on, you could see that he was just enjoying himself, smiling at the dumb patter and serio-comic tip-grubbing and then grinning widely during an apocalyptic version of "Red Hot," with its immortal lyric, "My gal is red hot, your gal ain't doodly squat." When it was over, DuBois walked up to one of the singers, Chuck Mead, and handed him two $20 bills. It wasn't clear if that meant, "I really love what you guys do, but I don't think I can make this work," or "I really love what you guys do. Let's talk." 
 It turned out to be the latter, and the result last month was the first studio recording by a band called BR5-49. (It's the phone number used by a hapless car dealer in a gag on the old TV show "Hee Haw.") The album, a collection of hopelessly retro, twangy howls from Nashville's past, is totally unsuited to today's country music radio -- which has quietly become the nation's largest radio format, with 2,600 radio stations, 1,400 more than the next format.
DuBois, who as president of Arista/Nashville built country music's biggest success story over the past six years, understands as much as anyone the demographic and musical currents that have made country the biggest growth area in American entertainment. Total record sales have more than quadrupled in seven years to $2.1 billion. In 1990, 39 country albums "went gold" (500,000 copies) or platinum (a million copies). In 1995, it was 135. Country's top artist, Garth Brooks, has sold 60 million albums in only seven years, making him second only to the Beatles in certified United States album sales.
But what DuBois and almost everyone in Nashville is trying to figure out is how to keep that momentum. For all its recent growth, country music is stalling. The 135 gold or platinum albums in 1995 were down a notch from the 143 in 1994, sales were down 11 percent for the first half of 1996 compared with the same period last year and almost everyone agrees that country radio is glutted with sound-alike, look-alike, toothy young Garth clones. These days, no one can tell Rick Trevino from Tracy Byrd, from Jeff Carson, from Clay Walker, from Kenny Chesney, from John Michael Montgomery, from Doug Stone, from Ty England.
"The record companies are feeding radio nothing but safe, lame, ear candy," says Evelyn Shriver, an acerbic publicist, voicing the minority viewpoint that a major shakeout is coming, like what happened at the end of the urban cowboy craze of the early 1980's. "It sounds fine to 14-year-olds, but for anyone else, how much of this can you take? The crash is coming, and I'd rather have it come sooner than later, because when you have chaos you always produce something new."
The majority view is that country has grown so far so fast that a pause and some casualties are probably inevitable, but that what's happening now is more a breathing spell than a prelude to a major tailspin.
"We probably peaked in 1994, and we flattened out in 1995 and we'll probably be flat again this year," says Ed Benson, executive director of the Country Music Association, the industry's trade group. "But last year we had 61 different artists certified gold or platinum. We've had the kind of growth that any industry would kill for. What's different now is that country music is part of the entertainment fabric of America in a way it's never been before. We've had a lot of growth, and we'll proably see a shakeout of some of the players before long. But we're at a level that's so much higher than ever before, no one's panicking. The bottom isn't going to fall out."
Not, anyway, if people like Tim DuBois can help it.
COUNTRY MUSIC HAS PROSPERED LIKE never before during the 90's for lots of reasons. Most white baby boomers, other than urban sophisticates on the two coasts, are turned off by rap, dance, hip-hop, punk and the other subsets of modern urban music. The generation that grew up on the Eagles and James Taylor is finding that the blend of folk, country and California-style rock that Nashville churns out sounds more like the rock they remember than Smashing Pumpkins or Dr. Dre ever will. And country found a way to get young and sexy in a culture that demands both.
Most of all, country has prospered because the nation is moving South and West and from cities to suburbs (or cities like Nashville that feel like suburbs). And in a nation increasingly shaped by the conservative politics and values of the South, most people would take the world of roots, retro values and simple pleasures represented by Vince Gill or Trisha Yearwood over the world of dislocation, urban grit and cultural dissonance represented by the Fugees or Tupac Shakur.
No one caught the boom that transformed Nashville better than Tim DuBois, the son of two Oklahoma schoolteachers, who had never worked for a record company until he was tapped by Clive Davis, head of Arista records, to start Arista/ Nashville in 1989. Since then, the company has sold 55 million records, and DuBois has vied with Tony Brown, president of country's leading label, MCA/Nashville, for the unofficial title of the Nashville executive with the golden touch.
DuBois, at 48, has graying hair combed straight back and a salt-and-pepper goatee that's mostly salt. He speaks in an Oklahoma twang and projects a genial, plain-folks quality that's almost Clinton-esque in its disarming qualities. He's the kind of guy who, when he hits one of his customarily lame chip shots in the general direction of the green at a charity golf tournament, can get away with a line like: "It's on the dance floor, but I'm not sure you can hear the band."
When DuBois was hired at Arista, the first act he signed was Alan Jackson, a tall, laconic Georgian who writes both traditional odes to home and family and deceptively witty songs like "Gone Country," which lampoons the Las Vegas lounge lizards, L.A. pop artistes and liberal folkies who descended on Nashville in the early 90's. Jackson has gone on to sell 21 million records, becoming second only to Garth Brooks as a 90's Nashville icon.
Not long afterward, DuBois took two struggling solo artists named Kix Brooks and Ronnie Dunn to lunch and said: "You guys don't know one another, but I think you're an act. Why don't you hang out and see if you like one another." They cooked up the definitive country-rock, line-dance sound and in four years sold 14 million albums, making Brooks and Dunn one of the best-selling duos in music history, in a league with Simon and Garfunkel and Hall and Oates.
"Part of country's success is generational," says DuBois, who is wearing a gray knit shirt with no tie, olive slacks and brown loafers at his office in Nashville. "I'm 48, and I think most baby boomers are alienated from urban dance, rap. It didn't translate with their value system, and it didn't translate with their musical taste. When they began to search around they found that some of the music that was coming out of Nashville was more like the rock-and-roll they were comfortable with than anything else out there. How much oldies rock can you listen to without getting tired of hearing 'Brown Sugar' 18,000 times?
"I grew up playing in rock-and-roll bands and writing rock-and-roll music," DuBois continues. "Very few of us grew up listening to the Grand Ole Opry. But when people started listening to country, they found something that wasn't all about truck drivers and cheating; it was about life, a lot of baby boomer issues, and there were still stories there, and there were still great harmonies, and it was just a comfortable place to be."
The trouble is, country may have become too comfortable and bland -- too much Eagles, not enough Merle Haggard. It's widely believed that Nashville needs to find a new direction, and exactly what that sound will be -- and how to get it played and exposed -- is what everyone in town is talking about. Many think Nashville will do what it has always done in slack times -- get rawer and closer to its roots; certainly closer than the current H.N.C. -- that's Hot New Country -- fare. As an example, they point to 14-year-old LeAnn Rimes and her enormous success this year with "Blue," a song with a hard-core, 1950's country sound.
 A LOT OF PEOPLE STILL FIND IT HARD TO take country seriously. After all, other than polka, and perhaps disco, no American music has been subjected to more ridicule than the music of the white South -- the music of Uncle Dave Macon and Grandpa Jones, of "Hee Haw" and Webb Pierce's customized Pontiac Bonneville with silver rifles mounted on the fenders, a silver pistol on the hood and horseshoes on the gas and brake pedals; the corn-pone music of Minnie Pearl's hat, Roy Acuff's twang and Dolly Parton's chest.
But, just as nearly all American music is essentially Southern music, country has always produced both stylized schlock and distinctly American marvels of wit and compression. Even on today's bland country radio, a song like Tracy Lawrence's "Time Marches On," written by the longtime Nashville songwriter Bobby Braddock, takes America from Hank Williams to Bob Dylan to Hank Williams, from beer to pot to boomers turned self-styled sexy grandmas, like a brilliant bit of American haiku. What has changed is that Nashville has become the center of American songwriting, the new Tin Pan Alley, and that, like the nation, the music's heart has moved from the country to the suburbs.
If you watched the Country Music Association Awards on Oct. 2, with their parade of young, attractive, all-white performers combining suburban sensibilities and rural styles, you would know what a reassuring environment it is for selling McDonald's, Wranglers and Chevrolet -- as safe and clean as the neighborhood mall. A lot of the performers are "country" more as a marketing category than as a musical identity, so that "country" acts include made-for-video hot bodies like Shania Twain from Windsor, Ontario, or feminist singer-songwriters like Mary-Chapin Carpenter from Princeton, N.J., via Brown University, or roots rockers like the Tractors.
The people running this music are headquartered in a thoroughly nondescript stretch of about 10 blocks near downtown called Music Row. Here the record companies and publishing firms are housed in blocky low-rise brick buildings that look like parking garages, with terrazzo floors, TV's forever set on Country Music Television and life-size cutouts of the label's hottest star next to the receptionist's desk.
In much the way Nashville borders on self-caricature, with huge billboards for country music stars looming over its freeways, Music Row has the appealing feel of a small town where the only industry is music. The same people turn up at the endless parties for new artists, new labels, platinum records and the rest. The streets have names like Roy Acuff Place and Chet Atkins Place. Successes are marked with banners hung from windows reading "Congratulations Larry Boone and Billy Lawson," or " 'Learning as You Go,' Recorded by Rick Trevino," in just the way the old Acme Farm Supply store across the street from the new Hard Rock Cafe downtown for a while had a banner outside reading "Free Dog Dip Every Saturday."
And if the ethic at the top of Los Angeles or New York is the edgy overdrive of Doberman-style gatekeepers and power lunches at Spago or the Four Seasons, the ethic of the movers and shakers in Nashville, is, well, country. No one wears a tie. Stars and label executives take Nashville's version of a power breakfast (at 9 A.M., not 7) at the Pancake Pantry or hang out over greasy burgers at Brown's Diner or on the cafeteria lines at favorite meat and threes (that's a meat and three vegetables), like Sweat's Barbecue, Arnold's or the Pie Wagon. The one would-be Spago-like industry hangout that has lasted is the Sunset Grill, which has all the glamour of a fallout shelter decorated with Christmas tree lights. When they hit it big, everyone heads for the mix of suburbs and exurbs outside town, where they build big rambling manses, raise horses, eat barbecue at places like Carl's Perfect Pig near Tim's farm outside town and live out the contemporary version of the country-lite American dream.
But if Nashville is still endearingly uncool, that hasn't stopped half the world from trying to get in on the action. In the mid-1980's, there were six major labels in Nashville. Now there are 25 majors or major independents. For instance, Imprint Records started up last year with a public stock offering, becoming the first all-country publicly traded record label. Usually, that sort of thing signals a top in either the stock market or the music market. But these days, who knows?
Both because of the success of the music and the allure of the life there (Nashville had the biggest gain in residential construction of any city in the country during the first half of this year), Nashville increasingly has become home base for an unlikely army of old rockers turned Nashville songwriters or producers trying to get away from the L.A. and New York rat races. They include Steve Winwood; Peter Frampton; Bernie Leadon of the Eagles; John Kay of Steppenwolf; Felix Cavaliere of the Rascals; Al Anderson of N.R.B.Q.; Kim Carnes; Mark Farner of Grand Funk Railroad; Janis Ian; Al Kooper, the great keyboard man on all of Dylan's greatest 60's albums; the legendary Memphis guitarist Steve Cropper; Bob Welch of Fleetwood Mac and Duane Eddy, the twangy guitar pioneer.
The big question in town, though, is what happens next?
 MORE THAN A FEW PEOPLE WORRY that country is winning the battle but losing the war, that it's succeeding by selling its soul. The artists who helped create modern country music -- Merle Haggard, Willie Nelson, Tammy Wynette, Waylon Jennings, George Jones, Johnny Cash, even younger icons like Emmylou Harris -- have long since been shut out by country radio, which prefers younger, sexier models.
"The best quote I ever heard about country music these days is that the reason Long John Silver's outsells Uncle Bud's Catfish Cabin on the edge of town is because they've made fish succeed in not tasting like fish," says Marty Stuart, a flamboyant 38-year-old guitarist and singer who represents much of what's left of Nashville's historical memory. "And I think it's the same thing with country music. There's only a measure of the heart and soul of country music left on the radio and on the concert trail these days, and the real thing, the Bill Monroe, Hank Williams thing, is that far from being long gone. But country always swings between innovation and imitation, and I think we're heading back to some innovation. You've got some great music all over town now, more than there's been for years, and it would be a sin for Nashville not to expose that culture that's trying to manufacture itself."
In fact, country and its outer branches are full of amazing music these days, not just the hot stars who will last like Patty Loveless, Alan Jackson, Vince Gill, Mary-Chapin Carpenter, Trisha Yearwood and George Strait. There is also the bluegrass-jazz banjo of Bela Fleck, the eclectic bluegrass of Alison Krauss, the Appalachian confessional folk of Iris Dement or Gillian Welch and rootsy country-rockers like James House, Kim Richey, the Mavericks and the Tractors.
And on country news groups on the Internet, in smoky clubs and in scrappy little magazines with names like Country Standard Time, the real excitement tends to be not about the big sellers but about smart singer-songwriters like Steve Earle, Al Anderson, Gretchen Peters and Jim Lauderdale or rough-edged acts that take various routes back to the past -- artists like Junior Brown and Wayne Hancock, bands like the Derailers and Wilco.
"It's harder because there's so many artists these days," says Patty Loveless, who this fall won C.M.A.'s Female Vocalist of the Year award. "But you just have to fight to make the music you want to. I always assume if I like something, someone else will, too."
The trouble is everyone has to fit through the same pipeline -- the narrow world of country radio, increasingly dominated by a few big consultants, who winnow out almost everything that's not right down the middle of the road.
"The great lesson of the last decade is that what serves the interest of radio in terms of attracting a maximum market share does not always serve the record business in terms of offering stylistic variety and novelty," says Bill Ivey, executive director of the Country Music Foundation, which runs country's museum and research center. "Radio thrives on familiarity and repetition, and the record business on novelty and innovation. The two are an imperfect fit."
To many people who care about country the real question is not what big thing Nashville cooks up next but, as Marty Stuart says, whether it will find a way to promote and expose some of the new music that's too off center for radio's hit machine.
The cost of playing the game -- $500,000 for a record and video, with a big push on radio -- argues against the likelihood of the off-center acts getting much of an airing, but people are looking for alternatives. One possibility is the so-called Americana format, a hybrid of off-center country and roots rock that could give exposure to the more adventurous acts. But it hasn't caught on.
The most interesting Nashville label these days may be a bare-bones independent called Dead Reckoning, started by some of the most accomplished, offbeat originals in town -- the performers Kieran Kane, Kevin Welch, Mike Henderson, Harry Stinson and Tammy Rogers. They operate out of what used to be the playroom of Kane's house and rely on word of mouth, direct mail, the Internet, radio play in Europe and joint Reckoner tours to sell their wares. They have bypassed radio and the big labels to produce some of the most innovative blues, country and roots records being produced anywhere.
In contrast to radio's seamless ditties about wise grandpas and grade-school romances that last forever, the Reckoners' songs reflect a more complicated America, like in Kane's "Dirty Little Town," which combines melodic, two-step music with a dour lyric about trying to escape from a dead-end place where you can't drink the water or breathe the air, and where the trees are dying under a sky that is purple and brown.
It's a long way from a sure thing commercially, and Dead Reckoning will never rival the titans of Music Row, but after two years Kane thinks there's a way to make it work.
"Anyone who isn't seeing the decline in sales and radio listenership is like a guy who sits on a beach and watches a hurricane roll in and doesn't move until the damn thing's on top of him," Kane says as he dashes out from his kitchen at 1 Reckoner Center to pick up his son at school. "Most of the music's just not very good. We figure if we're at a place where the music comes first, and we work hard at it, people will find us."
 "VINNIE," SOMEONE CALLS OUT AT THE Hermitage Golf Course, named after President Andrew Jackson's famous home nearby. "Hit 'em good, buddy."
It's the day after the Country Music Awards, the 30th version of Nashville's biggest night of preening, marketing and self-congratulation. "Vinnie" is the show's regular host, Vince Gill, whose boy-next-door looks, aw-shucks manner and arresting tenor have made him the quintessential Nashville figure of the 90's. On this day he's doing what everyone in Nashville does when they're not making music. He's playing golf, this time at the Diamond Rio Celebrity Golf Tournament, organized by Diamond Rio, one of DuBois's acts.
Arista had been saved from a C.M.A. shutout only by Brooks and Dunn, who won both Best Duo and the grand prize, Entertainer of the Year. It was a reminder that you can be Music Row's genius one year and back with the pack the next. But if DuBois was at all disappointed, he didn't show it. Instead he zipped around with his Brooks and Dunn golf bag, exchanging hugs and greetings with Gill and other Nashville insiders.
It's a long way from the coal miners' daughters and hillbilly wildmen of its past, but for better or worse, it's Nashville in the 90's. "I was talking to a fellow the other day who said, 'Wouldn't it be great to do a plain old country album,' " says Joe Diffie, an agreeably disheveled honky tonker with long stringy hair and an ample belly rolling around under his golf shirt. "But the problem is you've got to get it played, and you've got to get it sold." He pauses, and then dredging up the old country aphorism, he adds, "You've got to do what you can in the parameters you're given."
As it turns out, there is a group doing plain old country music that night in Nashville. It is DuBois's BR5-49, whose retro country with a punk sensibility -- so out, it's in -- has already developed something of a cult following. The band hasn't got anywhere with radio, but it has been touring Europe and America with the rock band the Black Crowes, getting tabbed by Rolling Stone as Nashville's hottest band and in general hovering in the air like the ghosts of Nashville's past.
Away for months, they are back at Robert's Western World downtown, and the place is packed with old country geezers, hip Nashville types, Vanderbilt college kids, all of whom are crammed in hip to hip or are milling around outside, where the owner Robert Moore is selling BR5-49 CD's, cassettes and T-shirts almost in the shadow of the giant radio tower in front of Nashville's almost-finished downtown sports arena.
It was all pretty surreal, but then the surreal is the routine these days in downtown Nashville. At the beginning of the 90's, downtown Nashville was a seedy wasteland of old clothing stores and dives where British tourists wandered in to find someone wheezing out old Willie Nelson songs.
But over the past few years it has come alive, seemingly in two eras at once. On the one hand, there is the regulation-issue downtown redevelopment of refurbished brick; designer coffee bistros; a Hard Rock Cafe and a Planet Hollywood; the country line-dance palace, the Wildhorse Saloon, and the new sports arena. But on the other, the venerable old dives like Tootsie's Orchid Lounge have come alive with some of the most raucous, real-deal music Nashville has heard in years.
No one knows whether the old dives will be able to withstand the gentrification of downtown. And in the same way, no one is sure what to make of BR5-49. Even their fans aren't sure if this goes anywhere, whether BR5-49 is anything other than a great country garage band, whether its live act translates to records and whether its popularity heralds a swing back toward Nashville's roots or just an interesting nostalgia trip.
But in a nation that is trying to head both into the brave new world of the Internet and back to an imagined America of roots and small-town values, the split personality of the scene and the music has an oddly familiar feel. Nostalgia aside, there's an economic and cultural logic to country gone suburban. But whether country can take a big slice of what the Dead Reckoners and BR5-49 represent may go a long way toward determining what kind of a future it has.
Still, no one at Robert's seems remotely interested in where this is all leading. The crowd jitterbugs and hollers as BR5-49 rips through ragged Hank Williams, Webb Pierce and Johnny Horton chestnuts; goofball oddities like "Humdinger" by the Farmer Boys, the world's least erotic love song, and originals like "Me and Opie Down by the Duck Pond," about an X-rated, lost episode of "The Andy Griffith Show." They play from 11 at night to 2 in the morning without a break, finishing with an inspired rendition of Johnny Horton's "Cherokee Boogie" that has people dancing in the streets outside.
Given the prevailing winds, it has the bittersweet air of something ending more than something beginning. But out on the street, the old electric sign is still spinning in front of the Ernest Tubb Record Shop, the winos are out in force in front of the Turf Club, Heilig Meyers Furniture is aglow under the street lights and, on this night at least, the grimy, ancestral rhythms of the old Nashville are holding their own against the big-bucks hum of the new Music City machine.

 BODY:

CRUISE
 
Fashion Statement
Passengers on a cruise and land tour through the Far East March 4 to 24 better leave their white vinyl belts at home. Mr. Blackwell, who annually selects the 10 worst-dressed women, will regale passengers aboard the Marco Polo, an Orient Lines ship, with tales of Hollywood Babylon's wardrobes. After stays in Bangkok and Singapore, guests take an 11-night cruise to Java, Manila and Hong Kong. Prices start at $3,225 a person, double occupancy, plus air fare to and from the United States.Robinsons-May Travel: (800) 800-2325.

GUIDEBOOK
 
Nights Out
 Guidebooks are generally broken down by such classifications as region and price. Now Frommer's has a series focusing on the time of day. Its By Night guides describe nocturnal life in Los Angeles, San Francisco, Manhattan, New Orleans, Miami, Chicago, London and Paris. The pocket-sized paperbacks, $12.95 each, offer suggestions on music and dance clubs, cultural activities, late-night restaurants, bars and more risque pursuits. Guides on Amsterdam and Las Vegas are expected to be in bookstores by December. To order, call Macmillan at (800) 428-5331.
 
AIRLINE
 
Spine Tingler
 Even in first class, airline amenities don't include a masseur for a stiff lower back. But United Airlines has a substitute. Its Connoisseur Class seats (between first class and economy) on Flight 895, between Chicago and Hong Kong, are now equipped with a device called a Backcycler, which automatically moves the lumbar area with the goal of easing lower-back strain and improving circulation. United plans to have Backcyclers installed in all Connoisseur Class seats on its North American and Latin American flights by the end of 1997, and on all Pacific flights by the end of 1998.

HEADLINE: Connecticut Q&A: Dr. Herbert S. Sacks;
A Psychiatrist Talks About Social Policy

BYLINE: By NANCY POLK 

 BODY:
AFTER a career as a child psychiatrist, international consultant and clinical professor of child and adolescent psychiatry at the Yale Child Study Center, Dr. Herbert S. Sacks will become president of the 41,000-member American Psychiatric Association in the spring.
Dr. Sacks, who lives in New Haven and practices in Westport, has, among other things, served 10 years as a senior psychiatric consultant to the Peace Corps, helped direct the Yale-AID epidemiological study in the Senegal River Valley in Africa for 12 years and been a consultant on the movies "Lorenzo's Oil" and "Rain Man." 
 He considers himself a strong patient advocate and is interested in the relationship of developmental psychological theory and clinical experience to social policy. In the early 1970's he led a successful battle to mandate group health coverage for outpatient psychiatric treatment, the first such policy in the nation. "It was a hell of a battle in the legislature because Connecticut is the capital of the American insurance industry," he said.
Recently Dr. Sacks discussed some of his organization's and his own concerns. Following are excerpts from that conversation.
Q. What is the impact of managed care on psychiatry?
A. Managed care will not go away. It is totally undesirable in terms of people getting adequate care. Our focus is patients who are suffering.
We are alarmed at the extent of managed care moving toward capitation. That means that a certain sum of money is allotted per patient. Managed care operations of course, would like to capitate at a very low level with extraordinary profit for insurance companies.
Q. You talk of the industrialization of American medicine. What is that?
A. We have a trillion-dollar industry, one-seventh of the economy. We have an insurance industry with over six million employees. Insurance companies and managed care operations wield an inordinate amount of power over legislatures and Congress. The greed is daunting. Aetna and US Healthcare merged recently, one example of a frenzy of profit-driven Wall Street consolidations. As an example of greed, the C.E.O. of US Healthcare will get in excess of $900 million plus Aetna's airplane, a crew and a pilot. Where does the money come from? It comes from the denial and interruption of medically necessary patient care.
Q. Why do you characterize mental illness coverage as a stepchild of managed care?
A. Managed care has chosen psychiatry as its prime target because it is most vulnerable. It is important that psychiatry be accountable, but we are capable of making clear and unambiguous diagnoses. Psychiatric illness is diagnosable. It is treatable. Treatment is cost-effective and it works. The National Alliance for the Mentally Ill does terrific work in educating the public about how treatable are schizophrenia, major depression, child autism, bi-polar disorder, severe obsessive-compulsive disorders, various pervasive developmental disorders. These may not have a clearly defined cure but people can become self-sufficient.
Q. Some people are not being treated. We see them living in the streets. Can they be helped more effectively?
A. In the 1970's the Feds tried to honey the bait by providing grants to states to deinstitutionalize patients. The notion was that states would help many of these severely mentally ill patients. They would return to their families and communities.
In Central and West Africa I saw families accept mentally ill people back into their communities and offer support and care. In this country, even in Connecticut, the number of resources -- outpatient facilities, monitoring, sheltered workshops, visiting nurse services, outreach -- were all underfunded or not funded at all. We found anywhere from 40 to 60 percent of homeless people on the streets were mentally ill.
Re-education, housing, monitoring are all critical. There is an absence of suitable housing for people who are struggling to get on their feet. They have been stigmatized rather severely. We'd rather not look at them when we walk down the street. Aggressive panhandlers are translated into potential assailants.
Q. Why is confidentiality so important?
A. One grave concern we have has to do with computer cross-linkages, the demands of managed care organizations for full medical records. They claim they need them for accountability. That is not true. They can do the same work by using aggregated data. They don't need individual identifiers. In Greenwich we have the Medical Information Bureau which collects information. Say you apply for disability or life insurance and you have been treated for depression. Computer cross linkages pick this up because it's been paid to a third-party payer. There's a question about whether you will get insurance. There are leaks all over the place. In my private practice dealing with upper middle class patients who work in corporations, banks and the media, perhaps half of them who have coverage will not use it. They pay out of pocket because of their concern about these breaches, because of what they observe in their own workplaces.
Q. Are children getting help they need to cope with violent behavior?
A. The amount of juvenile crime is terrifying. Three out of 10 murder arrests involve victims under 18. Victims of juvenile assaults are often children themselves.
Politicians in response to community fears have introduced legislation to send serious juvenile offenders to adult facilities. But there they will be subjected to physical and sexual assault, coercion. They will learn new modes of criminal behavior which they will predictably pursue once they are out.
Our grave concern is that the rehabilitation model has been trashed and there is a climate of hopelessness about our youth. There is a small cadre of youngsters who are not readily helped by current sophisticated techniques. Special facilities need to be put in to place to address these youngsters. Many have been reared in poverty, in fatherless families, by serial parenting figures. Often, though not always, they have limited intellectual capabilities, possibly as a consequence of inadequate stimulation in their early lives and nutritional deficits.
Q. What about the lives of middle class children?
A. We are living in very difficult times. We are suffering from an excessive amount of instant communication. Children are plugged into television sets instead of reading. The downside of computers is that it doesn't make for reflection. There is a major question as to whether those developments enhance the life of the mind. Worried about television violence? Researchers should look at Saturday morning cartoons. They have dreadful themes. When you have vulnerable children, external stimulation with a fiercely aggressive quality has an incendiary effect.
Q. What about the effect of welfare reform on children?
A. Recently Congress passed a welfare reform bill that is a very serious setback for the cause of children. It is politically palatable but it's inhumane. Children of the poor have no political power. Politicians all know that. It's easier to take away from children's intrinsic needs, which assures them of small futures. It is a national tragedy.
Q. Often care for the poor and institutionalized has been delivered by foreign doctors. Will changes in immigration policies affect that?
A. At this time 44 percent of first-year residents in psychiatry are international graduates. About 75 percent who come here for training do not return to their home countries. They have done terrific work in the public sector like correctional institutions and public hospitals. However, we are running into a major workforce problem: We have an excessive number of physicians. So Congress has agreed to put a restrain on the immigration of physicians. Predictably, after the November election, Medicare reimbursements for the training of international graduates will end. The resolution of these matters will very much affect not only psychiatry but also pediatrics and internal medicine, among other specialties.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Dr. Herbert S. Sacks finds managed care "undesirable" and welfare changes "inhumane." (Gale Zucker for The New York Times)

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

SECTION: Section 2; Page 33; Column 1; Arts and Leisure Desk 

LENGTH: 1445 words

HEADLINE: FILM VIEW;
Garden State? The Image Is Closer to Crab Grass

BYLINE: By Charles Strum; Charles Strum is the editor of the Sunday New Jersey section of The New York Times.

 BODY:
IN HIS LYRICAL, SENTIMENTAL essay "Here Is New York," E. B. White revealed with consummate grace and clarity what most New Yorkers, native or adoptive, seem to understand but do not often express:
"New York," he wrote, "is the concentrate of art and commerce and sport and religion and entertainment and finance, bringing to a single compact arena the gladiator, the evangelist, the promoter, the actor, the trader and the merchant." 
 "No one," he said, "should come to New York to live unless he is willing to be lucky."
Just so.
And what he made just as clear was that any place else was just, well, any place else. Or perhaps an un-place. The closest of these is New Jersey.
Are we picking on New Jersey again?
No, we are not. But film makers are. More and more, it seems, despite the fact that -- or perhaps because -- it is not New York or California or Florida. Or Fargo.
Among recent "Jersey films" is "Bogus" set partly in Newark, where Whoopi Goldberg runs a restaurant-supply business. Sharp-eyed members of the audience will recognize the train station and the site of the state's new performing arts center, now nearing completion. In "Caught," a love triangle set above a fish store, Jersey City is the locale, with lower Manhattan (the Emerald City?) shining across the harbor. A few years ago, a new director, Stacy Cochran, came out with "My New Gun," a delightfully wry tale of a suburban New Jersey housewife whose adventures are thrown into high relief by the blandness of her surroundings.
Let's face facts: New Jersey has too long been defined (and insulted) because of the few things it seems to be rather than the many different things it is. New York is easy; New Jersey is hard.
In some ways, that can be hard for film makers too especially if they are trying to sell a concept in 25 words or less. New Jersey is just too many things crammed into a small space between New York and Philadelphia (a valley of humility between two mountains of conceit, someone once said), and the best known of these are not always flattering.
It is a state with more Superfund toxic-waste sites than any other. It is malls, strip zoning, cookie-cutter suburbs, dying cities and overpriced pizza at the shore. Its industrial reputation mocks its own nickname, the Garden State, even though 60 percent of its land is still forest or farm. It also has mountains and horse farms and crystal clear trout streams. But the tank farms will out. That's frustrating, because despite all of New York's problems, it still has Fifth Avenue and the Empire State Building. Even its slums are awe inspiring, if only because they are among the biggest, the oldest or the most dreadful.
People pick on New Jersey with the same ease with which they glamorize New York or Los Angeles. It is part of the public imagination. New Jersey is not New York. Its cities certainly aren't. They are not even Chicago (itself reduced by A. J. Liebling to the status of the "second city"), or, to quote White again on New York, they are not "Spokane multiplied by 60 or Detroit multiplied by 4."
But they are most certainly post-modern, post-industrial, real. In gritty screenplays about lonely people, cheap hoodlums or failed schemes, New Jersey's cities -- and the anonymity of its suburbs -- are less imagination than stark truth. Real people live there, unadorned by nearness to celebrity. The rich and famous may step over the homeless on the doorsteps of their East Side town houses; in Camden or Newark, the poor simply step over the poorer.
In suburb after suburb, people strive to avoid urban tumult, but they cannot ultimately avoid one another. And when they collide, the New York of Woody Allen or Sidney Lumet is not there to comfort them or conceal them. Nor will a Jersey film distract the audience with its aura of big-town romanticism.
Of course, it's more glamorous, on film, to win or lose or get shot or make love in New York or L.A. But be clear on this: if it happens in New Jersey, it really happens. Because real life, the day-to-day routine of striving, and all too often losing, is not glamorous.
Three recent films come to mind: "Welcome to the Dollhouse," "Palookaville" and "Big Night."
The first is a scathing re-creation of the director's life as a nerd in a suburban junior high school. The second is the tale of three losers in a down-and-out part of Jersey City (essentially three palookas in Palookaville). The third is also about haplessness -- the pain and failure of two brothers whose dream is to own a successful restaurant.
You would have to look closely to suspect that "Big Night" takes place in New Jersey. Although the film's co-writer, co-director and co-star, Stanley Tucci, had no intention of singling out New Jersey in his search for a small, East Coast shore town, he did want to give the impression that "Big Night" took place in a hamlet by the sea. (He shot some of his film a bit north and inland, at Keyport, near Raritan Bay.) And it resonates.
As depicted in these films, New Jersey is not a place to go if you are willing to be lucky.
Todd Solondz's "Dollhouse," which opened in May and is due on video in December, was shot in West Caldwell, N.J. It is often described as a dark comedy. In fact, it is a grotesque investigation of intersecting suburban lives, bordering on surrealism. The Essex County suburb inhabited by the unattractive seventh grader Dawn Wiener and her unattractive family is depicted with the same odd invasiveness that Diane Arbus used in her portrait photography. Dawn's house, seen straight on in numerous shots, as if it were a police mug shot, is a building without architecture.
Could this film really be what we know, what we remember of what it means to live in the suburbs?
Its repulsive characters inhabit a place devoid of grace or beauty. You just know that cooking smells (broiled flounder, perhaps?) stick to the garish dining room wallpaper, and the sculptured, opaque water glasses on the table disguise the lukewarm beverages they contain. Dawn's pain at being the butt of her classmates' jokes is made worse by the anonymity she suffers almost everywhere she goes. She is part of a school, part of a family, part of a neighborhood, yet utterly alone.
The same is true for Sid, Russ and Jerry, the three unemployed losers from "Palookaville," a movie shot in Jersey City that opened on Friday.
The director, Alan Taylor, wastes no time in setting the tone, a tone that could only be set in Jersey City. As the opening credits roll, the audience looks out the passenger-side front window of an old car as it passes through a rickety, low-rise neighborhood just west of the waterfront. Across the Hudson, visible as the car glides past each intersection, are the World Trade Center towers. What they represent -- the big time, the big score, making it big -- is as unattainable as permanent employment for these bumblers, who try capitalism and crime and fail at both.
Yet they are sympathetic figures because they are recognizable in their failure and their striving, the more so because we see (though they do not) that they will probably never emerge from obscurity.
"Big Night" falls into this category by accident.
The director, Mr. Tucci, was looking for a shore town in the Northeast in which to set two brothers from Italy in the late 1950's. Here, they hope, they will find success for the restaurant that Primo, the chef who sees himself as an artist, will make famous. There are Italian communities up and down the East Coast, and certainly the Jersey Shore has always had its Italian precincts.
The town where Primo and his brother Secondo set up their Paradise restaurant is in such a place.
The food is unsurpassed; the clientele leaves something to be desired. Their chief rival, Pascal, owns the Grotto restaurant across the street, a restaurant that would fit in perfectly in Dawn Wiener's community. Instead of delicate seafood risotto (a la Primo), Pascal serves flaming platters and heaping plates of meatballs and thick spaghetti, to the accompaniment of a cheap singer who belts out "O Sole Mio" for the unworldy overeaters who squeeze into the vinyl booths.
For the men from the Paradise, the Grotto is hell. They are trapped by the ways of their adopted land and the small-time ways of the prospective clientele. The Paradise, ultimately, is lost.
Is all this loss, all this grit unbecoming? Not really. Each of these films depicts a triumph of spirit over adversity, however momentary. It may not be fully representative of New Jersey, anymore than "On the Waterfront" was.
That film, shot in Hoboken, won eight Academy Awards.

SECTION: Section 13CN; Page 8; Column 3; Connecticut Weekly Desk 

LENGTH: 1127 words

HEADLINE: Two for the Color, and Recycled Objects

BYLINE: By ELIZABETH ATTEBERY 

 BODY:
MELISSA BARBIERI, an artist who lives in Greenwich, trained as a classical portraitist in the Italian Renaissance style and derives her greatest satisfaction from painting religious and other heroically inspired figures on giant, stretch canvases.
But these days, walls and wooden furniture generally serve as her canvases. And instead of classical figures, she is more likely to be found painting murals depicting country themes, animal portraits rendered in the Old English style, or whimsical creatures like monkeys swinging on palm fronds, friendly camels with lips turned up in wry grins. For one client, she rendered a life-size giraffe on the wall flanking a grand staircase. 
 Ms. Barbieri, 34, and Matthew Tyrrell, 36, own and operate Painted, a decorative painting business in the Chickahominy section of Greenwich. In addition to being business partners, the two are longtime friends, who met in a high school art class in Fairfield and decided six years ago to pool their talents.
Mr. Tyrrell, at the time, had spent a dozen years working for high-profile decorators in New York City. Ms. Barbieri, a divorced mother of two living in Florida, was working as a waitress and bartender and painting whenever she had time. So they quit their jobs, moved back to Connecticut and invested the bulk of their savings in used furniture.
"It all started with $3,000," Mr. Tyrrell said, "and a rundown Cape Cod in Fairfield."
The dining room of that Cape Cod, which belonged to Ms. Barbieri's mother, served as their first base of operations.
"For three months we sat there with blow dryers, painting," Ms. Barbieri said. "We started out with the country look and then trained ourselves until we were very sure of our technique in a lot of other areas."
When they had completed a representative selection of their work, Ms. Barbieri and Mr. Tyrrell put it out for sale on their front lawn. Several months later, they made their first major sale -- to Tuscany, a Greenwich shop specializing in Italian hand-painted ceramics, furniture and decorative home furnishings. The owner of Tuscany, Barbara Brigham, who now sells the work of Painted regularly, recalled the event and her first meeting with Mr. Tyrrell.
"It was about 5 o'clock on a January day," she said, "when this shadow appeared at the front door and he came swooping into the store with a long black cape and those dark blue eyes. I thought to myself, 'What is this?' "
After talking to Mr. Tyrrell for awhile, Ms. Brigham asked to see his work, expecting him to produce a portfolio. Instead, Mr. Tyrrell rushed outside and carried in a painted chest of drawers that sold within the week for $1,500. Since then, Ms. Brigham said, "It's just kind of always worked. It's one of those so-called good marriages."
At a time when many Connecticut businesses are struggling, and when even Greenwich, a town often considered recession-proof, has seen several stores close their doors, Ms. Barbieri and Mr. Tyrrell are doing fine, they say. Annual sales last year totaled about $300,000, according to Mr. Tyrrell, who stressed that all their pieces are recycled.
"Our prices would triple," he said, "if we had to buy new things."
Ms. Barbieri attributes the success of Painted to the combination of talents she and Mr. Tyrrell bring to the operation. Unlike artists who specialize in one area of painting or design, the Painted partners cover a wide range of skills, from formal wall glazing, murals, stenciling and faux finish work, to timeless painted furniture for children and adults, and funky transformations of recycled lamps and accessories. Painted also incorporates a decorating service known as Dogwood Cottage, for clients wishing to buy rugs, fabrics and decorative accessories.
"Because we come from different ends of the spectrum," Ms. Barbieri said, "there's nothing we can't do. Matthew has the eye of a decorator and I was trained in fine arts, so we complement each other."
"Not only is their range so diverse," said Ms. Brigham, "but they can do anything in any color and in any look you want. They can do something very early American, or very French or Italian."
Ms. Brigham said that hand-painted furniture is especially popular among her European customers. "Europeans," she said, " have lots of painted pieces, while Americans tend to have a lot of mahogany and cherry. Americans are just being introduced to this type of furniture."
While Ms. Barbieri and Mr. Tyrrell characterize all their work as fun, their favorite projects are those in which they have free rein to design a space and develop their own motifs and color schemes.
One such project started out as a colorless laundry room and ended up a bright barnyard scene. On one wall, beneath a sky dotted with puffy white clouds, stands a clothesline covered with laundry fluttering in the breeze. Appliances and cabinets painted to look like weathered barnwood, complete with trompe l'oeil rusty nails, grace an adjoining wall. Poking its head around the corner to survey the scene is a giant spotted cow.
Another unusual project the pair worked on was the design and interior glazing of 64 Greenwich Avenue, a restaurant owned by Richard Mele and built to incorporate the main architectural elements from a dismantled, 27-room Scarsdale mansion. Glaze is "a semitransparent liquid with color added," Mr. Tyrrell explained, as opposed to paint, which is opaque. "People have been glazing for hundreds of years," he said, "but now it's mainstream."
In the case of "64," as the restaurant is popularly known, the pair glazed one room saffron yellow, another a burnt butternut squash yellow and the upstairs dining room hot tango red. They also painted several pieces of furniture, turning a beat-up trunk into a backgammon board lookalike and zapping Mr. Mele's Victorian chairs with black stripes on an ochre background, topping them off with leopard seats.
"We thought at first it might be too much," Ms. Barbieri said, "but Rich loved it.
Mr. Mele said that he had confidence in the pair's taste, but that others viewing the work-in-progress were occasionally skeptical. In order to achieve the color they wanted in the upstairs dining room, for example, Ms. Barbieri and Mr. Tyrrell had to first apply two rather bold shades of pink to the walls. "Some people thought it looked like a bordello," said Mr. Mele, "but I knew where it was going.
"I had wanted the restaurant to look a little country, but with shots of SoHo," Mr. Mele said. "What Matthew and Melissa did was to act as our colorists, and from there, they just kept recommending other things we should do. If there's one thing that is their strong point, besides being creative, it's that they're phenomenal colorists."

HEADLINE: ART VIEW;
A Rembrandt Evolution, Etching Light Out of the Dark

BYLINE: By Roberta Smith 

 BODY:
FOR THE STRONGEST SENSE OF Rembrandt as a living, thinking artist, nothing quite beats his etchings. And when it comes to seeing Rembrandt etchings in the United States, nothing at all beats the Pierpont Morgan Library, which has the largest collection in North America. For the first time in nearly 25 years, the Morgan has put a good chunk of its holdings on view through Jan. 5 in "A Fine Line: Rembrandt as Etcher."
This is a wonderful show: illuminating, pleasurable, often quite moving. It shows an artist of genius coming to grips with a relatively new medium -- etching being less than a century old when Rembrandt took it up -- and expanding its possibilities as his expressive needs grew. 
 Rembrandt made his first etchings around 1626, when he was barely 20. His brilliant draftsman skills suited the medium superbly, and until the late 1650's he devoted as much time to printmaking as to drawing and painting -- the first great painter to do so. He went on to revolutionize etching, introducing an unprecedented play of light against dark or, in some astoundingly velvety cases, dark against dark. He also gained a new subtlety of surface and psychological portrayal by working directly on the copper plate with dry point (a fine needle) and burin (a pointed cutting tool), refining the etched image, which is made by drawing into a resinous ground that coats the copper plate so that the exposed areas can be etched by acid. Some of his later prints were made with dry point and burin alone.
This exhibition's 83 prints touch on all Rembrandt's major themes and subjects -- the penetrating portraits and self-portraits, the alternately homey and magisterial scenes from the Bible, the unidealized renderings of nudes (female and male) and the increasingly unfettered treatments of the Dutch landscape, as well as genre scenes and his single etched still life, a magnificently isolated little seashell. The result is a succinct encapsulation of Rembrandt's development, in which the initial pull of the Baroque was transformed by sometimes conflicting elements: the classicizing influence of Renaissance art, close scrutiny of the actual world and, above all, an increasing emphasis on the materials and processes of art making.
Rembrandt was arguably the first artist to make this process a central part of his art's content; this is one reason his work always feels so familiarly modern. His paintings never let us forget, sometimes to the point of murkiness, that they are paint applied to canvas. They exude a sense of touch and emotional striving.
His etchings reveal this process just as much and often with greater clarity. First of all, they can have a crystalline, almost transparent quality. We don't just look at Rembrandt etchings; we often have the sensation of looking through them, of seeing how they have been built, mark by mark, from the inside out. In addition, since the etching plate can be printed and then reworked, prints can exist in different "states" that trace an artist's decisions and revisions, providing a series of interim reports.
Rembrandt produced some remarkable images in just one try. Etchings at the Morgan labeled "only state," include extremes like the nocturnal "Presentation in the Temple in the Dark Manner" and the evanescent "Christ Appearing Before the Apostles," in which the Saviour's radiance seems almost to fracture the image. But he also reworked plates extensively or returned to them when his concept of an image had changed.
Cara Dufour Denison, curator of drawings and prints at the Morgan and the exhibition's organizer, has emphasized this tendency by representing nearly 20 prints with two to four different states each. Other prints are represented by two impressions of the same state, revealing how subtle changes in the inking of the plate or a different kind of paper can alter the visual outcome and reflecting Rembrandt's interest in experimentation.
THE SHOW INCLUDES, FOR example, two impressions of the only state of Rembrandt's famous print "Christ Preaching: La Petite Tombe" on contrasting papers. On the soft, creamy Japanese sheet the image is gently blurred; shadows seem to flow around the figure of Christ like dark smoke. This could almost be an ink drawing. On lighter, less absorbent European paper, the same image has an altogether different sense of tone and form: the lines are crisp, the image shot through with sparkling light, and Christ has lost some of his supernatural aura.
Also here are two contrasting states (Nos. 1 and 7 of 8) of the monumental 1655 print "Christ Presented to the People," which was rendered entirely with dry point. Between them, they show that Rembrandt rethought both the image's internal scale and its emotional mood. Gone from the second image are a row of figures standing before the sunlit platform on which Christ appears, and the platform itself has been punctured by two black grottolike openings that suggest a dark, pantheistic underworld. Gone also is nearly an inch from the the top of the image, a change that thrusts the whole architectural setting forward, increasing its massiveness.
The Morgan show is full of similarly telling progressions of images, some grand, others personal, still others measured in the contrasting versions of various prints. All point to increasing maturity, a clearer grasp of the human condition and an ever more declarative use of the print medium.
It begins with small self-portraits made in the first flush of technical mastery. They show the artist grimacing and frowning, as if mugging for the camera. Their young-man-in-a-hurry exuberance is balanced by ineffably tender images of the artist's ancient mother, from the same period.
These are succeeded by two larger, more assured self-portraits that depict the artist as successful young Turk. Especially in the dandyish "Rembrandt With Turned-Up Hat and Embroidered Mantle" of 1631, the artist unleashes a virtuosic display of textures, carefully differentiating the fur, lace and brocade of his lavish garments from the luminous fluff of his hair.
Nearby, "Self-Portrait Drawing at a Window," from 1648, seen in two states, strikes an entirely different note. Rembrandt is older and wiser, having lost his beloved young wife, Saskia, and acquired mounting debts. This is a somber, straightforward image straightforwardly made. A rigorous grid of crosshatching defines the shadowy precincts of a dark interior flanked by a bright window. Next to it, merging with the shadows yet illuminated by the light, sits the artist, who is wearing work clothes and looking up from his task with resolute determination. The image provides a glimpse of the will and concentration that generated everything else on the Morgan's walls.
A similar progression operates among the portraits. The eye is initially caught by the four handsome states of "Clement de Jonghe, Printseller," an image from 1651 that Rembrandt fine tunes without bringing the sitter any closer to us. In contrast, a rich psychological availability characterizes several beautifully wrought portraits from the late 1650's: the surgeon Arnold Tholinx, who fixes us with an interested, appraising air; Old Haaringh, a city official, whose wrinkled face communicates a beleaguered fragility, and "Jan Lutma, Goldsmith," a grandfatherly figure whose downturned gaze is quietly imperious.
The give-and-take between process and image is played out again and again at different scales and intensities. In the homey little "Abraham Talking to Isaac," the figures are fully defined while the landscape is an open sketch, wonderfully transparent. In "The Three Trees," Rembrandt's most ambitious landscape print, there's more tension. The towering, rather tightly rendered trees are strikingly at odds with the loose linear evocations of cloud and wind that give the sky a life and a scale of its own. In other landscapes, Rembrandt used a similar economy of means to intimate entire vistas, as in "Jan Six's Bridge" and "The Goldweigher's Field."
Sometimes process and image seem almost in conflict. The exhibition's indisputable highlight is "The Three Crosses," the monumental image of Golgotha that Rembrandt produced in 1655 and returned to again as late as 1661. The Morgan owns the first, second and fourth of four states, which trace a sense of deepening human tragedy and an increasingly passionate manipulation of the plate. In the first and second versions, a strong shaft of light brightens the image, shining especially on the good thief and the huddled masses of Mary and the other faithful to the right of the Cross.
In the fourth, Rembrandt's entire conception has changed. The composition has been clarified and strengthened. The attending crowd has been thinned, so that the crucified figures are now echoed by three prominent witnesses: a man on horseback depicted in profile wearing a bulbous, unctuously official hat (taken from a medallion by Pisanello, it also evokes "Alice in Wonderland"); a soldier with a raised sword and the grieving St. John, whose arms are now extended up and out, as if crucified himself.
But opposing this new clarity of composition are long, repeating diagonals that Rembrandt also added. These rain down from above, like some graphic form of heavenly wrath, adding to grief a sense of impending doom. These lines are especially heavy on the right, where the good thief is now all but obliterated. They form a dark curtain that is about to be drawn across the scene, an image harrowed and nearly consumed by the process that brought it into being.

GRAPHIC: Photos: Refinements Two examples of Rembrandt's "Christ Presented to the People," 1655, above, on view at the Morgan Library, show how the artist altered architectural elements, developed and reworked the scale and even the mood of the etching.; A Favorite Subject Rembrandt's "Self-Portrait Drawing at a Window," 1648, left, shows the artist after the death of his wife, in work clothes and as a no-longer-exuberant young man-- A somber, straightforward image straightforwardly made. (Photographs by David A. Loggie/Pierpont Morgan Library)

HEADLINE: What's a Conservative?

BYLINE: By Alan Ehrenhalt; Alan Ehrenhalt is the executive editor of Governing magazine and the author, most recently, of "The Lost City: The Forgotten Virtues of Community in America."

 BODY:

THE WORLD TURNED RIGHT SIDE UP
A History of the Conservative Ascendancy in America.
By Godfrey Hodgson.
365 pp. Boston:
Houghton Mifflin Company. $27.50.
Over the course of the past generation, slowly and not always consciously, the American electorate changed its mind about what government was and what it might reasonably be expected to accomplish. Tens of millions of voters who once looked to Washington as the engine of positive social change came to see the entire Federal establishment, instead, as an alien presence in their midst. 
 So thorough has this psychological change been that many of us simply accept it as a familiar fact of modern life. But Godfrey Hodgson, an English journalist who has been studying American politics for three decades, quite rightly refuses to take it for granted. He wants to know just how the energy and enthusiasm of Washington in the Kennedy years managed to transform itself into the visceral hostility to government that created Newt Gingrich and the 104th Congress.
In "The World Turned Right Side Up," Mr. Hodgson asks a simple but beguiling question. Has there been a conservative revolution? Or is it just a product of the larger cynicism that seems to pervade American life at the end of the century? And he raises the underlying intellectual problem that any serious conservative has to confront sooner or later: What is one to think of a movement that stretches to include under its banner libertarians who detest authority and Christian moralists who long to reassert it?
It is this fundamental contradiction that prompted Mr. Hodgson to write his book in the first place. "What puzzled me," he says, "was the incoherent cocktail of ideas that were being melded together and offered under the marketing label 'conservative.' "
He is focusing on the nasty little problem that American conservatives never quite manage to put behind them, no matter how hard they try. For much of American postwar history, as Mr. Hodgson points out, the libertarians and the moralists were able to forget their differences because they were lucky enough to have an ideal enemy: Communism. The one thing virtually all American conservatives understood from 1945 to 1989 was that the Soviet Union was a threat to everything they wanted to achieve. Exactly what they wanted to achieve was something they could argue about later.
Later is now. "As the 1990's flowed back into the past and the millennium approached," Mr. Hodgson writes, "the contradictions between some of the impulses of conservatism began to look more ominous. It was one thing to want to restore the old ways, another to reconcile that nostalgia with the imperatives of economic freedom."
Mr. Hodgson's insistence on this point makes his book a helpful guide to some of the most confusing events in American politics in the past couple of years. It helps explain Pat Buchanan, whose sensitivity to corporate mistreatment of American workers seemed to point him toward a broader attack on global capitalism, but who mysteriously shrank from making it. It helps explain Newt Gingrich, who came to power trumpeting the virtues of the uncontrolled market and the traditional American family, seemingly unaware that one was the enemy of the other.
Mr. Buchanan and Mr. Gingrich, like many of their Republican contemporaries, have been trying to straddle the contradictions of conservatism, and finding it an embarrassingly difficult maneuver to perform. Mr. Hodgson faces those contradictions squarely, and in so doing delivers a book that anyone professing interest in conservative ideas ought to take seriously.
Granting their inconsistencies, the fact remains that people who call themselves conservatives have done something truly remarkable over the past 40 years. They have taken a label that once carried a popular connotation of hidebound rigidity and given it an aura of dynamism. "I am a conservative because I'm for change," Mr. Hodgson quotes the former Iowa Senator Roger W. Jepsen as saying. A generation ago, that would have sounded ludicrous. A generation from now, it may sound equally ludicrous, as perhaps it should. But in the last quarter of this century, with liberalism and the Democratic Party under siege as icons of discredited policy, it has been possible for candidates of the right to identify themselves as apostles of change and to be accepted as such.
A substantial portion of Mr. Hodgson's book is a narrative account of the events that rejuvenated the American right, from the founding of National Review by William F. Buckley Jr. in 1955 to the emergence of Irving Kristol and the neoconservatives in New York in the 1970's. The book painstakingly traces the ripening of Christian fundamentalism, the academic critique of Keynesian liberal economics and the emergence of a hard-line anti-Soviet cadre among foreign policy academics. Much of this history is familiar, and Mr. Hodgson breaks no new ground in reporting it at substantial length. Still, he performs a service in linking together the forces that transformed the "narrow stream" that was the conservatism of the 1950's into the "raging river" of 1994.
Is "revolution" too strong a word? In Mr. Hodgson's view, yes. As he sees it, a genuine conservative revolution would require not merely that the American people come to distrust government, but that they actively support efforts to dismantle it. On this point, he says, the record of the past year has been decisive. When Mr. Gingrich and the Congressional Republicans sought to begin the dismantling process, and shut the Government down to demonstrate their seriousness, public opinion gave them a spanking from which they have yet to recover. "However much they may applaud conservative politicians who say they want to 'get government off your back,' " Mr. Hodgson concludes, "they can hardly be said to have wholeheartedly adopted the conservative creed." Nor are they likely to, as long as that "creed" consists of a jumble of inconsistent ideas that point the society in wildly different directions.
"The World Turned Right Side Up" belongs on this fall's reading list for Congressional Republicans. Had they been able to read it last year, they might have spared themselves a considerable amount of embarrassment.

LENGTH: 1852 words

HEADLINE: Police Salaries Likely to Move Higher

BYLINE: By JOHN RATHER 

 BODY:
AN arbitrator's decision on a four-year contract for the Suffolk County police ushers in an era of $70,000-a-year officers, upsetting county officials and inspiring predictions that the added expense will lead to tax increases.
"The people of Suffolk County can't afford this," the senior county legislator, Joseph Rizzo, Republican of Islip Terrace, said. "They are going to bankrupt us if they continue at this pace." 
 If the history of police contracts on Long Island is a guide, wages for county, town and village officers in Suffolk and Nassau Counties will continue to rise steadily, far outracing inflation and reflecting gains under the state's Taylor Law, which requires that binding arbitration settle contract disputes.
The county says that from 1982 to last year the percentage increases for Suffolk officers, among the highest paid in the country, exceeded rises in the Consumer Price Index every year except 1992. When the increases were adjusted for the index, the officers gained $75,612.
By contrast, increases for many private and public workers have not kept pace with inflation in recent years, economists say.
The police, barred from striking and subject to sanctions for job actions, say that only through arbitration can they gain the increases and benefits that they deserve for working in a dangerous and stressful occupation.
Critics like Mr. Rizzo say binding arbitration removes control over salaries from elected officials and mandates that the sums be based on factors like levels in comparable nearby departments. "We can't control taxes if the arbitrators keep giving these awards," said Mr. Rizzo, who predicted that the contract would create a budget deficit.
County Executive Robert J. Gaffney said through a spokesman that his proposed 1997 budget, which is before the Legislature, included money to pay for the first-year increase. "These mythical multimillion-dollar holes in the budget quite frankly just don't exist," said the spokesman, Rick Belyea.
But Mr. Rizzo differed, faulting arbitration for allowing the police to win increases regardless of the ability to pay, adding: "They can just say: 'We don't need you. We'll get it from the arbitrator.' "
The minority leader in the Legislature, Maxine S. Postal of Amityville, said Mr. Gaffney had agreed too easily to let an arbitrator set the police wages for four years.
"Having an award that you are locked into for four years is a terrible burden," Ms. Postal said. "The County Executive's office barely negotiated before going into arbitration. Agreeing to do it for four years shows they just don't want to do their job."
Complaints about rising police wages and arbitrators' awards have been heard for years on Long Island. In 1978, after the police in Nassau County had won an arbitration to increase the base salary for top-step officers to $21,000, County Executive Francis T. Purcell criticized the decision and warned that taxpayers were not a "bottomless well of money."
The base salary for senior Nassau officers is $59,522, or up to $61,022 with longevity pay, and the officers seek increases similar to those in Suffolk.
"These increases underscore the problem with mandatory arbitration," Suffolk Legislator Steve Levy, Democrat of Bayport, said. "All these arbitrators do is compare neighboring counties and police departments to each other. It's a never-ending upward cycle."
The arbitrator was Martin F. Scheinman of Manhasset, a lawyer who has a summer home in Southampton.
Mr. Scheinman's ruling gives 1,800 officers an 18.6 percent increase over four years and raises the senior officers' base, from $59,540 to $70,658 by 1999.
Increases in longevity pay and other benefits will raise the wages even higher. The county's total cost of the raises will be $29 million, according to a county estimate.
The arbitration began in May, after mediation had failed and the two sides had declared an impasse.
The union, the Suffolk County Police Benevolent Association, sought a 6 percent increase in each of the four years.
The county asked for 2 percent annual increases over the four years.
Mr. Scheinman's decision gave officers increases in the base salary of 5.5 percent retroactive from Jan. 1, 1996, and of 4 percent a year for the next three years. The labor contract expired on Dec. 31.
An economist who advises Suffolk governments and business, Thomas Conoscenti, projected that the inflation rate of 2.9 percent would increase to 3.3 next year and 3.6 in 1998.
Mr. Scheinman said that his ruling was in accord with the guidelines of the Taylor Law and that it adhered to a "police pattern," in which salaries for comparable departments were held at the same level. He noted that the contract required concessions on overtime that would save the county $400,000 a year.
In an interview, Mr. Scheinman, who was chosen by the county and the police, said a pivotal factor was a 5.5 percent wage increase this year for higher-ranking officers, who are represented by another union.
Mr. Scheinman, a longtime arbitrator who has ruled in several police contract disputes in Suffolk and Nassau, said the 5.5 percent first-year increase for the rank-and-file officers matched their superiors' increase. Those officers won the increase in binding arbitration in which Mr. Scheinman was the arbitrator.
Mr. Scheinman said the increases were also based on comparisons with Nassau. "I find that the police officers employed by Nassau County are the most relevant basis for comparison," he wrote in the award. "This is not only because Nassau County is the neighboring county, and not only because its police are the only other county police force on Long Island, but also because of the past bargaining history between the parties, which has often used Nassau County police officers as an important basis for comparison."
The Nassau County Police Benevolent Association recently negotiated a five-year contract that would have increased top-step pay for an officer to $72,664 by the year 2000, but the Board of Supervisors did not ratify it.
The Suffolk P.B.A. said the 4.5 percent annual increases in the Nassau contract, which it believed that an arbitrator would eventually award, justified its own proposals.
Former County Executive John V. N. Klein said the two county departments had long used comparisons to each other and political power to obtain wage concessions.
"The perpetual problem going back to Dennison days was the whipsawing between Nassau and Suffolk," said Mr. Klein, referring to his predecessor, H. Lee Dennison, and the years preceding binding arbitration. "If the Suffolk P.B.A. settled at a number that was greater than Nassau, then the Nassau P.B.A. would demand an even greater increase.
"Ultimately they would be successful, whereupon the Suffolk P.B.A. would come after Suffolk government for an increase exceeding the Nassau increase. After arbitration the same thing still happened. The whipsawing has not changed."
Mr. Klein said the police unions were politically influential, "very effective lobbyists at the state level, as well as being intensely involved in the political process at the county level."
In the Suffolk talks P.B.A. negotiators also said the county department, as the largest and leading force in Suffolk, should be paid more than village and town forces.
But for the smaller forces, where the base levels for experienced officers were near and, in some cases, above the county level, the new contract will be a basis for future proposals.
In Suffolk, Mr. Scheinman ruled that the county's proposed 2 percent increase would drop the county department to sixth or seventh place among police forces in Suffolk. "Such a deterioration in the relative ranking of the county's police officers in terms of salary cannot be justified," he wrote.
The decision emphasizes maintaining a pattern in which salaries in comparable jurisdictions remain similar, on the theory that that will discourage leapfrogging in which one department tries to surpass another's gains.
The president of the Suffolk P.B.A., Thomas E. Tohill, and the president of the Nassau P.B.A., Gary D. Dela Raba, did not answer calls for comment.
The chief collective-bargaining negotiator for Nassau County, Peter A. Bee, said the county and the P.B.A. were awaiting a mediator's report on a proposed contract. He declined to say whether he thought that the Suffolk agreement was relevant to Nassau.
"Everything has relevance," Mr. Bee said. "It's a question of degree."
The Labor Relations Director in Suffolk, David S. Greene, said criticisms of Mr. Scheinman were misdirected. "He is a nationally renowned arbitrator," Mr. Greene said. "The process was fair. This is what the law allows."
Criticizing the arbitrator, Mr. Greene added, was "like blaming the umpire for calling you out after three strikes."
"If people would like to get some relief, it requires a change in the state law," he said. "The biggest issue for us is the 'ability to pay' argument. We need to more clearly define the municipality's availability to pay and comparability.":
In his award, Mr. Scheinman said, the county had proven that "it is not flush with money." But he added that a 2 percent increase in each of the four years of the contract under the county proposal would have lowered police morale, to the detriment of residents.
He said the 6 percent annual increase that the union had sought would be harmful, "given the economic climate on Long Island and in the county." His compromise award, he wrote, was affordable for the county and fair to police.
The county argued for comparisons with forces in metropolitan regions that pay lower salaries, forces in places like Buffalo, Elizabeth, New Haven, New York City, Newark and Syracuse.
New York City officers earn a top wage of $43,593, or $48,593 with longevity pay.
"Why don't they compare Suffolk to the city, where it's really risky being a cop?" Mr. Rizzo, a retired city subway foreman, asked. "If I was going to be a cop I would choose Suffolk over the city any time because of the danger, even if the pay was the same."
About 42,000 applicants took the examination for county police officer in June, nearly 20 times the number of officers on the force. The county argued that no wage increase was needed to attract recruits.
The president of the 30,000-member New York City Patrolmen's Benevolent Association, Louis Matarazzo, said city officers deserved the wages that were paid to suburban officers. "I'll take it," he said, when asked about the Suffolk decision.
The city P.B.A. is appealing to the State Court of Appeals, the state's highest court, to overturn lower-court rulings that affirmed the city's Office of Collective Bargaining as the arbitrator of wage disputes. Those rulings voided a a law that the Legislature passed in February over a veto by Gov. George E. Pataki and despite objections by Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani.
The law shifted police contract disputes to the state's Public Employment Relations Board, the agency in charge of arbitration outside the city.
