HEADLINE: Where the Rich Live, and Live Well

BYLINE: By ELSA BRENNER 

DATELINE: SCARSDALE

 BODY:
WITH a straw basket under his arm, Helmut Ludl recently strode through the aisles of the Hay Day market here assembling the weekend's menu: aged beefsteaks, the largest and freshest shrimp money can buy, imported French cheeses, exotic fruits and vegetables and some wickedly delicious treats from the bakery counter. When he finally made his way to the checkout counter, the last concern on the chief executive officer's mind was how much it would cost.
"I work hard, I earn good money and I want only the very best," said Mr. Ludl, who was shopping at the market which opened in this upscale village two years ago to cater to the tastes of well-to-do Westchester residents. 
 In a county that ranks among the highest in the nation in terms of average household income, stores like Hay Day and customers like Mr. Ludl are not unusual.
A recent issue of Worth magazine listed 20 Westchester towns, villages and hamlets among the 300 wealthiest in America, which is also why places like Purchase Estates in Harrison -- where new home values start at $2.5 million -- and the Mary Jane Denzer store in White Plains -- where ball gowns sell for more that $5,000 -- are reporting brisk sales.
There is so much money in the county that the designer evening gowns, even at those prices, have been flying off the racks. And the estate-like homes, with swimming pools and proximity to a golf club with membership by invitation only are similarly going fast.
"You see signs of money everywhere," said Noreen L. Preston, an economist with the County Planning Department, "in the expensive cars on the street, in the upscale neighborhoods, in the boutiques in downtown areas and in the variety of businesses that cater to the rich."
"And yet," she pointed out, "this is a county of tremendous diversity -- from the very wealthy to the poorest immigrants who are making their way up the economic ladder."
Ms. Preston's observations are borne out by statistics, which reveal significant poverty in the county. Mount Vernon, which is home to many immigrants, has a median household income of only $43,284, but which is nevertheless higher than the national median. By contrast, residents in Scarsdale, which comes in 30th on Worth's list, report an average household income of $222,000 and an average home value of $512,700. Scarsdale, according to Worth, is the richest municipality in Westchester, and Mount Vernon, according to the County Planning Department, is the poorest.
In the 1990 United States Census, more than one-fourth of the households in the county registered incomes of more than $75,000, and almost 18 percent showed incomes of more than $100,000. Westchester ranks second in per capita personal income in the state, surpassed only by Manhattan, or New York County.
The rich in Westchester, like those in most areas, fall into two categories. There are those whose families have owned their grand estates for generations, and then there are the nouveau riche -- celebrities and up-and-coming business executives among them. And according to economists and other observers, the two groups differ markedly in style.
"Those with old money tend to be more conservative," observed Mary Jane Denzer, the owner of the couture clothing store on Mamaroneck Avenue in White Plains, which features the outfits of such designers as Yves St. Laurent, Ungaro and Givenchy. "Old money wants beautiful clothes that don't make an obvious statement. New money wants to walk into a room and make a grand entrance."
Ms. Denzer pointed out that -- at least in her experience -- the more money the rich have, the nicer they tend to be, "compared to those who who are dying to own what they buy but are angry that it costs so much."
In Harrison, estates, like haute couture clothing, were also selling to both old and not-so-old money. But Frank C. Langworth, a spokesman for Purchase Estates, explained that while anybody's check might buy a designer suit or even three wooded acres and a luxury home with a swimming pool, it would not guarantee entrance into The Country Club of Purchase next door, which will feature, when completed, an 18-hole signature Jack Nicklaus golf course.
"It's not just a matter of dollars," said Mr. Langworth, president of the Langworth Pantel Group, a public relations firm in White Plains. "You can't just call up and say I have a check for $100,000 and I want to join your club. We're talking about an invitation-only membership."
The estates and golf club are set on 400 acres of gently rolling woodlands in the pristine hamlet of Purchase, where 10 of the 11 homes in the first section sold within six months.
"There's a very small sliver of the population that can afford these," Mr. Langworth said. "They're corporate transfers, downgrades from even bigger homes, Manhattanites looking for a get-away in the country. It's a Fortune 500 kind of place."
Meanwhile, in White Plains, in what was once a gracious 7,000-square-foot home, Beethoven's "Moonlight" Sonata was being played on a grand piano in Demirjian Salons while well-heeled customers -- corporate executives and the matrons who run the county's charity balls among them -- sipped Perrier or white wine and had their hair cut and styled.
Vasken Demirjian, the 34-year-old owner of the salon, made no bones about who his customers are. "They are the creme de la creme, the most elite," the former art dealer turned collector said, "and they come here to be pampered."
Across town at The Westchester, a decidedly upscale shopping center in White Plains, Jose Eber -- the Beverly Hills, Calif., hairdresser to the stars -- recently open a salon in a similar bid to groom the wealthy.
Nieman Marcus, which is also at The Westchester, beguiles its wealthy customers with lush incentive programs. For customers who spend $125,000 in a year, the store offers free trips to Barcelona or London. The reward for those spending more than $500,000 in a year is a free tour of Prague.
"Right from the beginning of its history, Nieman Marcus has been an upscale store," said the chain's chairman and chief executive officer, Burt Tanksy, who noted that Nieman Marcus opened its first store in 1907 in Dallas to cater to the rich. The White Plains store opened in 1980.
Tiffany & Company has also been thriving in Westchester. The company opened a store 18 months ago in The Westchester, and its executive vice president, James E. Quinn, reported that it has become a top performer.
"We knew it would," Mr. Quinn said. "The demographics are here for Westchester. These are precision shoppers with high expectations."
Albert J. Pirro Jr., a lawyer to the rich and famous in Westchester, offered yet another perspective on the rich. His client list includes Donald Trump, the real estate developer, and Nelson Peltz, chairman and chief executive of the Triarc Companies, owner of the Royal Crown Cola Company and Arby's.
"These are giants," said Mr. Pirro, who has offices in White Plains. "But it's not true that they necessarily present the opportunity for others to make big dollars. These kinds of clients tend to be extremely prudent in spending their money."
Where do the wealthy play? William Weaver, owner of Westair Flying School in White Plains, said some fly private planes, which sell for $25,000 and up. They also hang out in their yachts, docked at clubs along the shores of the Hudson River and Long Island Sound. And they work out with personal trainers, who they pay $250 a week or more, in well-appointed gyms at their homes, said Bill Beck, general manager of Club Fit, which is located in White Plains, Briarcliff Manor and Jefferson Valley.
"Some preferences never change," Worth magazine said in its article. "Look for the rich by the water, close to horses or near a great golf course."
In its report on the rich, Worth magazine said that Bronxville followed Scarsdale in Westchester with an average household income of $189,300 and an average home value of $524,900. Also listed in the top 300 wealthiest communities, in order of their wealth, were Pound Ridge, Purchase, Chappaqua, Bedford, Briarcliff Manor, Rye, Millwood, Larchmont, Armonk, Cross River, Waccabuc, Pelham Manor, Mamaroneck Village, Harrison, Irvington, Rye Brook, South Salem and Katonah.
First on the magazine's list overall, was Rolling Hills, Calif., where the average household income is $305,700 and the average house costs $587,900.

HEADLINE: Another View Of a Dog Track

 BODY:
Now that the less-than-one-year-old Shoreline Star Greyhound Park and Entertainment Center is about to go belly up ("A Dog Track's Woes Added to a City's," Oct. 13), those politicians and business leaders who predicted it would mean economic revitalization for a deteriorating city seem to be nowhere to be quoted.
Perhaps it is because the decision to allow a dog track in Bridgeport has resulted in one embarrassing event after another: the Division of Special Revenue exempting the Shoreline proprietor from a background check; the proprietor's subsequent arrest on charges of destroying evidence; an ensuing State Organized Crime Task Force investigation of him; the track's recent declaration of Chapter 11 bankruptcy, and the finale -- the use of Connecticut taxpayers' money to pay for the proprietor's investigation. 
 How could a dog track have been perceived as a good investment to begin with, in light of dog racing's nationwide decline in popularity?
Six greyhound tracks have closed across the country since January 1995, 13 tracks have closed since 1990. Of the two that opened in 1995 (Camptown in Kansas and Shoreline in Connecticut) both have gone into bankruptcy and one has closed.
For the 1996 fiscal year, the revenue to the state from both dog tracks in Connecticut has amounted to less than one one-hundreth of 1 percent of the general fund.
From 1989 through 1994, revenue from greyhound racing in Connecticut has declined 84 percent.
Even when the business of greyhound racing was more lucrative, state revenue that the activity has generated has never been allocated to provide for the welfare of the dogs once their brief careers are over, not even to euthanize them humanely. Two New England states (Vermont and Maine) have reviewed the human and financial issues surrounding greyhound racing and have banned it.
Connecticut should be ashamed of its complicity in the destruction of an estimated 30,000-plus racing greyhounds in the United States each year, as it contributes to that number.
Connecticut racing greyhounds meet a fate that is similar to most racing greyhounds around the country; the animals are routinely sent to low-rent, less competitive tracks where they are abused, then ultimately destroyed by methods that have included bludgeoning, electrocution and shooting.
The people of Bridgeport deserve more; the dogs deserve more.
 
MELANI NARDONE
Stamford
 
he writer is a member of the Greyhound Protection League.

HEADLINE: THEATER;
An Italian Questions The Pope

BYLINE: By ALVIN KLEIN 

 BODY:
DARIO FO, the celebrated trouble-making Italian playwright, is having his say at Yale a year late and not in person.
Last season, Mr. Fo's scheduled appearance in his one-man show, "Johan Padan," which was to have been produced in repertory with "Sex? Thank You. My Pleasure!" a one-woman show by the playwright's wife and collaborator, Franca Rame, was canceled when Mr. Fo suffered a cerebral ischemia. 
 Not that Mr. Fo's translated words are being heard at Yale for the first time. The vast oeuvre of the 70-year-old playwright/performer/designer has twice been represented at the Yale Repertory where "About Face" was produced in 1983 and "Almost by Chance: A Woman" in 1987. This time, Mr. Fo's "The Pope and the Witch" is opening the season of the Yale School of Drama.
The production, with a cast of 12, all second- and third-year actors-in-training, playing 24 roles is the brainchild of 31-year-old Stephan Genn, a third-year student of directing.
Landing the rights to stage "The Pope and the Witch" was not enough for Mr. Genn, who quibbled with previous adaptations and wanted to come up with one of his own. "One was a bit Monty Python -- very funny but missing the political punch and power of the piece; another was more faithful to the original, but it didn't embrace the American, or let's say, the non-Italian audience," Mr. Genn said.
The other adaptors gave Mr. Genn permission to incorporate their work into his new version, with Melanie Deas, a third-year student of dramaturgy. In Mr. Fo's antic play, the Pope is holding his first press conference while abandoned children from everywhere are gathered in St. Peter's Square. Believing that the children were brought to the Vatican by pro-choice activists, the Pope suffers a breakdown. Enter a television psychiatrist-showman and a healer, the witch of the title, who runs an illegal drug treatment center.
Unsurprisingly, a 1992 production of the play by the American Conservatory Theater in San Francisco "created a big stink," Mr. Genn said. The play is not anti-Catholic, he added. "Fo, who is a member of the Communist Party, which is respected in Italy, is writing from an extreme left wing perspective," Mr. Genn said. "He is asking whether practices of enormous institutions, like the Vatican, are really following spiritual teachings. And he is using the Catholic Church as a symbol to deal with issues surrounding scandals and political intrigue, like the assassination theory about John Paul I. It's a real mystery. There's a kind of Oliver Stone movie in there."
While the sensibility of Dario Fo and the ways of Broadway hardly seem compatible, the iconoclastic playwright is no stranger to the commercial theater. Note a production of "Accidental Death of an Anarchist" in 1980, although it ran a mere five weeks, including two weeks of previews, with a cast that included Jonathan Pryce, Bill Irwin, Patti LuPone and Joe Grifasi.
Mr. Genn explained the play's structure -- which has moments of "vaudeville vamping," commedia dell'arte and involves, in the words of a publicity release, "rather unlikely monks and nuns." "It is written as a farce," he said. "In comedy, we try to make everything absurd. But in farce, we make the outrageous normal. And Fo is playing with the notion of tragedy without catharsis, as a direct call to action."
"It forces the audience to rethink everything. This is not standard left wing critique of the church. It has to do with facing a new socio-economic reality. That creates a crisis for the Pope. Whether we're Catholic or not -- and I'm not -- the play asks us to just take stock. If we do that, it will be an enormous success."
Dario Fo's "Pope and the Witch" can be seen at the University Theater (220 York Street, New Haven; 203-432-1234) Tuesday through next Saturday.
The Yale School of Drama's season also includes the following productions: "Troy Women," an adaptation of Euripedes' work by Karen Hartman, directed by Laura Stribling (Jan. 14-18); Peter Handke's "Ride Across Lake Constance" directed by Andrew Utter (March 11-15), and the Brecht-Weill musical "Happy End" directed by Eleanor Holdridge (May 2-7).

HEADLINE: Vehicle Pollution Testing Is Delayed by Dispute

BYLINE: By JULIE MILLER 

 BODY:
WAITING is a common theme in stories about the State Department of Motor Vehicles. And the latest story is about a new emissions testing program that is said to be more convenient and more effective in detecting pollution than the test currently used. The program, delayed for three years, is scheduled to begin early next year. But there are questions about it that are still unresolved, and more waiting is a possibility.
The delay is due to a dispute between the Motor Vehicles Department and the Envirotest Systems Corporation of Arizona, the company operating the emissions test. The financial terms of the department's $138 million contract with Envirotest are among the issues in debate. 
 Michael Kozlowski, the Commissioner of the department, said he and Envirotest have settled most of their differences, and a draft of the revised contract will be sent to the state's Attorney General, Richard Blumenthal, for review.
While vehicles in the current emissions test are inspected during idling, the new test will require those with computer controls to be placed on a dynamometer, a device that simulates actual driving, for 93 seconds. In addition, gas cap seals will be checked to make sure they are tight, and certain vehicle models will be required to have catalytic converters. Vehicles manufactured between 1968 and 1980 will continue to be tested while idling.
Controversy over the program began in 1993, when then-Gov. Lowell P. Weicker Jr. dismissed Louis Goldberg, then-Commissioner of the Motor Vehicles Department, after Mr. Goldberg opposed the awarding of the contract to Envirotest. The contract, which runs for seven and a half years, was signed in 1994 after heavy lobbying. Mr. Kozlowski reviewed the contract in 1995, shortly after he took office under Gov. John G. Rowland. Included in the agreement was a 240-second emissions test, known as the I/M 240, which was mandated under the Federal Clean Air Act.
Mr. Kozlowski said he felt the I/M 240 was too stringent and would create longer lines at the testing stations. So he backed away from the deal and began renegotiating with Envirotest. "I was worried about pushing too many people through too many lanes and causing a problem with a test I didn't think was necessary," he said.
At the same time, the Federal Environmental Protection Agency, under pressure from several states, decided to allow tests shorter than the I/M 240. Breaking the contract with Envirotest at that point would have cost the state between $40 million and $50 million, Mr. Kozlowski said. "A lot of people asked, 'Why don't you toss it out?' But the contract requires the state to pay Envirotest a certain amount if we terminate it, even if the company can't perform. How's that for a deal?"
Envirotest has saved at least $2 million since the contract was signed, because the more expensive 93-second test and other commitments have not been carried out. The Motor Vehicles Department wants the company to return the money, Mr. Kozlowski said. Laura Baker, public information manager at Envirotest, said company officials cannot comment on the contract because negotiations are still going on.
Last April, Envirotest requested a settlement by arbitration, a solution unacceptable to Mr. Kozlowski. "If we go into arbitration, the losing party will appeal, and this won't be resolved for two or three years," he said. "Connecticut can't wait two or three years, because the Federal Government requires us to have something in place by the end of 1997. Otherwise, we stand to lose $350 million in Federal highway funding."
As State Senator William Aniskovich, a Republican and chairman of the transportation committee, sees it: "We're at the whim of Federal changes. The I/M 240 was required. Now it's voluntary. We're struggling with the fact that we signed a contract before that change occurred. This is an all-too-typical example of the bureaucrats' tendency to ignore the real situations they create when they change regulations."
State Representative Jessie Stratton, a Democrat and chairman of the environment committee, said: "One of the enormous advantages to us is that we're not left like Pennsylvania, which is buying itself out of a contract. We'll end up with an emissions test."
The Motor Vehicles Department is attempting to be friendlier to motorists by making emissions testing less time-consuming, Mr. Kozlowski said. Five of seven new testing stations called for in the contract were opened last year in East Lyme, Southington, Manchester, Thomaston and Bridgeport. The shorter, 93-second emissions test is as effective in detecting pollution as the I/M 240, since a problem caused by faulty equipment shows up in the first minute, said John DeCicco, an analyst with the American Council for an Energy-Efficient Economy in Washington, D.C.
Other changes are being made. Older vehicles continue to be tested annually, but the fee has been dropped to $10 from $15. The emissions inspection fee is $20 for newer vehicles, and drivers receive a two-year sticker. Drivers whose vehicles fail the new test will be allowed 30 days for repairs and one free reinspection.
As in the past, an application can be made for a waiver if a vehicle fails the initial test and retest. Under the new policy, at least $250 worth of emissions repairs are required for a two-year waiver for a vehicle manufactured after 1980. Drivers of vehicles made between 1968 and 1980 must spend at least $125 in repairs for a one-year waiver. The Federal Government recommended a waiver requirement of $450 in repairs.
Under the contract with Envirotest, fleet vehicles must be driven through the emissions test. But the Motor Vehicles Department plans to allow fleet owners to inspect their vehicles themselves, Mr. Kozlowski said. "We want to be friendly to business," he explained, "and we don't want to add cars to lanes."
Another convenience to motorists will be the addition of inspection lanes at 11 emissions testing stations, Mr. Kozlowski said. Most safety checks are currently done at the department's branch offices. As he assembles the new program, Mr. Kozlowski said, he balances the need for shorter waiting lines against reality. "We have a pollution problem," he said. "We're trying to find a way to accomplish both ends."

HEADLINE: Commercial Property/Real Estate Investment Trusts;
Amid an Improving Climate, REITs Grow Steadily

BYLINE: By JOHN HOLUSHA 

 BODY:
I REALLY did not want to do $330 million in acquisitions in one year," said Scott Rechler, executive vice president and chief operating officer of Reckson Associates Realty Corporation. "We really had planned to do only $100 million, but some strategic opportunities became available, so we took them."
Indeed, Mr. Rechler has been on a tear, acquiring the Halpern Enterprises of Tarrytown, which owned 935,000 square feet of office space in Westchester County for $86 million last November. In September, he added the F.D. Rich-developed Landmark Square office complex, which has 800,000 square feet of office space in Stamford, Conn., for approximately $79 million. 
 Not bad for the Melville-based company that formerly confined its activities to industrial parks and office developments on Long Island. But Reckson is no longer the family real estate company it was for three generations.
Thanks to a public stock offering in May, 1995, Reckson has become a real estate investment trust, a publicly owned company with access to capital markets and shareholders to satisfy. It has broken out of its Long Island base with the acquisitions in Westchester and Fairfield Counties and is looking elsewhere.
"We have defined our operating area as within a 50 mile radius of Manhattan, with Manhattan and the boroughs carved out," Mr. Rechler said. He said the city was omitted from the company's target area, "because New York City is a very different game and we do not have the expertise to play in that game." Nevertheless, the company has grown from $400 million in assets to $775 million, and from 5 million square feet of property to 9.2 million square feet in a little over a year.
If Mr. Rechler starts looking at office property in New Jersey, he is likely to run into the Cali Realty Corporation, the other fast-growing, locally managed REIT (pronounced "reet") in the suburban area. Cali, which is based in Cranford, N.J., recently agreed to acquire the 1.9 million square foot Harborside Financial Center in Jersey City for $282 million.
"In 1994, when we did our initial public offering, we had $280 million in assets," said Thomas A. Rizk, the president and chief executive of Cali, which was a family-owned residential and commercial company for 45 years before selling stock. "Now, with Harborside, we are over $1 billion. That would have been unthinkable under our old structure."
He said the company's goal was to become a "superregional" real estate operation focusing on north central New Jersey. "We have a couple of 100 million square feet of office space to choose from, so it is a very big playing field," Mr. Rizk said. And he is busy playing. "We have a $1 billion in acquisitions in the pipeline," he said.
With banks and insurance companies badly wounded in the last real estate recession, REITs have grown rapidly and continue to do so, even though some traditional lenders have moved cautiously back into the market. "Insurance companies have pulled out of owned real estate and gone into lending because they want to be more liquid," said Darcy A. Stacom, a senior director at the Cushman & Wakefield brokerage in Manhattan. "So some of the funds and money managers have been putting capital into REITs."
Now, according to industry executives, pension funds have become the biggest investors in REITs. "At the moment, there is approximately $75 million a week of new money being invested in REITs," Mr. Rizk said.
Not all REITs are the same. The first of the current wave were floated by owners of large numbers of regional malls, such as A. Alfred Taubman of Bloomfield Hills, Mich., who were seeking to reduce their debt burden. Those were followed by REITs that invested in residential property, with office REITs a relatively recent development.
The current round of REITs differ sharply from the ones popular in the 1970's. Today's REITs are equity investors and are managed directly. The 1970s REITs were mostly mortgage lenders managed by advisers who, as one executive put it delicately, "did not always have their interests aligned with investors."
SINCE 1990, 131 REITs have gone public, raising a total of $20.2 billion in their initial public offerings, according to the National Association of Real Estate Investment Trusts in Washington. The entire REIT industry has a stock market capitalization of $70.5 billion.
According to many in the real estate industry, the rapidly growing public ownership of equity in real estate, notably by REITs, will largely put an end to family-owned commercial real estate companies. Mr. Rechler flatly predicts it.
"Within the next three to five years, a majority of domestic real estate will be publicly owned," he said. "The family company is dead."
"There may be some role for family operations in land development," said Jon Halpern, whose family company is now Reckson's Westchester division.
"We believe the REIT industry is here to stay," Mr. Rizk said. "There is a significant competitive advantage over a family company."
But Simon J. Milde, chairman of the Greenwich Group, a New York-based real estate marketing and investment services group, cautions that individuals are better innovators than organizations. "The REITs will own property that had been owned by institutions, but the family companies will continue to build and grow," he said.
Among the advantages of the REITs, their partisans say, is the ability to conclude deals rapidly, without the need to jump through hoops trying to arrange financing. "We have credibility, because we can close within 30 days," Mr. Rechler said. "We can bid less than other developers and still make the acquisition because of our ability to close."
Good properties are becoming available at significant discounts to replacement costs, industry executive say, because they reverted to lenders during the most recent downturn or the owners find themselves pinched for cash and facing big tax problems if they do a conventional sale.
"In the late 1980's a lot of people were forced to put in capital to attract tenants," Mr. Halpern said. "Now the property owners are stuck with debt coming due." Even though there will be little cash left on the table after these debts are paid if a property is sold, many have large paper capital gains that would lead to a large tax liability.
By conveying the property to a REIT through a device called an umbrella partnership and accepting partnership units rather than cash or stock, the capital gains tax is deferred until some other event takes place, such as the conversion of the units to REIT stock.
"It's a way to defer a problem," said Lawrence S. Jones, chairman of the REIT practice at Coopers & Lybrand. "It is often better to do it this way, rather than sell a property outright."
A REIT provides a nice tax break for investors as well because if it pays out 95 percent of its taxable income in dividends the income is taxed only at the individual level, rather than taxed at both the corporate and individual levels. This leads to dividends that are fat by corporate standards, often in the 5 to 7 percent range annually.
Although they are obviously becoming active in the New York suburbs, REITs have largely avoided the city itself. Real estate executives say this is because the city's scale is hard to square with the typical REIT goal of spreading credit and geographic risks over many properties. Buying a New York skyscraper for $300 million or $400 million would unbalance most REIT portfolios, they say.
SOME industry executives say that the liquidity provided by REITs will smooth the real estate cycle and that the discipline of the stock market will prevent the kind of lending that fueled the building boom of the mid-1980's. During that time, developers were putting up $400 million office skyscrapers in midtown Manhattan with virtually no money of their own invested in the project.
Many of those undertakings failed when they hit the soft market at the end of the decade and reverted to the lenders who absorbed the loss between the cost of development and the amount for which the property could be sold.
"Wall Street will rein the investments," said Steven L. Kantor, managing director of the real estate group at Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette. "It is a self correcting machanism."
He said that when analysts start souring on a stock, or individual investors become alarmed at the deals a REIT is announcing, shares will be sold, the price will go down and the funds for further investment will dry up. "If you don't like what's happening you can sell the stock," he said. "That's the point, you can get out."
But others worry that the search for high yields will drive REITs into ever riskier investments.
"It's getting tougher to get that 25 percent return," said Mr. Milde. "Two or three years ago you could buy good, well located buildings, but they are getting scarce. Now the only way you can get high returns is from new development. I think the next building boom will be fueled by Wall Street."

HEADLINE: Women, the Issues And the Elections

BYLINE: By SUSAN PEARSALL 

 BODY:
AS the 1996 campaign season draws to a close, women's advocates in Connecticut say this may be remembered as the Year of the Woman Voter. Women's groups that are active politically are poised to capitalize on state and national polls that give President Clinton a large lead over Bob Dole.
"I think women voters in Connecticut are going to be critical, especially Republican women, whom I expect are going to cross party lines," said Laurel Tiesinga, executive director of the Connecticut National Abortion and Reproductive Rights Action League. Many Republican women disagree with Mr. Dole's anti-abortion rights position, she said. 
 Ms. Tiesinga predicted that women would head for the polls in greater numbers than in 1994, when less than 72 percent of registered women voted in gubernatorial and Congressional races, compared to 79 percent of registered men, according to the Center for the American Woman and Politics in Rutgers, N. J. "I think women have seen the outcome of their staying home in '94, which was the election of this anti-woman Congress," Ms. Tiesinga said, referring to Republican majorities on Capitol Hill.
The woman's vote has become increasingly important this fall as pollsters have identified a record gender gap. G. Donald Ferree Jr., a pollster at the University of Connecticut Institute for Social Inquiry in Storrs, cited the most recent state survey, which was conducted from Oct. 17 to last Wednesday and included Ross Perot as a contender. Mr. Ferree said that men came down as 40 percent for Mr. Clinton, 29 percent for Mr. Dole and 24 percent for Mr. Perot. Conversely, he said, "Women were 59 percent Clinton, 24 percent Dole and 8 percent Perot."
Four years ago, an institute survey predicted Mr. Clinton would garner 51 percent of the female vote and 41 percent of the male vote in a three-way contest with then-President George Bush and Mr. Perot.
In general, women tend to be more interested than men in human service issues including health care, day care, education and growing older, said Leslie Brett, executive director of the Permanent Commission on the Status of Women in Hartford. "In many cases, they are in charge of those problems in their lives," Ms. Brett explained.
"I expect a high turnout in this election," she said. "Things that are very important to women's lives are being debated." These include late-term abortions, support for public education, school vouchers and managed care reform, she said.
In February, the General Assembly is likely to address other issues that concern women. As the state moves more people off welfare, there will be a greater need for affordable child care, education and job training, Ms. Brett said. In addition, there could be talk of economic development for small businesses, frequently owned by women, and continuing action on managed care reform, she said.
Lara Manzione, director of the Connecticut chapter of the National Organization for Women, said her group is interested in presenting a domestic partnership law next year allowing homosexual couples to receive the same benefits as married couples.
One surprise -- Connecticut NOW has endorsed Charlotte Koskoff of Plainville, a Democrat, over incumbent Rep. Nancy L. Johnson, a Republican.
"We think that Nancy Johnson, who has been a friend of ours in the past, has shifted her focus away from representing the women in the district and has too closely aligned herself with Newt Gingrich," said Ms. Manzione.
In the State Senate, Republicans hold a slim 19-17 majority, which could fall to the Democrats if they win three races where Republican incumbents are considered by some analysts to be vulnerable. Connecticut NOW has endorsed all three Democratic candidates in those districts: Gary LeBeau of East Hartford, Mary Ann Handley of Manchester, and Tom Ganim of Fairfield. The Connecticut National Abortion and Reproductive Rights Action League, however, preferred the incumbent, Kevin Rennie, over Mr. LeBeau in East Hartford. What should women do to make the political process work for them?
"Women need to be unapologetic about saying that women's issues are priorities and that they will vote on the basis of candidates' positions on them," said Ms. Brett. "But also, women need to run, and they need to run at all levels."
Connecticut has a good reputation for electing women to office. In 1984, Ella T. Grasso became the first woman in the country to be elected in her own right as Governor. Today, both the Lieutenant Governor and Comptroller are women, and the Congressional delegation is 50 percent female. Connecticut ranks 11th among the 50 states for its number of female legislators, according to the Center for the American Woman and Politics. Currently, nearly 27 percent of the 187 state legislators are women. This fall, women are on the ballot in 40 percent of General Assembly contests.
"In many ways, we have a lot to brag about in Connecticut," Ms. Brett said. "But I don't think we can sit back and be too happy until it starts to hover around 50 percent all the time."
In 1994, Andree Aelion Brooks of Westport, a writer, founded the Women's Campaign School at Yale University. Each June, the school offers an intensive, five-day course taught by political consultants and successful candidates. Between courses, the school holds occasional one-day workshops, the next one scheduled for Dec. 7 and featuring Kathleen Harrington, former aide to Elizabeth Dole.
"I saw that there was an increasing pool of talented, experienced women in business and the professions who were not coming forward to go into elective office," Ms. Brooks recalled. "They were not making the leap into politics like successful men."
So far, more than 150 women have taken the course or attended a workshop, Ms. Brooks said. Last year, 10 graduates ran for municipal office and eight were elected.
This fall, three graduates are on the ballot in Connecticut: Terry M. Nevas of Westport is the Natural Law candidate for the Fourth Congressional District; Lisa E. Mason is challenging Michael J. Jarjura in Waterbury's 74th District, and Stephanie H. Sanchez is opposing the incumbent, Janet K. Lockton, for the 149th District seat serving Greenwich and Stamford.
This is the first campaign for Ms. Mason, a gregarious 43-year-old Republican who is as a sales representative for a golf equipment company. She said she entered the race after learning Mr. Jarjura, the Democratic incumbent, was a partner in a large development project in the district, a choice she believes compromises his integrity.
Mr. Jarjura says he does not see any potential conflict of interest stemming from this project or other commercial property he owns. He is not seeking zoning changes, and there have been no complaints filed with the state Ethics Commission, he said.
"I'm stressing my experience," Mr. Jarjura said. He cites helping secure $31 million in state money to demolish the former Scovill Manufacturing Company complex (off Interstate 84) and to do an environmental clean up of the site. A huge, regional shopping mall is planned for the location. Mr. Jarjura also mentions his vote to reduce income taxes this year for the first $15,000 of income.
Ms. Mason said gender, per se, has not been a problem for her. But fund raising is another matter. "When it comes to raising money, I don't have that 'old boy' network to go back to," she said. "And that is a gender issue." Generally, when women support Ms. Mason's campaign, "It will be $25, which I'm thrilled to take," she said. "But a man giving to another man, those checks are bigger."
Mr. Jarjura scoffed at the idea women have a more difficult time raising money for campaigns. "It's hard for everybody to raise money," he said. Mr. Jarjura said he expects to raise about $20,000 for his campaign; Ms. Mason said she has budgeted $10,000.
In the Greenwich-Stamford district race, both candidates are pro-choice women. Mrs. Lockton has been endorsed by Connecticut NARAL and Ms. Sanchez has the support of Connecticut NOW.
On the campaign trail, Ms. Sanchez is talking about improving education, the environment and the economy. Mrs. Lockton has recommended eliminating the income tax in Bridgeport to attract more middle class families, establishing medical I.R.A.s and enticing the state's best teachers to serve in city schools.
Ms. Sanchez expects to spend $10,000 to $15,000 on the race; Mrs. Lockton said her campaign will cost $14,000 to $16,000.
What happens in these races will depend on voter turnout, which could be a wild card this year due to some unusual circumstances.
Howard Reiter, a professor of political science at the University of Connecticut for 22 years, said voter registration is up thanks to a strong effort by Secretary of State Miles Rapoport and a new Federal law that allows voters to register at many state and Federal offices. On the other hand, Dr. Reiter says he doesn't see much excitment among voters for the Presidential candidates.
"You have these two factors working against each other," Dr. Reiter said. "I think it's very hard to predict." Voter turnout has been decreasing since 1960, except for 1992, Dr. Reiter said.
Mr. Ferree, the pollster in Storrs, said there are fewer undecided voters than in previous Presidential elections, but "the attention to the campaign seems to be somewhat less than four years ago."
"We don't see a single, dominant issue this year," Mr. Ferree said, "unlike 1992 when the situation was pointedly summed up by Clinton adviser James Carville, who said, 'It's the economy, stupid.' "
Mr. Ferree also expressed some reservations about how women's issues might play out in the election. "There is no clear evidence that women take a stronger pro-choice position than men do," he said.
Since September, Jean Rexford of Westport has been canvassing the state registering new voters for Women Voting for Our Future '96, an alliance of 45 civic, church and women's groups. This week, alliance members will be busy mailing postcards and making phone calls to get out the vote. She estimated the alliance had registered at least 2,000 new voters, most of them women.
Last year, Kate Cosgrove, was elected First Selectman for Oxford, a town of 9,300. It was the first time in the town's 197-year history that a woman sought the position. With her victory came a new sense of duty.
"I work very hard at what I do," Mrs. Cosgrove said. "I feel a responsibility so that other women can follow in this position.
"It can be done."

Mason, left, a Democrat, is challenging Michael J. Jarjura in the 74th House District. (Photographs by Helen Neafsey for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: In the Region/New Jersey;
Easing the Re-Use of Once-Polluted Industrial Sites

BYLINE: By RACHELLE GARBARINE 

 BODY:
FOR the last 12 years the big industrial plant on Finderne Avenue in the Somerset County community of Bridgewater has sat vacant, idled by time and environmental concerns.
Now the 316,000-square-foot building set on 36 acres where the Singer company once manufactured electric motors has been given a second chance. The derelict site in the south-central portion of the township has been cleaned and sold to a new owner who is turning it into a distribution center. 
 ATS Wood Products, a local wood-processing and recyling firm, bought the property in August for $4.85 million to meet its expansion needs. The seller was Prospect Industries, a now-defunct manufacturer of steel cans that cleaned the site of the petroleum hydrocarbons left by Singer, which operated there until the early 70's.
The project reflects the slowly changing attitudes among buyers to re-use former contaminated properties. It also is evidence of the changing regulatory climate, in the state and the nation, toward relaxing government rules and offering incentives to steer development to such sites, known as brownfields. Generally, they are sites that are less polluted than a toxic-waste dumps, but need a more extensive cleanup than undeveloped green spots in suburban or rural areas.
In 1993, New Jersey acted to facilitate the reclamation of such sites by passing a law that adjusted pollution treatment standards. Subsequently the state provided tax incentives to buyers and, in some cases, eased the threat of liability .
Bills are working their way through the Legislature that would move those efforts even farther. And the Federal Environmental Protection Agency is, among other things, giving grants of up to $200,000 to cities across the nation to study the potential for brownfield development.
Of New Jersey's 23,000 known or suspected contaminated sites, nearly 50 percent have been cleaned up or are being investigated, according to the state Department of Environmental Protection.
"The change in the regulatory climate is meant to protect the environment and at the same time encourage development," said Fredi L. Pearlmutter, a Summit lawyer who specializes in environmental issues.
The Bridgewater site only partially benefited from the change.
In 1984, when Nathan Milikowsky, who bought Prospect Industries two years earlier, went to sell the site it triggered the tough new state law requiring that such tracts be cleaned of pollutants before they can be sold. A year later the sale unraveled and the state closed the building.
Mr. Milikowsky, who by then had consolidated the business in North Brunswick, was liable to clean the contaminants Singer had left on the site, which over time had seeped throughout the building and into the ground beneath it.
Mr. Milikowsky filed a lawsuit against Singer to finance the cost of the cleanup and a 1989 settlement awarded him $5 million. The cleanup began a year later and by the time it was completed in 1995 Mr. Milikowsky said he had spent a total of $18 million -- including legal, engineering and carrying costs, such as security and property taxes.
Until that point the site was virtually unmarketable, said Paul D. Cohen, executive vice president at the Edison office of SBWE Inc., the brokerage marketing the property.
The cleanup was approved two years after changes were made in the state law governing the cleanup of such sites. In essence that 1993 law, the Industrial Sites Recovery Act, set up different standards for residential and nonresidential cleanups. It also permits alternative methods, including fences and impermeable caps to stop wastes from seeping out instead of requiring their removal.
THE Bridgewater case helped bring about those changes. And in turn, Mr. Cohen said, the change in standards expedited the state approval for the cleanup of the site, which was brought up to industrial standards. The site also carries deed restrictions to insure its industrial use.
The law also provided other incentives, including a $55 million fund that qualified municipalities and developers can tap to receive grants or low-interest loans to finance cleanups. To date, the state has approved some $20 million in grants or loans for about 164 projects.
Because the cleanup of the Bridgewater site was nearly complete by the time of the sale Mr. Milikowsky said he had received only $800,000 from the state fund. That along with the proceeds from the sale and some insurance money, however, was not enough to keep Prospect Industries out of bankruptcy, he said.
There have been other changes since the 1993 cleanup law went into effect. To further defray the cost of cleanups the state last January passed the Environmental Opportunity Zone Act. It permits municipalities to offer up to a 10-year tax abatement to reclaim industrial tracts and revive them for commercial or industrial use. And in July the state passed another law that offers purchasers of sites that have been, or are being, cleaned according to state standards immunity from liability from the state for past environmental problems if they are in qualified municipalities -- 66 of the state's total of 567.
Yet, the cost of cleaning up such sites and the potential liability risks they still carry remain hurdles said State Senator James E. McGreevey, a Democrat who is also Mayor of Woodbridge. His township is trying to spur the redevelopment of 800 industrial acres within its borders.
To that end Mr. McGreevey has introduced a bill that would, among other things, extend the Environmental Opportunity Zone Act to include converting industrial sites to residential use. The bill would also extend the state immunity to all qualified municipalities. Several other bills have been introduced that would further streamline the cleanup process.
"Removing the liability issue is one of several keys in attracting new developers and investors to these sites," said Ira Whitman, who heads an environmental consulting firm bearing his name in East Brunswick.
Also, the Federal Environmental Protection Agency is working out agreements with buyers to insulate them from E.P.A. suits. And it is backing demonstration projects in 76 cities across the nation, including Trenton, Newark and Camden.
"Any changes in the current laws and the adoption of new ones that make it easier to correct the problems are valuable," said Karen Waldron, manager of Trenton's brownfields project. She said Trenton initially was investigating three of its 50-plus brownfields to market to developers for re-use, including the former Champale brewery on Lamberton Street, near the city's new minor league baseball stadium.
Lawrence D'Andrea, brownfield coordinator at the E.P.A. regional office in Manhattan, said the agency would give out 25 more grants totaling $5 million and would provide $20 million more to help finance the cleanup of some of the demonstration projects as well as others.
DESPITE the problems, some investors see such sites as an opportunity to buy land at a reduced cost, said Mr. Cohen of the SBWE brokerage. But Ronald T. Corcory, assistant director of the site remediation division in the Department of Environmental Protection, added: "Brownfields only work if the economics are there." What it comes down is location, he said.
Location is what drew ATS Wood Products to the Bridgewater site, which Joseph Horner, the company's president, said was close to its recyling operation and convenient to Route 22 and Interstate 287. It was also the right size and price. "We couldn't duplicate this building in Somerset County for this cost," said Mr. Horner, adding that "we had done our homework and felt comfortable the site was absolutely clean."
As part of the deal Mr. Horner idemnified Mr. Milikowsky from any future liability and agreed to continue to monitor the groundwater on the site -- a state requirement.
ATS received an $11.5 million at below-prime-rate, 20-year loan from the New Jersey Economic Development Authority to acquire, renovate and equip the building. Mr. Horner said the work would be completed by next fall.

SECTION: Section 9; Page 7; Column 1; Real Estate Desk 

LENGTH: 1240 words

HEADLINE: In the Region/Connecticut;
For New Haven, a Million-Square-Foot Indoor Mall

BYLINE: By ELEANOR CHARLES 

DATELINE: NEW HAVEN

 BODY:
SEVENTEEN large enclosed malls might be thought to be enough for a small state like Connecticut. But plans are well advanced for a million-square-foot complex in New Haven, facing the harbor at Long Wharf. It is being hailed as the salvation of a city too long becalmed in the economic doldrums.
"It will mean millions of shopping trips into New Haven a year," said Mayor John DeStefano Jr., "2,200 permanent jobs, $2.6 million in tax revenues. And the developer gets no tax breaks or subsidies." 
 This is not the first time that a mall has been on New Haven's agenda. For more than a decade a mall developer was sought to rehabilitate the city's gateway -- a stretch of Church Street lined with boarded-up stores, graffiti and litter. Deal after deal to replace the vacant Malley's and Macy's department stores and the faltering Chapel Square Mall have fallen through for lack of funds.
After considering 10 new proposals, the city chose New England Development of Newton, Mass., and the Fusco Corporation, a New Haven construction firm, to plan and build a $100 million regional mall at Long Wharf, tentatively called The Marketplace at New Haven. It will occupy 60 acres at the intersection of Interstate 95, I-91 and Route 34. The developers expect to apply for site plan approval in 1997 and complete the project by 2000.
"Financing we pretty much do on our own," said Stephen Karp, chairman of New England Development. His company has built 17 malls, mostly in New England. The Crystal Mall in Waterford is his only other Connecticut project.
"New England Development's strength is in being one of the premier high-end retail developers in the country," said Mayor DeStefano, "and Fusco brings a history of three generations of investment in the city and state."
Negotiations have begun on acquiring properties from the Pirelli-Armstrong Tire Corporation, Schiavone Realty Development Corporation, Modern Food Equipment and the Connecticut Department of Transportation. The eight-acre property of Connecticut Freezer, a cold-storage warehouse, has been optioned by the developers.
"Everyone is coming to the table understanding that we wish to do business without getting the city involved in condemnation," said Mr. Karp, who is also negotiating with high-fashion department stores to fill four anchor spots.
Abandoning the idea of a downtown mall has opened up new vistas for New Haven. "A mall right off the highway will make this a destination," said Salvatore Brancati, the city's Director of Economic Development. "Property values will rise and waterfront activity will be stimulated." Inquiries have already been made by excursion boats wanting to dock near the mall.
WHILE several businesses have operated at Long Wharf for decades, the area is isolated from the rest of the city by the railroad tracks and by I-95. New Haven's master plan envisions improved roads, a new railroad bridge and shuttle buses taking shoppers and their pocketbooks from the mall into this city's downtown, which will be dedicated to the arts, entertainment, specialty shops and restaurants.
A first step was taken by obtaining a commitment from the mall developers to create a plan for Macy's, now owned by the city, and a developer will be sought for Malley's. In a state of terminal disrepair, Malley's would be demolished if New Haven wins three lawsuits brought against it by the owner, Mordecai Litkis of New York City, for grievances varying from a condemnation appraisal to denial of permission to open a flea market.
The downtown entertainment district would expand New Haven's rich base of cultural amenities that includes Yale University's art galleries, concert halls and the Yale Repertory Theater, the New Haven Symphony Orchestra, the Shubert and Palace Theaters, numerous other art galleries, boutiques, restaurants and the mix of exemplary Colonial, Gothic, Victorian and contemporary architecture in which they are all housed.
Mayor DeStefano has suggested moving the prestigious Long Wharf Theater from the harborfront to downtown, a proposal that Edgar Rosenblum, director of the theater, would consider if, he said, "somebody builds us a theater with good access from the highway at a probable cost of $35 million."
Another element in the downtown revival is the rebuilding of the Park Plaza Hotel on Temple Street by the Cordish Company of Baltimore and Omni International, partners in the venture. The $25 million conversion of the 18-story structure into the Omni Hotel at Yale begins next month and is scheduled for completion in late 1997.
"Yale will be a prime source of hotel patrons," said Blake Cordish, vice president of the company. A $9.9 million grant by the state will keep room rents at "an acceptable level."
In addition, Cordish has optioned the Chapel Square Mall, which abuts the hotel on three sides and is owned by the Greater New Haven Chamber of Commerce. It contains 250,000 square feet of retail space and an 11-story office tower, now 40 percent and 60 percent vacant, respectively. Mr. Cordish plans to renovate it into a 20-screen cinema, restaurants, a comedy club and shops.
Getting to all these diversions will involve major highway construction headaches. About 120,000 cars a day traverse I-95 at Long Wharf, or use its interchange to enter and leave New Haven. The mall will bring more than 30,000 additional cars daily. And just east of the interchange I-91 merges into I-95 from three lanes to one, creating a motorist's nightmare.
Plans by the state's Department of Transportation have been in the works for some time to reconfigure the merge, widen the Quinnipiac Bridge leading to the Long Wharf interchange, and move the interchange slightly west. "We will present six alternatives for public hearing by the end of this year," said Carmen P. Trotta, supervising planner for the department. They range from rehabilitating the existing three-lane bridge to demolishing it and building a 10-lane structure south of it. Developers, he noted, are required to pay for whatever improvements must be made to accommodate traffic generated by their projects.
WHILE support for the mall is virtually universal, the Westfield Corporation of Los Angeles and the Jerde Partnership, a Venice, Calif., design firm, oppose it and are jointly drafting a presentation of their own for reviving downtown New Haven. Westfield has built four Connecticut malls -- in Milford and Trumbull, both within 16 miles of Long Wharf, and in Enfield and Meriden.
"What is proposed is over-retailing for New Haven," said Richard Green, president of Westfield. "The demographics are not there for an upscale mall. Bloomingdale's and Neiman Marcus will not go into New Haven. They will not see it as a viable market. If the goal is to revitalize downtown it will not be achieved with a mall at Long Wharf that will suck every retailing opportunity out of the city."
His view is not shared by Frank Logue, New Haven's elder statesman and two-term mayor from 1976 through 1979. He said he had strongly opposed a proposed mall in North Haven "because it was likely to take jobs and taxes away from New Haven."
Concerned about declining population, white flight, and punishing property taxes of $61.14 per $1,000 of assessment, he said, "this mall might take some jobs, but it will also create them. Any adverse effect on downtown businesses would be offset by a net gain in jobs and taxes."

HEADLINE: NEW JERSEY & CO.;
What Makes a Toy Fun? Ask a Child Who Wants to Own It

BYLINE: By BILL KENT 

DATELINE: MOUNT LAUREL

 BODY:
Dressed like a big sister home from high school, Amy Pace asked six 5-year-old girls what their favorite colors were.
Some of the girls looked at the yellow, turquoise and maroon tiles on the ceiling of the test playroom at Tyco Toys here and repeated what they saw. Others looked at some brightly colored dolls invitingly arranged a few yards away. One girl studied her reflection in a large mirrored wall. 
 Then she saw the dolls. "Can we play now?" she asked impatiently.
Behind the mirrored wall, which lets a half-dozen Tyco employees observe the children without being seen, Donna Trepiccione cheered. As the designer of the dolls that were being tested, she was delighted that one girl wanted to skip Ms. Pace's carefully worded introduction about how it was O.K. for each girl to have her own favorite color, toy preferences and opinions. That a tester was eager to play with Ms. Trepiccione's dolls meant that the dolls had visual appeal.
Standing behind Ms. Trepiccione, Lori Gardner, a research analyst, was subdued. As the coordinator of this session, one of three she conducts each week, Ms. Gardner would issue a confidential, highly technical report about play patterns, toy durability, "can-do aspects" and other "playability characteristics" that could ultimately influence whether Ms. Trepiccione's dolls would be on toy stores' shelves for Christmas 1998.
Ms. Trepiccione beamed as the children pounced on her dolls. Then she heard the snap of plastic cracking. "Break out the zip and kicker," she sighed.
As she gathered her bottles of zip (a fast-drying glue), and kicker (a spray that makes the glue dry even faster), Craig Spitzer, the Tyco executive in charge of what he calls the pre-search program, said: "These are exactly the kinds of things we want to happen. At this stage, we're not concerned with how much it will cost to make the toys or if they'll appeal to the retailers. We just want to see what goes on when you put toys in a room with bunch of normal kids."
Tyco's most popular products include the View-Master, Magna Doodle, "Sesame Street" preschool toys, Matchbox diecast cars and a line of radio-controlled model vehicles. The company has 2,200 employees worldwide, with 350 at its Mount Laurel headquarters. It reported $709 million in gross sales last year and last week announced a sales record of $256 million for the third quarter.
Although a majority of its toys are currently made in Asia, Tyco, America's third largest toy maker, has tried out prospective toys on South Jersey children since 1926, when John Tyler of Mantua founded the Mantua Metal Products company to make model railroad equipment.
"Most of the time, the toys were tested on employees' children or their neighbors' children, long after toys were out of the design phase," Mr. Spitzer said. "We didn't really get scientific about it until I came on board."
That was in 1992. Mr. Spitzer, who has a Ph.D. in psychology from the University of Illinois, was hired from Mattel, America's largest toy maker, to design a testing facility at Tyco's headquarters here.
What began in an unused corporate meeting room, where Tyco employees fidgeted in a corner, has evolved into an airy, carpeted, well-lighted playroom with a spacious observation room, audio and video monitoring systems, a toy-filled waiting room for parents, and a database of 6,800 South Jersey children, from preschoolers to teen-agers.
The children, recruited through advertisements in schools and local family publications, are added to Tyco's database and called randomly. Racial, ethnic and economic backgrounds are not considered. The children are given toys -- not the ones they test -- for spending 20 minutes or so playing under the watchful eyes of Tyco employees.
Toys must pass a safety inspection before children test them, Mr. Spitzer said. "Child safety is our No. 1 concern," he said. "We try to stop the kids from throwing the toys or playing with them roughly. But we also want to see what it is about the toy that brings out that kind of reaction."
Toys occasionally break, but Mr. Spitzer said no children had suffered any harm in the testing. "About the worst that happens is that some of them who want to keep the toy they're testing will cry when they have to give it up," he said.
So, at the end of each testing session, Ms. Pace, who supervises the children, passes out their free toys as rapidly as possible.
Toys that do not test well are usually sent back to their designers for more work. "Sometimes it's something so obvious that the inventors don't catch it until the the kids show them," Mr. Spitzer said. He mentioned the prototype of a children's video camera that Tyco is selling this Christmas, which was redesigned when the controls were found to be too large for young fingers.
Consulting her notes from the observation room, Ms. Trepiccione said she was pleased that the children "really liked the concept."
"But some had a hard time with the moving parts," she said, anticipating more sessions at the drawing board before the dolls would be tested again.
Toys that do well in Tyco's pre-search sessions undergo additional evaluations that determine how expensive the toy is to manufacture. Tyco then uses regional adult focus groups to determine the toy's appeal to retailers and parents. The process, from initial conception of a toy to the finished, packaged product, tends to take at least two years, although a toy that pre-searches well can be in the stores in as little as six months.
Which was too long for 5-year-old Gwyneth Darrow of Mullica Hill. After giving the dolls a workout in the playroom, Gwyneth told her mother to take her to a toy store so she could buy one. Her mother, Marion Hume-Darrow, encouraged her daughter to be patient.
Parents may register their children as toy testers by writing to Kidtesting, Tyco Toys Inc., 6000 Midlantic Drive, Mount Laurel, N.J. 08054. Children are selected for testing sessions on the basis of age and sex. Sessions are held weekdays at 3:30 P.M.

GRAPHIC: Photo: The most rigorous kind of consumer test: a session in the playroom at the headquarters of Tyco Toys in Mount Laurel. (Allen Oliver for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: NEW JERSEY & CO.;
Paying the Piper, and the Bills, for Salem Nuclear Reactors

BYLINE: By KIT R. ROANE 

 BODY:
They were planned before nuclear became a bad word: twin reactors expected to supply cheap, clean energy to New Jersey. It was the late 1970's, before Chernobyl brought fear, and energy deregulation made them less attractive.
But the Salem reactors, managed and predominantly owned by Public Service Electric & Gas Company, are on the ropes. Dogged by structural problems and mismanagement, they have not lived up to their promise of cheap, safe power. 
 This year the two units ranked among 2 of the 10 worst reactors in the country, according to Public Citizen, an environmental watchdog group. Salem 1 ranked first on the list. The reactors have drawn more than a dozen fines from the Nuclear Regulatory Commission in the last 15 years, and neither has been operating since spring 1995 because of safety concerns.
Their problems at Salem come to a head as the New Jersey Board of Public Utilities is reviewing options for deregulation of the state's energy market within the next five to seven years. Currently, New Jersey depends on nuclear power for 40 percent of its needs, but the history and fate of the Salem site may say a good deal about where New Jersey will head in the next two decades, in an uncertain future in which utilities are expected to compete with one another for both customers and power.
Salem's latest shutdown, one of 22 at the site, resulted in P.S.E.&G.'s offer on Wednesday of credits to rate-payers taling $83.9 million. These rebates, which will be disbursed in early 1997 if approved by the state Board of Public Utilities, roughly equal the amount paid by consumers that exceeded replacement energy costs. The company also promised not to seek reimbursement of another $12 million it spent on replacement energy.
But more important, said Michael Ambrosio, executive director of the Board of Public Utilities, the settlement departs from the past practice of allowing utilities to charge ratepayers even for plants that were not operating. Under the settlement, if the plants are not up and running within two years, the utility will have to absorb the cost of keeping them idle.
"We have shifted the risk of operating the plant to the utility," he said. "It changes the way we regulate the nuclear industry and is more reflective of what a competitive market would be."
Consumer groups and environmentalists say that the New Jersey Board of Public Utilities has ducked the more important issue of whether the plants should even be allowed to operate. They note that Salem 1 is about to undergo a $200 million overhaul to replace faulty steam generators. Salem 2, which is newer, has also had generator problems.
"We want them closed because they are not only dangerous but economically useless," said Staci Berger, the utilities organizer for New Jersey Citizen Action, the state's largest consumer group. "These plants have worked only about half the time they have been up, and have a dismal safety record. And once deregulation comes, we're worried they will let safety go right out the window in an attempt to compete."
The Board of Public Utilities says the Nuclear Regulatory Commission would continue to monitor safety issues even under deregulation. Critics don't find this argument convincing, noting that the commission has found it necessary to fine P.S.E.&G. $1.5 million since Salem has been operating, $680,000 of it for failure to fix already cited safety violations or for harassing inspectors who reported safety violations.
Despite the fines, a P.S.E.&G. spokesman, Neil Brown, said, "Public safety has never been compromised."
Mr. Brown said P.S.E.&G. itself was worried about how deregulation would affect safety. "The financial viability of the company operating these facilities needs to be maintained so that there is never an issue of safety being compromised for financial reasons," he said.
Noting that nuclear reactors were once encouraged by states as a clean source of fuel, many utility analysts say it is unlikely that the utilities that built them will be forced to swallow the cost of their inefficiencies or of shutting them down.
Another problem is that even if reactors cannot compete with the low-cost kilowattage of other energy sources, like natural gas, New Jersey has no other options that provide the amount of constant power the state requires. Energy can be conserved. "Dirty power" can be imported for coal-fired co-generation plants, and there is also gas. But none of this can be depended on to meet all of New Jersey's needs.
"There has to be some commitment to providing power to the region long term so you have to look beyond simple dollars and cents," said Barry Abramson, a utilities analyst with Prudential Securities Inc. "And weighing all the different factors, if these reactors begin to operate in the way most do, they can be efficient resources because of the indirect benefits like pollution abatement and the security of having a plant not dependent on the spot market for purchasing power."
This leads to the question of how deregulation will be defined. Mr. Ambrosio said that this is undecided, noting that there has yet to be a decision on whether utilities will be able to pass on to ratepayers the cost of shutting down a plant.
Deregulation may have its limits, he said, saying that there is some merit to the nuclear industry's argument that it has qualities deserving of protection.
"That is one argument for a continued role in the market for regional or state planning," he said. "We are looking at stability and fuel supply, whether we want to become vulnerable to price and supply swings. So far this is unresolved."
Analysts said Salem could become profitable. New management has been brought in to improve safety procedures and the company is working on replacing defective parts in the reactors. P.S.E.&G. says Salem 2 could meet N.R.C. regulations by the end of the year, while Salem 1 could be producing power by the end of 1997.
But the N.R.C. is still hedging.
"Salem reduced its margin of safety to what we would consider an uncomfortable point and because of that we have to come to an independent conclusion that they are safe before we let them restart," said Larry Nicholson, an N.R.C. official. "But they are making good progress."
One of the main concerns of environmentalists is that design, as much as management, is a problem, not only at Salem, but also at other reactors. Of the 110 nuclear power reactors operating in the United States, 73 are pressurized water reactors, at which superheated and pressurized radioactive water is channeled through thousands of tiny tubes in a steam generator, then brought to a boil. Once the water boils, steam transfers the heat into power, running turbogenerators.
The tubes at Salem 1 and 2, as in many other reactors, have been found to fracture. In Salem 2, fractured tubes have been plugged, but at Salem 1 the problem is so extensive that whole generators are being brought in as replacements. The Salem 1 cost is estimated at more than $200 million, raising the cost of any energy that is produced once the reactor is restarted.
P.S.E.&G. is suing Westinghouse, the maker of more half of the country's nuclear generators, including those at Salem.
"Our concern is that steam generator replacement is an expensive piecemeal approach to save an uneconomic power system," said Paul Gunter, spokesman for Nuclear Information and Resource Services, an environmental group in Washington. "These plants are aging much more quickly than the industry ever predicted. This is not where we want to be heading."

HEADLINE: MINDING YOUR BUSINESS;
Going Bankrupt: The Scarlet 'B' or the Great Escape?

BYLINE: By BRUCE FELTON 

 BODY:

Q. I've built up a heavy credit card debt load and can't seem to meet even the minimum monthly payments. Is declaring bankruptcy a practical solution?
A. It could well be, although a generation ago you probably wouldn't have dared. As a teen-ager, whenever I tried to cadge some extra cash from my father on the weekend, he would reluctantly hand me a few bills with a half-joking warning: "One of these days you'll bankrupt me." 
 Going bankrupt, I was to understand, was synonymous with going to the poorhouse or to hell in a handbasket. No fate could be more unspeakable.
No more. This year, a record 1.1 million individuals and businesses, 20 percent more than in 1995, are expected to shuck at least part of their debts by filing petitions under the Federal Bankruptcy Code. The trend can be explained by many factors, including the lure of legalized gambling and the easy availability of credit cards.
For many people, though, declaring personal bankruptcy is an effective way to escape situations they cannot control.
"I see many cases where a family is just getting by as long as both spouses work," said M. Daniel Cantor, a bankruptcy lawyer in Lawrenceville, N.J. "Their finances are stretched to the max, and then an illness or a job loss pushes them over the edge. Suddenly, they can't meet monthly expenses or even minimum credit card payments."
In such situations, a Chapter 7 bankruptcy is the most popular approach because it allows you to wipe out your debts and start fresh. It discharges bank and charge-card balances, medical and utility bills and lawsuit damages, and it bars creditors from further efforts to collect. About 750,000 Chapter 7 filings are expected this year.
"If you were to adopt a new austerity budget -- no travel, no new clothes -- and still couldn't pay off your nonmortgage debt in three to five years, bankruptcy could make sense," said Andrew Feinberg, the author of "Downsize Your Debt: How to Take Control of Your Personal Finances" (Penguin Books, 1993, $10.95).
However, bankruptcy comes at a price. For example, not all debts are dischargeable. You will still have to pay your most recent back taxes, student loans, alimony, child support, criminal fines and certain other legal judgments against you. You could also be relieved of some of your own financial and material assets, which will be liquidated and distributed to your creditors. These assets include bank accounts, stocks and bonds, artwork, jewelry of more than a certain value and other luxury items. But in reality, Mr. Cantor said, "Many people wind up having to give up surprisingly little."
If you own a home, you stand a chance of losing it, because you may be permitted to keep only part of your equity. The amount varies by state. In New York, the courts will seize any real estate you own, sell it and give the lion's share of the proceeds to your creditors, allowing you to keep just $10,000. In Florida and Texas, your home is completely exempt.
But bankruptcy won't reduce you to dressing in newspapers. You are usually permitted to keep one car, household furnishings, clothing, retirement accounts and personal-injury awards.
A bankruptcy notice remains on your credit report for up to 10 years. During that time, it will be difficult to obtain an auto loan or a mortgage, and some credit card companies will cancel your account. (Others, secure in the knowledge that you are legally ineligible to file for bankruptcy again for six years, will be only too happy to issue you a new card, but with a sky-high interest rate.)
"Tread carefully, because you can't undo a bankruptcy," warned Charles Benning-
hoff 3d, a lawyer in San Juan Capistrano, Calif. "I've had clients who filed only to discover that they couldn't get financing for a new business idea that might have made them wealthy."
The toughest part of a bankruptcy may be the sense of shame and failure it often brings. "Most people agonize over the decision to file and are deeply embarrassed," said Ronald Weiss, a partner at Berman, DeLeve, Kuchan & Chapman, a law firm in Kansas City, Mo.
But if you're troubled by the stigma of bankruptcy, you can take comfort that celebrities like Kim Basinger and Mickey Rooney have gone the same route. Even Thomas Jefferson filed for bankruptcy.
The Chapter 7 filing process is not as onerous as you might expect. You should first consult a lawyer who specializes in bankruptcies but has other areas of expertise as well. The idea is to work with someone who knows the territory, but doesn't view bankruptcy as a cure-all.
"It's important for the lawyer to play devil's advocate, just to make sure the client understands all the choices," said Lee Madere, an economist and lawyer in New Orleans. Sometimes, less drastic alternatives to Chapter 7 may be suitable, like negotiating a payment plan with your creditors or filing a Chapter 13 bankruptcy, which lets you keep some of your assets while you partly repay your debt over three to five years.
After you've decided to move ahead, you must provide details on all your creditors, debts, assets, income and expenditures. Make sure the list is complete, because debts you omit will not be discharged. Deliberately hiding assets can land you in jail.
As soon as your lawyer files your petition with the United States Bankruptcy Court, your creditors will be notified and ordered to stop dunning you. A few weeks later, you will be required to attend a meeting with a Federal trustee and any creditors who wish to be heard. These hearings usually take less than 10 minutes. You will be asked some questions and, assuming nothing irregular is uncovered, you will receive within three months a discharge of your debts. Legal and filing fees often come to less than $1,000.
Even if you are filing for bankruptcy, it is advisable to settle some of your debts, like those with doctors and local merchants. "If your child has just been fitted for braces, you'll want to pay your orthodontist's bill in full, even though you are legally off the hook," Mr. Weiss said. "Otherwise, you may find yourself in a tough spot when it's time for the braces to come off."
 
Questions about investing, careers and personal finance may be sent to Minding Your Business, Money and Business, The New York Times, 229 West 43d Street, New York 10036, or by electronic mail to yourmind@nytimes.com. Questions will be answered only in the column, not by mail, E-mail or telephone.
