HEADLINE: MUTUAL FUNDS;
Generation X Is Getting a Financial Life

BYLINE: By BARBARA WHITAKER 

 BODY:
GENERATION X'ERS are:
A. Slackers.
B. Savers.
C. More inclined to believe in U.F.O.'s than in the future of Social Security.
D. All of the above. 
 Those who selected "D" may be closest to the truth. The members of Generation X -- the roughly 50 million Americans between the ages of 18 and 32 -- are many things to many people. But when it comes to investing, they have been largely ignored. Only recently have financial service companies and mutual funds begun reaching out to this group with educational materials or enhancements like reduced sales fees.
"The people who are running mutual fund companies assume this generation is full of people who are not paying attention to their money and are sort of frivolous slackers who don't really care about investing," said Beth Kobliner, who wrote "Get A Financial Life: Personal Finance in Your 20's and 30's" (Fireside/Simon & Schuster, 1996). "What I found is this generation is extraordinarily interested in the topic of personal finance in general and investing in particular."
To be sure, it is a generation with some financial characteristics that make it less attractive, as a target market, than older Americans. Studies have found that, as a group, members of the generation are making less money, adjusted for inflation, than their parents did at their age, and that they tend to carry a lot of debt, largely as a result of student loans or credit cards.
Still, Ms. Kobliner and others familiar with the market said that the financial service industry had been short-sighted in not focusing more on this group.
"It's probably not as much financial service companies saying Gen X'ers don't have an interest," said Alexandra Lebenthal, the 32-year-old president of Lebenthal & Company, the municipal bond brokerage firm. "Being more realistic, they probably are saying they don't have enough money right now."
That is the case for Stephen Herschkowitz, a 23-year-old senior at the University of Washington who is set to graduate in weeks.
"It would be really nice if I had the disposable income to put into a savings account or mutual fund," said Mr. Herschkowitz, who, in addition to working on the student newspaper, holds down a job as a grocery checker for $9 an hour. "Unfortunately, I have so many credit card bills."
Though Mr. Herschkowitz paid off about $2,000 he had placed on credit cards for a trip to Europe, he then used them to buy a new computer. He now has four credit cards, with balances ranging from $600 to $2,500.
"Instead of investing money for my future, I'm in equipment for a more solid future," he said, noting that the computer would be important as he pursued a career in journalism.
One factor affecting Mr. Herschkowitz's strategy is his belief that he does not have a big enough pool of money to invest. Given the option of putting away smaller amounts on a regular basis, he said, he would take advantage of it.
Companies trying to reach Generation X are beginning to emphasize that possibility.
American Express Institutional Services, for example, used the phrase "What a Can of Pop Can Do for Your Future" in developing a marketing campaign for the 401(k) plan at Shopko Stores, a discount department store chain based in Green Bay, Wis. The dimes a day that pay for a soft drink, the campaign suggests, can be the start of a retirement program built on mutual fund investments.
American Express also developed a sprightly marketing program for Toys "R" Us employees that emphasizes the tax benefits of the company's 401(k) program. The promotion plays off the Presidential election. A poster proclaims, "When it comes to reducing your taxes this year, you've got three parties to choose from," and shows an elephant, a donkey and a giraffe. The giraffe represents the Toys "R" Us mascot, Geoffrey Giraffe, and his message is to put money into the company's plan.
John Palombo, a senior vice president of employee education with American Express Institutional Services, said that two years ago, younger employees were generally not participating in savings plans.
"We kind of wrote them off generally because we just assumed they fell into that broader, under-30 category that believes they are going to live forever and will do it later," Mr. Palombo said.
But that perception changed as the American Express unit worked with companies that had high concentrations of younger employees or that needed to increase employee participation in their 401(k) plans to meet Government requirements. Though the younger employees were initially more focused on jump-starting a career than on investing, that attitude seemed to change quickly as the employees got their feet on the ground.
"They began to say, 'I saw some of the struggles my parents or friends of the family were going through and I'm going to start dealing with this now,' " Mr. Palombo said.
Besides focusing on the workplace, some mutual fund companies are reducing sales charges or waiving minimum investment amounts on certain funds.
AT Lebenthal & Company, customers age 35 and under can get a break of half a percentage point on sales fees for the company's three bond funds. While bonds remain a hard sell to a younger market, Ms. Lebenthal said, the reduced fees have attracted some younger investors.
Ms. Kobliner said mutual funds were offering attractive programs that allowed people to invest as little as $25 in a fund, if additional investments were made on a regular basis. The minimum initial investments had generally been $1,000 and up.
T. Rowe Price has what it calls the Automatic Asset Builder program, which waives the minimum investment in virtually any fund but requires a minimum contribution of $50 a month. Steven Norwitz, a vice president with the company, said that the program had not been planned with Generation X in mind but that it had attracted that group because of its appeal to investors who were just starting out.
But the potential impact of Generation X on the investment world may be gaining appreciation. Recent studies show members of this age group are investing for their future in greater numbers than expected.
According to a 1995 survey by Roper Starch Worldwide of nearly 2,100 middle-income and affluent Americans, 61 percent of 20-somethings said they or their spouses had put aside money for retirement, with three out of four saying that retirement was a priority for their savings. Indeed, the survey found that 58 percent were participating in 401(k) plans, compared with 52 percent of baby boomers, aged 30 to 49.
The Investment Company Institute, a trade association for mutual funds, provided additional evidence of the trend earlier this year. A survey of 1,200 fund holders found that those aged 18 to 30 were investing 38 percent of their assets in mutual funds, a greater percentage than for baby boomers, who were investing 27 percent in mutual funds.
"The results clearly identified that there had been some myths about the generations as far as savings and investments were concerned," said Sandra J. West, director of marketing and policy research at the institute.
Not all financial decisions made by young people are necessarily sound. Early this year, a Louis Harris poll of 412 men and women aged 20 to 39 found that while 62 percent had bought lottery tickets in the last year, only 30 percent had invested money in stocks, bonds or mutual funds.
Still, the fact that many young people have little faith that the Government will provide for them in the future may bode well for investment companies.
"One reason they might be more prone to save is they don't think the Government programs that are designed to help them in their retirement will be there," said Richard Thau, executive director of Third Millennium, a nonprofit group focusing on long-term economic problems facing people in their 20's and 30's.
In a 1994 survey of 500 people aged 18 to 34, Third Millennium found that while 46 percent thought U.F.O.'s existed, only 28 percent believed that Social Security would be there when they retired.
"You're talking about a generation that would like to redirect their money into private accounts," he said.

HEADLINE: SPENDING IT;
The Bonus Isn't Reserved for Big Shots Anymore

BYLINE: By BARBARA B. BUCHHOLZ 

 BODY:
A SMALL shoe store rewards workers each week for exceeding the previous week's sales. A big company cuts a special check for employees in January, using a complex formula based on last year's operating profits. No matter how simple or sophisticated, each plan is a form of bonus pay, a fact of life for more and more Americans.
Next year, more than a third of hourly workers will get some bonus pay, which was once reserved mostly for a company's top brass and sales staff. 
 Bonuses may be disguised in corporate jargon as incentive or variable pay, but the concept is an old one: paying employees for performance. Instead of singling out an individual's work, the performance of a division or an entire company is rewarded.
According to an annual survey by Buck Consultants, an employee benefits and compensation consulting firm, the portion of Fortune 1,000 companies that offer bonus pay plans for hourly employees has risen from 26 percent in 1993 to 34 percent. Thirty-seven percent of the companies surveyed said they would offer some type of incentive pay next year.
At many companies, bonuses are in addition to annual merit raises, which are based on an individual's work, not the group's. But experts say incentive plans could eventually replace merit-based pay, just as declining inflation in the 1980's led some employers to eliminate cost-of-living increases.
Why the change? With merit raises, a percentage of salary becomes a permanent part of paychecks, is factored into pensions and is announced at the end of the year. Bonuses are often distributed in the first quarter, usually in a lump sum, if the company or work group met or exceeded expectations in the previous year, said Paul Gavejian, a principal with Buck.
For hourly workers who received bonuses this year, the amounts were typically 3 to 8 percent of base salary. Mr. Gavejian said. Middle managers received 10 to 17 percent, and executives often 100 percent or more.
Jeff Maiken embraced the bonus idea when he became president and general manager of the Zaca Mesa Winery, a small operation in Los Olivos, Calif., 50 miles north of Santa Barbara. He wanted a way to bolster productivity while keeping a lid on fixed costs. In the wine and spirits industry, sales and profits can fluctuate widely based on unpredictable factors like weather and the growing season. Productivity is something he can try to control.
His solution was to pay all employees a low base salary, plus a bonus once a year if the company reached its goal for operating income by year-end. "Bonuses have run from last year's zero, when the company ran out of certain varietals and performance was below expectations. to an anticipated 20 percent this year due to increased case sales and margins," Mr. Maiken said.
Brian H. McPherson, 27, a clerk in the company's accounting department, considers incentive pay "generally good" even though he didn't receive a bonus last year. The year before, he got $400, but this year he expects a few thousand dollars.
"It gives you the motivation to work harder," he said. "It makes you better aware of operations and gets you away from the tunnel vision of your own department." In some years, "you have good luck as well as bad luck," he added. "This year we expect to do very well."
There are other reasons for bonus pay. Merit pay is often pegged to inflation. Since the inflation rate is about 3 percent, Alan Johnson, managing director of Johnson Associates, compensation consultants, said companies had found it hard to motivate workers with such tiny pay raises. That's especially true as employees perform more tasks because of layoffs. Merit increases have been steadily inching down, from 4.2 percent in 1993 to a projected 4 percent next year, Buck Consultants said.
While it is difficult to know how many employers use merit-based pay, fewer are doing so, particularly in the financial services sector, Mr. Gavejian said.
Bonuses are also thought to be better for promoting teamwork. "The thinking is that when the company does well, the employees do well, and all save money and should be rewarded," said Susan B. Rowland, a principal at Towers Perrin, benefits consultants.
Bonuses can be distributed in a slew of variations to meet specific company needs, said Ira Kay of Watson Wyatt Worldwide, compensation and benefits consultants. Among the most popular are annual incentive awards paid in a lump sum, and broad-banding, which reduces the number of salary grades at a company but increases the amount of money available in each grade.
And while bonuses can be given to individuals or to an entire staff, Todd R. Zenger, associate professor at the Olin School of Business of Washington University in St. Louis, said the trend was to reward small groups so people felt they had contributed.
Edith James, president of Comfort Shoe Specialists, which manufactures and sellscustom shoes, support hose and accessories, pays bonuses to her six employees, but does so weekly instead of annually. They receive a $50 bonus if weekly sales exceed those of the previous week. The goal was challenging, but spurred the company to achieve annual sales of $1 million in four years.
"I'm a firm believer that you only get quality people if you pay," said Ms. James, whose business is in a strip shopping center outside St. Louis. "We all pitch in."
But bonuses can backfire. Companies can inadvertently encourage the wrong behavior in employees by tying compensation to increased sales, said N. Fredric Crandall, a partner at the Center for Workforce Effectiveness, a management consulting firm.
Sears, Roebuck & Company used to reward auto-shop employees according to the size of the repair bills. The upshot? Overbilled customers and lawsuits.
Nevertheless, many experts on compensation say the advantages outweigh the disadvantages. Since the change at Zaca Mesa, production has jumped 140 percent to an estimated 42,000 cases of wine, an increase Mr. Maiken attributes in part to bonuses and more motivated employees. While gross profits have tripled in the same period, expenses have risen just 35 percent.
Mr. McPherson, the clerk, finds the incentive more a plus than a minus. "You can't count on it, but that's business," he said. "Stockholders don't always get dividends, either."

GRAPHIC: Graph: "A Little Extra for Their Employees" shows the number of Fortune 1000 companies offering bonus plans for hourly workers, 1993 to 1997. (Source: Buck Consultants)

HEADLINE: NEIGHBORHOOD REPORT: NEW YORK UP CLOSE;
Gay Leaders Look to the Ballot Box

BYLINE: By ANDREW JACOBS 

 BODY:
Through decades of fighting discrimination, anti-gay violence and, most recently, AIDS, gay and lesbian New Yorkers learned that often the most effective way to shake up the status quo was through street protests and similar activism. But not until recent years did gay leaders learn to use the ballot box as a vehicle for change.
Now, in an attempt to build a cohesive gay electorate to rival the city's coveted ethnic voting blocs, the Lesbian and Gay Community Services Center on West 13th Street has begun a campaign, Promote the Vote, to identify and mobilize homosexual voters. 
 The center has hired a full-time organizer and hopes to build a gay bloc that could comprise as much as 16 percent of the city's electorate. "Gays and lesbians are following in the footsteps of blacks, Hispanics and even women, trying to assert their interests at the polls," said Phil Friedman, a Democratic political consultant.
So far, Promote the Vote has registered 4,000 voters and distributed thousands of pledge cards urging gay voters to turn out for every election, from local school board contests to Presidential primaries. "Just as the Christian right asks its supporters to sign pledge cards saying they will not vote for candidates who promote homosexuality," said Carlie Steen, the campaign's coordinator, "we're asking people to support candidates who keep our interests in mind." Among those interests are financing for AIDS research and support for public school curriculums that promote respect for gays.
Although gay voters can have a significant impact on national races, it is in local elections where a mobilized bloc can make a measurable difference. In last spring's school board races, for example, five of the six openly gay candidates on the ballot won, thanks in large part to a heavy turnout among homosexual voters.
But unlike other minority groups, many of whose members vote Democratic, gay voters are not monolithically Democratic, experts say. Robert Bailey, a Rutgers University professor, says that as the gay rights movement achieves more goals, the unity of the gay vote may weaken. "So many things divide our community, from economics to race and religion," he said. "As the younger generation comes of age, gay-related issues might become less important than concerns over crime and high taxes. Ironically, the so-called gay voting bloc may become a victim of our movement's success."
 ANDREW JACOBS

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Shooting Manhattan

BYLINE: By CONSTANCE L. HAYS 

 BODY:
JOHN WALSH came to New York University's film school as a bright-eyed freshman from Tarrytown, N.Y. Before long he had befriended Sally Roy, a classmate from Hartford who was originally a drama student but switched to film after realizing that a stage career meant she'd have to work nights.
Just two players in the strange puree of ego, ambition, insecurity and competitiveness that is the film school, they collaborated on their undergraduate work, making the black-and-white shorts that are meant to be steppingstones to real-life movie careers. Sally would edit John's films; John would act as cameraman for Sally's. They graduated in 1984 and went their separate ways. Ten years later, they made a movie together that opened in theaters last Friday. 
 The movie, "Ed's Next Move," is the gently told tale of how a young scientist from Wisconsin learns to make his way in New York, from finding an apartment to securing a first date -- a kind of "Local Hero" meets E. B. White's essay "Here Is New York." John wrote and directed it; Sally produced it.
That's just the outline of the story of how this movie -- one of perhaps hundreds of independent films being made in the city at any given time -- came to be. Over the last half-dozen years, New York has become the capital of the independent film, generally defined as a low-budget feature with no financial backing from a major studio. "Probably two-thirds of our feature films are independent," says Patricia Reed Scott, Commissioner of the Mayor's Office of Film, Theatre and Broadcasting, which issues permits to movie crews.
They have become the self-defining medium of choice. "It's like writing your first novel was, 10 years ago," says Valerie Sheppard, market director of the Independent Feature Film Market, a film festival held every September at the Angelika Theater on Houston Street. "Now it's making your first film."
People point to certain forces creating the boom, like the increasing numbers of film students in New York -- from N.Y.U. to Columbia to the School of Visual Arts -- and an increase in production-related companies here that can do everything for an independent film, from renting a camera crew to dubbing the soundtrack for a foreign release. First-time film makers can find themselves working down the hall in post-production with Woody Allen or Martin Scorsese, or taking over a set abandoned in a sound stage by someone equally well known.
Reinforcing this are the film festivals: at prestigious ones like Sundance, many of the films that set people buzzing -- about Chinese restaurants, Brooklyn teen-agers, youthful lesbians -- are made here, by people the average moviegoer has never heard of, on budgets the size of the proverbial shoestring.
Out of this recent tradition comes "Ed's Next Move," which was finished despite the many headaches that seem to accompany most independent efforts. With hardly any money to film with (a grand total of about $93,000), and most of it leveraged with a stack of credit cards, Mr. Walsh and Ms. Roy had to wrest agreements out of people to do things for less, if not for free. A few key things fell gracefully into their hands; several others collapsed before their eyes. Certain New Yorkers surprised them with helpfulness. And they got towed a lot.
Along the way, a recurring theme developed -- something about making up for a shortage of cash with an abundance of goodwill. And about asking people, often for a lot, at this panhandling extreme of a glitzy industry.
"A lot of the production assistants learned a good lesson on this movie," Ms. Roy says. "And that was, if you don't have a lot of money, you have to be kind."
Now, with Orion Classics picking up the American rights and a German company paying to show the film in Germany, they have broken even -- meaning their income has now met up with their filming and production costs, if not the $990,000 in deferred salaries to cast and crew and some other costs, Mr. Walsh says. The future -- profit margins, financing for a second film he's writing, even their reputation in the gossipy movie world -- is a little less clear.
"The big question is, when they do their second film, how are they?" says Bill Nisselson, studio manager at Sound One, a sound-editing company, who has seen plenty of promising film makers transmogrified by money and power. "Everybody doesn't necessarily stay the same way."
 
Going It Alone. Scraping Up the Money
The movie was originally titled "More Bad Times," and Mr. Walsh wrote it in 1991 when he was collecting unemployment. (He had worked on a couple of major movies, "Married to the Mob" and "Dogfight," in off-screen supporting roles.) Over the next 18 months, he rewrote it six times. He shopped it to a couple of studios, where executives said things like, "We need a famous actor," to the point that Mr. Walsh decided to just make it himself.
Then he started talking to Ms. Roy about it. "Ultimately, John and I must have talked to each other every day for three years about this movie," Ms. Roy says.
She wasn't going to be the producer. Mr. Walsh had already talked her husband, Peter Nelson, into being his director of photography, and Ms. Roy had said she didn't think it was feasible for both of them to work without pay. At the time, she was the production manager for a PBS series with Bill Moyers, "Healing and the Mind."
But then it appeared that a woman Mr. Walsh knew, whose father ran a cable company, might underwrite the filming for as much as $500,000. He approached Ms. Roy again and said, "You wouldn't have to do it for free." She agreed to be the producer. Then the woman's father didn't like the script, and the plan collapsed. But Ms. Roy never did resign from the project.
They started gathering everything they would need to make the film, like money. They came up with $13,000 between them; $70,000 more came from Visa and Mastercard. (Much later, a crew member tossed in an additional $10,000.) Mr. Walsh had 12 cards bearing his name by the time the movie finished shooting.
"A lot of places take credit cards now," Mr. Walsh says, reflectively. "Sometimes there were vendors that let us use a credit card to pay our bills. I did have to take out a few huge cash advances. I'd go into a bank and say, 'I need $9,000 in cash,' then hand them my Capital One card."
Such financing is not unusual, but not recommended, said Dana Harris, managing editor of the Independent Film and Video Monthly. "But John's got that strange combination of logic and blind faith, which is an excellent combination for an independent film maker," she said.
At the same time, they were auditioning actors, lining up locations, learning to accept the word "No!" and figuring out how to move on. They cajoled people they knew into working on the movie gratis, using their network of film school friends and friends from subsequent projects. They enticed others with notices tacked to bulletin boards, promising experience in exchange for weeks of unpaid hard work.
One of the biggest traumas for Ms. Roy and Mr. Walsh was realizing they could not afford to work with the Screen Actors Guild, a source of good, experienced people. But at $75 a day, even the union's lowest rate would be too expensive for them.
Then along came people like Kevin Carroll, a 24-year-old New York University graduate who was then appearing as Belize in "Angels in America" on Broadway. Susan Shopmaker, the casting director, knew him and brought him to audition for the role of Ray, Ed's roommate. The part had not originally been written with a black actor in mind, Mr. Walsh said. But when Mr. Carroll tried out, it was a perfect match.
Locations posed another puzzle. Some of the things Mr. Walsh desperately wanted, like a quaint Russian coffee shop in the East Village for his several counter scenes, turned out to be too expensive. As in $4,000 or more. "So Sally decided to ask the Just-Pik't guy who the nice coffee shop owners were," Mr. Walsh recalled, referring to a fruit-juice distributor. "And he came up with Eisenberg's, at Fifth and 22d."
There were many more compromises. Penn Station said they couldn't film there. But Grand Central Terminal said they could. The Cloisters wanted $5,000 in insurance; they were replaced by Trinity Church, which wanted only about $700. St. Mark's Church on East 10th Street originally figured in the script, but the church said they could not film in front of its facade. The scene ended up taking place in the Miracle Garden on East Third Street. Ms. Roy and Mr. Nelson's building in the East Village appears a couple of times in the film.
People scoured their apartments for props, like suitcases, alarm clocks, wooden spoons. A few companies donated products in return for getting them on screen: Lee Jeans, Budweiser, the Sealed Air Corporation (with bubble wrap that appears as part of a contemporary sculpture). When Mr. Walsh wanted to shoot Ed's first New York City cab ride and show the meter clicking along at an extraordinary rate, Ms. Roywent to a taxi garage on 11th Avenue, near the sound stage, where the mechanics were perhaps too willing to help.
"I went in and said, 'We need to have a meter go really fast,' " she recalled. "And they said: 'No problem. We can make it do whatever you want.' "
Some things were eerily serendipitous. They found a band already named Ed's Redeeming Qualities to play themselves in the movie. When they went to the sound stage, Raw Space, on West 42d Street, they discovered an entire tenement set abandoned by the last film that had been in there. Film itself, one of the highest costs in any project this size, turned out to be available as "short ends," left over from the movie "Smoke." Each "end" was less than 10 minutes' worth of film, but there were 120,000 feet of them.
"They sold it to us for 20 cents on the dollar," Mr. Walsh says, his eyes lighting up at the memory. What was left over, he added, Ms. Roy resold to another movie. For more than 20 cents on the dollar.
Now they were ready to make the movie.
 
The Tension Mounts: Will People Quit?
In October 1994, they began their four-week shoot, an intense period of 16-hour days that took them from Morristown, N.J. (the closest reasonable substitute for small-town Wisconsin, whence Ed came) to the chilly waters of the Hudson (for its view of the railroad tracks) to the meat counter at Balducci's, where the camera lingers on a $109-a-pound slab of Kobe beef.
Tensions were high. They were up against money, and not long on time. Ms. Roy and Mr. Walsh lost a lot of sleep, principally over the question of whether 45 people working for nothing would stick with the project.
"It's not that it's O.K. not to get paid," says Cindy Kaplan, who plays a translator in an early scene, though she would have liked to play Lee, Ed's romantic interest. "It's also about how you're treated when you're not getting paid. It's sort of a leap of faith you make: I know this is a good project, I know these are good people, I know it's worth my time."
The producer, meanwhile, wasn't always so confident. "You always held your breath to see who was coming back," Ms. Roy said.
They spent two days at Eisenberg's Sandwich Shop, which is a classic-looking place that doesn't actually serve blintzes, as it does in the movie. The owner, Steve Oh, insists he has the best tuna salad and chocolate egg creams in town. He has trouble remembering the crew from "Ed's Next Move," even though they paid him $2,000 to use the shop. "They make a lot of movies here," Mr. Oh confessed."The only one I can remember clearly was 'Lover Man.' "
They seem to have made more of an impression at Balducci's, where they filmed without charge. "They were very nice," said Julio Collado, a butcher. "We did it in three takes. They asked for some chicken breasts, told me to give them to the actor. They told me to be very natural."
Halfway through, the production manager quit. Mr. Walsh said she had not got along with one of the other crew members. When she left, she took her live-in boyfriend with her, who happened to be the location manager -- another important person. That meant Sharon Fitzpatrick, who had answered a help-wanted ad on an N.Y.U. bulletin board to become the movie's location assistant, moved up.
There was at least one other person who might have exited, but for the money situation. "I wish I could have fired him," Ms. Roy said, refusing to identify him, "but I couldn't, because I couldn't find anyone else to work for free."
Takes were not shot over and over in search of perfection because of the limits on money and time.
"We were all sensitive to the fact that these were John's credit cards, so let's be as clean as we can be," Mr. Carroll said. "He didn't necessarily say it. It just got out."
The organization was memorable to at least one other actor. "They were on time for everything," says Lisa Harris, who has a small role. "When you work on an independent film and you're non-SAG and everything goes smoothly, it's a real treat."
Outside the set, the city went on as usual. People's cars were towed. One assistant's, in particular, Mr. Walsh said. "He was from a Slavic country, and he didn't quite know what a fire hydrant looked like," he said.
Another time, Ms. Roy remembers, a man cutting tiles with a screeching saw was disturbing filming on West 76th Street, which is no longer in the movie. An assistant walked across his tile project to ask him to turn off the machine. The man erupted.
"So I had to take four or five irate calls from that guy," she says.
And there was the day they decided to film on a basketball court on Horatio Street. A block away was the filming of "City Hall," with about 100 teamsters, Ms. Roy says. "One came up and started asking who's your producer, what's your budget," she said. "When he heard it was $85,000, he just said, 'Good luck to yez.' "
The crew got a daily hot lunch and plenty of coffee and doughnuts during this time. Mr. Walsh later called it the best time he ever had. Ms. Roy said she lost 15 pounds attending to various urgent issues.
"They came to me with everything, like there's no ice in the cooler, or the location is throwing us out," she said. "John Walsh lived in a bell jar. Our job was to keep all the problems away from him so he could get this movie shot."
Once that was done, the movie moved into a complicated and costly process known as post-production, in which everything from film editing to sound effects takes place in hushed, darkened studios that charge by the hour. A further $115,000 was spent on this, getting the film in shape to submit to festivals. Before it went to theaters, it would require an additional $150,000 in polishing, including an original soundtrack by Benny Golson, a legendary jazzman. Mr. Walsh and Ms. Roy raised $95,000 more from investors, people they knew who had seen the movie and liked it, and kicked in $10,000 or so. They also worked on other jobs, putting their money back into the film.
As they were getting ready to submit their creation to the Sundance Film Festival, the two film makers decided "More Bad Times" was not a title that would draw audiences like a magnet. So they sat around trying to think of something better.
"That was the hardest thing John and I had to do," Ms. Roy said. Their candidates ranged from "Ed's Redeeming Qualities" (the band said no) to "Ed's New Life" to, in a moment of panic, "Cheese Boy in the Big City." Finally came "Ed's Next Move."
 
Ed's Epilogue: What the Future Holds
The film was screened at the Independent Feature Project's film market at the Angelika last fall. One of the Sundance organizers saw the last 20 minutes, Mr. Walsh said, and invited them to show it there.
There Orion Classics bought the rights for American distribution, and now Mr. Walsh's company, Ed's Films Inc., is selling the foreign rights, one country at a time. He's also working on another script.
"Ed's Next Move" is playing in one or two theaters in the city, after mixed reviews. Its creators, Mr. Walsh said, owe about $990,000 in deferred wages to their crew and $35,000 in credit card interest, which is to be paid from income from ticket sales, new foreign rights, video sales and other sources.
Then, of course, there are other movies to make, with or without a big budget.
"Ultimately, if people think you care about them and care about what you're doing, they will work very hard for you," Ms. Roy said. "It's worked out pretty well."

 Filming in Manhattan involves constant negotiating and willingess to settle for second best.
 
THE PICKUP SCENE
 THEY WANTED -- The Cloisters, for a scene in which Eddie tries to meet interesting museum-going girls and instead tumbles down a flight of stairs.
 THEY GOT -- Trinity Church, after the Cloisters said they would need $5,000 in additional insurance. Trinity cost only $750.
 
THE COFFEE SHOP
 THEY WANTED -- A vintage blintz-serving East Village coffee shop, like the Kiev or the Odessa, but they all wanted to be paid $4,000.
 THEY GOT -- Eisenberg's Sandwich Shop, a Korean-owned establishment at 22d and Fifth, which cost $2,000 for two days.
 
THE HEARTFELT TALK
 THEY WANTED -- St. Mark's Church, on Second Avenue at Tenth Street, for the movie's penultimate scene featuring Ed and his girlfriend having a heartfelt talk.
 THEY GOT -- The Miracle Garden, on Third Street between Avenues B and C, for $200 after St. Mark's said forgetaboutit.
 
THE LAB SCENE
 THEY WANTED -- The genetics lab at Columbia University, which is full of interesting technology.
 THEY GOT -- The New York City Quality Assurance Lab, now closed, which charged $1,000, after Columbia refused to give them access.
 
THE SHOPPING SCENE
 THEY WANTED -- Balducci's, for a scene for provisions with which to impress his date.
 THEY GOT -- Balducci's, at no charge. (pg. 1)

HEADLINE: NEIGHBORHOOD REPORT: DOWNTOWN BROOKLYN -- AT A GLANCE;
Dowtown Brooklyn: The Promise and the Peril

BYLINE: By SOMINI SENGUPTA 

 BODY:
Since the construction of the Pierrepont Plaza office tower near the foot of the Brooklyn Bridge in 1989, Downtown Brooklyn has witnessed an infusion of millions of dollars in public funds. Its goal: to lure back large-scale private investment. In 1990, the 16-acre giant known as Metrotech opened its doors. Now, aided by more than $50 million in city subsidies, construction is under way on the Renaissance Plaza hotel and office complex on Adams Street. A major face lifting has been proposed for the Federal courthouse nearby. On Nov. 7, the Atlantic Center mall is to open. Next door, the Brooklyn Academy of Music plans to unveil a bookstore and cafe next year, followed by the construction of a cinema. 
 The impact of this commercial development is subject to debate. Some economists, planners and Brooklyn-watchers applaud the emergence of new stores, office buildings and a hotel as a source of potential jobs and tax revenues. But others worry that the upgrading of Downtown Brooklyn will sap jobs and income from neighboring communities, and cause problems for nearby small-business owners. SOMINI SENGUPTA

GRAPHIC: Chart/Photos:
 
PROFESSOR RON SHIFFMAN,
Former City Planning Commissioner and director of the Pratt Center for Community and Environmental Development.
One issue is job creation. The other issue that's critically important is the environmental impact: whether we are beginning to destroy the urban quality of Downtown Brooklyn, whether we are turning it from a pedestrian-oriented area into what is really a shopping center, an automobile-oriented facility. Atlantic Center is really the beginning of that.
 
S. ERIC BLACKWELL,
Executive director, Fort Greene Community Action Network.
I don't think the consumers that live in Fort Greene would go as easily to Atlantic Center with a Myrtle Avenue working. They're going to go to Atlantic Center. Atlantic Center offers middle-end shopping. Fulton Mall offers a low-end shopping alternative.
I think Atlantic Center is a good thing. When you look at other models in terms of stimulating urban economic growth and job development, you can't point to much that's better than retail.
 
ROBERT FITCH,
Author, "The Assassination of New York" (Verso, 1993) and professor of metropolitan studies at New York University.
What you have in Brooklyn is a dying city. I think we have to recognize the magnitude of the disaster, which is a plan to create a Brooklyn center with office buildings, luxury residences and government structures that would serve as the engine of growth. And we're going to get rid of poor people and factories in order to do that. They've achieved the latter but not the former. It has failed spectacularly in Brooklyn.
 
DEBORAH C. WRIGHT,
Executive director, Upper Manhattan Empowerment Zone and former Commissioner of the City Department of Housing Preservation and Development.
Why shouldn't Brooklyn have quality amenities that Manhattan has? There isn't one quality hotel in Brooklyn. My father is a minister and he has ministers fly in from all over the country to speak at his church. Why is it that he has to put them up at the Hilton in Manhattan? The explicit premise of the empowerment zone is that you are trying to be a catalyst for the rebirth of both the geographic area as well as the people.
 
RODERICK B. MITCHELL,
President, Bedford-Stuyvesant Restoration Corporation, a nonprofit group that owns a majority stake in a Pathmark supermarket on Fulton Street.
You can never knock development from the standpoint of building up the borough. The question is, exactly how great of a multiplier effect do these developments have in terms of creating jobs and meaningful jobs for people who live in the surrounding neighborhoods and to what extent will small businesses be able to become suppliers to these major developments? Based upon what's happened so far, the jury is still out. My guess is that it's been very minimal.
 
HARVEY LICHTENSTEIN,
President, Brooklyn Academy of Music.
Someone once said to me: 'I want to see Martha Graham, who you're doing in Brooklyn. But she's performing in London next fall and that's easier.' What we've done to a large extent is build our artists' base and build our audience despite the difficulties in the neighborhood. Now things are turning around and this will be of enormous help to us. I think the area is important.

HEADLINE: Gauging the Rubin Effect

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Re "An Old Wall Street Pro's Voice in the Campaign" (Sept. 22): 
 Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin gives true meaning to the adage that the harder you work the luckier you get. I met Mr. Rubin almost 20 years ago when he was at Goldman, Sachs and I was a junior member of the risk arbitrage desk at a rival firm, Salomon Brothers. I always admired his style and his understatement. He is the perfect balance in the Clinton Administration because of his insights gained on the firing lines of Wall Street trading desks.
Mr. Rubin might be thinking "I told you so" when it comes to the deficit-reduction plan and its benefit for the bond market, or when it comes to the successful brinkmanship with the Republican Congress about the full faith and credit of Treasury debt, but you won't hear him say it.
It galls me that the Republican business community constantly complains about President Clinton's economic policies when it is readily apparent that the markets are at record highs, inflation is in check, jobs have been created and the dollar is steady.
No one disputes the desire for tax cuts, but realism dictates that every cut must be paid for. Thank you, Mr. Rubin, for making Democratic policy credible to business. On inauguration day 1997, you should gloat, but knowing you, you'll be reading memos privately. I guess that's how you stay luckier than most.
 
STEVEN A. LUDSIN
Sagaponack, L.I., Sept. 22
 
The writer is a lawyer and investment adviser.

HEADLINE: Gauging the Rubin Effect

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Your article on Mr. Rubin noted the Clinton Administration proposal that gains on sales of principal residences be excused from taxation. But restricting this type of concession to residences reflects a populist approach in an election year. 
 All very long-term gains should be taxed at a very low capital gains rate. "Ten for Ten" would be a good policy, which would mean a 10 percent tax on all holdings of more than 10 years instead of the current 28 percent. If the price of an asset doubled in 15 years and a 28 percent tax was paid on the gain, there can be an economic loss when taking inflation into consideration.
This is a reason for excusing the gain on certain home sales, but the same theory should apply to any other holdings, like the family farm, businesses or other forms of investment. And why should a property held for 30 years be taxed the same as a property held for one year and a day?
If very long-term holdings were taxed at a lower capital gains rate, it would cause the unfreezing of billions of dollars in assets that are now being held because of the 28 percent tax rate. This would create mobility of capital for reinvestment and therefore benefit the economy. Furthermore, the Government would be collecting taxes it is not now collecting because property owners have been reluctant to sell and pay 28 percent of the gain in taxes.
 
JESSE H. OPPENHEIMER
San Antonio, Sept. 24
 
The writer is partner in the law firm of Oppenheimer, Blend, Harrison & Tate.

HEADLINE: Word For Word/Fraud in Your Mailbox;
Postage To-Do: The Flim-Flams Brought by the Mailman

BYLINE: By TOM KUNTZ 

 BODY:
SO far this year, the United States Postal Inspection Service has received well over 400,000 complaints about scams in the mail. Last week it released the "five biggest lies told to consumers," a list of the most common ploys used by con artists to fleece the unsuspecting via the mail. Here are excerpts from typical letters. TOM KUNTZ
 
Lie No. 1: You're a "guaranteed winner"!
And you've been sent a cheesy, diploma-like certificate to prove it. When a recipient calls the number provided, he is asked to contribute to a fake charity, pay an exorbitant "processing fee" or buy useless "vitamins," a cheap home-security system or a water purifier. The postal service blotted out recipients' names and addresses, so they are made up here:

Certificate of Guarantee
CONGRATULATIONS
JOE SCHMOE
If you are JOE SCHMOE, residing at 13 Realsap Lane, Idunno, Idaho, then you have successfully completed the first two stages of our National Promotion and you have been selected as a:
GRAND NATIONAL FINALIST AND GUARANTEED AWARD RECIPIENT
You are ABSOLUTELY GUARANTEED to receive ONE of the six fabulous awards listed below simply for calling in your Control Number . . . NOW!!
JOE SCHMOE, YOUR AWARDS ARE:
1. A NEW CADILLAC COUPE DE VILLE!!
2. $5,000.00 IN CASH!!
3. HIS OR HERS GENUINE DIAMOND WATCH!!
4. A SONY(R) VIDEO CAMCORDER!!
5. A HAWAIIAN VACATION!!
6. A 46" SONY(R) WIDE SCREEN TV!!

CALL WITHIN 72 HOURS TO CLAIM YOUR AWARD OR IT MAY PASS TO AN ALTERNATE RECIPIENT.
 
Lie No. 2: This chain letter is legal!
Just about everyone gets a chain letter eventually. But if you pay for copying and mailing out additional letters as instructed, you wind up deep in the hole. Also, as the postal service notes, any chain letter that asks for money is illegal when sent through the mail. But in one such letter, legal eagle "Phillip A. Brown" begs to differ:
Dear Friend:
As an attorney at law, I am doing very well in my own Criminal Law practice. I am not revealing my address or phone number for a very good and simple reason: neither I nor my staff would have the time to answer all the calls and questions that would come in from people all over the country. I would not be able to give my clients my fullest attention to their problems, which must come first. . . .
THIS IS NOT A CHAIN LETTER, but a legal, money-making opportunity. Basically this is what we do: As with all multi-level businesses, we build our business by recruiting new partners and selling our products . . . Each $5.00 order you will receive will have a self-addressed stamped envelope in it! . . . This is the GREATEST multi-level mail order marketing anywhere!
1. Order all four reports listed and numbered on Page 5. Do this by ordering the report from each of the four names listed. For each report send $5.00 Cash and a self-addressed stamped envelope (business size #10) to the person listed and request that specific report . . .
2. Replace the name and address under REPORT #1 with yours, moving the one that was there down to REPORT #2. Move the name and address under REPORT #2 to REPORT #3. Move the name and address listed under REPORT #3 to REPORT #4. The name and address that was under REPORT #4 is dropped off the list and [he] is no doubt on the way to the bank. . . .
3. Take this entire packet, including the corrected names list, to your "quick print" shop and have as many copies of each made as you can initially afford to print. You can always print more later. (The more copies the bigger the discount.)
4. Send a copy of the entire package (it is all important) to everyone whose name and address you can get your hands on. . . . You'll find that a good name list [an additional expense] will make you more money than you could ever imagine. IT'S A GREAT INVESTMENT!

Lie No. 3: Stuff envelopes at home and make big money!
Some envelope-stuffing scams ask victims to send money for a "plan." The plan: Send out letters asking others to send you money for a plan. Other schemes are more transparent:
OUR SYSTEM OF MAKING MONEY IS VERY EASY TO OPERATE.
For as long as you wish to participate, by following our instructions you will receive pre-addressed envelopes with postage stamps already affixed. As you can see, the biggest part of your work will be securing envelopes, stuffing them with circulars and sending them back for payment. . . .
WHY DO YOU GLADLY GET SUCH A HIGH AMOUNT AS $3.00 PER ENVELOPE? Answer. The reason is to more than cover your small expense involved in receiving the envelopes, plus leave you with a large profit. . . . Since you will make good money through your efforts, you are glad to get $3.00 per envelope and see that you earn good profits from the comforts of home. . . .
IN ORDER TO GET YOU STARTED IMMEDIATELY, we must require a one-time payment of only $20.00. This covers our expense in showing you what to do. . . . In as much as we would like to send you our program without the small charge of $20.00, we must protect ourselves from those few who are not serious. . . .
Lie No. 4: Nigerian riches await!
Officials say businessmen and others continue to be enticed by the lure of a windfall from Nigeria, paying "bribes" or "good-faith money," or even winding up abducted for ransom. Scams begin with a syntactically intriguing letter:
Dear Sir,
CONFIDENTIAL
I have the pleasure to write you this letter which I am sure you will receive with great delight though with little surprise as we have not met in person before.
Without mincing words, I am the Chief Auditor with the NIGERIAN NATIONAL PETROLEUM CORPORATION and I got your address of contact from a Nigerian Merchant, who does business in your country. Considering your position and personality also with regards to your country of residence, I was moved to transact this life opportunity business with you.
The business is that I, in collaboration with my colleagues with the Central Bank of Nigeria and the Federal Ministry of Finance have after several meetings held, have decided to syphone [sic] some money totaling US$45.5M, U.S. Dollars (Forty Five Million, Five Hundred Thousand, United States Dollars) into a reliable foreign account after which we shall come over to the country and share the fund, hence my contacting you for your assistance to make this dream a reality. As Auditors, we came about this money (US$45.5M U.S. Dollars) when auditing the account of the Nigerian National Petroleum Corporation during the Civil Administration and found out that this money cannot be claimed as the politician who formed these contractors with their foreign counterparts are no longer in power as the Civilian Government was not able to pay these debts then because of the economic predicament that was prevailing before the Military took over power. . . .
We want to use your account and company name to present as the original owner of this fund and syphone the money into your account for safe-keeping pending our arrival for the sharing.
All you have to do immediately on receipt of this letter is to send the following materials. . . .
1. 3 copies of your company's letter headed papers (blank, signed and stamped by you).
2. 3 copies of your company's invoices (blank, signed and stamped by you).
3. Your bank account where you want this money to be paid into including the bank particulars.
4. Your private telephone, fax and telex numbers for easy communication. . . .
NOTE: We have held several meetings on this behalf and the sharing ration of this money was agreed to be:
1. 35% for you for your assistance.
2. 60% for us the Nigerian Party.
3. 5% for miscellaneous expenses. . . .
I am assuring you of 100% safety in this transaction as it involve highly placed civil servants.

Lie No. 5: You're going on a fabulous vacation!
All you have to do is pay a membership fee or handling charge, the offers typically claim:
JOE SCHMOE . . . PACK YOUR BAGS!
This certifies that you will receive a World Class Cancun/Florida/Caribbean Vacation Package offer for you and your family including all accommodations!
This certifies that JOE SCHMOE, residing at 13 Realsap Lane, Idunno, Idaho, is the registered holder of this Certificate of Authorization. We are delighted to advise you via FIRST CLASS MAIL, that . . . in celebration of our National Promotion, you will receive our Spectacular TROPICAL VACATION offer for you and your family! A TOLL FREE HOTLINE has been established for your immediate confirmation!
* Enjoy 5 Days/4 Nights in beautiful hotel accommodations in Cancun, Mexico!
* Delight in 4 Days/3 Nights additional accommodations in Magical Orlando, Florida, home of Magical Walt Disney World(R), Epcot Center(R), and MGM Studios Florida(R)!
* Complimentary rental car with unlimited mileage while in Florida!
But inspectors say such trips turn into horrors of bad hotels, cancellations or undisclosed costs like airfare -- making you wish you'd stayed home, though maybe not stuffing envelopes.

SECTION: Section 4; Page 4; Column 1; Week in Review Desk 

LENGTH: 822 words

HEADLINE: The Nation;
Why Bob Dole Can't Be Reagan

BYLINE: By SAMUEL G. FREEDMAN; Samuel G. Freedman is the author of "The Inheritance: How Three Families and America Moved from Roosevelt to Reagan and Beyond."

 BODY:
TWO summers ago, early in his latest quest for the Presidency, Bob Dole appeared before a gathering of the Republican National Committee. "I'm willing to be another Ronald Reagan," he offered, "if that's what you want." Of the hundreds of thousands of words the candidate has uttered since then, few pack nearly as much irony.
The conventional understanding of Mr. Dole's promise that day was that he was willing to play down his profile as a compromiser and a budget balancer in the Senate in favor of a more Reaganesque image. With his attacks on Hollywood films as immoral, he suddenly presented himself as a partisan in culture wars waged by the right wing of his party. Then, with his selection of Jack Kemp as running mate and his endorsement of across-the-board tax cuts, Mr. Dole completed his makeover: he reversed his longstanding opposition to supply-side economics. 
 Mr. Dole's attempts at reinvention have not brought him any closer to unseating President Clinton. And it is not only because Mr. Dole faces a clever incumbent astride a healthy economy. Mr. Dole is a victim of his own biography. His past in Russell, Kan., sets him apart from the demographic forces that brought conservative Republicans to power in the White House and eventually in Congress during the last 25 years. Far from being an heir to the Reagan revolution, he is the inconvenient remnant of an earlier dynasty.
In the script for a Dole victory, Russell was supposed to have symbolized the civic and moral values threatened by the baby-boomer profligacy of Bill Clinton. But there is another way to view Russell: as a a town that is ossifying while all around it America changes.
In places like Russell, Republican identity was more a matter of habit than ideology. But just as Mr. Dole was entering national politics in the early 1960's on the strength of his war record, the G.O.P. was recasting itself as a conservative coalition based in the suburbs of the South and West.
Ronald Reagan, more than anyone, added the final, decisive element to the mix of new conservatives -- those ethnic Catholics in the urban Northeast and industrial Middle West who came to be called Reagan Democrats. Mr. Reagan took a majority of the Catholic vote in both 1980 and 1984, as did George Bush in 1988 and Republican Congressional candidates in 1994. Several polls this fall put Mr. Dole at least 20 points behind Mr. Clinton with Catholic voters. And the reasons may be personal as well as political.
Mr. Reagan touched the collective memory of many Democrats because he embodied their experience. The connection went deeper than quoting Franklin D. Roosevelt or Harry S. Truman. As Mr. Reagan often reminded voters, he began his own political career as a New Deal Democrat and a trade unionist. In the late 1940's, he even traveled the nation on behalf of Americans for Democratic Action, the vanguard of the anti-Communist left.
Such credentials allowed Mr. Reagan to hallow the Democratic past while reviling the Democratic present. Though he grew rich as a corporate spokesman and called organized labor a special interest, he could appeal to much of the rank and file. The 1980 Reagan campaign penetrated labor strongholds from Flint to Bayonne and deluged union homes with a mailing touting Mr. Reagan's tenure as a union president. "He led the Screen Actors Guild in its first strike," it read, "and he won it!"
Mr. Reagan's victories realigned the Republican Party as well as the nation. In his wake, the prototypical Republican candidate in urban states became an ethnic one, usually Catholic. From Attorney General Dan Lundgren in California to Gov. Tom Ridge in Pennsylvania, many of the party's rising stars fit the mold. When George E. Pataki upset Mario M. Cuomo to become New York's Governor two years ago, his slate consisted of three Catholics and a Jew, with immigrant roots ranging from Hungary to Ireland.

 
 
Casting Aspersions
 This was not lost on Mr. Dole. In selecting a running mate, he considered three popular Catholic governors from the Rust Belt -- John Engler of Michigan, Tommy Thompson of Wisconsin and George Voinovich of Ohio -- before deciding upon Jack Kemp, who calls himself a born-again Christian. Mr. Kemp, a product of Southern California and later a Congressman from blue-collar, suburban Buffalo, himself typified the party's geographical tilt.
But now Mr. Dole's own party is distancing itself from him. Republican candidates for the House, assuming a Clinton victory, are running on a platform of denying the President a Democratic majority in Congress. Mr. Thompson has assailed the Dole campaign (an attack for which he apologized), while Mr. Voinovich has reportedly stopped returning its phone calls.
One might think of the discord as the sound of the generation gap. Or, perhaps, it is the voice of an exasperated director telling an actor he is all wrong for the part.

HEADLINE: Charles Barrett, Architect, 40

 BODY:
Charles Barrett, an architect who was highly regarded as an architectural draftsman, died on Oct. 19 at Mount Sinai Hospital in Miami. He was 40 and lived in Miami Beach.
The cause was complications from a liver illness, his family said. 
 Mr. Barrett was closely associated with the town planning office of Duany & Plater-Zyberk, Miami architects, although most recently he was in his own practice. He designed the Bayshore Villas in the Coconut Grove section of Miami, among other projects. His drawings were exhibited at many museums and galleries over the years and were published in several books, including "Building Classical," "The New Urbanism" and "Modern Classicisim."
Mr. Barrett was a graduate of California State and Yale Universities and worked for Robert A. M. Stern in New York and Allan Greenberg in Washington before moving to Miami. He was also a visiting lecturer at the University of Miami School of Architecture.
He is survived by his parents, William and Joyce Barrett, and a brother, Randall G., all of Fresno, Calif.

HEADLINE: Washington Is Producing Apple Crop In Quantity

BYLINE: By The New York Times 

DATELINE: ROYAL CITY, Wash.

 BODY:
Washington is harvesting what is likely to be the state's largest apple crop, using advanced technology and economies of scale that may make the family-run orchard a thing of the past here as well as in other apple-growing regions in New York and Michigan.
The Columbia River Basin, in the relatively isolated central part of the state, is being converted from wheat and row-crop agriculture to more lucrative crops like apples by corporate farmers that are reaping increased profits from huge orchards. 
 Dozens of young orchards 250 acres and larger have been planted by large fruit packers since the early 1990's. In some areas of the basin, orchards have been planted 1,000 acres at a time. The orchards can grow apples, especially the premier Red Delicious, better and cheaper than orchards in other areas of the state, certainly better than other areas of the country, because of the dry pest-free climate, rich volcanic soils and abundant water from the Grand Coulee Dam.
Washington already produces more than half of the nation's apples. The United States Apple Association estimates that the state will produce 94 million 42-pound boxes of freshly packed apples in the current harvest, which runs from late August to early November. In 1994, the state produced 92 million boxes. New York, the No. 2 apple state, is expected to produce 26 million boxes.
"Efficiency is the key," said Karen Maib, an extension agent, who is the local adviser to tree-fruit growers and who works for Washington State University. "It comes through good management and economies of scale."
Orchards make up 50,000 acres of the basin, Ms. Maib said. Nearly all the orchards are planted to the latest Red Delicious strains or to varieties like Fuji, Braeburn and Gala.
Experts say it is difficult to gauge how much fruit is produced, because nearly all of it is sent to other regions for packing or processing. About half the 350 orchards in the north basin have absentee owners. Orchards stretch across the rolling desert landscape in long rows of small uniform trees, many grown on wire trellises. Within a few years of planting, the orchards can yield more than mature orchards elsewhere.
Absentee corporate owners send in migrant crews from the West and Mexico to train, thin, pick and prune the trees with assembly-line efficiency. The new orchards are in stark contrast to the generations-old 40-acre family-owned orchards that prevail in other regions of the state and the nation.
The sweet aroma of fresh apples smashed beneath the fat wheels of a tractor still blend with the foul smell of diesel at harvest time. But much else has changed. The new orchards are rarely run by an owner-operator in pinstriped overalls who may serve on the local school board. Instead, they are controlled by a board of directors, firms of lawyers and accountants, corporate managers and field agents.
"Average isn't good enough," said Brooke Peterson, a former banker and extension agent who oversees several large orchards here. He defines an average grower as one who is going broke.
A good grower, Mr. Peterson said, can produce 50 to 60 bins an acre, well above the state average of 35 bins an acre. Each bin has about 1,000 pounds of apples.
"If he can't do that," he added, "he's in trouble. We're competing against ourselves and our neighbors. Anybody in Washington can beat out everybody in New York or Michigan. That's almost a given. People in this state have a bullish attitude."
Decisions are made with the help of computers, satellite dishes and Internet communications that connect shippers with global markets.
Field agents like Mr. Peterson live in their pickup trucks at harvest time, which continues into November with new late varieties like the Fuji. Washington is expected to produce more than five million boxes of that sweet dessert apple this year. Just a few years ago the state had none.
Even third-generation growers like Todd Cowan have rushed to plant factory-style orchards. Mr. Cowan said he could grow apples at a cost of $2.25 a box, compared with $4 to $5 a box in the old orchard that surrounds his home 70 miles away.
Blue Bird, the cooperative that Mr. Peterson works for and that Mr. Cowan owns with 300 other people, is one of the many small players in the basin, compared with giants like Stemilt Growers, Dovex, Zirkle Fruit, Borton and Sons, Washington Fruit and Produce and the Evans Fruit Company, all of which have thousands of newly planted acres.
The orchards are beginning to produce their crops. The effects that they will have on small family orchards in other regions will become clear in a few years.
"There isn't an apple-growing region in the world that isn't affected by how things are done here," Ms. Maib said. "This is a showplace for a lot of people. They come here to see how it's done."

HEADLINE: A One-Armed Bandit Makes a House Calls;
Virtual Casino Is Coming, but Regulation Is Still a Big Question

BYLINE: By JAMES STERNGOLD 

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, Oct. 27

 BODY:
There are few patches of legal turf the states guard more fiercely than gambling, which is why it seemed strange this summer when the National Association of Attorneys General urged the Federal Government to seize control of wagering on the Internet -- even though hardly anyone was doing it.
Even more bizarre was that the recommendation, including an alarming proposal for arresting gamblers from in front of their computers, was turned down by the Justice Department. 
 "The department does not agree that Federal law should be amended so broadly as to cover the first-time bettor who loses $5, particularly when Internet gaming is expected to mushroom and Federal resources are shrinking," John C. Keeney, an official in the criminal division at the Justice Department, wrote the states.
The episode highlighted the growing anxiety and confusion over one of the least developed, but potentially most vexing areas of Internet business -- gambling. The reach of the World Wide Web is erasing not only borders but also the ability of the authorities to control wagering (not to mention their ability to tax it). And gambling over computer networks is still new enough that it remains unclear exactly what is legal and what is not.
"We built it to be Russian-proof, but it turned out to be regulator proof," said Craig I. Fields, the former head of the Pentagon agency that helped create the Internet during the cold war, and who is now vice chairman of Alliance Gaming, a Las Vegas concern developing on-line gambling.
In principal, gambling by computer sounds promising, placing an increasingly popular habit, which can quickly become a compulsion for some individuals, within just about anyone's reach. Indeed, nearly every worker in the country with a personal computer and a few minutes to spare will soon be able to slip in some blackjack, poker, slots or baccarat for money at almost completely unregulated, virtual casinos.
To give some notion of the potential, Americans wagered $550 billion last year at traditional operations, from lotteries and horse races to bingo and casinos, and gambling companies had revenues of $44.4 billion. The Internet operators need to take only a fraction of that business to produce explosive growth -- or explosive scams.
"In my view, gambling is the fastest way to destroy the credibility of the Internet system," said John Kindt, an economics professor at the University of Illinois at Champaign and a gambling critic. "If you lose, you'll lose, and if you win, you could lose because there's no way to collect from these offshore operations."
He added that with personal computers now ubiquitous the on-line casinos could prove a devastating temptation for the growing numbers of compulsive gamblers. "People will be trapped," Mr. Kindt said. "They won't be able to get away from it."
All that is driving some law enforcement officials crazy, especially since the business, though made up of a tiny number of marginal companies operating from offshore havens like Antigua, Belize and Monaco, is on the cusp of a major expansion. A showdown, in short, is looming in the one area of the Internet that may well produce billions of dollars of commerce in just a couple of years.
Minnesota has sued Granite Gate Resorts, a company in Las Vegas, Nev., that is preparing to offer a sports betting on-line service called Wager Net, as a test case. The state contends it is a consumer fraud because the service says, improperly, it is legal. The state court hearing the suit has yet to rule on whether the state has jurisdiction.
California is attacking by threatening the telephone companies with prosecution if they do not cut off service to Internet gambling concerns in the state.
Several states are also insisting that they will not recognize the use of offshore sites if the companies are attracting American gamblers. "We're going to take an aggressive stand on this, and we'll interpret the law as broadly and prohibitively as we can, and there will be no havens," said Tom Gede, a special assistant to the Attorney General of California.
Senator Jon Kyl, an Arizona Republican, offered a bill earlier this year that would have made all Internet gambling explicitly illegal. Although it was killed in committee, he has insisted he will offer it again. A National Gaming Commission was established by the Congress this year, and a big focus of the study it will be conducting will be on-line wagering, which could also lead to legislation.
And there is an even trickier issue, some believe.
"There's another I-word that really scares the government, Indian gambling," said I. Nelson Rose, a professor at the Whittier Law School here and a gambling law expert. "If the Indian tribes get involved in this then the way the law is written all sorts of sports betting and other kinds of on-line betting could take place from Indian lands in an unregulated fashion."
That has left many state officials worried, but unwilling to just wait for the explosion.
"The Federal Government is either going to want to get into it now, or they'll be drawn into it later," insisted Hubert Humphrey 3d, the Attorney General of Minnesota and a prime mover behind the request this summer for Federal involvement. "This is moving so fast that we have to get out in front of it."
All these worries are over a business that is, at the moment at least, underwhelming. Sue Schneider, managing editor of Rolling Good Times On-Line, an on-line magazine that tracks the industry, said that there were fewer than a half dozen virtual casinos that were taking real bets at the moment, and many of the games are frustratingly slow. There are also unresolved concerns over the security of on-line transactions and whether an unlicensed casino would maintain fair odds on the games.
In fact, the established casino companies, while watching warily, appear unfazed. "My personal view is it's not very interesting," said Stephen Bollenbach, chief executive of Hilton Hotels, the country's largest casino concern. "It's a different business than we're in."
Nevertheless, about 40 prospective operators have set up Web sites, and many offer games that, at some point in the future, could easily be transformed into gambling for real money.
Ms. Schneider said that as many as a dozen more on-line casino or bookmaking operations may open for business within the next few months.
While all the companies are seeking essentially the same thing -- to get into the lucrative gambling business without the costs of building casinos or having to pay dealers or other high-priced staff -- they are taking many different approaches because of the murky legal status of the business and the uncertainty over just who will really want to spend money this way.
Virtual Vegas is one of the more active on-line casinos and one of the most cautious. The company's formula is simple: treat Internet gambling as another type of computer game and leave the money out of it for now to avoid hassles with the police.
David Herschman, chief executive of the company, which is based in the Venice district of Los Angeles, said Virtual Vegas had already spent $3 million developing its technology and would spend another $10 million.
It has created twists on traditional games to make them more like computer games, and therefore more appealing to the people who already spend perhaps more time than they should in front of their personal computers. Virtual Vegas offers Turbo Black Jack and Assault Poker, which is like combining Doom and five card stud. Virtual Vegas will be available on Time Warner's cable on-line service.
Peter Demos Jr., president of World Wide Web Casinos, which is based in Orange County, said his company had decided to trust the idea that having an offshore computer location will keep it out of jail. World Wide Web Casinos has acquired a tiny casino on Antigua, the St. James Club. "It does provide us with a little credibility," Mr. Demos said.
He added: "We want to be a huge grind joint. We're not going to attract high rollers. We're looking for someone who will blow $20 or so in an hour and a half." World Wide Web Casino, which will offer black jack, five kinds of video poker and slots, is in testing and hopes to open around Thanksgiving.
Internet Gaming Technologies Inc., based in San Diego, is also using an offshore strategy: it has a gambling license in Ecuador.
Joseph R. Paravia, president of the company, said he was less confident in the offshore loophole, so the company will offer its games only to people outside the United States, which he insisted was technically feasible. Mr. Paravia said the company plans to pursue known gamblers, giving them computers if necessary. "We didn't want to take a propeller-head and have to teach him how to gamble," Mr. Paravia said. "We want to go after a known market."
You Bet International Inc., however is what might be called an anti-Internet Internet gambling company. The company, which will operate through the New York Racing Association, offers a horse race bookmaking service that makes only tangential use of the Internet: to register accounts and download software.
"The Internet as it exists is the wrong place for this kind of application," said Stephen A. Molnar, executive vice president of the company. "The biggest problem is political. We believe that at some point the government is going to come down on this."
You Bet, which expects to begin taking bets in February 1997, has constructed a private on-line service over which its account holders will get information and place their wagers. And it appears to be legal, since it operates within New York State parimutuel betting laws.
"It's all interesting, but most of what is being bandied about is hocus pocus at this point," said Jason Ader, an analyst with Bear, Stearns & Company. "It's a very scary situation. There's no regulation. There's tremendous potential for abuse out there and maybe a little fun until then."
