HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE REPUBLICAN;
Dole Assails White House as 'Animal House' of 'Elitists'

BYLINE: By KATHARINE Q. SEELYE 

DATELINE: ELK GROVE, Calif., Oct. 27

 BODY:
Bob Dole unleashed a stinging volley of attacks on the Clinton Administration today, describing it as a "group of elitists who have never done anything." At one point Mr. Dole called the White House an "animal house," yet he gloated that on Election Day, he would wait up to hear that California went Republican and then, as the future President, he would "drive by the White House and honk!"
Speaking from bales of hay to a crowd of Republicans at a steak-and-oyster picnic here in Elk Grove, near the state capital, the Republican Presidential nominee warned again that a rash of brewing scandals in the Administration could explode in the next four years. 
 "We have one week to set the record straight," Mr. Dole said. "One week to tell the American people, 'Watch out!' "
Contending that he was speaking only of ethical lapses since the Clintons moved into the White House, Mr. Dole said that "they fail in every test" as guardians of the public trust.
"Double-talk has a bad name because of this group, this group of elitists in the White House who've never done anything, never done anything, and now they're running the country, running the country," he said. "Now think about it. If you want leadership. If you want mature leadership, if you want honor, duty, country, integrity, vote for Bob Dole and Jack Kemp."
Referring to Craig Livingstone, the former security chief of White House personnel, who used to be a bouncer in a bar, Mr. Dole described an imagined session of Administration officials rummaging through confidential F.B.I. files.
"He sits there drinking a beer and saying, 'Let's look through Bob Dole's file today, yeah, and see what he's got going. Set 'em up boys. Have another beer. Let's look through some more F.B.I. files.'
He added: "They've turned the White House into something else, I don't now what it is. It's the animal house! It's the animal house!"
The scorching assessment followed another assault on The New York Times -- his fourth in four days -- calling the newspaper "the biggest apologist for Bill Clinton in the world."
As the campaign pace quickens and time shortens, Mr. Dole is summoning up ever-more vivid language to portray the White House in unflattering terms. In Stockton on Saturday night, he declared at a rally that it was time Americans had "a real man in the White House."
But Mr. Dole began his day in San Jose at a relatively placid display of support for legal immigrants, offering full-throated praise for them as he showcased that city's vibrant Vietnamese-American population. Outside a high school, he stood before an array of Vietnamese-American children dressed in the shimmering yellows and pinks of traditional Vietnamese dress.
But from this tableau, he aimed darts at Mr. Clinton, accusing him of failing to secure the borders from illegal immigrants, who, Mr. Dole suggested, were "violent criminals." And he promised that if he could not seal the borders as President, he would compensate California taxpayers for their $3 billion "burden" in caring for illegal immigrants.
Mr. Dole also denounced affirmative action as he offered his support for a popular initiative on California's November ballot that would erase such state efforts.
Just nine days before the election, Mr. Dole focused himself on the overriding issue of immigration here in the nation's biggest state, where he is waging a make-or-break campaign on the theory that as goes California, so will go the election.
But he also kept up his assault on Mr. Clinton's ethics, and the responsive crowd -- estimated at over 4,000 by the Secret Service -- initiated and joined in several chants. When Mr. Dole quoted the Rev. Dr. Martin Luther King Jr., he began, "Our goal must be, as Martin Luther King so eloquently said, 'a color-blind country that judges all persons, not by the color of their skin' " -- and here the audience shouted along with him -- " 'but by the content of their character!' "
Reacting to several signs in the largely Vietnamese-American crowd, Mr. Dole yelled out, "Character does count!"
He quickly discovered how in tune his audience was with him when he began his speech referring to The New York Times.
"It probably comes as no great surprise that The New York Times endorsed Bill Clinton -- I know that's a shock to all of you," he said, the audience booing loudly in response.
"But even they had to apologize for his ethics. 'Why apologize? Why not send him back to Little Rock, Ark.? So when a great newspaper has to apologize, and say, 'Well, maybe he'll do better the next four years,' I don't want to take that chance."
Several listeners called out, "We can't afford it!".
At another point, Mr. Dole made a gentle reference to the "liberal media." Without prompting, many in the audience turned toward a section of cameramen and reporters and jeered with their thumbs down.
In criticizing The Times, Mr. Dole said: "And I read a very interesting editorial endorsement of Bill Clinton in The New York Times. They apologized for him. They're the biggest apologist for Bill Clinton in the world. But even in their editorial they had to say, 'Well, we don't agree with his ethical conduct. Maybe in the next four years he'll clean it up.' He's not going to clean it up! Now, when you have to apologize for your candidate, you shouldn't endorse him in the first place."
Considering his audience, and his message, Mr. Dole also skipped references he made as recently as Saturday about foreigners. For example, when his bus caravan pulled into the town of Merced, Mr. Dole said he wanted to bar noncitizens from contributing money to political campaigns in the United States. "America is for Americans," he asserted.
He also dropped his recent lament that the Clinton Administration was "rushing through" citizenship for immigrants so they could vote, presumably for Democrats. He has said the proceedings were so hurried that about 10 percent were criminals.
Today, Mr. Dole ladled on superlatives for Vietnamese-Americans.
"No people of this country, a country so rich in immigration success stories, has flourished more abundantly in the freedom offered by America," he said. "No people have ever given more back to this country in hard work, economic growth, strong families, close-knit communities and sound values."
Making it clear that he supported legal immigration, Mr. Dole went on to denounce illegal immigration.
"Thousands of Californians have been victims of violent criminals who should have been stopped at the border before they ever set foot in America in the first place," he said. "And that is the price people pay for the Federal Government's failure to secure the border."
He added: "I promise in a Dole Administration we will secure the border, and if we can't secure it, we'll make certain you're compensated for the cost it takes -- it's $2 billion in education, about a half billion dollars for incarceration, half billion dollars for medical expenses."

HEADLINE: A Suitor Woos Bayonne With a Grand Plan

BYLINE: By ANDY NEWMAN 

DATELINE: BAYONNE, N.J., Oct. 26

 BODY:
John S. Rogers, who plans to build the world's biggest ocean liner, stood behind the lectern in the City Council chambers here and addressed the audience in measured tones.
"I was up in the World Trade Center," he said, "and I looked out across the harbor. And I saw the Promised Land." 
 The 11 members of the Bayonne Base Re-use Commission chuckled. The 70-odd Bayonne residents in the audience chuckled. Mr. Rogers chuckled.
But everyone knew that Mr. Rogers, who heads a New York company looking for a good place to dock the quarter-mile-long, 21-story-high, 6,200-passenger cruise ship of tomorrow, or perhaps the day after tomorrow, was hardly joking. Bayonne's Military Ocean Terminal, 437 acres of prime waterfront jutting into Upper New York Bay, is a hot property. And deciding what to do with it after the terminal, Bayonne's largest employer, closes in 1999 might be the most important choice this well-preserved if unspectacular city of 61,000 has ever faced.
So for three hours on Thursday, the people of Bayonne listened as Mr. Rogers and a supporting cast described the riches that would flow in if the city were the summer home of the modestly named America World City, which if it existed today would be two-and-a-half times the size of the next largest passenger ship.
Mr. Rogers also showed a video. "America World City is a liberation from old ways," a narrator said, "the first in a new generation of floating cities." Images of the ship's amenities flashed across the screen: three hotel towers, more conference space than the Waldorf-Astoria, 30 shops and restaurants, a 2,000-seat concert hall and 6 swimming pools.
Then there were the plans for the base itself, a sprawling complex of warehouses, barracks and docks. An architect's drawing showed a bustling downtown of office buildings, movie theaters, a small golf course, a sports amphitheater and -- "one of my favorites," Mr. Rogers said -- an old-fashioned carousel.
World City Corporation would redevelop the base and divide the profits with Bayonne. Thousands of jobs. Hundreds of millions in tax revenue. And all the city had to do was lease World City the entire site for a dollar a year.
Almost as impressive as the America World City were the names behind it. Knut Ulstein Kloster, the founder of Norwegian Cruise Lines, conceived of the ship and has put up $34 million toward its design. World City's board of advisers includes a former commandant of the Coast Guard and former heads of the Federal Maritime Commission and the Maritime Administration of the Transportation Department. They told the audience that the ship was technologically and economically feasible.
Mr. Rogers said that as an American-made ship, the World City would be singularly positioned to capture not just the $5 billion-a-year cruise market but the $50 billion meeting and conference market as well. Only American-made and registered ships are allowed to sail between United States ports and there are no American cruise ships currently afloat.
The World City, which would call at ports up and down the East Coast, has a winter home lined up -- Port Canaveral, Fla., the nation's biggest cruise port. And Westin Hotels and Resorts in Seattle agreed last month to manage the ship.
All the project lacks, Mr. Rogers said, is a loan guarantee from the Maritime Administration to back 87.5 percent of the ship's $1.2 billion construction cost.
There have been plenty of suitors for the base, said Bayonne's Mayor, Leonard P. Kiczek, who as chairman of the re-use commission has given about 60 tours since the base closing was announced last year.
The longshoremen's union wants to lease part of it for a bulk freight port. The Coast Guard plans to anchor cutters there when it leaves Governors Island. Someone wants to build a huge recycling operation. Someone else wants to blow up one of the warehouses for a movie. Other groups want to build container ports like those in Newark and Elizabeth.
But no one, Mr. Kiczek said, has presented a plan as detailed or as ambitious as World City's.
"It's one of those projects that you wish could get built," Mr. Kiczek said. "If they can get the loan guarantee within the next 18 months, there's a good chance that that kind of concept would fit into our overall re-use plan."
The "if" is a big one, however.
The ship has been on the drawing board for six years, and after World City submitted a 150-pound application for the loan guarantee last year, the Maritime Administration returned it for lack of supporting documentation.
"We'd like to have evidence from a U.S. shipyard that they are interested in doing this project," said a Federal official, who spoke on condition of anonymity. "We have nothing. In fact, we have a tremendous body of contrary evidence."
The size of the guarantee gives the Government pause. The Maritime Administration would have to set aside 5 to 10 percent of the amount of the loan to back it, or about $50 million to $100 million. Last year, the entire guarantee program ran on less than $50 million.
Some Bayonne residents shared the Government's skepticism. During a break, Paul Yusack, a 72-year-old former pipe fitter, said he smelled a land grab.
"I don't think they're selling a ship," he said. "They're selling developments, and they want the land for a buck."
Suzanne Ciano, who works in the Navy's accounting department at the base, left before the show was over. "I think it's going to be exciting and great and all that," she said, "but we have seen men and women leave from this base for Desert Storm and Desert Shield to fight for our country, and to replace it with all this is very heartbreaking."
The meeting adjourned, with the reactions decidedly mixed. Mike Vitale, a retired import-export worker at the base, expressed enthusiasm.
"This is the best thing that could ever happen to the city of Bayonne," he said. "This is bigger than King Kong!"
But Marietta Dicroce, a retired schoolteacher, said she was not so sure.
"It sounds inspiring, but I wonder what the downside would be," she said. "I wonder if we'll lose our true character. Bayonne is a very traditional city with very real values. Will it ruin our youth? They'll be attracted to all that glitz and glamour."

HEADLINE: The Bible, a Perennial, Runs Into Sales Resistance

BYLINE: By DOREEN CARVAJAL 

 BODY:
For professional Bible-thumpers -- whose task it is to promote the world's longest-running, recession-proof best seller -- this is a strangely sluggish season for marketing God's word.
The $200 million market for Bibles is as flat as a leather Bible cover and this trend has naturally resulted in much reflection. 
 Earlier last summer, the Family Bookstore chain shipped back to publishers more than $200,000 worth of rejected King James Versions that were gathering dust motes on shelves. Three new Bible translations were introduced this year amid much hoopla, but already some are being consigned to the book remainder bins.
Bible sales also took a steep plunge in June -- traditionally a time when hefty, embossed copies are given away as classic gifts to newlyweds and college graduates. Monthly sales fell to $3.7 million, an almost 48 percent drop from the comparable period a year earlier, according to the Association of American Publishers, which primarily tracks general trade publishers that produce Bibles rather than traditional religious-book publishers. Sales rebounded the following month, but remained down 9 percent for the year.
"I've never seen a time in which well-positioned and well-financed and well-done translations have entered the market with such horrible results," said Hargis Thomas, director of sales and marketing at Oxford University Press, one of the smaller publishers in the Bible market. "I think that we've reached saturation point. What we're really doing is redividing market share."
Over the years, sales of the Bible were considered immune to the vagaries of the economy because readers faithfully turned to its comforting passages in times of economic crisis. And unlike trade publishing -- which has struggled with returns of little-wanted books -- Bibles generally stayed on the shelves until they were sold.
"What we used to say was bring on the recession," Mr. Thomas recalled. "Recession drove people to church, and they realized that they were missing their Bible. But now it's no longer recession-proof because it's a much larger business than it was 20 years ago. There's an explosion of product."
It is the fifth deadly sin of marketing -- a glut -- that may account for the current flat market, according to many Bible publishers and Christian booksellers. There are now several hundred versions of the Bible, catering to every niche of reader -- from Bibles for runners and study books for teen-agers to devotionals for mothers and couples, boys and girls.
The result is too many Bibles for too few faithful, according to Michael A. Kupinski, an analyst with A. G. Edwards, who tracks Thomas Nelson Inc., the largest publicly held publisher of Bibles. "They broadened the distribution channels, but they didn't broaden the customer base."
Like trade publishers of general fiction and nonfiction, Bible publishers rode a wave of growth with the advent of superstores like Barnes & Noble and of discount warehouses that peddled books along with detergent. And like trade publishers, they also suffered when the Bibles languished on the shelves and the chains started returning books by the truckload.
Nelson reported losing almost $1.4 million in its fiscal first quarter ended in June. Its Bible revenue was down more than 6 percent from the year-earlier period, and the company attributed the drop to reduced sales and high returns from some general and Christian bookstore chains.
The company also introduced a new translation earlier this year called the Contemporary English Version, which has so far been able to capture less than 1 percent of the Bible market, according to a major Bible wholesaler, Spring Arbor.
Frank Couch, the head of Nelson's Bible division, remains optimistic, however, as the company approaches the traditional peak Bible-selling season leading up to Christmas.
"Sales are doing fine in the period that we're in now," Mr. Couch said. "It's still a sluggish year, but our Bible sales are picking up. Some people say it has to do with the election. It could be that people are preoccupied with political things."
But even if readers are getting choosier, that has not deterred publishers who try to tempt them with modernized versions of the Bible that are intended to be more relevant and accessible.
The latest entrant into the market, the New International Reader, comes this month from Zondervan, a unit of HarperCollins, which is in turn owned by the News Corporation. Zondervan is following a popular industry trend to simplified text. Of all Bibles on the market for adults, this version is geared to the lowest reading level: third grade.
Largely to counter the softness in the market for more traditional Bibles, Zondervan has devised a strategy to emphasize its front list, or newest versions, which anchor the Bible best-seller list maintained by the Christian Booksellers Association.
"Consumers are looking for what's new and relevant in their lives and they're not going to buy a Bible for the sake of buying a Bible," said Tom Mackabee, vice president and Bible publisher for Zondervan.
To determine what makes a reader open a Bible, the publisher Tyndale House commissioned a survey of 1,000 adults by Barna Research before it introduced its own breezy paraphrase of the Bible, the New Living Translation.
An overwhelming number of those polled ranked the Bible as the most influential book in history -- ahead of the second-place finisher, Dr. Benjamin Spock's "Baby and Child Care." But fewer than half actually read the Bible, and most complained about impenetrable text or the lack of time to wade through passages.
Conscious of those complaints, Tyndal introduced the New Living Translation in August with a print run of 1.1 million run and orders for more than 500,000 copies. Written at a sixth-grade reading level, this Bible took seven years to translate and prepare. A team of 90 scholars pored over the modernized passages and checked their meaning against the original text. (Psalm 23: "The Lord is my shepherd; I have everything I need.")
The New Living Translation has already moved up the Bible best-seller list to the No. 4 position, behind the perennial King James Version. The list is maintained by the Christian Booksellers Association, which is based in Colorado and represents more than 2,700 stores in the United States.
Bill Anderson, the association's president, said some of his members speculated that customers were delaying Bible purchases until the release of a new translation. But he has his doubts: "I'm not sure that customers are holding off. It's not like the release of a mustang."
It is his hope that one of the new translations will take off, promoting general Bibles sales.
"If history repeats itself," he said, "then the introduction will spur Bible sales. The excitement and the awareness causes people to read. That's what we need."

HEADLINE: Essay;
Lippo Suction

BYLINE: By WILLIAM SAFIRE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
In 1994, a former U.S. naval officer named Nicholas Eftimiades, who now works for our Defense Intelligence Agency, wrote and cleared an article titled "Chinese Intelligence Operations" for the Naval Institute Press.
"Case officers make extensive use of commercial covers," he reported. "For example, a vice president of the China Resources Holding Company (Hua Ren Jituan) in Hong Kong is traditionally a military case officer from Guangzhou. This officer coordinates the collection activities of other intelligence personnel operating under Hua Ren cover." 
 In 1995, David Harris, ex-chief of strategic planning of Canadian intelligence, confirmed in The Globe and Mail of Toronto that "U.S. Intelligence even says Hong Kong's China Resources Holding Co. traditionally reserves one vice-presidential position for an MID [Military Intelligence Department] intelligence officer."
What does China's espionage use of a company in Hong Kong have to do with us? Only this: In November of 1992, China Resources bought control of the Hongkong Chinese Bank, part of the Lippo empire headed by Indonesia's Riady family.
That put Lippo in business with the Chinese Communists. This is the same family that bought a Little Rock bank in the mid-80's that turned out to be of great help to the Clintons. And this was a family whose patriarch boasted of placing one of his employees -- John Huang, who worked at the Hongkong Chinese Bank in the mid-80's -- in a sensitive trade post in the Clinton Administration.
The Riadys' Mr. Huang has been as missing as Suharto's first name for three weeks, and has recently been untouchable by marshals with subpoenas. The Democratic National Committee, despite public assurances from Chairman Chris Dodd, is trying not to surface its fund-raising vice chairman to answer reporters' questions until the election is over.
Most media interest in Huang concerns funny foreign fund-raising and laundered contributions, and is brushed off by White House cover-uppers with an airy everybody-does-it. But the line of inquiry that troubles them is what the Riadys' man did at the Department of Commerce when he was principal deputy assistant secretary for international trade.
Merely a low-level flunky, Secretary Mickey Kantor would have us believe. Top-secret clearance meant nothing; the Lippo million-dollar bonus baby was supposedly only a paper-pusher, of no influence on policy, privy to no really sensitive material.
I think that's baloney. "Huang was high enough to see everything," a foreign-trade hand says, "and low enough not to be seen."
When a mid-level official at Commerce gets to attend two private meetings in the Oval Office with the President and James Riady, word gets around that this guy has clout. If Huang had such access to the Oval Office, logic suggests he would have access to trade secrets and trade negotiating policy, economic intelligence of great interest to his former employer and its new partner in China.
I have seen no evidence at all to suggest that John Huang is anything other than a loyal American citizen eager to advance himself and his friends through aggressive fund-raising for Democrats -- aided, as Newsweek now reveals, by a Clinton political diplomat on Taiwan.
But what Congress, the Justice Department and the White House should want to know is this: The Lippo bank in Los Angeles at which Huang worked signed the F.D.I.C.'s cease-and-desist order regarding anti-money laundering laws. Was Huang questioned about the F.D.I.C.'s inquiry before getting a top-secret post?
And given the Defense Intelligence Agency's knowledge of Chinese spying conducted out of a Lippo affiliate, did no spook raise a question about placing Lippo's longtime employee in a position to see our economic secrets? (D.I.A., worried about offending Clinton, may yank Eftimiades off China operations.)
"Please be advised that the F.B.I. did not conduct an investigation of Mr. Huang," faxes the F.B.I., correcting my assumption that top-secret clearance required its full fieldwork. Only a name check came from the F.B.I.; perhaps the security-unconscious Office of Personnel Management waved Huang through.
We'll find more to the Asian connection than fund-raising -- unless Clinton's corruption is abetted by an anything-goes Democratic Congress.

HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE VOTERS;
In Battleground State, Weapons Are Falling Silent

SERIES: STATE BY STATE: Pennsylvania

BYLINE: By R. W. APPLE Jr. 

DATELINE: HARRISBURG, Pa., Oct. 27

 BODY:
What happens when a Presidential campaign all but disappears?
Pennsylvania, historically one of the crucial cockpits of national politics, has found out in the last 10 days. For strategic reasons, Bob Dole and Bill Clinton stopped visiting the state and pulled television advertising off the air -- one because he was behind, one because he was ahead, both because they thought they could use scarce resources to better effect elsewhere. 
 Mr. Dole staged a big rally at Point State Park in Pittsburgh just after the Republican convention in San Diego. Mr. Clinton spent $400,000 on one Philadelphia television station in September alone. But the Dole campaign "went dark," as they say in the trade, on Oct. 6 in Pittsburgh and Scranton, Oct. 14 in Philadelphia and Harrisburg. The Clinton campaign followed suit last week.
With little coverage on local television news, Pennsylvanians have been left with mere glimpses of the campaign on network broadcasts and in newspaper accounts. Only an occasional radio commercial pierces the silence in the media markets around Philadelphia, Pittsburgh, Allentown and Wilkes-Barre.
In Lancaster, capital of the Pennsylvania Dutch country, Thomas O'Brien, the chairman of the local Clinton organization, bought advertising space just below the windshields on 33 Red Rose Transit Authority buses, and then wrote to President Clinton, imploring him to visit the area, a Republican stronghold.
"I guess people remember there's an election," said Tony Podesta, the Washington lobbyist and political strategist who oversees Pennsylvania for Mr. Clinton. "I haven't heard about anyone waking up in the middle of the night and shouting, 'My God, what will I do without campaign commercials?' "
Not likely, said Kathleen Hall Jamieson of the Annenberg School of Communications at the University of Pennsylvania, a student of political advertising. The absence of Presidential commercials from the state was "good riddance," she said, "because the kinds of ads we were seeing, two ethics ads, one from each side, both lies, only create cynicism and further dispirit the voters."
The laws of inertia being what they are, the Presidential race has stayed where it was, with Mr. Clinton 14 to 16 points ahead in the polls. And maybe, just maybe, Mr. Dole's absence, combined with the lack of races for senator or governor this year, will hurt Republicans down the ticket. Two Republican House seats appear to be at risk, with a third a little bit shaky as well.
Democrats are thrilled. The state's 23 electoral votes, the fifth largest bloc, bigger than those of hard-fought Ohio and Michigan, look to be in the bag for the President. The day the word spread that Mr. Dole was diverting advertising money from here to California, gleeful Clinton operatives parked a car outside Republican headquarters with its radio blaring, "California, Here I Come," just to rub it in.
The Republicans are used to winning here. Both Senators and the Governor are Republicans, and the party controls both houses of the State Legislature. But this year, they can only talk bravely, as underdogs do, about grass-roots campaigning, yard signs, direct mail, phone banks and getting out the vote.
"We're still here," said Pat Conway, the executive director of the Dole campaign in Pennsylvania. "We haven't gone anywhere. We have six full-time people in the field, five at headquarters, and we're still fighting the ground war, even if the air war has been suspended. We can still close the gap."
Mr. Conway got some help this weekend from a visit to Erie by Jack Kemp, the Republican Vice-Presidential nominee, and Mr. Clinton, relenting, has scheduled a visit to the University of Pennsylvania in Philadelphia on Tuesday.
But that's probably it for the campaign. In this state, said David Buffington, who publishes a political newsletter, "nothing has changed and nothing is going to, as far as the Presidential race -- Dole gets massacred."
"Cut through the overanalysis," he said, "and listen to what the voters say. Fairly or unfairly, people tell me Dole is too old, an old, bad campaigner against a young, good campaigner. And we have peace and prosperity. If the economy was in the tank and Bosnia was blowing up, it might be different. But they aren't."
The disappearance of Presidential commercials means more air time is available for others, not only local candidates but also national organizations like the A.F.L.-C.I.O., which has been bombarding swing districts with anti-Republican advertisements; a business coalition headed by the Chamber of Commerce, taking the opposite tack, and Citizens Action, a public advocacy group, running ads on the environment.
Ed Mitchell, a Wilkes-Barre consultant, said he was getting calls from television stations asking whether his candidates wanted more time and in some cases offering cut rates, which are as rare in October of an election year as self-deprecating candidates.
G. Terry Madonna, director of the Center for Politics and Public Affairs at Millersville University, said he considered it "a disastrous mistake" for Mr. Dole to have gone off the air, in terms of New Jersey, which has a close Senate contest and is reached by Philadelphia television, as well as Pennsylvania.
"With no one active at the top of the ticket," he explained, "the Republican Senate and House candidates are out there naked. The President is the President, and his voters will come out, but the Republicans, I doubt it. It could make a difference -- you pick a number -- of 8, 9, 10 points."
The most vulnerable Republican in Pennsylvania may be Representative Phil English of Erie, in the northwest corner of the state. A freshman, he won two years ago by fewer than 5,000 votes in a marginal district, and this year he is running from his party. While his challenger, Ron DiNicola, slams him for having voted with Speaker Newt Gingrich 97 percent of the time, he puts President Clinton in his commercials and said during a debate with Mr. DiNicola this month, "I broke with my party to fight to raise the minimum wage."
After an unimpressive start, Mr. DiNicola seems to have pulled his campaign together. An immigrant's son from Erie who made his way to a high-powered Los Angeles legal career by way of the Marines and Harvard, he has raised $100,000 in the last few weeks and has brought in Bob Shrum and Tad Devine, a pair of well-regarded Democratic guns for hire, to handle advertisements and strategy.
Pennsylvania's other House freshman, Representative Jon D. Fox, represents the lush Main Line suburbs west of Philadelphia. His district elected a Democrat, Marjorie Margolies-Mezvinsky, in 1992, the "year of the woman," and rejected her in the 1994 Republican tidal wave. Although places like Bryn Mawr and Radnor remain profoundly Republican, they are also strongholds of the soccer moms whose antipathy to the party's stand on abortion has caused many to defect.
There as in Erie, the A.F.L.-C.I.O. is pouring hundreds of thousands of dollars into the effort to oust the Republican incumbent, and the Democratic challenger, in this case Joseph M. Hoeffel, a Montgomery County commissioner, is trying to capitalize on Mr. Gingrich's unpopularity. Mr. Fox may be in trouble.
A Philadelphia Daily News poll 10 days ago showed Mr. Fox ahead by 8 percentage points, 43 to 35; it is a warning sign when an incumbent has not reached 50 percent late in the campaign. Furthermore, 52 percent of the respondents gave Mr. Fox a fair or poor job approval rating, a very weak showing.
Private polls show Mr. Clinton running well ahead in the county, and the outcome of the Fox-Hoeffel race may depend on how much Mr. Dole's inactivity affects Republican turnout. Will Republicans offended by the Republican right stay home, or will they turn out, vote against Mr. Dole on ideological grounds, then vote for their local Republican, Mr. Fox, to make amends?
No one had expected a particularly close contest in the Fifth District in central Pennsylvania, a mountainous domain that includes State College, Bradford and Oil City. Representative William F. Clinger Jr., a Republican who is retiring, drew no Democratic opposition in 1992 or 1994. But State Representative Ruth C. Rudy, a longtime member of the Democratic National Committee, is given an outside chance of beating her Republican rival, State Senator John E. Peterson, whose campaign has been damaged by newspaper allegations of sexual harrassment.
The Harrisburg Patriot-News printed accusations last week that he had made unwelcome sexual advances to four female legislative employees, one a teen-age page. He called it a political attack, and though the accusations have gained a certain level of credibility in state political circles, he is still favored. 

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Series

HEADLINE: In Dole's Hometown, a White-Knuckle Finish

BYLINE: By ADAM NAGOURNEY 

DATELINE: RUSSELL, Kan., Oct. 26

 BODY:
To find the heart of Bob Dole's hometown in these last unsettled days of his campaign, do not go to Dole headquarters on Main Street. Or to the afternoon coffee-and-pie gatherings of the town elders at Meridy's. Or even to the V.F.W. hall with the plaque honoring Dole outside and old war friends inside.
Go no further then the cozy cocoon of sunflower prints, silk floral arrangements, cuckoo clocks and Bob Dole photographs that is the living room of Mr. Dole's eldest sister, Gloria Nelson, on West Fifth Street. 
 "The wrong man," Mrs. Nelson said, her bright yellow sunflower earrings bouncing as she rocked energetically in her easy chair, "is getting chomped."
There are more than a few people here distraught over the troubles that have befallen the man celebrated in a banner over Main Street as "Our Hometown Hero." And they are further concerned about the disfavor a defeat of Mr. Dole might bring to the reputation and economy of this proud, tiny town, whose fortunes have been tied to his for almost 40 years.
But there is probably no one who expresses that more plainly and plaintively then Bob Dole's 75-year-old sister. Mrs. Nelson is Bob Dole unvarnished, defending her home and her little brother against what she sees as an assault by the news media and the Democrats. Mrs. Nelson speaks for Russell when she declares: "We just don't deserve this. This town is a close-knit family. We don't want Bob to get hurt. We just don't deserve this."
Mrs. Nelson recited the questions that have been raised about Mr. Clinton's character, like philandering and marijuana experimentation, and the tales of scandals she hears on the Rush Limbaugh's talk show each day. "Why don't they write about those things?" she demanded. "Why don't they leave Bob Dole alone? He hasn't done anything wrong."
"Why are they putting Bob in this bad light?" continued Mrs. Nelson, her voice quivering with indignation, her five rings and Bob Dole wristwatch glinting in the sun. "To just start kicking him -- kicking him when he's down. They're just kicking him because, in their estimation, he's not going to win this."
The sentiments shared here by Mrs. Nelson -- "I'm his big sister; you've got to stand up for him" -- offer a glimpse at the anxiety of this central Kansas town of 4,800. Like Bob Dole, Russell holds its emotions close. But it is clear that the people here are suffering with Mr. Dole in these last, difficult days of his candidacy, almost as if Russell itself were running for President.
In the streets and stores and homes and restaurants of Russell, there is some talk of Harry S. Truman in 1948 and much denunciation of polls. But there is mostly scorn for Mr. Clinton and the news media, contempt for voters who don't see Mr. Dole the way his friends and neighbors do and anguish for Mr. Dole himself.
"They're just like me," said G. B. (Bub) Dawson, 79, whose father owned the drugstore where Mr. Dole worked as a boy, speaking of the people of Russell. "They can't understand the polls. They can't understand why Whitewater and sexual harassment and all that stuff---- All that should have annihilated the man. Nothing seems to stick to him."
Until these past few weeks, this year has, by everyone's account, been a thrilling one for Russell, with Presidential campaign visits, a welcome stream of tourists off Interstate 70 and the attention paid by reporters from all over the world. "Everybody's been on television," Mr. Dawson said.
Now, though, there is an elegaic feeling that all this attention might be about to end. It is poignant that a town that was ready to measure the drapes for a Dole Presidential library -- and to join Abilene, Kan., and Independence, Mo., on the lucrative Midwestern Presidential tourist circuit -- is talking about lowering its sights. "There's no reason why we can't have just a Dole library," said the Mayor of Russell, Neal Farmer. "A Presidential library would be super. But there's nothing wrong with a Dole library."
When Mr. Dole quit the Senate last year, he declared that he had "nowhere to go but the White House or home." But even as his friends here reach out to cushion him if he should fall, no one in Russell really believes that Mr. Dole would return to a town where the only place open on Main Street last Friday night was a pool hall named "Something to Do." And while most people hope that he will squeeze in a last visit before Election Day (he plans to), no one is surprised to hear that he expects to return to Washington for election night, before heading that evening to his Florida condominium.
In truth, the relationship between Mr. Dole and Russell has always been more complex than presented by his campaign. On one visit here this year, Mr. Dole refused to use a teleprompter, saying the use of such a Washington affectation would be disrespectful. "I couldn't do that to the people of Russell," he told a frustrated adviser. Still, Mr. Dole, unlike Mr. Clinton four years ago, placed his headquarters in Washington, rather than in his home state, which some critics felt made the campaign too captive to the rhythm of the Washington establishment.
But if Mr. Dole has sought to turn the small-town image of Russell to his political advantage, Russell has also done well because of its association with Mr. Dole. "I've probably had more foreign and out-of-state visitors in the last six months than I've had the last 20 years," Mayor Farmer said.
As what could well be Mr. Dole's last campaign comes to an end, the analysis offered by Mr. Dole's former neighbors for his difficulties is not that much different from that offered on the Sunday morning talk shows: He is seen as too old, too unpolished -- the wrong man running at the wrong time. Even here, people generally say they are better off then they were four years ago. "I think that most people feel -- even Republicans -- that Clinton has done a reasonably good job," said Allan D. Evans, the publisher and general manager of The Russell Daily News.
Still, all of that has combined to produce a sense of evident frustration at Mr. Dole's plight. A small notice taped to the counter in Mr. Dole's headquarters -- it is a former pool hall -- asks if it makes sense for a "veteran to vote for a draft dodger; a woman to vote for a womanizer; or an honest person to vote for a chronic liar." And at Chuckie's beauty saloon, Chuckie Schmidt, the owner, said she hoped that "our society kind of sees the light and comes through." She added, "We need some honesty and integrity back in the White House."
Whatever the outcome, Mrs. Nelson, Mr. Dole's sister, is intent on making sure that the man everyone here calls Bob (never Senator) emerges from this experience with his reputation intact. She is a bit of a renegade who stealthily agreed to an interview even after her sister, Norma Jean Steele, called before dawn, on the instructions of skittish campaign officials in Washington, to cancel an appointment for both sisters with a reporter. "I want to get this off my chest," Mrs. Nelson said, in explaining why she was crossing her sister.
"This idea that he's going to go down in disgrace and be a nobody," she said with exasperation. "He'll go down as an elder statesman.
"Bob Dole will take this right on the chin and go on. It's sad for the country. I'd like to be a winner's sister. I don't think I'll be, but whatever I'll be, I'll be his sister, that's for sure. Win or lose, we'll make this just fine."

GRAPHIC: Photos: At the Republican headquarters on Saturday in Bob Dole's hometown of Russell, Kan., G. B. (Bub) Dawson, at left, and Russ Townsley pondered the political fortunes of their longtime friend. The townspeople who know Mr. Dole "can't understand the polls," Mr. Dawson said; Gloria Nelson, Bob Dole's eldest sister, and other longtime Dole fans in his hometown are anxious about what the election will mean for him. "I'm his big sister," Mrs. Nelson said. "You've got to stand up for him." (Photographs by Charlie Riedel for The New York Times)

LENGTH: 730 words

HEADLINE: Bosnia Is Strewn With Danger as Resistance and Blunders Delay Mine Clearance

BYLINE: By MIKE O'CONNOR 

DATELINE: SARAJEVO, Bosnia and Herzegovina

 BODY:
When the West turned to the question of helping Bosnia's people reclaim their fields, their homes and their schools after three and a half years of war, it became clear that the most immediate obstacle was the widespread presence of land mines.
But after more than 10 months of acknowledged bumbling by international groups and what some aid officials say is obstruction by the Bosnian Government, mine clearing has barely begun. With snow and frozen ground only weeks away, progress will not be possible in most of the country until next spring. 
 International officials say that the Bosnian Government has delayed several programs by attempting to siphon off money in the form of taxes and that some Bosnian leaders are preparing for another war and do not want the mines removed.
"This is a humanitarian mission to help the people of Bosnia, but the Government doesn't care," said Richard Sklar, President Clinton's envoy for reconstruction programs in Bosnia. "The Government's first interest is to make money."
Maj. David Pardy, the NATO officer overseeing the limited mine removal being carried out by Bosnia's three armies, said many officers think the war could resume.
"They want to keep defensive walls around themselves," he said. "They are in no hurry to lift these mines up."
A leader of Bosnia's governing party, Ismet Grbo, agreed that delays in mine clearing were inexcusable, but he said they were caused by the countries donating the money.
Foreign officials say Serbian and Croatian authorities have cooperated, although they acknowledge delays in the Croatian and Serbian areas of Bosnia because of poor organization in international efforts.
Early this year, international officials say, the Bosnian Government insisted that mine clearing be done by Bosnian companies under Government control. "They thought a bag of money was going to be put on a bureaucrat's desk and no one would watch how it was spent," said George Focsaneanu, the United Nations official in charge of coordinating mine clearing.
Instead, with much delay, the United States, Norway and the European Union all devised their own programs. The World Bank is also lending the Government about $8 million to hire Bosnian contractors. But the total cost of international programs could reach $40 million next year, about one-third of that for salaries to the Bosnian workers on which the Government wants to collect a tax of 53 percent. Mr. Sklar, the American official, said of the tax: "We are not going to put up with that. I think the U.S. should be out of this business by the end of November if we don't have a satisfactory program."
Mines and unexploded shells are scattered across the country. Mr. Focsaneanu, the United Nations expert, said perhaps 40 percent of the minefields had not been located. "I wouldn't walk off a paved surface anywhere in this country," he said.
The United Nations reported 61 injuries and deaths caused by mines in Sarajevo alone in the last two months.
The effort to clear mines had a troubled start in January. The European Union bought more than $700,000 of equipment and gave it to NATO forces. But NATO found the gear unsuitable, said Tom Walker, a Union spokesman.
After waiting a further nine months, the Europeans decided to spend $7.4 million to train and equip 350 Bosnian civilians and then pay them to remove mines. But the training has not begun.
A similar program, sponsored by the State Department, has recently begun to train workers. They are to be sent to warmer, though less populated areas of Bosnia to work this winter. The American program was delayed for months when the Bosnian Government refused to provide buildings they had promised for offices and training centers, officials said.
According to American officials, the Bosnian Government also said it could find no candidates for the training, even though there are more than 100,000 demobilized soldiers who are without work.
"The international community has acted disgracefully, all of us," Mr. Sklar said. "We dawdled."
In September, Mr. Focsaneanu arrived as a coordinator.
"Until three weeks ago we didn't have a coordinated plan," he said, adding that there were now not enough donors.
He said that the four programs must be able to train at least 2,000 people by April if they are to make notable progress next year.

HEADLINE: POLITICS: NATIONAL SECURITY;
Clinton as a Military Leader: Tough On-the-Job Training

SERIES: THE CLINTON RECORD: Defense

BYLINE: By TIM WEINER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 27

 BODY:
In his first term as President, Bill Clinton, who came to power having commanded nothing bigger than the Arkansas National Guard, has confronted national security problems that would have bedeviled a battle-hardened cold warrior.
His spy service discovered that it had been betrayed from within by a traitor for years. His Pentagon was reluctant to follow his orders to fight small battles in faraway places for political goals. And President George Bush had left behind political land mines in places like Bosnia, Haiti, Iraq, North Korea and Somalia. 
 Mr. Clinton groped for clear-cut paths through the mine fields, reversing course, searching for direction. As a commander of soldiers and spies, he was far less experienced than his predecessor.
Today he can rightly claim to have used American military force, without losing a life in battle, to save lives in Bosnia and Haiti. He proudly points to the fact that Moscow's nuclear missiles are no longer aimed at the United States. But the President and his national security team had a lot to learn when they took power, as interviews with present and past Clinton Administration officials suggest. In 1993 and 1994, they learned by making mistakes.
"The whole national security apparatus of the President was in terrible disarray," said Philip Zelikow, a National Security Council staff member under President Bush. "There was poor central direction from the White House and a weak N.S.C. staff -- the worst since the first Reagan Administration. They didn't know what they didn't know."
Clearly, Mr. Clinton did not inspire confidence among the generals and admirals and C.I.A. barons who ran the national security bureaucracy. Mr. Bush had seen combat, as has Bob Dole, but the first President with an anti-war past had a hard time taking command. It did not help when young White House aides tried ordering old soldiers around.
"It was terrible," said a high-ranking Pentagon official. "I had the feeling the White House wanted the Department of Defense to cease to be a part of the United States Government. We had terrible relations with the White House. They did not understand national defense."
The men Mr. Clinton chose to run the military and intelligence establishments were forced to resign. And then, embarrassingly, the men the President chose to replace them bowed out.
Mr. Clinton's first Defense Secretary, the late Les Aspin, was an absent-minded professor who had appalling relationships with the military. He was eased out, but the man nominated to succeed him, Bobby Ray Inman, a retired admiral, gave a weirdly rambling speech declining the honor. The Director of Central Intelligence, R. James Woolsey, ran into a buzz saw: a mole inside the C.I.A. had sold its deepest secrets to Moscow. After Mr. Woolsey resigned, an excruciatingly long search produced a retired Air Force general, Michael P. C. Carns, who withdrew with mumbled explanations about the immigration status of his Filipino house boy.
But things began changing for the better two years ago. After months of hand-wringing, Mr. Clinton sent troops to Haiti to restore a democratically elected president ousted by a violent military junta. The action seemed to give him courage. Nor was it the only success. The national security team has worked hard to reduce the nuclear threat to the United States from Russia and the newly independent states of the former Soviet Union. It persuaded North Korea to stop a nuclear-weapons program. Working with overseas allies, the C.I.A. helped take apart the Cali cocaine cartel and helped catch the prime suspect in the World Trade Center bombings.
The man who took over the C.I.A., John M. Deutch, is visibly trying to improve the ways in which the agency gathers and analyzes secret information. The man who took over the Pentagon, William Perry, made the White House make decisions about national security, and either back them up with force or handle them diplomatically.
"A strong defense secretary -- the strongest in a generation," said Mr. Zelikow. "Bill Perry is a man with deep experience, strong convictions, intellectual horsepower. Perry ran the Pentagon competently, and the rest of the team learned from their mistakes."
In the battle for the White House, Mr. Clinton has moved to protect his right flank on national security issues, taking up positions close to or indistinguishable from those held by Republicans. He kept weapons production lines open to save jobs, especially in California, the biggest electoral state. This year, after promising to veto an $11 billion spending increase passed by Congressional Republicans, a sum over and above what the Pentagon said it needed, Mr. Clinton signed it into law. Today his five-year military budget differs from Republican plans by about 1 percent. As on other issues -- social, economic and political -- he has become something of a Replicrat, or a Duplican, to use terms coined by the novelist Norman Rush.
One of the real differences between Mr. Dole and Mr. Clinton is over the immediate need for a system that could theoretically shoot down enemy missiles over the United States. Mr. Clinton is continuing research on a ballistic missile defense system; Mr. Dole promises to deploy one. But the technology remains uncertain and the cost could run to $60 billion or more.
The question of Mr. Clinton's leadership in national security, often raised by Mr. Dole, will persist if he is re-elected and a new national security team comes off the bench to replace the exhausted first string. That bench is not very deep. And just as he inherited crises from his predecessor, Mr. Clinton could bequeath himself a nest of booby traps, a backlog of potential crises that he has handled gingerly but in no way defused.
"The war in Bosnia could start again," said William E. Odom, the National Security Agency director under President Reagan. "Korea could blow up. Trying to contain both Iran and Iraq can't last. The Middle East peace process could unravel. These problems are festering. They've been able to put them off with tactical measures. But the second Administration will likely be shot through with disasters of their own making."

Fighting
Finding a Job For the Troops
Every cold-war President with a serious problem in a faraway place could call on the C.I.A. to secretly ship weapons to a rebel leader, pay off a foreign potentate or rent a guerrilla group. Or he could send in the Marines.
That is not so easy for the first post-cold-war President. Without an enemy equal to the United States, Mr. Clinton has had to use power in ways that are not war.
In Somalia, where President Bush had committed American forces to provide famine relief, the troops became caught in the middle of tribal warfare and 18 Army Rangers were killed in October 1993 while trying to track down a leader of one faction.
And he has used covert action sparingly. His only large-scale covert action was a $20 million-a-year effort to undermine Saddam Hussein, without success. He has used soldiers to solve political problems, like enforcing the cease-fire in the Balkans. But that is not what most soldiers are trained to do -- nor what some their commanders want to do.
"There are times when only America can make the difference between war and peace, between freedom and repression, between hope and fear," Mr. Clinton said on Tuesday in Detroit, in his first and only foreign-policy speech since the Democratic convention in August. "We cannot and should not try to be the world's policeman; but where our interests and values are clearly at stake and where we can make a difference, we must act and lead.
"We must lead in two ways. First, by meeting the immediate challenges to our interests from rogue regimes, from sudden explosions of ethnic, racial and religious and tribal hatreds from short-term crises, and second, by making long-term investments in security, prosperity, peace and freedom that can prevent these problems from arising in the first place."
It was as close to making a statement approaching a Clinton Doctrine as he has ever come, and it was nearly four years in the making. On Tuesday, referring to the deployment of troops in Bosnia and Haiti, Mr. Clinton said he was "able to succeed because, first, we accepted the responsibility to lead."
But learning to lead took time. The President and his national security aides seemed thunderstruck at first by the idea of using military power. They deferred to Gen. Colin L. Powell, chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff for nine months under President Clinton, whose doctrine called for overwhelming force, or no force at all. "In Bosnia and Haiti, the only sensible policy was limited force for limited ends," said James Chace, editor of World Policy Journal, an international-affairs quarterly. "This caused a partial paralysis."
Joseph Nye, dean of the Kennedy School of Government, who served the Clinton Administration in military and intelligence posts, said: "After that very difficult first year, they took their lumps and went ahead to an understanding of the sensible use of force. After a rocky start, they've been effective in trying to steer a path between becoming the world's policeman and realizing that America is the only country with the capability to project power."
It is still an awkward fit between the Pentagon and a President some officers regard as a draft-dodger. To win acceptance as Commander in Chief, Mr. Clinton has been deferential to the military. To win the troops' trust, he has spent billions improving military pay, housing and day care. To win over the brass, he has changed his mind on some issues, spending billions on weaponry he once said the nation did not need and backing off his pledge to let homosexuals serve openly in the military.
And more than any President in memory, he has used his Defense Secretary as a gunboat diplomat. While Haiti and Bosnia grabbed the headlines, Mr. Perry and the more experienced members of Mr. Clinton's national security team were trying to tie up one of the messiest loose ends of the cold war: the control of nuclear weapons in the chaos of the dismantled Communist empire.
In 1994, after an excruciating internal debate, which included plans to re-enforce American troops in South Korea -- a move some feared would start a second Korean War -- they offered North Korea economic assistance in exchange for an end to a suspected nuclear-weapons program and a lessening of tensions in the Korean peninsula.
"There was a real prospect of war in a country where we had 37,000 soldiers," said Ashton B. Carter, who resigned last month as the assistant Secretary of Defense for international security policy. "We were head to head with North Korea. They had a million men under arms and a totally unpredictable nature. But they actually signed up to what we wanted: an end to their program. And somewhat miraculously, it has stuck."
They enlisted 19 former Communist states in the Partnership for Peace, a kind of neo-NATO, and worked with them to dismantle more than $2 billion worth of weapons. "There is one piece of very good news and one piece of bad news here," said Graham Allison, who served as assistant Secretary of Defense for policy plans. "The good news is the elimination of nuclear arsenals in Ukraine and Khazakstan." The bad news, he said, is that 30,000 nuclear warheads and 70,000 lumps of highly enriched uranium and plutonium are under increasingly poor control in Russia.
While those warheads are no longer aimed at the United States, the new threat is that "a warhead or a softball-sized lump of highly enriched uranium gets loose and into hands of a terrorist group or rogue state," Mr. Allison said. "They fashion a crude device. And they do something horrible." That threat, he said, has increased since 1993.

Spying
The C.I.A. Rarely Gets Any Respect
This, above all, is why the United States has an intelligence agency: to guard against a nuclear Pearl Harbor. But under Mr. Clinton, few branches of the Government have got less respect than the Central Intelligence Agency.
Mr. Clinton rarely admitted his first intelligence chief, Mr. Woolsey, to his inner circle, possibly because Mr. Woolsey was often a bearer of bad tidings, and the President did not want to hear what he had to say. That is a kiss of death for the C.I.A., whose power flows from providing secrets to the President.
"Woolsey is a compulsive truth-teller with a contrarian impulse, and he thought the job was a license to tell the truth, the real story," Mr. Zelikow said. "He did this with the President. And it was unpopular." Mr. Woolsey, who has endorsed Bob Dole, declined to be interviewed.
Then came one of the greatest disasters in the agency's history. The case of Aldrich H. Ames, the mole for Moscow arrested in February 1994, showed that the C.I.A.'s covert operators had harbored a drunken misfit, ignored compelling evidence of treason and shielded that evidence from the Federal Bureau of Investigation and the agency's directors. Then the agency revealed last year that it had knowingly sent reports laced with disinformation, from double agents put in place by Mr. Ames's treachery, to the highest levels of the White House and the Pentagon.
Among those who received those reports was John Deutch, the former Deputy Secretary of Defense who unwillingly took over as Director of Central Intelligence in 1995. In dealing with the Ames case, with the agency's hidden ties to the Guatemalan military, with a botched economic-espionage operation in Paris and other demoralizing mix-ups, Mr. Deutch has meted out unprecedented discipline and set high standards of accountability for covert operations.
While acknowledging the past problems, he said in an interview that citizens and officials had "regained confidence" in the agency's clandestine operations. That opinion is not universally held.
Instead, some senior Administration officials, particularly at the State Department, seem to regard the C.I.A.'s covert-operations directorate as illegal, immoral or irrelevant. "The depth of disdain between State and C.I.A. is remarkable," said Loch Johnson, a former staff member of the Senate and House intelligence committees and author of "Secret Agencies: U.S. Intelligence in a Hostile World" (Yale University Press, 1996). "I know people in State who think C.I.A. is a greater enemy than Russia ever was, and that feeling is reciprocated."
Fewer than 50 top national security officials rely daily on the agency's spies for secret insights. Several of them, unwilling to be quoted by name, said that they were instead offered "high-class rumor" or low-grade facts. One Pentagon official said the C.I.A. had sent him a top-secret interview with a Russian colonel containing information he himself had obtained by talking to a Russian general. Another said he had been sent a grainy satellite photo of nuclear missiles in Ukraine being dismantled days after he had seen the event with his own eyes.
But there are signs of change. Under John Gannon, a new director appointed by Mr. Deutch, the agency's intelligence directorate, which analyzes secret and open information, improved its reports and its reputation by simply asking policy makers what they want to know, and trying to deliver. It did a good job sorting out rumor from fact in the Balkans -- no easy task. And some analysts say Mr. Gannon has fostered a healthy climate of intellectual debate. "It's a work in progress," Mr. Gannon said. "It's a bumper-car progression to a changed culture."
After the Ames case, there was a hue and cry to change that culture from top to bottom, to change the C.I.A.'s mission, to change its name, its very nature, or to eliminate it altogether. But all nations have spied for centuries. The call for change eventually died down. A high-level commission appointed by Mr. Clinton and Congress considered how best to deal with the agency, as did the Congressional intelligence committees and several think tanks. In the end, few of the ideas came to fruition. The danger for the C.I.A. was not that it would be abolished or overhauled. The danger was that the people it served, including the President, would ignore it.

Spending
The Peace Dividend Doesn't Materialize
The great issues of war and peace that give generals and station chiefs sleepless nights seem to matter less and less to most Americans as memories of the cold war fade. Ever since the Berlin Wall became a speed bump in 1989, polls and interviews suggest that people are far more interested in their pocketbooks, schools and neighborhoods than in America's role as the last great superpower. When it comes to national security, they care mainly about a few big questions.
Are American soldiers dying overseas? Not today. Are the chances of an all-out nuclear Armageddon rising? Few think so. Are foreign terrorists blowing up skyscrapers and jumbo jets? The threat remains; some say it is growing. Only one Pentagon issue hits the pocketbook: is the military spending too much money? People remember $10,000 wrenches and $2 billion bombers.
But if they expected Mr. Clinton, as a Democrat, to spend significantly less on the military than a Republican President would have, they have been disappointed. In 1992, the Pentagon's budget stood at $298 billion. Today it is $266 billion, smaller, but not quite as small as Mr. Clinton promised and barely less than President Bush projected.
Mr. Clinton's Pentagon has spent more than $1 trillion, about $30 million an hour, more than the rest of the world's top 10 armies combined. It provided no real peace dividend save the uneasy prevailing peace.

HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE DEMOCRAT;
CLINTON CAMPAIGN PUTS AN EMPHASIS ON FEMALE VOTERS

BYLINE: By ALISON MITCHELL 

DATELINE: SPRINGFIELD, Va., Oct. 27

 BODY:
Seeking to insure a strong turnout from a crucial voting bloc, President Clinton made a special appeal to women today by highlighting Government efforts to fight breast cancer and telling a rally audience in Virginia that the election "is not about party, it's about you."
The President's appearances at the White House and in this Washington suburb today capped a weekend of rallies and organizing by his campaign in 35 states to make sure that women -- the voters whom polls consistently show as central to Mr. Clinton's majority -- turn out to vote. 
 Standing on a high school field in Springfield this afternoon, a blue banner behind him proclaiming "Strengthening America's Families," Mr. Clinton stressed the health care, education and child-based themes that have been central to his campaign and that have a particular resonance with women.
Drawing a contrast between his proposals and those of the Republicans, he asked, "Do we really believe that we would be better off if their vision had prevailed over the past four years?
"Would we be better off if there were no Family and Medical Leave law?" Mr. Clinton asked, referring to a law that guarantees many workers 12 weeks of unpaid leave for family emergencies.
"Would we be better off if we cut student loans instead of increasing them? Would we be better off if we cut Head Start instead of increasing it? Would we be better off if we had not made those 300,000 loans to women business owners?" On cue, his several thousand listeners repeatedly shrieked back, "No."
Bob Dole, campaigning today in California, increased the sharpness of his attacks on the Administration's ethics and called its members a "group of elitists who have never done anything." [Page A15.]
The President's rally appearance in Virginia came hard on the heels of one at the White House where he emphasized breast cancer as a health care issue of particular interest to women.
Across the country, many Clinton campaign workers fanned out to mobilize women. In Arkansas, workers were sent out to seek those who have become the embodiment of this year's swing voters, the soccer moms, the mission being to hand out campaign fliers to mothers at 51 soccer fields. In Georgia and Pennsylvania this weekend, campaign volunteers descended on grocery stores and malls to encourage women to vote.
In tandem with these events, the Clinton campaign went on the air with its most direct appeal yet to mothers, a commercial featuring a baby girl and a voice saying: "Today you'll decide what she eats. You'll decide what she wears. On Nov. 5, you'll decide what kind of America she'll grow up in."
Mr. Clinton began the campaign day by emphasizing a women's health issue. Appearing at a Rose Garden ceremony before embarking on a three-day campaign swing, Mr. Clinton announced that he was shifting $30 million in Federal money into research on the genetic basis of breast cancer. The money had previously been earmarked for other biomedical and breast cancer research.
"Nothing is more devastating to a family's strength than when someone is diagnosed with a life-threatening disease like cancer," said Mr. Clinton, whose mother died of breast cancer. "I know about this from my own family experience, and nearly every family does."
Before he spoke, Mr. Clinton met in the Oval Office with four women who have survived breast cancer and a 9-year-old girl who has fought childhood leukemia. During the meeting he signed a yellow ribbon that symbolizes the nation's fight against breast cancer.
The day was the culmination of an effort by Mr. Clinton to gain back the votes of women who supported him in 1992 but who stayed away from the polls in 1994. The apathy of these women -- in large part young, lower income and less well educated -- contributed to the Republican sweep of Congress that year.
To win these women, Mr. Clinton has moved far beyond the issues of abortion rights and workplace equality that were long at the heart of the feminist agenda, and on to a wide range of matters of particular concern to working mothers trying to balance child-rearing with economic needs. "A lot of the women we're targeting are squeezed for time and for money," said Ann F. Lewis, Mr. Clinton's deputy campaign manager.
Mr. Clinton's emphasis on the Family and Medical Leave Act, for example, with its guarantee of 12 weeks of unpaid leave for a family medical emergency, is said by his strategists to carry particular resonance for women because they are more likely to become the care givers for sick children or elderly parents. Mr. Clinton's focus on Medicare and Medicaid, the Federal health insurance programs, appeals to women for the same reason, his aides say, as well as because women generally live longer than men.
And education, a Clinton staple, also is a prime concern of mothers. "It's the issue that suburban families rate the highest now," Ms. Lewis said. "It's how they make decisions about where they live, and it's also what women think is the most important thing that government does."
Mr. Clinton struck hard at the theme today in Virginia, a state no Democrat has made a priority since 1976, when it was the only Southern state that Jimmy Carter lost.
Without mentioning the Republicans directly, the President asked: "What is their education vision? Abolish the Department of Education." The crowd booed long and loudly. "No word about standards," Mr. Clinton said. "No word about accountability. No word about empowerment of teachers and principals and parents. No word about teaching children to read or hooking them up to the Internet."
A poll released by the Pew Research Center on Friday showed that among registered voters women now favor Mr. Clinton over Mr. Dole by 57 percent to 29 percent, while men support Mr. Clinton over Mr. Dole by 45 percent to 39 percent. The poll was based on a national survey of 1,928 adults, with an error margin of plus or minus two percentage points over all and of three percentage points for results on registered voters.
Mr. Clinton's aides said the President would finish the last full week of the campaign with speeches summing up his argument for a second term, and many of them -- a speech on education planned for Tuesday, a speech Wednesday on economic opportunity especially for women -- are clearly intended to continue the effort to mobilize female voters.

HEADLINE: METRO DIGEST

 BODY:

NEW YORK
Yankee Fans Celebrate . . .
They called it historic, majestic, sublime. They compared it to Christmas morning as a kid or the best birthday ever. They said it was like a wedding or winning the lottery. New Yorkers were gleefully lost among the metaphors yesterday, but any way you looked at it, the New York Yankees were the toast of the town -- and it was strong stuff, like the fumes of that first whisky. [Page A1.]
 
. . . but a Hangover Looms
This may seem like crepe-hanging, what with the Yankee celebrations still in mid-swing, but it is not too early to think about the billion-dollar hangover that may follow. A billion dollars, in the unlikely case that you forgot, is the price of a new stadium proposed for Manhattan's West Side. NYC by Clyde Haberman. [B1.]
 
Tensions at SUNY-Binghamton
Anthony J. Benardello is a 37-year-old former Army Ranger who opposes affirmative action and calls advocates of multiculturalism "white haters" who want "white oppression taught from a leftist perspective." Rush Limbaugh, he says, is one of his heroes. But Mr. Benardello is not a radio talk show host; he is president of the Student Association at the State University of New York at Binghamton. Students and administrators say he is at the center of the tensions over race relations and affirmative action that led to a weeklong sit-in. [B1.]
 
Great Lawn to Get a Facelift
If Central Park's Great Lawn were in a backyard somewhere, the owner might long ago have erected a high fence around it, or perhaps paved it and painted it green. Its surface had become pounded hard as asphalt, pocked with sinkholes and almost totally denuded of grass. Since the 1970's, park officials concede, it has received essentially no care. But starting this morning, a Central Park Conservancy team promises to transform the tattered moonscape into that ultimate suburban status symbol, the perfect lawn. [B1.]
 
The Future of Apple Orchards
Washington State is harvesting what is likely to be the state's largest apple crop, using advanced technology and economies of scale that may make the family-run orchard a thing of the past there as well as in other regions like New York, the nation's No. 2 apple state. "There isn't an apple-growing region in the world that isn't affected by how things are done here," a Washington extension agent said. National report. [A13.]
 
A healthy baby girl only a few hours old was found in a cardboard box outside a Brooklyn apartment building, the police said. [B3.]
 
REGION
 
More Transit Discounts
At the same time that New York City's transit system begins offering its first-ever volume discounts, a new discount will also be given to Long Island Rail Road and Metro-North commuters who are also regular users of the city's subways and buses, transit officials said. The discount, to be offered only on monthly commuter passes sold by mail, will be made possible in part by an innovation transit planners have talked about for decades: a unified regional transit pass. [B1.]
 
NEW JERSEY
 
Zimmer Draws Back From Dole
Representative Richard A. Zimmer, the Republican candidate for the United States Senate, distanced himself from the flagging Presidential candidacy of Bob Dole, joining a national trend in which Republican candidates, trying to limit the damage from an anticipated Dole loss, are acknowledging that it is O.K. to split the ticket. [B4.]
 
Mrs. Clinton Helps Torricelli
Hillary Rodham Clinton came to southern New Jersey, with its large number of undecided voters and suburban women, the most sought-after group this election year, to urge women to vote for her husband and for Representative Robert G. Torricelli, the Democratic candidate for the United States Senate. [B4.]
 
Bayonne Faces a Big Choice
Bayonne's Military Ocean Terminal, 437 acres of prime waterfront jutting into Upper New York Bay, is a hot property. And deciding what to do with the terminal, Bayonne's largest employer, after it closes in 1999 might be the most important choice the well-preserved but unspectacular city of 61,000 has ever faced. One applicant is looking for a good place to dock a quarter-mile-long, 21-story-high, 6,200-passenger cruise ship of tomorrow, or perhaps the day after tomorrow. [B5.]
 
CONNECTICUT
 
Kelly's Court Role: Mint Man
In the courthouse where he is being tried on rape charges, Alex Kelly is in charge of the mints. He ceremoniously offers mints from a fresh roll to his two lawyers as they arrive at the defense table each morning. Next, he offers mints to relatives in the front rows. The offering of the mints is usually Mr. Kelly's last official act at the defense table. After that, he usually sits there. The lawyers seldom confer with him, and he seldom speaks with them. Reporter's notebook. [B6.]

HEADLINE: Fight Europe Rightists With Even More Trade

 BODY:
To the Editor:
An Oct. 24 editorial points out a nationalistic, far-right backlash in Europe that is an unintended consequence of the European Union's rush toward a grand federalism that is lacking in grassroots support. 
 Its success has been based on trade liberalization that has improved the economies of member states. While the past decade has seen the rise of a European community spirit among the various nationalities, European Union bureaucrats have been slow to realize that these people are still separate nations in many ways besides language.
The nationalist backlash can range from benign like Britain's Euroskeptics, who oppose more power flowing to the European Union, to the dangerous growing power of the far right in France and Austria that you note in your editorial.
However, Euroskeptics who merely react against those who push for what they believe to be a premature federalism could point instead to a more positive but mundane policy alternative that has been the European Union's success: yet more trade liberalization within the member countries. Supporters of the Maastricht treaty on European unity would agree.
 
GARY L. DARE
Evanston, Ill., Oct. 24, 1996
