HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE VOTERS;
Ohio County Reluctantly Tilts Toward Clinton

SERIES: AN AMERICAN PLACE: Rating the Candidates

BYLINE: By MICHAEL WINERIP 

DATELINE: CANTON, Ohio, Oct. 27

 BODY:
In this place that votes as America does for President, Bill Clinton is winning the Battle of the Lesser Evils, stealing away Republican support that Bob Dole must have and attracting many of the independents it takes to carry a swing county.
"Clinton's the lesser of three evils," said Ron Patterson, 32, a computer technician, explaining why he was switching his vote from Ross Perot in 1992 to Mr. Clinton this time. 
 "The best of two bad choices," said Dave Klingensmith, a corporate lawyer and registered Republican who plans to vote for Mr. Clinton after supporting George Bush and Ronald Reagan in past elections. "I haven't been real happy with the Dole campaign -- too negative. I associate Dole with Gingrich and the things they were pushing."
"The lesser evil," said Sherry Chaney, a nurse and registered Republican who intends to vote for Mr. Clinton, after supporting Mr. Bush in 1992. She said she felt Mr. Clinton would better protect Medicare and did not believe Mr. Dole's promise of a 15 percent tax cut. She also said she worried about Mr. Dole's age and thought he had been campaigning like a mudslinger. "It's disgusting me," she said.
The one issue that seems to give Mr. Dole the upper hand in a new poll of this bellwether county, the character question, is not resonating strongly enough. Bernie Crilley, 34, a union steelworker at the Timken Company, voted for Mr. Perot in 1992, when he was laid off. But now, he said, "we're working good." Asked why he was voting for Mr. Clinton, he said: "I don't like Clinton's abortion issues; I don't care much for Clinton's morals. But like I said, the economy's been good, and that's what feeds my family."
President Clinton enjoys a sizable lead in the new poll here, with 53 percent of likely voters saying they prefer him, compared with 30 percent for Mr. Dole and 11 percent for Mr. Perot. The poll of 678 registered voters living here in Stark County, which was done by the Ohio State University survey center and The New York Times, was conducted from Oct. 19 to Oct. 24 and has a margin of sampling error of plus or minus four percentage points. Likely voters were defined as registered voters who said they would vote in November.
Mr. Clinton's lead here is similar to the results being reported in recent days in national polls by The New York Times and CBS News (22 points) and CNN/USA Today/Gallup (20 points). The most recent national ABC News poll has Mr. Clinton ahead by 15 points.
While not even Democrats here expect the President to win by a 23-point margin in Stark County, the reaction from the two local Presidential campaign chairmen to the poll results is indicative of the problems Mr. Dole is facing.
Asked about the poll numbers, John Ferrero Jr., the Clinton chairman, said, "That's what I thought it would be." And Janet Weir Creighton, the Dole chairwoman and county auditor, said, "I'm not shocked."
The last time a Presidential candidate carried Stark County by a comparable margin was 1984, when Mr. Reagan, an incumbent President at a time when the economy was on the upswing, collected 60 percent of the vote here. That year, Stark County almost exactly mirrored the national electorate, which gave Mr. Reagan 59 percent of the vote.
Stark County has voted as the country has for President every election except one in the last 30 years. In 1976, it supported Gerald Ford by a narrow margin over the winner, Jimmy Carter.
Several responses in the Stark poll work to Mr. Clinton's advantage. About 73 percent here called the national economy "very good" or "fairly good." Sixty-two percent approved of the way Mr. Clinton has been handling his job as President, and just 13 percent said they were worse off than four years ago.
Unemployment here is 4 to 5 percent, about half what it was in 1992.
Fifty percent of those in the survey gave Mr. Clinton a favorable rating, and 31 percent said their view was unfavorable. In contrast, only 26 percent said their opinion of Mr. Dole was favorable; 45 percent said it was unfavorable. Making matters worse for Mr. Dole, the man he has been so often associated with this election, Speaker Newt Gingrich, was given a favorable rating here by just 14 percent, while 48 percent viewed him unfavorably. Indeed, 55 percent gave the Congressional Republicans most of the blame for the temporary shutdown of the Government last fall and winter; 23 percent said the President was more to blame.
If the results here are not good for Mr. Dole, the poll indicates there still is opportunity for gain by Nov. 5. Although only 6 percent said they were undecided, 19 percent said they "might change" their allegiance by Election Day.
The poll was completed 12 days before the election, when a lot of people still had not turned their full attention to the campaign. While 84 percent of Democrats surveyed said they were voting for Mr. Clinton, only 63 percent of Republicans said they were voting for Mr. Dole, and it seems likely that some of those Republicans will come home to their party before Election Day.
During the six-day period in which the Stark poll was conducted, Mr. Dole scored worst in the earliest days, closest to the final Presidential debate on Oct. 16, trailing by as much as 30 points. He did better on days toward the end of the survey period, furthest from the debates, getting as close as 13 points behind one day.
Since the vast majority of Stark County residents polled who watched the debates felt the President won them -- 70 percent said Mr. Clinton; 11 percent, Mr. Dole -- the more time that passes, the more the debates' demoralizing effect on Republicans could ease.
In recent days Mr. Dole has made some gains in the Ohio Poll, the statewide survey done by the University of Cincinnati. A week ago he was trailing by 14 points, but he is now 9 behind. In 1992, Ohio voted for Mr. Clinton 40 to 38 percent, and he carried Stark County by 40 to 35 percent, almost exactly his national spread.
Low turnout helps Republicans, who tend to be more faithful voters than Democrats, and there are several factors that point to a relatively poor turnout here this time.
Mr. Ferrero, the Clinton chairman, said he worried that the President's lead in the polls might lull Democrats. And Ms. Creighton, the Dole chairwoman, who has a reputation as one of Stark's savviest campaigners, said, "I'm personally hoping it's a horrible voting day, that the weather is bad and people will stay home, because I know Republicans will come out to vote."
Asked if she believed Mr. Dole could win, she said: "You never give up. Anything can change in the last 10 days."
Doug Lane, the morning host on WHBC radio in Canton and a member of the Republican executive committee in the county, said he believed Mr. Dole could still succeed because so many people are bored with this election. "In 1992, I was getting calls on the show all the time from Clinton people, Bush people, Perot people, all worked up," he said. "I don't have any of that this time."
Stark's board of elections director, Jeff Matthews, is predicting a turnout of 72 percent, which would be the lowest figure in recent memory for a Presidential year. In 1992 the turnout was 81 percent.
If Mr. Dole is to gain ground, he must capture an awful lot of Martha Hookways fast. Mrs. Hookway, who spoke in a follow-up interview after being in the poll, is a floral delivery driver. She voted for Mr. Clinton in 1992 and would vote that way again if the election were today. But she may change her mind.
"As far as moral things, I'm conservative," she said. "I don't believe in abortion, I feel Dole is the more decent man, and so in that respect, I'm more Republican -- my heart says, 'Dole.' But we're just a working-class family, and I feel Democrats are more for working-class families. I'm so torn."
Only 32 percent of those polled said Mr. Clinton had more integrity than most people in public life; 58 percent said he did not. Forty percent said Mr. Dole had more integrity, while 43 percent said he did not.
Mrs. Hookway also worries about taxes and wonders if Mr. Clinton would really raise them and Mr. Dole would really cut them. "I really need to know which is the truth," she said.
Most of those in the survey do not trust Mr. Dole's pledge of a 15 percent tax cut -- 27 percent said they believed he would carry out the pledge, 57 percent said they did not.
It is plain from the poll that Mr. Clinton has succeeded in recapturing issues that the Republicans have had the upper hand on for years. The crime issue hurt Michael Dukakis badly in 1988, the year of the Willie Horton advertisement. Yet this time in Stark, 48 percent said they believed Mr. Clinton would do a better job fighting crime, while only 28 percent said Mr. Dole would do better.
Mr. Klingensmith, the corporate lawyer and registered Republican, said that what he liked about President Clinton was that he is "friendlier, livelier, more personable."
"He seems like one of the people and not so much the guy who sits in the Oval Office," he said. "Reminds me of Reagan."
Living midway between Democratic Cleveland and Republican Columbus, the citizens of Canton, Ohio, and surrounding Stark County have a history of voting for the winning Presidential candidate. This is the 33d article in a yearlong series that seeks to explore the forces shaping the political debate at the grass roots. (Previous articles are available to computer users on The New York Times on the Web, at http://www.nytimes.com/specials/canton.)

HEADLINE: Peter Hoguet, 82, Lawyer in Cold War Case, Dies

BYLINE: By ERIC PACE 

 BODY:
Peter W. Hoguet, a lawyer, economist and investment banker who figured in a cold war incident -- the defection of Oksana S. Kosenkina, a Soviet schoolteacher who jumped from a window of the Soviet Consulate in New York City in 1948 -- died on Tuesday at his home on the Upper East Side of Manhattan. He was 82.
The cause was apparently heart failure, his daughter, Denise Hoguet, said. 
 Mr. Hoguet (pronounced ho-GAY) acted as a lawyer with an anti-Soviet organization, when, in August 1948, he submitted documents to Justice Samuel Dickstein of State Supreme Court contending that Mrs. Kosenkina, who had taught children of Soviet officials in the United States, was being held in the consulate, then at 7 East 61st Street, against her will. She had been ordered to return to the Soviet Union, but wanted to become a refugee from her homeland.
Justice Dickstein ordered the Soviet Consul General in New York, Jacob M. Lomakin, to appear in court the net morning, Aug. 12, and to bring with him "the body of Oksana Stepanovna Kosenkina by you detained and imprisoned, as it is said."
Mr. Hoguet served the order on Mr. Lomakin, but the Soviet Consul defied it, and Justice Dickstein put off further action to provide time for consultations about the matter.
The author Fulton Oursler wrote in 1949 that on Aug. 12, Mrs. Kosenkina, who was being held in a third-floor room in the consulate, knew that at midnight that night, she was to be put on board a ship bound for the Soviet Union.
As Aug. 12 went on, he wrote, she heard a radio broadcast about the legal activity on her behalf. "Now she knew why crowds filled the street below," he reported. "She was not abandoned. Somebody did care! All those people cared! She turned resolutely to the open window. She jumped."
Mrs. Kosenkina, then 52, survived the fall, was granted asylum in the United States, became a United States citizen in 1954 and died in 1960.
Mr. Hoguet was born in Manhattan, attended Portsmouth Abbey School in Portsmouth, R.I., and graduated in 1937 from Harvard College and in 1947 from New York University's law school.
He served in the Navy and the Office of Strategic Services in World War II and was a Washington-based Marshall Plan administrator in 1949 and 1950.
He went on to be vice president and then president of the Econometric Institute, a New York-based economic forecasting organization, from 1956 to 1961. He was a co-author of a textbook, "Business and Economic Forecasting" (1961).
His work in investment banking included being associated with Lehman Brothers for a time later in the 1960's and serving as president of Hoguet Associates, a firm specializing in mergers and acquisitions, in later years until he retired in 1978.
Long active in Republican politics, he was briefly a candidate for the Republican nomination for Congress from the 18th Congressional District in Manhattan in 1948 but withdrew from contention. He was also an East Side district leader in Manhattan and was the Republican candidate in 1968 for the State Assembly from the 64th District in Manhattan but was defeated.
In addition to his daughter, of Manhattan, Mr. Hoguet is survived by his wife of 37 years, the former Sheila Zehntner; a son, Mark, of Manhattan, and two sisters, Eleanor de Give of Southampton, L.I., and Marie Khatami of Manhattan.

CORRECTION-DATE: October 30, 1996, Wednesday

 CORRECTION:
An obituary on Monday about Peter W. Hoguet, a lawyer, economist and investment banker who was involved in the defection of a Soviet schoolteacher from the Soviet Consulate in Manhattan in 1948, misspelled the teacher's surname in some editions. She was Oksana S. Kosenkina, not Kasenkina.

HEADLINE: Russian Rockets Get Lift in U.S. From Cautious and Clever Design

BYLINE: By WILLIAM J. BROAD 

 BODY:
THE Russians lost the cold war, but they are starting to win the international race to fire hundreds of new satellites and spacecraft into the heavens, challenging Western rocket scientists and companies for launching jobs worth billions of dollars.
The rise of Russia as a commercial space power is based on engines and vehicles that are simple, reliable and cheap yet often fly circles around their Western counterparts. Eager for an edge, many American companies are adopting the foreign gear and forming East-West alliances meant to exploit tons of thundering Russian metal. 
 "They put more into it," Charles P. Vick, an expert on the Russian space program at the Federation of American Scientists, a private group in Washington, said of the Russians. "They made so many more varieties."
Dr. John H. Gibbons, the White House science adviser, who closely monitors the gear and has quietly encouraged its use in the West, called the Russian work "very ingenious."
During the cold war Moscow perfected possibly 10 times as many kinds of liquid-fuel rocket engines as Washington did, analysts estimate. It clearly tried harder and refined a host of rocketry tricks that ended up giving its engines leads in performance and reliability.
For instance, a single drive shaft often spins both fuel and oxidizer turbo pumps, in contrast to separate shafts and pumps on many Western engines. The step limited the number of moving parts and reduced the points of potential failure. Pressing the innovation home, the Russians often use one of these novel pumps to feed up to four combustion chambers.
Such advances are now starting to pay commercial dividends as private companies rush to launch new generations of communication satellites and as the Federal Government selects a new rocket to replace its aging fleets.
The Air Force is preparing to build the Evolved Expendable Launch Vehicle, a program worth at least $2 billion. Lockheed Martin, the aerospace giant, is a leading contender for the job, and its candidate rocket is powered by a Russian engine.
"What's going to dominate the field is what costs the least," Marc T. Constantine, director of business development at Aerojet, a top rocket engine manufacturer, said of rocketry's future. "Right now," he added in an interview, "the vehicles that are very cost effective are using Russian parts or Russian designs."
Aerojet, based in Sacramento, Calif., is marketing a Russian rocket engine, the NK-33, that at least two American rocket makers are vying to use for commercial launchings. One company would use it to power a rocket and the other would use it for a reusable shuttle-type space vehicle.
As companies race to exploit the Russian prizes, Western experts are probing, admiring and sometimes imitating the secrets.
Jan C. Monk, chief engineer for advanced space transportation at the Marshall Space Flight Center of the National Aeronautics and Space Administration, has repeatedly traveled overseas to inspect the Russian gear. In an interview, he noted how the Russians had mastered the stratagem of routing kerosene fuel around hot rocket nozzles to cool them, improving efficiency.
"We just never thought of that," Mr. Monk said, clearly impressed. "It was outside our way of thinking."
The Russians founded the space age with the flight of Sputnik in 1957, lofted the first man into orbit in 1961 and achieved a number of other manned firsts. Secret military programs, meanwhile, took advantage of the Soviet rocketry lead to perfect generations of intercontinental ballistic missiles meant to threaten the West.
The Soviet arsenal was built almost exclusively on liquid engines, whereas the military in the West eventually abandoned liquid engines for solid-fuel booster rockets, which were deemed easier to launch quickly. Over all, the Russian fleet of liquid fuel rockets was far more diverse than the West's and its rockets were far more powerful so they could hurl heavier and deadlier warheads.
All these factors combined to redouble Russian work on liquid fuel technology. Significantly, the Russians excelled without pushing technological limits -- the opposite of the West's approach.
Moscow often relied on kerosene, an inexpensive fuel that can work at room temperature. In contrast, Washington, when dealing with fluid fuels, used not only kerosene but pushed to perfect liquid hydrogen. This is a costly, high-energy propellant that must be refrigerated down to about 400 degrees Fahrenheit below zero, a temperature that can freeze, shatter or otherwise play havoc with moving parts.
In their hunt for high performance, the Russians avoided hydrogen but sought to perfect higher combustion-chamber pressures, achieving ones for kerosene twice as high as those in the West, boosting the fiery push.
They also managed to take advantage of every ounce of energy. The West tended to use open-cycle engines in which some fuel, after powering large pumps, was dumped overboard even though it was only half burned, forfeiting its potential kick. The Russians tapped such flows.
Their closed-cycle engines, often using a clever process known as staged combustion, begin the explosive burning of fuel in a preburner that powers pumps. That blistering hot residue then speeds into the main combustion chamber, jacking up heats and performance. Today in the West, only the main engines of the space shuttles use this advanced technique.
The strides by the Russians made their engines robust, cheap and powerful.
"Our evolutionary path was more difficult and gave increased performance," said Mr. Monk of the Marshall Space Flight Center. "But what the Russians did was very interesting."
Most importantly, Moscow kept working at it, hard. Apart from the space shuttle main engine, the United States developed no new power plants for liquid rockets after the 1960's.
As the cold war ended, the Russians tried but failed to sell their launching prowess abroad. Western states, fearful of the competition, blocked most efforts, especially as Moscow offered to launch foreign payloads into space from Russian spaceports. Washington, for instance, banned the launching of most American payloads atop Russian rockets.
In contrast, the current wave of activity is characterized by the export of Russian ideas, engines and vehicles to Western states, even as warming East-West relations have begun to ignite a run of commercial launchings from Russian soil.
The White House now says its aim is to aid the shaky Russian economy and lessen the temptation for Russia to sell rocket technologies to rogue states and to nations like India, which it worries might use advanced engines for military missiles.
An example of the current action is the NK-33, which powered Russia's secretive N-1 moon rocket. A colossus more than 34 stories tall, the N-1 had 30 of the NK-33 engines arranged in a first stage with an astounding 10 million pounds of fiery thrust, compared with 7.6 million pounds for the Saturn 5, America's moon rocket.
The mightiest launcher ever flown, the N-1 roared aloft four times from February 1969 to November 1972. All the flights ended in failure, but never because of engine trouble.
Today, the NK-33 engines of the N-1 rocket are being marketed by Aerojet. Boasting staged combustion and other advanced features, they nonetheless run on kerosene and are cheap to buy and run. Extremely sturdy, they are considered reusable. The engines have been successfully test fired in the United States, beginning last November.
Kistler Aerospace Corporation, based in Kirkland, Wash., is working to have the NK-33's power a fleet of new rockets whose engines would parachute back to earth for re-use. The company plans to start flight tests next year and to have a fleet of vehicles by 1999, mainly for launching the new generation of commercial satellites that fly in low orbits to relay messages between personal communication devices back on the ground.
Meanwhile, Kelly Space and Technology, based in San Bernardino, Calif., wants a single NK-33 to power a reusable winged spaceship that would blast into space off the back of a 747 jumbo jet. Motorola Inc., a big player in the communications industry, has signed a contract with Kelly worth up to $89 million to have the winged launcher carrry Iridium satellites into space, where Motorola wants 66 them orbiting and constantly within range of special cellular telephones.
A more powerful and modern Russian engine is the RD-170, a kerosene-fired brute whose design began in 1974. Starting in 1987, the engine helped power the Energia, a Russian rocket 198 feet tall that developed 7.8 million pounds of thrust. In 1988, the rocket sent the Soviet Union's space shuttle into orbit.
Now the focus of wide Western interest, the RD-170 uses staged combustion and high pressures and is so powerful that Pratt & Whitney, its Western vendor, is working with NPO Energomash, the Russian builder, to perfect a model with weaker thrust. Known as the RD-180, the new engine has two thrust chambers and nozzles instead of four, producing half the power.
"These engines provide at least a 10 percent performance advantage over similar U.S. engines," Ted Casner, Pratt & Whitney's program director for the RD-180, said in an interview.
"As knowledge of the Russian expertise becomes more public," he added, "everyone is trying to arrange the appropriate partner that has access" to Moscow's rocket technology.
Lockheed Martin Corporation, the world's largest military and aerospace contractor, has picked the RD-180 to power its entrant into the Air Force's competition for the Evolved Expendable Launch Vehicle. The Government's plan is to buy about 15 of the rockets each year starting around the turn of the century, with the intent of replacing the Atlas, Delta and Titan rockets.
Separately, Lockheed Martin is having the Russian engine power a new commercial version of the venerable Atlas rocket as well, which is due to make its debut next year.
Routinely, the Russian RD-170 engine powers a Ukrainian launcher known as the Zenit, which is 187 feet tall and carries mostly Russian military payloads into orbit.
In an innovative twist, the Boeing Company, the aerospace giant, is adapting the Zenit and its RD-170 engine for commercial launchings at sea. As part of a multinational team, Boeing is building in Norway a giant floating platform bigger than a football field that will be towed to the equatorial Pacific. The Zenit rocket, unloaded from a 620-foot supply ship onto the floating launching pad, will then blast into orbit, with the Earth's relatively fast rotation at the Equator giving it an extra kick for heavier payloads.
In August, the Sea Launch Company, based in Oslo, broke ground at a former Navy site in Long Beach, Calif., that will serve as the operation's home port. The first launching of the Ukrainian rocket is due next year, and the plan has so far won contracts for 15 satellite launchings.
Experts say such partnerships and deals are increasingly common, with virtually every major aerospace player around the globe involved to some degree. In early October, the French company Societe Europeenne de Propulsion signed a deal with Moscow to study the RD-0120, a Russian engine that powered the Energia rocket's core.
Many space analysts hail the Russian invasion, saying it will promote peace and fuel humanity's reach for the stars. Some credit the Clinton Administration for the trend.
"It's almost unprecedented that one single policy could solve so many problems, both domestic and foreign," said John E. Pike, head of space policy at the Federation of American Scientists. "It lowers launching costs while reducing the danger of missile proliferation."
Stephen Hopkins, a space analyst at Anser, a research institute in Arlington, Va., that closely studies the Russian gear, predicted that "more and more partnerships will develop between companies in Europe and the U.S. and Russia."
But fire-sale prices are destined to end, he and other analysts say.
"The Russians are very savvy business people," Mr. Hopkins said. "Space technology is their premiere technology. They understand that and are very proud of that and will protect that," no matter how rocky the transition to a market economy and how turbulent the world of commercial rocketry may be.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Advances Against Yen, But Mark and the Pound Rally

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
The dollar touched a nearly three-and-a-half-year high against the yen yesterday, strengthening over unexpected Japanese political bickering that diminished demand for yen.
The yen's retreat also contributed to rallies in the mark, pound and other important currencies, indirectly leaving the dollar mostly lower in global transactions. 
 Currency traders said the main theme of the day was the money flowing out of Japan, as investors seeking higher yielding opportunities elsewhere exchanged yen for currencies needed to purchase assets in other countries.
"The story seems to be there are a lot of Japanese accounts trying to invest abroad,"said Kisoo Park, a senior trader at the Bank of Boston.
In late New York trading, the dollar rose to 114.20 yen, up from 113.31 yen on Friday and nearly 43 percent higher than its all-time low reached in April of 1995. During intraday dealings the dollar hit a high of 114.43 yen, its highest level since April 1993.
The dollar slipped to 1.5190 marks from 1.5210 marks.
The British pound was quoted at $1.6128, up from $1.5973.
"The easiest way to describe it is a continuation of the trend we've seen," said Jack Griffin, vice president of corporate foreign exchange at the New York branch of Fuji Bank Ltd. "As long as the investor flows out of Japan continue, and the interest rate differential remains as wide as it is, there isn't any real reason to be a big buyer of yen."
Traders said selling of yen intensified after weekend reports that Japan's dominant Liberal Democratic Party faces trouble finding coalition partners for a new government, only a week after the Liberal Democrats gained in parliamentary elections but failed to win a majority.
The reports reinforced a view that Prime Minister Ryutaro Hashimoto might be ineffective and was unlikely to create changes that would lead to higher Japanese interest rates.
"Basically, I think the hung Parliament they have has brought a lot of uncertainty," said Jan Alvarez, chief dealer at Corestates Bank in Philadelphia. Coupled with Japan's tentative economic recovery, she said, "the money is just flowing out of the country."

HEADLINE: Medicare Trust Fund Ends Fiscal Year With a $4.2 Billion Deficit

BYLINE: By ROBERT PEAR 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 28

 BODY:
The Medicare trust fund that pays hospital bills for the elderly spent $4.2 billion more than it received in taxes and other income in the last fiscal year, the Clinton Administration reported today. And it is expected to run larger deficits in coming years.
By contrast, the Hospital Insurance Trust Fund lost just $36 million in the prior year and had previously run a substantial surplus for more than a decade. The trust fund was clearly losing money but the size of the deficit was known until today. 
 Democrats and Republicans said the data showed a need to shore up Medicare. Representative Pete Stark of California, the ranking Democrat on the Ways and Means Subcommittee on Health, said: "We must take bipartisan action in the next Congress to extend the life of the Medicare trust fund. This should be the first thing on the agenda of the Ways and Means Committee in January, no matter who has the majority in Congress."
The $4.2 billion deficit represents the difference between two huge numbers: outlays of $127.7 billion and receipts of $123.5 billion. It was covered by a surplus accumulated over the years.
Representative Bill Archer, the Texas Republican who is chairman of the Ways and Means Committee, said the $4.2 billion loss was "the largest financial drop in the history of the Medicare program, with the exception of a one-time transfer from Medicare to Social Security in 1983." Congress ordered that transfer to keep Social Security solvent.
"The only solution to the Medicare crisis is a bipartisan one," Mr. Archer said. "That's why our nation will live to regret the fearful and misleading campaign the Democrats have waged over Medicare this fall."
Today's report was surprising in several respects. Just a few months ago the Clinton Administration predicted that the hospital trust fund would lose $8.2 billion in the fiscal year 1996, which ended Sept. 30; the actual deficit was much smaller. The trust fund often loses money in September, but showed a gain of $1.8 billion last month.
Indeed, the Hospital Insurance Trust Fund did not lose money in the final six months of the fiscal year, taken together. It lost $4.2 billion in the first half of the year, and its condition did not deteriorate further. The balance in the trust fund fluctuated, but the outlays equaled taxes and other income over the last six months, so there was no net change in the trust fund.
Chris Jennings, a White House aide who coordinates health policy for President Clinton, said, "The increase in health care expenditures appears to have slowed with improvements in the economy." But Mr. Jennings said the Administration did not want to overstate the importance of a few months' numbers.
The growth of Medicaid spending, for low-income people, also slowed, perhaps because of greater use of health maintenance organizations and other types of managed care. Federal Medicaid outlays rose 3.3 percent last year, to $92 billion, after rising an average of more than 14 percent a year from 1985 to 1995.
At the end of the last fiscal year, on Sept. 30, the Medicare trust fund had assets of more than $125.8 billion. Administration officials said they still believed that the trust fund would be exhausted early in 2001 if the current law was not changed.
Doctors' bills are paid from a different account, which is continually replenished with general revenues and with monthly premiums paid by Medicare beneficiaries. This part of the Medicare program is not in danger of running out of money, because it has access to general revenues, but the cost is growing at an unsustainable rate of roughly 9 percent a year.
In a separate action, Labor Secretary Robert B. Reich today criticized companies that cut back health benefits for retirees. He said President Clinton, if re-elected, would take steps to protect such benefits.
"If these companies promise lifetime benefits to retirees, they should be required to keep that promise," Mr. Reich said.
Labor Department statistics show a steady decline in retiree health coverage by American companies, Mr. Reich said. The Labor Department issued a notice advising workers how retiree health benefits could be cut, so employees could challenge such cutbacks or plan for the possibility.
Even while promising health benefits to retirees, sometimes as an incentive for early retirement, Mr. Reich said, companies often reserve the right to "modify, revoke, suspend, terminate or change" the benefits at any time. Such reservations may be in fine print and are sometimes omitted entirely from the summary of benefit plans given to employees.
At a minimum, Mr. Reich said, employers must make fuller disclosure to employees. In addition, he said, the Labor Department and the White House may propose legislation to compel employers to fulfill any promises they make to employees.
At a meeting with journalists today, Mr. Reich said: "It's a balance. We don't want to deter companies from offering health care benefits to retirees. But it's important that employees who have health benefits promised to them get those promises fulfilled."
Employers say they have cut back retiree health benefits because of the cost of such benefits and because of new accounting rules that require them to subtract from each year's earnings a part of the costs of health benefits promised to future retirees.
Mr. Reich said the proportion of retirees in private industry covered by health insurance from a former employer dropped to 27 percent in 1994, from 37 percent in 1988. Thus, he said, only 4.7 million of the 17.4 million retirees have coverage.

HEADLINE: POLITICS;
In His Own Words

 BODY:

BILL CLINTON
 
Speaking yesterday in University City, Mo.:
 
"When I ran for President four years ago, no challenge loomed larger or seemed more difficult to solve than the deficit. The deficit had soared to $290 billion, a record high and growing.
"In the 12 years before I took office, our national debt had quadrupled over what it had accumulated for the 200 years before. 
 "We worked hard to change that. We passed a tough economic plan without a single solitary vote on the other side. My opponent and others said we could never reduce the deficit. They said we would wreck the economy. They said these tough decisions would bring no good.
"Well, we knew sooner or later there would be 'show me' time.
"And we know now that for four years in a row, we have reduced the deficit. That's the first time a President has reduced the deficit in all four years of a term in the 20th century.
"We know now that our budget would be in surplus today if it weren't for the interest we pay on the debt run up in just the 12 years before I took office.
"I pledged to you when I ran for President that we would restore fiscal discipline to our Government, cut the deficit in half in four years. I said it was wrong to leave our children a legacy of debt and we would never get Missouri and America's economy growing again until we got the deficit down to get interest rates down so that business loans, home mortgages, car payments and student loan payment were going down. We had to get those interest rates down.
"The deficit was a ball and chain holding back our economy. Well, today we got some new good news about exactly how far the deficit has dropped since I took office. . . . The 1996 deficit has been cut to $107 billion. That's a reduction of 63 percent. That's the lowest level since 1981. But when you adjust for inflation, it is the lowest deficit in 22 years.
"Today the United States has a deficit in its budget that as a percentage of our income that is lower than that of any other major industrial nation on the face of the Earth.
"This has meant real benefits to the people in this audience. It means cheaper auto, home, credit card, business loan rates. More business expansion, more job creation, a growing economy, providing opportunity again.
"My friends, America has heard a lot of calls in the several days. I would say that these results prove that these results prove that America is awake and moving in the right direction to the 21st century.
"We are moving toward a balanced budget. We are going to continue building prosperity and creating jobs."

HEADLINE: Labor Leader Accused of Looting Union Fund

BYLINE: By SELWYN RAAB

 BODY:
Frederick W. Devine, who until June drew an annual salary of $410,000 as the head of the carpenters' union in the New York City area, was indicted yesterday on state charges of stealing $240,000 from union and management funds to finance a regal spending spree.
Prosecutors in Manhattan said that in the last three years Mr. Devine siphoned off funds that were intended to create jobs for unemployed carpenters to pay for winter trips to Florida for himself, his wife, a female friend and union allies. The expenses, prosecutors said, included $10,955 to rent jet planes for two visits with another female friend, in Rochester, $2,000 a night for 17-day stays in Florida hotel suites for himself and $950 for his wife, $1,500 for numerous dinners with union cronies and $1,300 for frequent golf parties with friends. 
 Robert M. Morgenthau, the Manhattan District Attorney, whose office brought the charges, said that in addition to private plane trips to Rochester, Mr. Devine doled out a no-show $15,000 consultant's job to the female friend whose Florida trip he paid for, "who had a personal relationship with him."
"His extravagant spending of the union's money supported his lavish life style," Mr. Morgenthau added.
For five years, Mr. Devine, 57, was one of the region's most powerful labor leaders as the president of the 26,000-member District Council of Carpenters of New York City and Vicinity. He was abruptly ousted from the post in June by the carpenters' national union on charges of corruption and helping mobsters and their relatives get lucrative jobs at the Jacob K. Javits Convention Center at a time when about 25 percent of the union's membership was unemployed.
Mr. Devine, who lives in Cliffside Park, N.J., pleaded not guilty yesterday in State Supreme Court in Manhattan to grand larceny and fraud charges and was released on $50,000 bond. Through his lawyer, Paul Victor, Mr. Devine asserted that the charges had been manufactured by District Council rivals who want to gain control of the union and its multibillion-dollar health and welfare funds.
"This indictment is the chilling result of the D.A. lending his office and prestige to rivals who are being resurrected from the graveyard of rejected and tainted union officials," Mr. Victor said outside the court. The lawyer maintained that the evidence against Mr. Devine came from witnesses who falsely implicated him to avoid being prosecuted themselves on union corruption charges.
Prosecutors, at a news conference, said Mr. Devine had a salary of $235,000 a year as the council's president and $175,000 as a trustee and the administrator of the Labor-Management Cooperation Trust Fund that was established in 1993 to promote work for union carpenters in the city, on Long Island and in Westchester, Rockland and Orange counties.
The $2 million fund was financed by employers, and Daniel J. Castleman, the chief of Mr. Morgenthau's Investigation Division, said expenditures had to be jointly approved by Mr. Devine and Paul O'Brien, the managing director of the Building Contractors Association of New York. Mr. Morgenthau said that Mr. O'Brien signed blank checks that Mr. Devine later used for improper payments that were unrelated to the fund or union business.
Asked why Mr. O'Brien was not implicated in the charges, Mr. Castleman said, "He was not the administrator of the fund or a beneficiary of the crimes."
Prosecutors suggested that Mr. O'Brien would be a major witness in the case. Mr. O'Brien, who represents builders of commercial and residential buildings in the city, did not return telephone calls to his office for comment yesterday.
In a related indictment, Jonni Clause-Stanton, 41, of Oradell, N.J., a former professional golfer, was charged with stealing $15,000 from the fund and falsifying business records. Mr. Morgenthau said that Mr. Devine retained Ms. Clause-Stanton, the woman who went on the trip to Florida, as a consultant to arrange golf parties for union officials -- parties that were never held, he said.
Ms. Clause-Stanton pleaded not guilty at her arraignment.
As president of the carpenters' union, Mr. Devine's decisions in labor negotiations affected residential and commercial construction in the area. His endorsement and financial support also was sought by national and state politicians.
The District Council has been under Federal monitorship since 1994 after Federal prosecutors accused Mr. Devine and other union officials of being tools of the Genovese crime family.

HEADLINE: Dow Drops 34 on Concern Over Inflation

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
Stocks slumped in late trading yesterday as interest rates rose on concerns that an impending flood of new economic data might upset optimism about inflation.
The Dow Jones industrial average surrendered an early 36-point gain and fell 34.29 points, to 5,972.73, as an afternoon rebound fizzled. It was the Dow's second finish below 6,000 in three sessions and its fifth losing performance in six sessions. 
 Broad market measures also surrendered early gains as bond prices slipped, sending interest rates higher. The yield on the 30-year Treasury bond, a key determinant of corporate and consumer borrowing costs, rose to 6.83 percent from 6.81 percent on Friday.
As the flow of major earnings reports slowed, the market's attention shifted to the economic reports to come this week, which include readings on employment costs, the manufacturing sector, and the nation's overall third-quarter growth.
The first of those reports was not due until today, leaving an air of hesitance that was worsened by tax-related selling among mutual fund managers.
Also cooling the market is the approach of the Presidential election next week, which makes investors more reluctant to take chances.
Because of the "enhanced uncertainty" of "whether the House and Senate will remain Republican, you have a lot of people sitting on the sidelines right now," Russ Labrasca, senior vice president at Principal Financial Securities of Dallas, said.
Much of the market's early strength came in energy-related shares, which benefited from news that gasoline prices jumped nearly a penny a gallon nationwide during the last two weeks, according to an industry analyst's survey.
The prospect of even higher fuel prices hurt transportation issues -- the Dow Jones transportation index slid nearly 1 percent -- and energy issues gave back most of the day's gains.
The reports to come this week could provide important signs whether various inflationary pressures have remained tame enough for the Federal Reserve to withhold an interest rate hike.
After tumbling sharply in July because of worries about inflation and interest rates, the markets have rebounded to record levels amid numerous signs of moderating economic growth that have kept the Fed on the sidelines.
Contrary signs of strong growth, however, have added to investors' unease.
"What we're faced with now is uncertainty regarding the fourth quarter," Scott Bleier, chief investment strategist at Prime Charter, said. "We may have a strong second half of the fourth quarter, and that might put a damper on the notion of interest rates staying low."
Higher Fed lending rates would help contain such inflationary pressures as rising production costs, slowing consumer borrowing and demand. But that could hurt company profits by slowing revenues and raising operating costs.
Higher inflation or interest rates, meanwhile, make the fixed payoff on bonds less attractive, forcing down prices to improve the yield.
Declining issues outnumbered those that gained by nearly 5 to 3 on the New York Stock Exchange, where volume totaled 383.6 million shares.
The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index fell 3.66 points, to 697.26, and the New York Stock Exchange composite index fell 2.28 points, to 370.98.
The Nasdaq composite index fell 6.71 points, to 1,215.89.
The Dow's oil-related stocks, which provided much of the index's early strength, ended lower: Chevron fell 1 1/8, to 66, after a brokerage firm downgrade, Texaco fell 1, to 102 1/4, and Exxon fell 1/4, to 88 1/4.
Technology shares had a mixed performance after a report of a possible slowdown in personal computer demand, Mr. Labrasca said. In Nasdaq trading, Gateway 2000 fell 3 to 47 1/4, and Dell fell 1 3/8 to 81.
Transition Systems lost 5 to 8 3/4 after issuing an earnings warning.
 Shares of Electronic Data Systems continued to show weakness after the company warned last week of slower growth in the fourth quarter. It was the most active New York Stock Exchange issue, falling 5, to 41 1/8.

HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE DEMOCRAT;
Clinton Hails Drop in Deficit, Declaring 'America's Awake'

BYLINE: By ALISON MITCHELL 

DATELINE: CHICAGO, Oct. 28

 BODY:
As his quest for re-election moved into its final week, President Clinton today pointed to new figures showing that the Federal deficit had dropped to its lowest level since 1981 and proclaimed, "America's awake and moving in the right direction."
Mr. Clinton's focus on the economy was one in a series of thematic rally speeches his aides say he will deliver in the closing days of the Presidential race to sum up his case for a second term. The addresses are to culminate on Friday when the President -- returning to a theme he unveiled in the summer of 1995 -- plans to speak of the "common ground" that he believes now defines the nation's political center. 
 The President opened his daylong swing through three Midwestern states in University City, Mo., a St. Louis suburb, where he stood before a riotous red, white and blue array of flags, charts and banners meant to show that he had more than fulfilled his 1992 campaign pledge to cut the Federal deficit by half.
Speaking over a distant crowd of hecklers, Mr. Clinton said that four years ago, voters had had to take his pledge to cut the deficit on faith. He continued: "Today there is a record, and we can show you. And that's why they're trying to shout us down because we can show you," he said, playing on Missouri's reputation as the "Show Me" state.
A giant bar graph hanging along the facade of City Hall charted the deficit's downward path. And the pillars of the building bore long arrows pointing downward that carried the slogan: "down 63 percent during President Clinton's watch."
As thousands looked on, two young people called to the outdoor stage by Mr. Clinton pulled a string attached to the bar graph banner, which unveiled a section showing that the deficit had dropped to $107.3 billion in the Federal fiscal year that ended in September. White House officials said the deficit was the lowest since 1981, the first year of the Reagan Administration.
Mr. Clinton never mentioned his opponent, Bob Dole, by name, but he directly answered the Republican's late campaign call for America to "wake up" by pointing to the health of the economy. "America is awake and moving in the right direction," he said.
The President repeated the economic themes throughout the day as he swung through Missouri and Minnesota and pushed on to Illinois, where he held an evening rally in Chicago. Although these states are usually considered an electoral battleground in Presidential races at this time of year, Mr. Clinton is running comfortably ahead in all three.
His aides said the primary purpose of his appearances was to lend energy to the campaigns of Democratic Congressional candidates, including Senator Paul Wellstone of Minnesota, who is being furiously assaulted by his opponent as a liberal. Mr. Wellstone was the only Democratic Senator up for re-election who voted against the welfare bill that Mr. Clinton signed in the summer. Mr. Clinton praised him today as having "a great heart."
Confident and cocky as the Presidential race entered the final stretch, Mr. Clinton's aides did not even bother to refute any of the charges that Mr. Dole has slung the President's way in recent days, as he has questioned Mr. Clinton's ethics and called the Clinton White House the "animal house."
Michael D. McCurry, the White House press secretary, dismissed Mr. Dole's attacks as "serial flailing" and said, "It's increasingly becoming less necessary to respond as the shrillness and absurdity of the remarks accelerates."
At each stop, Mr. Clinton drew huge, boisterous crowds, though there were protesters, too. One woman outside the Target Center sports arena in Minneapolis hoisted a homemade protest sign that read, "Smoke Dope, Dodge the Draft, Cheat on Your Wife, Become President, The American Dream."
Time and again today, Mr. Clinton noted that when he had taken office four years ago, the Federal deficit had reached $290 billion and that the Republicans had refused to vote for the package of tax increases and spending cuts he pushed through the Congress in 1993, when it was controlled by Democrats, to attack the problem.
He praised Mr. Wellstone for supporting that economic plan. "You need to know when we brought the deficit down, there was not a single member of the other party who voted for it," Mr. Clinton told the more than 15,000 people who filled the vast sports arena of the Target Center.
"Well," he added, his voice getting aw-shucks folksy, "Paul Wellstone said, 'I think we ought to bring the deficit down.' " That decision, Mr. Clinton said, seeking to inoculate Mr. Wellstone from the charge of being a liberal, "was the conservative thing to do -- to protect our future, to conserve our people and our resources and get our house in order." The Republicans, however, refer to the 1993 deficit-reduction package as an example of liberalism because of the tax increases it contained.
Mr. Clinton lashed out at the Republican Congress for its budget priorities. He reminded the audience that he had vetoed the Republican budget plan in the face of two Government shutdowns.
"When I vetoed that budget, they shut the Government down and said, 'All those Democrats, they love the Government so much, they'll give us what we want just to get it open,' " he said. "And I said, 'I'd rather see you hurt people for three months than 30 years. No, thank you very much.' "
Even though the Federal deficit has dropped in every year of his term, Mr. Clinton's own original budget plan for the 1996 fiscal year still foresaw continuous deficits in the $200 billion range. The President did not embrace the call for a multiyear balanced budget until months after the Republicans took control of Congress and began drafting a seven-year budget-balancing plan.
For that reason, the Republicans said today that they were responsible for the drop in the deficit. "This is a huge credit to the common-sense Republican Congress which fought for spending constraints," said Haley Barbour, the chairman of the Republican National Committee.
Franklin Raines, the director of the White House Office of Management and Budget, attributed most of the deficit reduction to stronger than expected economic growth, which brought in more tax revenues than anticipated, and reduced spending.He said the spending cuts made in discretionary domestic programs had been "relatively modest."

HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE THIRD-PARTY CANDIDATE;
Perot Says Inquires Would Hobble a Clinton Second Term

BYLINE: By ERNEST TOLLERSON 

DATELINE: BLACKSBURG, Va., Oct. 28

 BODY:
Ross Perot predicted today that the Whitewater investigation and related inquiries would develop into "Watergate II" if President Clinton was re-elected, and asserted that Mr. Clinton should have done "the responsible thing" by stepping down.
"I think the responsible thing for any President that has this many problems to do would have been to resign," Mr. Perot, the Reform Party's Presidential nominee, told an audience of 3,000 people at Virginia Polytechnic Institute here. "But he certainly is not going to do that. The people will have to make the decision." 
 Mr. Perot then asked, "Is it responsible and in the best interest of our country to elect a President who has the next two years of his life facing Watergate II?"
Although Mr. Perot has rejected the Dole-Kemp campaign's entreaties for him to withdraw and endorse the Republican ticket, his recent attacks on the President's character closely resemble Bob Dole's own latest criticism of Mr. Clinton.
In speeches and other public remarks, starting with his address at the National Press Club in Washington last Wednesday, Mr. Perot has leaned heavily on the analogy of Watergate -- the scandal that forced President Richard M. Nixon from office less than two years into his own second term -- in reciting accusations echoing Mr. Dole's contention that the President is not a leader whom the American people can trust.
Pushing the attack today, to several rounds of polite applause, Mr. Perot invoked principles that guide the Boy Scouts. "A lot of people here must have been in Scouting as children," he said. "Is it asking too much that our President be honest, loyal and trustworthy? Is that asking too much?"
And he exhorted his audience, primarily students: "If you love your country and you love your children, how can you even consider voting for a President who has huge moral, ethical and criminal problems facing him? Just think about that."
As evidence of the depth of difficulties that he said the President would face in a second term, Mr. Perot cited the endorsement that the editorial page of The New York Times gave Mr. Clinton on Sunday.
A part of the endorsing editorial, Mr. Perot noted, dealt with questions about the President's ethical standards. Mr. Perot then read from the editorial, underscoring the section that urged the President to pledge not to pardon anyone convicted in prosecutions arising from Whitewater and other inquiries, and to promise that he, the First Lady and others would cooperate with investigators.
"Now I will tell you," Mr. Perot said, "here's a fearless forecast: You're going to get those promises in the next few days. Then, as you do, realize this before you get those promises. The New York Times is asking for a grown man in his middle age to have a personality change. That is not too likely to occur."
Mr. Perot has long argued that at some point the nation will suffer what he calls a "meltdown," an economic crisis resulting in part from the size of the Federal debt. Should that crisis be at hand, he said today, the nation "does not need another Watergate investigation."
While he hammered Mr. Clinton during most of his 45 minutes on stage, Mr. Perot specifically criticized Mr. Dole only once, reiterating his contention that the sugar magnate Alfonso Fanjul, a vice chairman of the Dole campaign finance committee, had benefited from sugar subsidies enacted with Mr. Dole's support. The Dole campaign has denied that assertion, declaring that its nominee has worked to reduce sugar subsidies.
As his speech neared a close, Mr. Perot invoked a military image -- Mr. Clinton alone among the Presidential candidates did not serve in the armed forces -- to pose a question:
"If you were wounded on the battlefield in no man's land and only had three people with you, the three candidates, which one do you think would come out to get you?"
As many in the audience laughed and applauded, he added, "And which one do you think wouldn't be out there to get you?
"Which candidate," he continued, to further laughter, "would you be comfortable having take your daughter on an around-the-world educational trip?"

HEADLINE: POLITICS;
Excerpts From Dole's Address on Affirmative Action

 BODY:
Following are excerpts, as transcribed by the Federal News Service, from a speech by Bob Dole yesterday in San Diego, where he endorsed a California ballot initiative that would bar all state-sponsored affirmative action programs.
 Now we've reached another turning point, this time for quality and opportunity in America. And the California Civil Rights Initiative allows the voters of this state to endorse a great principle, the principle that racial distinctions have no place in our lives or in our laws. That's the way it should be in America. . . .
This initiative will not affect the employment policies of private companies. We're talking about the state government, just as we're talking government in the bill that I've introduced. It will not weaken any existing laws against racial or gender discrimination. It will not prevent active outreach in recruitment of minorities. 
 It will, however, guarantee that race will find no significance in the laws of California. It will elevate individual civil rights above group entitlements. It will put the government firmly on the side of equal justice and equal treatment, where it belongs in the first place. And the overwhelming majority of Americans in California believe as I do, that we should promote equal opportunity for all, without regard to race or gender. That's the bottom line.
But at the same time, we believe it's wrong to use quotas, set-asides and other preferences that serve only to pit one American against another American, or group against group. The real focus should be on helping citizens who are economically disadvantaged, to provide assistance based on need and not on skin color. . . .
The California Civil Rights Initiative represents, and it applies, America's best principles. And I strongly endorse it. And today I want to carefully explain my reasons, because I think it's very important for all of us to be careful, because feelings on this issue run high. And it's easy for the demagogues on either side to trade on fear or play on resentment. And this places a special burden on anyone who enters this debate.
Even as we reject preferences, we must also reject prejudice. And even as we oppose quotas, we must also oppose scapegoating and stereotyping. . . . Yet while it's important to be careful, there's a difference between careful and silence. We cannot be silent. The meaning of equal, equality is not just another political debate. It is a first principle of American government. It cannot be avoided any more than we can avoid the Declaration of Independence or the 14th Amendment or the Civil Rights Act of 1964, which I supported. And I view C.C.R.I. as the extension of a commitment I made in 1964, when I voted for the Civil Rights Act. And the purpose of that law is to remove the barrier of race, remove the barrier of race from public life. . . .
But the reason behind it is even more compelling. And let me quote Senator Humphrey. He felt very strongly about this issue. . . . This is what he said: "Our standard of judgment, in the last analysis, is not some brute's power, but an equal opportunity for persons; not groups, but persons. Do you want a society that is nothing but an endless power struggle among organized groups? Do you want a society where there's no place for the independent individual? I don't." . . .
 It is true that many of us in the years following 1964 -- and I did -- supported some race-conscious measures designed to speed the process of inclusion, a measure that was supposed to be transitional, transitional and temporary. But it didn't work. Every time I drive to work in Washington, D.C., or drive down North Capitol Street and I see dozens and dozens of black men without work, I say to myself: "What has this law done for them? Absolutely nothing. Absolutely nothing." Maybe one-half of 1 percent at the top have been benefited.
But this was a blind alley in the search for equal justice. And we should have learned, and I hope I did learn, from it. Programs that started as temporary and limited have become permanent and broad. And it's increasingly clear that such preferences are irrelevant in the problems faced by many minorities: violent crime, the disappearing families.
And most of all, as Hubert Humphrey predicted, preferences have become a source of polarization . . . pitting one group against another group. They have given government sanctions to racial tension, creating distinctions that become barriers that become battlegrounds. . . .
We have seen systemic discrimination against Asian-Americans at California schools and universities. Asian students in recent years have been judged to be, quote, "over-represented" and their numbers have been limited.
Now, don't the defenders of race-consciousness see the sad irony in this? Have they forgotten the injustice of our recent history? America must step back and think about this for a moment. School applications have been denied for the single reason that there's been an Asian name signed at the bottom. . . .
Not only does Clinton oppose C.C.R.I. but he has worked to defend and expand racial preferences, even when states and courts try to remove them. And on this issue, as the Governor said, the President said, well, he wants to mend it, not end it. In reality, the President defends even the most indefensible forms of racial preferences. In 1995, in fact, I asked the Congressional Research Service to identify all the Federal programs that granted quotas and preferences. When the White House found out I had it, they asked for a copy, and I was happy to send it to them; they needed all the information they can get in this place. And then he started this great review. Do you know how many programs he eliminated? I think there are 168 programs. He eliminated one. One. . . .
Preferences and quotas are not the answer, but our nation is still in need of answers. Equal opportunity is an empty concept in neighborhoods occupied by gangs and school where both safety and learning are rare. And you know where some of those schools are. It is also a distant concept in those cases where women and minorities are victimized by specific acts of discrimination. The end of quotas does not exhaust our responsibility to equality of opportunity. It only begins it. . . .
We must vigorously enforce all the laws on the books against discrimination in the workplace. And my administration will make this a top priority. . . .
And I just want to end this on a positive note. We must bring quality education to every child in every community. And I believe the surest route to economic mobility for all Americans lies in the access to a good school. Letting parents choose the best school for their children is perhaps the most urgent civil rights issue of our time. . . .
But we must understand that crime is also a civil rights issue. Forty-six percent of African-American adults say their greatest fear is violence against their children, or children they know. And no one suffers more from violent-crime repeat offenders than the large, silent, decent majority in our inner cities. And no one will benefit more from keeping violent criminals behind bars, which we will do in our administration. . . .
And, finally, we must bring a growing economy to every community. . . . Give people an opportunity to make it in the private sector. They don't need quotas and preferences.

SECTION: Section A; Page 20; Column 2; National Desk 

LENGTH: 574 words

HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE DEFICIT;
Steep Drop Is a Surprise, and a Mystery, Too

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 28

 BODY:
By almost any statistical measure, the reduction in the Federal budget deficit, hailed by President Clinton today, is impressive: it has dropped to its lowest level in 15 years, so low that the United States now has the smallest deficit -- as a percentage of its economy -- of any of the world's largest industrialized nations.
But even Mr. Clinton's advisers, who have been massaging every figure these days into a campaign sound bite, acknowledge that part of the reason that the deficit narrowed so quickly is that the Treasury is awash in an unexpected $27 billion windfall. That is the good news. The bad news is that no one is exactly sure why. 
 "It's a mystery," Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin said today. "We have theories, but no one has quite figured it out."
The most popular theory is that investors, convinced that they have ridden the stock market surge as far as they dare, are beginning to reap their profits, which funnels capital gains tax revenue to the Government. Others note that since most of the increased revenue seems to be coming from individual tax returns and speculate that the swelling of the employment rolls is turning nontaxpaying households into taxpayers. But usually people entering the work force for the first time are not paying big tax bills.
But what everyone agrees on is this: the revenue surge is not likely to last very long.
"There are definitely question marks out there," Gene Sperling, one of President Clinton's domestic economic advisers, said today. "You can't build into your expectations that every year is going to look as good as 1996."
Mr. Sperling also cited some unexpected savings, particularly for health care, but warned, "You can't assume that one-year reductions are going to go on forever."
The annual deficit reached a record of $290 billion in President George Bush's last full year in office. It has declined every year since, and by the end of the fiscal year that ended on Sept. 30, it dropped to $107.3 billion. That is a drop of 63 percent.
Naturally, both parties took credit today for the size and speed of the decline. Haley Barbour, the chairman of the Republican National Committee, said the chief reason was the "common sense Republican Congress," which took over in January 1995. But that explanation overlooks the first two years of declines.
Mr. Rubin had a different view. "We started on this on Jan. 7, 1993," when Mr. Clinton's transition team first set the course, he said today. He estimated that roughly $40 billion of the decline arose from "technical factors," including the unexpected drop in the growth of health-care spending. "There is no question, though," he said today, "that 130 billion or so is directly attributable to what the Government did" to create good economic conditions.
The truth is that the good news about the deficit could create future stumbling blocks for both parties. Mr. Clinton's plan to balance the budget by 2002 includes $50 billion in revenue gains over the next six years, which means that the White House assumes that roughly $8 billion of the $27 billion gain will continue. If these assumptions are wrong, the budget would be out of balance.
Bob Dole's economic plan assumes that roughly $80 billion of the increased revenue will keep rolling on over the next six years -- even though the plan calls for a major cut in capital gains taxes, the likely cause of the revenue surge. 

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

COUNTRY: UNITED STATES (95%);

HEADLINE: Jury Service Less Onerous, Report Says

BYLINE: By JOHN SULLIVAN 

 BODY:
Jury duty, long the dreary bane of New Yorkers, has become significantly less onerous, thanks to changes in the system since 1993, with people called less often and for shorter periods, according to a report issued yesterday by the court administrative office.
In fact, the average juror now spends half the time on jury duty as in 1993, the report indicated, and in most counties in the state must be selected for a trial after one day or released from service. 
 Court officials attributed the improvements to several changes made in court procedures in the last several years. They said the expansion of the jury pool, the elimination of automatic exemptions from jury service for many professions and the streamlining of jury selection have produced the most dramatic results.
While officials acknowledged that many citizens remain unhappy with the conditions of jury service, they said that system would continue to improve over the next few years.
"This is a work in progress," Chief Administrative Judge Jonathan Lippman said at a news conference at State Supreme Court in Manhattan announcing the results of the study. "It is not perfect, but it is certainly getting much, much better."
The changes in the New York jury system are part of a national trend of seeking to improve the lot of jurors, according to the National Center for State Courts. Tom Munsterman, who directs the center's program on juries, said New York has joined several large states, including California and Arizona, that have recently begun to change their jury systems.
"I think there is a recognition of the hardship of serving, as well as the importance of jury service," he said. "Many states are looking at what they can do to improve the jury system."
For many years, jury duty has been one of the more burdensome duties of the ordinary citizen, often involving repeated trips to the courthouse and days or weeks away from work. The experience had become so bad that by 1993, the courts were regularly receiving complaints from jurors, Judge Lippman said.
"Jurors are the crown jewel of our legal system, and we cannot operate without them, but for years they were treated as the system's pawns," said Gary Brown, director of the Fund for Modern Courts, a nonprofit group that monitors the state court system. "They were shifted and moved around at will."
At that time, New York State's newly appointed Chief Judge, Judith S. Kaye, began to institute a series of changes intended to ease the burden on citizens called for jury duty. One of the most high-profile changes was the elimination of special exemptions from jury service for groups ranging from physicians to lawyers to podiatrists.
In all, there were 20 groups excused from jury duty when the law was changed in 1993. That change alone added a million people to the state's pool of 12 million. The use of welfare and unemployment rolls to select jurors, in addition to the long-used lists of voters and drivers, added an additional 500,000 people.
"I think the main thing that happened here is a sense of fairness," Judge Lippman said. "I don't think that people complain when they believe they are treated fairly, when no one is singled out for special treatment."
As part of the same effort, the courts also eliminated so-called permanent qualified juror lists, which were lists of people frequently called for jury service. Court officials say the elimination of the permanent qualified lists and the expansion of the jury pool have led to less frequent calls for service.
In 1993, 17 counties in the state, including four of the five boroughs in the city -- Queens, the Bronx, Manhattan and Brooklyn -- had to call people to jury duty every two years, instead of the legally specified four years. Today, only Manhattan and the Bronx remain on the two-year schedule because of a shortage of qualified jurors, with all other counties on a schedule of four years or more. Most counties also have instituted a one-day or one-trial system for jury service, meaning that jurors who have not been selected for a trial are released from duty after one day. Manhattan, Brooklyn, Queens and the Bronx are the only places that have not switched to the system because of a shortage of jurors.
The courts have also eliminated the process of jury stacking, in which a juror could be selected for trial duty and told to report back to the courthouse in several weeks. The old system allowed more trials to move forward but caused increased disruption for jurors.
In all counties, the length of jury service has been reduced because of streamlined administrative procedures. In 1993, the average juror served for 5.2 days. This year, court officials said, the average is 2.6 days. Also, jurors can now automatically receive a six-month deferral of jury duty over the telephone without having to appear at the courthouse and request exemption from duty.
Judge Lippman said the courts have also tried to upgrade jury rooms and stress the need for civility by court employees. The courts have contracted with a nonprofit group, the Vera Institute of Justice, to run a complaint line at courthouses. Christopher Stone, the institute's director, said that in the two years the line has operated, complaints have fallen 50 percent. "That is a pretty good indication," Mr. Stone said. "The jurors are still complaining, but they are complaining a lot less."
Outside groups like the New York State Bar Association and the Fund for Modern Courts agreed that the changes to the jury system have been positive. "We are finding that jurors serve less time and there has been a substantial broadening of the jury pool," said Mr. Brown of the Fund for Modern Courts. "Part of what is good about these changes is that they are raising the consciousness of the court system about the need to treat jurors like the valuable resource that they are."

HEADLINE: An Atrium for the Citadel of Capitalism

BYLINE: By THOMAS J. LUECK 

 BODY:
In a development plan that would drastically alter the look and street life of the historic core of the city's financial district, the New York Stock Exchange said yesterday that it hoped to expand its cramped trading floor with a glass-enclosed eight-story atrium over Broad Street.
The proposal, described to Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani by Richard Grasso, the exchange's chairman, at City Hall on Thursday, was first reported yesterday by The Wall Street Journal. The stock exchange has said repeatedly that it needs more space. 
 "Is it doable?" said Fran Reiter, the Deputy Mayor for Economic Development and Planning. "We don't know yet. But it certainly is a unique proposal. It sounded right to me as something that would be exciting and right for lower Manhattan."
Ms. Reiter attended the briefing on Thursday, and said yesterday that she was the only city official besides the Mayor who had been given detailed information by the stock exchange.
While eliciting a tentative thumbs-up from Ms. Reiter, the audacious proposal took other city officials off guard
Joseph B. Rose, the city's Planning Commissioner, said, "Keeping the exchange in lower Manhattan is an important priority, but this proposal raises an extensive series of issues for an array of agencies, including planning, transportation and landmarks."
Under the plan, the exchange's 93-year-old trading floor would be expanded on a platform extending one story above Broad Street, connecting the facade of the stock exchange building with that of the 83-year-old Morgan Guaranty Trust building, directly across the street. Both buildings are protected from demolition or alteration by city landmark designations.
Atop the new trading floor would be a glass-enclosed atrium rising eight floors, giving traders and other exchange employees on the floor a dramatic work space with unbroken views of the sky and neighboring buildings.
But exchange officials said the design of the atrium was not selected to benefit the employees, but to provide an architectural window through which people could still see the historic facades of the exchange and Morgan Guaranty buildings, placating preservationist critics of the plan.
Because it would be just above the street, the new appendage to the Stock Exchange would cut off all vehicular traffic, but would allow pedestrians to walk under it, on Broad Street.
If built as proposed, the trading floor expansion would bring a strikingly modern edifice to Broad and Wall Streets, an intersection as steeped in American history as it is a vortex of global finance. Only a few steps north of the glass structure would be the Federal Hall National Memorial, built in 1842 over the site where George Washington was inaugurated as President.
But if the stock exchange's proposal seems out of character with surrounding buildings, it comes when City Hall and many business people are making concerted efforts to draw more life to the area.
"We are delighted that the exchange seems to be focused at remaining at the financial core of Manhattan," said Carl Wiesbrod, president of the Alliance for Lower Manhattan, a 10-month-old business improvement district trying to attract restaurants and stores to the blocks around Wall Street in hopes of creating more diverse activity in a neighborhood that is desolate after dark and on weekends.
Since the stock exchange is expected to respond to growing demand by foreign investors by increasing its trading hours to 16 or even 20 hours each weekday over the next decade, its continued presence on Broad Street would complement the aims of the Wiesbrod group.
It might also bolster the city's plan, announced last year by Mayor Giuliani, to attract developers to lower Manhattan who would convert old, outdated office buildings to housing. The city is offering property tax breaks and other incentives for such projects.
Although officials of the stock exchange declined comment yesterday on whether they would seek financial incentives from the city for their project, Mr. Grasso has said in the past that it was not seeking the kind of long-term tax breaks that many large corporations have received for promises not to leave the city.
The main incentive the exchange is hoping to obtain, according to people close to the negotiations, is the right to build on Broad Street.
Robert Zito, an exchange spokesman, said yesterday that the proposal had been put together in coordination with the exchange's board, whose members were in support even though they had not yet formally endorsed it.
He said the next step, to be taken before the end of the year, would be to submit a formal proposal to City Hall, which would be reviewed by the Planning Commission, Department of Transportation and Landmarks Preservation Commission.
The squeeze on space on the exchange's current trading floor has resulted from the rapid growth in trading volume, which has more than doubled since 1991, and in the number of companies listed, which has grown to 2,800 from 1,800 in the same period.
Although the growth has not created the need for a sharp increase in the number of trading-floor workers, it has led to a shortage of space for equipment, particularly the electronic billboards that instantly display information on trades.
As business on the stock exchange continues to grow, Mr. Zito it will generate the need for more workers as well as equipment.
He said the expansion proposal, which is expected to cost at least $100 million, is being submitted to the city now so that, if approved, the project can be completed within five years.
