HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE REPUBLICAN;
DOLE SEES FAILURE OF THREE DECADES IN ANTI-BIAS FIGHT

BYLINE: By ADAM NAGOURNEY 

DATELINE: ANAHEIM, Calif., Oct. 28

 BODY:
Bob Dole today declared that a generation of affirmative action programs, including many he had backed, had led down a "blind alley in the search for equal justice." He said the Government's sole obligation should be to guard against explicit discrimination and provide assistance based on economic need, but not race or sex.
"We can not fight the evil of discrimination with more discrimination, because this leads to an endless cycle of bitterness," he said in a speech in San Diego. "We must fight discrimination with equal justice and increasing opportunity." [Excerpts, page A21.] 
 Mr. Dole made his remarks to explain his support of an initiative on the California ballot next Tuesday that would prohibit state and local affirmative action programs. The speech had the political goal of linking Mr. Dole's fortunes to an initiative -- known as Proposition 209 -- that is doing well in polls in a state where Mr. Dole has anchored his Presidential hopes. President Clinton opposes the initiative.
As he spoke, Mr. Dole went much further than merely attending to immediate political tactics, offering his most explicit and extensive discussion of his opposition to quotas and racial preferences. He described how his views on the subjects had changed over his 35 years in Congress, in which he voted for many of the measures that he now believes were misguided. Then, last year, in one of his last acts as a United States Senator, he proposed legislation to outlaw Federal affirmative action programs based on race and sex. The measure was ultimately shelved by fellow Republicans.
"We believe it's wrong to use quotas, set-asides and other preferences that serve only to pit one American against another American," Mr. Dole told an overwhelmingly white, invitation-only audience that had been assembled by local Republican organizers. "The real focus should be on helping citizens who are economically disadvantaged, to provide assistance based on need, not on skin color."
"A Dole Administration will try to be guided by certain basic principles," he said. "We will not discriminate by race. Discrimination is wrong. We will not discriminate by race. We will defend a vision of individual equality in Congress and the courts, because individuals matter more than racial categories."
Mr. Dole's speech today came amid signs of turmoil within the Dole campaign about what to do and where to go. Mr. Dole is known for taking control of his campaigns when things turn bad, and by all indications, that has occurred as he enters the last week of the campaign.
As of this afternoon, Mr. Dole and his aides were unsure where the candidate would decide to spend the next night. (The options included New Orleans, Washington or Denver.)
By contrast, though, Mr. Dole's speech this morning today was noteworthy in its focus: after a weekend in which Mr. Dole has ricocheted from attack to attack on the President and the news media, he devoted 30 minutes to a sober and tightly argued presentation of his view on affirmative action.
Mr. Dole left out the ever harsher criticism of Mr. Clinton and the news media that have become staples of his speeches. But he returned to those themes in an interview with CNN, saying that there is a "little tilt to the left" among news organizations and, as he has for the last several days, singling out The New York Times for special criticism. "They hammer us on a daily basis," he said. "We make a major speech, they bury it back in Section D. They put a front page story that Bob Dole and Jack Kemp didn't get along together."
Mr. Dole argued that his support of the California initiative was a logical extension of his support of the 1964 Civil Rights Act, for which he voted as a House member and which barred discrimination in public accommodations, employment and programs that get Federal support. Mr. Dole said programs since then had gone beyond the goal of simply trying to protect minorities from discrimination and had instead used tactics like quotas and preferences in hiring and awarding contracts.
Those, he said, had become a "source of polarization" in American society, and had "given Government sanctions to racial tension."
He had supported an effort which was supposed to be "transitional and temporary," he continued, "but it didn't work. Every time I drive to work, in Washington, D.C., or drive down North Capitol Street, I see dozens and dozens of black men, without work, I say to myself: 'What has the law done for them?' Absolutely nothing. Absolutely nothing."
North Capitol Street runs north from the Capitol through some of Washington's more impoverished neighborhoods. Mr. Dole lives at the Watergate, in one of the city's more elegant neighborhoods, and there is, generally speaking, little visible evidence of poverty between that part of the city and where he used to work.
Mr. Dole's speech today reflects a final, if late, conclusion on a long debate within Mr. Dole's camp, over how much he should focus on socially divisive issues. Mr. Dole had resisted discussing these issues for most of the campaign, concerned that any gain it might bring him in California would be countered by losses in other states or among women, who are helped by some of these programs.
However, once he decided to base his strategy for winning the Presidency on fighting for this state's 54 electoral votes, he bowed to California advisers who have long pressed him to talk both about affirmative action and immigration restrictions.
In raising the issue, Mr. Dole said he was choosing his words carefully because it was so emotional. "It is easy for the demagogues on either side to trade in on fear," Mr. Dole said.
He did not mention it, but Mr. Dole received a reminder of just how contentious this issue is as he entered the conference hall where he spoke in San Diego, running a largely hostile gauntlet that included Clinton supporters and opponents of Proposition 209 who jeered at Mr. Dole as he made his way through. At one point, he spotted a knot of supporters in the crowd and stopped to shake their hands, but was urged on by obviously concerned Secret Service agents.
Mr. Dole's speech today represented a tactical about-face as much for him as for his running mate, Jack Kemp. A month ago, Mr. Kemp told reporters that while he had come to support the initiative, "We are not going to campaign on a wedge issue" that could divide people.
Mr. Kemp joined Mr. Dole for an appearance in this normally solid Republican community in Orange County, where Mr. Dole's aides acknowledge that the race between him and Mr. Clinton is distressingly close. But Mr. Kemp was spared any immediate discomfort. Mr. Dole did not mention affirmative action in the course of his remarks here.
Proposition 209 would bar state and local governments from using quotas or preferences based on race, ethnicity and sex in hiring, in the awarding of government contracts and admission to public colleges and universities. It would not affect private employers.
A survey by The Los Angeles Times published on Friday found that 54 percent of likely voters supported the measure, 31 percent opposed it, and 15 percent were undecided. Mr. Clinton has said he opposes it because he sees value in having some programs -- though not quotas -- to provide an edge to minorities who are discriminated against. It is not, however, an issue Mr. Clinton raises in his campaign appearances.
Mr. Dole listed instances during the Clinton Presidency in which the Justice Department had argued in favor of preferences. Mr. Dole warned that Mr. Clinton, if re-elected, would work against the California initiative.
"There is no doubt that if there should be a second Clinton Administration," Mr. Dole said, to a quick standing ovation, "it would do everything in its power to undermine 209."
That said, the issue is politically problematic for Mr. Dole as well, since it threatens to worsen his already weak standing with women.

HEADLINE: Don't Surrender to Lower Housing Standard

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Back off on enforcing New York City's housing codes: that's Peter D. Salins's answer to the miserable housing conditions of hundreds of thousands of impoverished immigrants (Op-Ed, Oct. 26). If his prescription sounds familiar, that's because it is like frequently heard advice to go along with sweatshops and minimum-wage violations, since those are means of giving low-skilled immigrants a foothold and of preserving small businesses. 
 Both approaches involve public assent to the lowering of hard-won standards that are morally and economically crucial. Better management of immigration itself, not surrender to third world working and housing standards, must be this nation's response to the squalor that threatens low-income immigrants and natives alike.
 
DAVID SIMCOX
Louisville, Ky., Oct. 28, 1996
 
The writer is policy-board chairman of the Center for Immigration Studies.

HEADLINE: Soweto Journal;
Who Counts in South Africa? Finally, Everyone

BYLINE: By DONALD G. McNEIL Jr. 

DATELINE: SOWETO, South Africa, Oct. 25

 BODY:
"Yes, I've been counted this time," said Jabulani Mhlambi, 25, a citizen of this vast city inhabited by a soon-to-be-determined number of people. "I was counted last time, too," he added, pointing skyward. "By airplane."
He wasn't joking. The last time South Africa held a census, Soweto was counted by aerial photograph: count the shacks and multiply by four. 
 This time, the process is more detailed. In Maria Matsimpi's tiny fiberglass-roofed living room, where every piece of furniture has a lace antimacassar on it and Rodin's "Kiss" shares the bookcase with African National Congress flags, two enumerators sat for half an hour filling out the form with her.
How much money did the seven people who lived in these four rooms make? What churches do they belong to? What languages do they speak? Does she cook with gas, electricity, kerosene, wood, coal, candles or animal dung? Does the house have a flush toilet, pit latrine, bucket or none?
"It's O.K., all these questions," she said afterward. "Because maybe it will help the Government build homes."
South Africa is in the middle of its first true census.
In the past, there have been whites-only censuses, aerial censuses, censuses of nominally independent homelands like Venda and Bophuthatswana, and various partial samplings and estimates. But never before have all South Africans been counted, nor has such a wealth of detail about income, education, birth rates, national origins, employment and housing conditions been gathered.
The reconstruction of this country after decades of neglect of the nonwhite population depends on it. Essop Pahad, the Cabinet deputy minister in charge of the count, said grandly that it "will provide the statistical platform from which to launch South Africa's social and economic liberation." By that he meant that everything from where to lay water pipes to where to start small-business assistance programs will be guided by it.
Moreover, there's the chance to solve South Africa's addiction to shaky statistics. Figures, correct or not, are quoted quite freely in news reports here, and take on lives of their own: The murder rate is routinely said to be five times America's. Unemployment among blacks is estimated by some at 50 percent, by others at 20 percent. Soweto has two million people in it -- or four million, or six.
"Population statistics have been a major Achilles heel," said Dennis Dykes, chief economist for Nedcor, one of the country's largest bank holding companies. Financial numbers here are generally accurate, he said, but when they're crunched on a per capita basis, things fall apart. "For years, we haven't really known what the unemployment rate is," he said.
The census began Oct. 10 and is scheduled to end on Thursday, although some areas, including Johannesburg and the Eastern Cape and Northern Provinces, are behind schedule.
Soweto is actually a bit ahead. So many people signed up to be enumerators that some were transferred elsewhere. "The rate of unemployment here is very high," said Simon Mokoatle, the regional manager.
About $81 million has been budgeted for the count, which the Central Statistical Service says works out to $1.77 per person -- a calculation that seems to presume that the population will turn out to be 46 million, not the 41 million it is usually said to be now.
Because little of that money went for big advertising campaigns like those that herald censuses in the United States, many people were surprised by the arrival on their doorsteps of enumerators in lemon yellow fishnet vests bearing forms, a smile and a polite mention that failure to fill them out is a crime.
But other ways of reaching out, particularly to the poorest of the poor, were used. Street children, lured by tables of food, were counted the first night. In men's hostels, enumerators sought out the "nkosis" -- chiefs -- and in squatter camps, members of informal "city councils." On the first morning, there were 40 "dawn gumbas" -- street parties with barrel fires, drinks and bands to get the word out.
Different neighborhoods find different questions touchy. Residents of the wealthy white suburbs on his beat are reluctant to answer the income questions, said Luke Ngenwa, an enumerator. And they seem puzzled about the four separate questions about how many living children a woman has.
"That's because infant mortality is still high in rural areas," said Marinda de Lange, a spokeswoman for the census. "We tell them that mapping the country includes fertility rates, and we need to know where more clinics are needed."
In Soweto, people are more reluctant to disclose what church they belong to or what business they're in. Census officials say they get better than 95 percent cooperation, but the few who do resist do so either because they are illegal immigrants or because they do not belong to the African National Congress and think of all Government functions as Congress efforts.
When someone refuses, the enumerator is legally entitled to return with the police, but normally returns with a supervisor instead. "We don't want to create enmity with the people," said Stephen Ziqubu, controller for the Orlando district in Soweto. If it's simply fear, especially when a woman refuses to open the door to a man, a female enumerator is sent.
In Soweto, enumerators usually head straight for the back door, which is commonly the main entrance to the house and lets them see how many shacks or rooms are in the backyard.
By contrast, in the white suburbs, Mr. Ngenwa said, he is often left standing at the gate while the gardener is sent down the driveway to speak to him. Since he's a student from Zambia and speaks Bemba, which is only marginally related to South Africa's major black languages, he then must negotiate in English to see the owner of the house.
There have been a few ugly incidents that got wide publicity. One real enumerator was accused of rape. A fake one was shot in the stomach as he tried to force his way into a home.
But the count has gone on. The first results -- including, presumably, a definitive answer to how many people live in Soweto -- are expected in January.

Map of South Africa showing the location of Soweto's.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Off From High vs. Yen And Lower Against the Mark

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
The dollar briefly touched a three-and-a-half-year high near 115 yen yesterday but ended mostly weaker, falling to its lowest level against the German mark in five weeks.
A prediction by Secretary of the Treasury Robert E. Rubin of a stronger dollar, coupled with statements by German central bankers suggesting that interest rates there would fall no further and might rise, were the main forces influencing the dollar's activity in the foreign exchange market. 
 Mr. Rubin provoked a spree of dollar-buying in early Asian trading after an interview on CNN, in which he discussed the reduced American budget deficit and forecast "a higher dollar coming out of this."
The dollar reached an intraday high of 114.88 yen, the strongest since April 1993, before traders sold the currency as a senior Japanese economic aide said a dollar-yen exchange rate of 114 yen was realistic. The remarks, by Shinpei Nukaya, a vice minister of Japan's Economic Planning Agency, were made at a lunch with foreign journalists in Tokyo.
"I think we had pushed the dollar up to some attractive levels and people started locking in some profits," said Dennis Pettit, foreign exchange manager at the Long-Term Credit Bank of Japan's New York branch.
Late in New York trading yesterday afternoon, the dollar cost 114.34 yen, down from its intraday high but up from 114.20 yen on Monday. It sold at 1.5100 marks, down from 1.5190 on Monday and the lowest since Sept. 24. The British pound fetched $1.6070, down from $1.6128.
Dealers attributed the dollar's weakness against the mark largely to fresh hints that the Bundesbank would keep interest rates stable, countering speculation that the central bank might ease rates to stimulate the German economy.
Hans-Jurgen Kobnick and Olaf Sievert, both members of the Bundesbank's policy-making council, told separate conferences in Europe that they saw no reason for further declines in German interest rates.
Other late dollar rates in New York, compared with those late Monday included: 1.2562 Swiss francs, down from 1.2609; 5.1100 French francs, down from 5.1330, and 1,515.00 Italian lire, down from 1,521.30. The Canadian dollar fetched 74.38 American cents, compared with 74.40 cents on Monday.

HEADLINE: From 'Les Miserables' To Just Plain Miserable

BYLINE: By MEL GUSSOW 

 BODY:
A sweeping change in the cast of "Les Miserables," with many actors suddenly dismissed from their roles, has left the company confused and upset. Some have been in the cast for years and thought of themselves as having continuing employment. With the announcement on Sunday that they would lose their jobs on Jan. 26, they will be looking for work, reading Backstage and going to auditions for other shows.
Joel Robertson joined the ensemble of the musical in February 1988, a year after it opened. This means that when he gives his last performance, he will have been in the show for nine years. The letter of termination was "staggering, to say the least," he said. 
 Mr. Robertson, 45, is a working professional who had thought of acting as a career with stability. Before he joined "Les Miserables," he was in the original cast of "Cats" for five and a half years. He is married, with two children and a house in Clifton, N.J. -- and a mortgage.
With income earned from "Les Miserables," he managed to start an acting conservatory in Clifton with his wife, Kathleen Kellaigh, and Robert Sapoff, one of the stars of the revival of "The Cocoanuts." The Robertson family is accustomed to long runs. Ms. Kellaigh acted in the television soap opera "The Guiding Light" for two and a half years.
In a telephone interview yesterday, Mr. Robertson made it clear that he loved "Les Miserables" as a show and that he believed he was making a valuable contribution to it. He plays more than a dozen supporting roles, including a factory foreman, a student named Combeferre, a convict, a farmer and a bartender. When he is offstage, he is making one of his 16 costume changes, preparing to rush back onstage. With pride, he said, "I think I have done more 'Les Miz' than anybody in the world."
For him, the musical has been "a wonderful platform," allowing him to be seen, to work at his craft and also to have a normal life. In contrast to many actors who, necessarily, do not work for long periods, he is not a familiar face at the unemployment office.
Officially a member of the chorus, Mr. Robertson has a so-called pink contract, which means he cannot be fired except for "just cause." The change at "Les Miserables" runs counter to that contract, which is why Cameron Mackintosh, the producer of the show, is offering financial compensation to the actors: $25,000 for those in the chorus who are being dismissed.
Many in the cast will be affected, including two of the three principals and at least three who are members of the chorus with featured roles. There will be a new Jean Valjean and a new Fantine, with Ivan Rutherford moving back to his role as the Innkeeper after temporarily playing Valjean. Florence Lacey, currently playing Fantine, will leave the show. Of the three principals, only Christopher Innvar, who plays Javert, has been invited to remain in his role.
There will be a new Marius, Cosette, Thenardier and Madame Thenardier. (Her role is being played by an understudy and was already open for recasting.) This means that Tom Donoghue, Tamra Hayden and Drew Eshelman will be looking for work, along with actors like Mr. Robertson who play smaller roles. Those who play Eponine and Enjolras, the leader of the student revolution, remain question marks.
Actors in the current cast may be invited to audition, but may not be asked to play the same roles. Christeena Michelle Riggs is in the undecided category. She has not yet been told if she will be part of the new company. If she is, there is no guarantee she will be recast as Eponine. In any case, there will be a very extensive audition process.
Ms. Lacey, who has been in the show for only six weeks, is one of three on a principal contract. This means that employment can be terminated at the end of the contract, which can run from four weeks to one year (in her case, for three months, expiring Dec. 8). At the same time, a principal could also ask to leave the show at the end of that contract.
"It was, of course, heartbreaking and humiliating," said Ms. Lacey, who added that she would have stayed on if she had been invited to do so. She said that she was "frightened about what it would mean to other actors in other productions."
In making the announcement about the new company, Mr. Mackintosh tried to offer the most salutary spin, saying that he wanted "a completely refreshed production" to take the show "into the 21st century." He and John Caird, co-director of the musical with Trevor Nunn, had seen the show in September and were disappointed by the performance.
Alan Wasser, the general manager, said a careful evaluation was made of each actor and decisions about which ones to release, rehire or re-evaluate. Mr. Caird said the change was being made to combat "long-run-itis."
The musical's national company will take over the show on Broadway beginning on Jan. 28. Starting on March 12, the 10th anniversary of the musical's New York opening, the new cast will perform at the Imperial Theater.
After Mr. Robertson received his letter of dismissal, he immediately left the theater and drove to his conservatory. He read the letter to his acting students. On the basis of his experience, he told them, "you've got to be nuts to want to be actors." He said the students were sympathetic to his situation but were still eager to enter the profession.
"You can't squash somebody's dream like that," he said. As for himself, he plans to begin auditioning. Today, he and the other members of the "Miz" family are scheduled to give two performances.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Interest Rates Drop Sharply In Bond Rally

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury securities reached their highest levels in six months yesterday, as new data on employment costs and consumer confidence increased investors' expectations that inflation would not accelerate.
Interest rates, which move in the opposite direction from prices, made their sharpest drop in more than a year. The 30-year bond surged 1 30/32 to a price of 100 29/32 to yield 6.68 percent, down from 6.83 percent on Monday and the lowest since April 4, when it was 6.67 percent. 
 Matthew F. Alexy, chief market strategist at CS First Boston, said the 30-year bond had not rallied this much since June 13, 1995, when the yield also declined 15 basis points. At that time a weak retail sales report raised expectations that the Federal Reserve would cut rates, which it did in July 1995.
While many believe that the Fed will maintain a bias toward a future tightening -- but not necessarily next month -- a few are starting to speculate that as a result of yesterday's economic data, the central bank might shift to a neutral bias.
Kim Rupert, an economist with MMS International, San Francisco, said, "Given the data seen today, it would take an incredibly negative payroll report to derail the market, and the median estimates are not close to being strong enough to do that."
She predicted the market rally would continue. Ms. Rupert estimates the employment number will increase by 175,000, while the median estimate is for a rise of 195,000. For the current mood to sour, she said, it would take a combination of an increase of 300,000 jobs, a gain of five-tenths of a percent in average hourly earnings and a decline in the unemployment rate.
Adding to the market's bullish mood yesterday, both the Commodity Research Bureau index and the Goldman, Sachs commodities index registered huge drops. Large declines in prices of metals and oils pulled down commodity prices as a whole and lessened inflation fears even further.
But the most significant economic release yesterday was the Employment Cost Index for September, which showed a subdued 2.8 percent year-over-year rise, easing concerns over inflation. The Labor Department reported the index was up six-tenths of a percent in the third quarter, less than its gain of eight-tenths of a percent in the second quarter.
"The favorable E.C.I. statistic," said Donald J. Fine, chief market analyst at Chase Asset Management, "not only adds further credence to the view of an unchanged monetary policy at the Federal Open Market Committee meeting in November, but also opens up the possibility, if supported by upcoming data, that the Fed could move to a neutral stance."
Mr. Fine said the yield curve, or the spread between the longest maturity and the shorter maturities, was moving in this direction; the Federal funds rate, which banks charge each other for overnight loans, and the yield on the two-year note are now much closer to a "neutral Fed," as opposed to suggesting that the Fed will tighten, he said.
Mr. Fine said the drop in the Consumer Confidence Index during October, as reported by the Conference Board, further buoyed the bond market, because it suggested that there was less pressure in the labor market. The percentage of respondents reporting "jobs plentiful" declined, while the percentage reporting "jobs hard to get" increased.
Marilyn Schaja, an economist with Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette, said the weakened consumer confidence indicated there would be a reluctance on the part of consumers to buy automobiles, homes or major appliances during the next six months.
Today the attention will focus on the gross domestic product for the third quarter and on September new- home sales, which may also promote the positive tone in the market.
The Treasury is expected to announced today the size of its coming quarterly refunding, which will include 3-year and 10-year notes and a 30-year bond.

In His Own Words

 BODY:

BILL CLINTON
 
Speaking yesterday at Ohio State University in Columbus:
 
"Think what it would mean for America if every 8-year-old in this country could hold up a book and say, 'I can read this all by myself.'
 "The next thing we have to do is to hook up every classroom and library in America to the Internet by the year 2000. You know, four years ago, nobody but nuclear physicists had ever heard of the Internet. Today, even my cat, Socks, has his own Web page. Now, I'm amazed at that.
 "I meet kids all the time who have been talking to my cat on the Internet.
 It's an amazing thing. By the time a child born today is old enough to read, there will be 100 million people on the Internet. We must connect all of our classrooms and libraries to that information superhighway by the year 2000.
"You know, we heard a lot of talk from the other side about fiscal responsibility. And you heard Senator Glenn say that the deficit's gone down in all four years of this Administration for the first time -- he said since President Truman. Actually, President Truman had to raise the deficit one year because of the war in Korea.
 "This is the first time in the 20th century in all four years of a President's administration the deficit's gone down. And that's a good thing for America.
 "It means lower interest rates, lower credit card rates, car payment rates, home mortgage payments. It's moving us in the right direction.
 "We're moving from a welfare system based on dependence to one based on independence. The welfare rolls are nearly two million smaller than they were four years ago.
 "Inconvenient for those who would shout down speakers, but it is. Two million.
 "Now this welfare reform bill gives us a chance to move people forever from welfare to work, but we still have to create jobs. If you're going to tell people you've got to go to work if you're able-bodied, there has to be work there for them to go to."

HEADLINE: In Turnabout For Workers, Wages Grow More Slowly

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr. 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 29

 BODY:
Despite the strongest labor market in at least seven years, the growth of American workers' wages and benefits slowed in the third quarter, the Government reported today. The slowdown partly undercut earlier indications that workers were beginning to enjoy increases in pay that outpaced the rise in the cost of living.
The Employment Cost Index, the broadest and probably most accurate compensation gauge, climbed just six-tenths of 1 percent in the third quarter, the lowest reading in a year, as wage and salaries as well as fringe benefits slackened, the Labor Department found. 
 The report touched off a rally in fixed-income securities that pushed interest rates down sharply for all but the shortest-term issues. The cheer from the bond market, which has been on the lookout for signs of inflation but has so far found little to worry about, was reinforced by a report 90 minutes later that consumer confidence, as plumbed by the Conference Board, ebbed in October for the second consecutive month. The stock market failed to sustain a spike of 60 points in the afternoon but ended up solidly for the day, with the Dow Jones industrial average rising more than 34 points to 6,007.02.
"It's astonishing that the economy can remain so long at virtually full employment and see not one hint of accelerated wage growth," said Russell W. Sheldon, a senior economist at the Mellon Bank in Pittsburgh.
The other side of modest cost increases for employers, of course, is sluggish income gains for workers.
Pay and benefits climbed 2.8 percent over the latest 12 months while the Consumer Price Index over this period rose 3 percent. The slight decline in real worker compensation, said Brian S. Wesbury, a Chicago economist who until recently worked for the Republican majority at the Joint Economic Committee of Congress, "reflects the continuing problems that the U.S. economy is having in turning economic growth into rising standards of living."
The modest rise in the index, which Alan Greenspan, the Federal Reserve chairman, highlighted in his most recent appearance before Congress, further reduced the possibility that the central bank would raise short-term interest rates any time soon to arrest inflationary pressures.
The Government is expected to report Wednesday that the economy's overall growth in the July-September period was less than half the second quarter's robust 4.7 percent pace, a slowing that Fed policy makers had bet would occur without their having to raise rates during a Presidential campaign.
Analysts said today that if gross domestic product did indeed ease back to a slower pace, it would then take an extraordinarily strong report on the October labor market, to be published Friday, to cause the Fed to raise interest rates at its Nov. 13 strategy session.
Friday's employment report, the final major indicator before Election Day, will show whether September's relatively weak performance, in which the number of jobs fell about 40,000, was just temporary or was the first sign of a possible decline in the economy.
In issuing its monthly report on consumer confidence, the Conference Board, a New York research group, said its index fell to 106.2 from 111.8 in September, a still healthy level but one that reflected a dampened outlook for the next six months. This element skidded to 94 from 100.7 while consumers' appraisal of the present situation eased to 124.6 from 128.5.
The results are "no cause for alarm," said Lynn Franco, a board official. "Confidence levels in virtually all major areas of the country are running at relatively strong levels, suggesting a strong holiday spending season."
The Labor Department's compilation of employment costs found growth in wages and salaries alone slowed to six-tenths of 1 percent in the latest period, significantly lower than the increases of 1 percent and nine-tenths of 1 percent, respectively, in the first two quarters. This was despite a tight market that brought the unemployment rate down to a low of 5.1 percent in August.
Mr. Greenspan told Congress in July that what he called the "surprising strength" in pay "raises the possibility that workers' willingness to surrender wage gains for job security may be lessening."
But this seems not to have occurred even though average hourly earnings rose smartly during the third quarter, apparently reflecting a shift to more workers in higher-wage industries rather than big increases in individual pay packets.
Rosanne M. Cahn, an economist at CS First Boston, explained the minimal wage pressure: "Labor is poorly organized to take advantage of their scarcity."
Benefit costs, meanwhile, also rose six-tenths of 1 percent in the three months through September and 1.8 percent for the latest 12 months. After years in which benefit increases exceeded those for wages and salaries, fringes have recently become a moderating force and actually showed no increase at all in the first three months of 1996. The second-quarter increase was seven-tenths of 1 percent.

HEADLINE: Aircraft Deal With Chinese Is Questioned

BYLINE: By JEFF GERTH and DAVID E. SANGER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 29

 BODY:
Nearly two years ago the McDonnell Douglas Corporation announced a deal with China that seemed like a victory for both American commercial diplomacy and the aircraft maker's work force: a $1.6 billion order for 40 airplanes, half of them built in California, half in Shanghai.
The deal had been years in the making, and President Clinton touted it as an example of the Administration's deft handling of a huge project that could have easily been derailed by Washington's rocky relationship with Beijing. And for McDonnell Douglas, it was a chance to make some profits in China. 
 But now a Federal grand jury is investigating whether McDonnell Douglas or its Chinese partner, the procurement arm of the Chinese Aviation Ministry, knowingly violated American export-control laws. And this criminal inquiry has prompted a round of finger-pointing in the Administration, as well as a growing debate over how the United States should respond to China's increasingly loud demands for technology, some of it of potential military value, in return for billions in contracts.
At issue is a little-noticed, $5 million side agreement that called for McDonnell Douglas to sell the Chinese a range of huge machine tools -- factory-sized equipment used to make everything from commercial aircraft to bombers and missiles. The Chinese said they were bound for a new machinery plant in Beijing to help build the aircraft, called the Trunkliner.
That plant was never built. Violating the terms of the Commerce Department export license, which specified where and how the machine tools could be used, some of the machinery was diverted to a military factory that produces missiles, among other armaments.
McDonnell Douglas says it learned of the diversion in March 1995 and alerted American officials. But Government officials say investigators have uncovered evidence that some company officials may or should have been aware before March 1995 that the Chinese had abandoned plans to house the equipment in Beijing. Eventually, under American prodding, the equipment was shipped to a McDonnell Douglas joint-venture installation in Shanghai.
The case raises questions about the Commerce Department's ability to control so-called dual-use exports -- equipment that can be used for both commercial and military purposes -- destined for countries like China, where the line between military and nonmilitary production is blurry at best.
But the department's job is made even more difficult by the Administration's ambiguous policy over how to treat China: as a thriving emerging economy that American companies should race to build up, or a potential military threat and economic competitor.
American officials want to avoid sending any signals that would fuel China's belief that the United States is trying to "contain" China's power, militarily or economically. And they know that if they deny a range of industrial technology to China, other competitors -- chiefly France and Germany -- are ready to leap in and fill the void.
"In dealing with big emerging markets like China, the U.S. faces excruciating tradeoffs," said Jeffrey A. Garten, who was the Under Secretary of Commerce when the Trunkliner deal was struck and is now dean of the Yale School of Management. "On the one hand we have overwhelming commercial goals. On the other, we have to be careful about transferring technology, first because there could be unintended military consequences, and secondly because we could be transferring our competitiveness."
Clyde Prestowitz, a former Commerce Department official and now the head of the Economic Strategy Institute in Washington, said: "There are parts of the Government that look upon China as a friend in these kinds of deals, and parts that look on it as a foe. And the result is that you often have a difficult time getting anyone to take a solid position."
 
Commerce Dept. Says System Works
In fact, the Commerce Department hails the case of the diverted machine tools as proof that the system works. As soon as the equipment showed up in the military factory, they said, top Commerce and State Department officials went to work to remove it from that factory and place it under tighter supervision.
But that took nearly a year. And now the criminal inquiry is focusing on a range of questions about the relationship between China and McDonnell Douglas, especially whether the two partners knowingly misled the Commerce Department to get approval for the export of the equipment, a deal some in the Pentagon opposed.
McDonnell Douglas says that it played by the rules and that the Trunkliner deal is proceeding on schedule.
"This isn't that complicated," said Larry McCracken, the company's spokesman. "We sought export licenses, we got them, we discovered the equipment wasn't where it was supposed to be, and we got it moved. In the end, we think the export-control system worked."
But many Government officials say the issues are far more complicated, and that is why the Commerce Department referred the issue to the Justice Department for possible prosecution. The criminal inquiry has also become diplomatically sensitive as investigators consider subpoenaing records from the Chinese company. That, in turn, could take them into the workings of the procurement arm of the Chinese Aviation Ministry.
When Ronald H. Brown, the Commerce Secretary who died in a plane crash in Croatia in April, took his first trip to China in August 1994, there were two big deals on the table: reviving McDonnell Douglas's deal to sell 40 MD-90 aircraft to China -- the Trunkliner deal -- and getting Chrysler the chance to build minivans in China.
They were big transactions, with billions at stake, and Mr. Brown clearly hoped to emerge from his venture with contracts in his pocket. He took both deals to the highest level, making his pitch to China's Prime Minister, Li Peng, and its President, Jiang Zemin.
A draft of Mr. Brown's speech in China shows that he wanted American businessmen to understand that "sales of sensitive technologies have been made despite public and political opposition."
McDonnell Douglas had worked for years on a joint venture with the Chinese in Shanghai but its rival, Boeing, continued to dominate the Chinese aircraft market. McDonnell figured it could begin making money in China only if the Trunkliner deal finally came together, after falling apart time and again during the last decade.
 
The Chinese Ask For Machine Tools
A year before Mr. Brown's visit to Beijing, China added a new wrinkle to the negotiations. It wanted to buy machine tools to increase its production capacity and improve its capabilities. Most of the equipment was in a huge factory in Columbus, Ohio, and it was filled with the giant machine tools that shape and bend large aircraft parts.
According to military experts, the machines would enable the Chinese military to improve significantly the performance abilities -- speed, range and maneuverability -- of their aircraft. And if diverted, they could do the same for missiles and bombers.
When Chinese representatives visited the plant, many of the workers were outraged -- in part because they feared losing their jobs -- and blocked their path, causing a considerable commotion. Later the negotiations hit a snag, and the Chinese sent a letter to McDonnell expressing concern that the equipment sale was at a "stalemate" and telling the company that if the deal came through, it would "have a big influence in the Trunkliner program."
A few months later, McDonnell Douglas agreed to sell the machines, some of which were more than a decade old, for $5 million. In May 1994, it applied for an export license from the Commerce Department. The Chinese, they said, planned to build a huge machine tool center for the equipment in Beijing, which was the destination listed on the license application for the most sensitive of the tools.
But the plan of China's state-run airplane company, Catic, to use the equipment at a machine tool center, as promised in the license, is suspect, officials say.
There is considerable evidence now that the plan began as an afterthought, and was abandoned well before the license was ever approved. Everyone agrees the center was never built. Moreover, officials say, the Chinese actually constructed another building 800 miles away, at a military factory in Nanchang. And that is where several of the machines were ultimately discovered.
Catic declined to respond to questions submitted last week.
In the wake of the illegal diversion, other Federal agencies involved in issuing the license now say they warned the Commerce Department that the shipment was risky. Pentagon officials who opposed the transfer of the machines argued, among other things, that the Chinese would probably divert them to help boost the performance abilities of their fighter aircraft and missiles, according to documents and officials.
In fact, just before the license was issued, the Administration asked its embassy in Beijing to get a "Government-to-Government assurance" that the equipment would go to its intended destination. China provided that assurance.
Commerce Department officials say they acted properly.
"The Commerce Department is the only agency that has the resources, the expertise, the mindset and the capability to handle this kind of issue," said William A. Reinsch, the under secretary for export administration.
 
Short-Term Gain Said to Hurt Security
But Representative Tillie Fowler, a Florida Republican who is a member of the Armed Services Committee, says that allowing McDonnell Douglas to transfer technology to a "company that didn't even exist" reflects an "emphasis on short-term gain at the expense of national security and long-term economic gain."
One of the biggest and most critical pieces of equipment was immediately diverted to the Nanchang military plant: a "stretch press" that is used to manufacture the skin of an aircraft. It was shipped directly from Long Beach, Calif., to Shanghai rather than to Beijing, suggesting that the diversion may have been planned far in advance. By the time McDonnell Douglas officials say they learned of the whereabouts of the equipment and were permitted in to inspect it, the stretch press had been uncrated and partly installed, but was not yet operating.
Commerce Department officials say they waited seven months before beginning an investigation into whether Catic or McDonnell Douglas misled the interagency group of officials that initially agreed to issue the export license because it took them a year to simply get the equipment back under American control.
"We didn't want to do anything in the short run that would make cooperation from McDonnell Douglas and Catic less likely," a senior Commerce Department official said.
Commerce investigators in California recommended tough sanctions against Catic, the Chinese procurement company, barring it from future acquisitions. But John Despres, the Assistant Secretary of Commerce for Export Enforcement, said in an interview that the recommendation was not adopted because Commerce can invoke such a sanction only if it can demonstrate that an imminent violation of the law is about to take place. This violation had already been committed.
The criminal inquiry has raised diplomatic sensitivities at a time that the Clinton Administration is trying to improve its relationship with Beijing. Secretary of State Warren Christopher is scheduled to visit China next month, and hopes to arrange a summit meeting next year.

GRAPHIC: Photo: This McDonnell Douglas aircraft, operated by China Eastern Airlines, was produced in Shanghai under a co-production agreement that was a forerunner of a $1.6 billion deal that is to produce 40 other planes. (pg. A10)
 
Chronology: "CHRONOLOGY: A Military-Industrial Whodunit"
 
AUGUST-SEPTEMBER 1993 -- Representatives of China's state-run airplane manufacturing company, Catic, visit McDonnell Douglas plant in Colmbus, Ohio, where they examine large machine tools used to fabricate aircraft. A Catic offical writes McDonnell Douglas complaining that talks on the sale of such equipment are at a stalemate but that any deal would have a "big influence" on the proposed Trunkliner program, under which McDonnell Douglas and the Chinese would co-produce 40 aircraft.
 
EARLY 1994 -- Catic and McDonnell Douglas reach $5 million agreement to sell the machine tools.
 
MAY 1994 -- McDonnell Douglas applies to Commerce Department for an export license. The application calls for the equipment to be shipped to Beijing where it will be used at a soon-to-be-constructed machine tool chenter owned by a Catic subsidiary.
 
SUMMER 1994 -- U.S. officials review the license. Some Pentagon officials oppose the sale, fearing the equipment will be diverted for military use, but eventually an interagency group gives the go-ahead. By now Catic's American partner in the Beijing machine tool center is backing away form the project.
 
AUGUST 1994 -- Secretary of Commerce Ronald H. Brown visits China, where he presses China's leadership to make a deal with McDonnell Douglas on the Trunkliner program.
 
SEPTEMBER 1994 -- Chinese Government assures the U.S. Embassy in Beijing that the equipment will go to its intended destination, but the location and start-up date for the Beijing machine tool center remain in question. The Commerce Department approves the export license, but requires strict monitoring by McDonnell Douglas.
 
NOVEMBER 1994 -- President Clilnton announces that the Chinese Government agreed to buy 20 aircraft built in the U.S. and manufacture another 20 in China with McDonnell Douglas.
 
EARLY 1995 -- The machine tools begin to arrive in China. Two shipments go to Beijing, as promised in the license, and two go to SHanghai.
 
MARCH-APRIL 1995 -- McDonnell Douglas discovers that several pieces of equipment are at a military facility in Nanchang, which is a violation of the export license. One piece of its equipment has been mounted inside a newly construted building at a military factory that also produces cruise missiles. McDonnell Douglas alerts the Commerce Department to the diversion. The license for the equipment not yet shipped is suspended.
 
NOVEMBER 1995 -- The Commerce Department opens an investigation into the diversion.
 
FIRST HALF OF 1996 -- A Federal grand jury in Washington subpoenas thousands of documents from McDonnell Douglas and other parties in the case. (pg. A10)

SECTION: Section B; Page 1; Column 2; Metropolitan Desk

HEADLINE: POLITICS: PROFILE -- A Quiet Man's Fierce Fight;
Zimmer Has Set Aside Calm for His Political Passions

SERIES: CONTENDERS: The New Jersey Senate Race -- First of two profiles

BYLINE: By BRETT PULLEY 

DATELINE: DELAWARE TOWNSHIP, N.J.

 BODY:
Like many things about Richard A. Zimmer, the farm he calls home, with its 152-year-old clapboard house and rusty, rooster-shaped weather vane, can easily be misunderstood. Although he and his family have lived here for 20 years, Mr. Zimmer has never actually been much of a farmer.
Buttoned-down and erudite, this three-term Republican Congressman who is now running for the United States Senate is a study in contradictions. Raised in a solidly Democratic household of meager means, he made his way in politics through devotion to the Republican Party. While he is an impassioned conservative on many issues, he is mild and moderate on others. And while many other politicians are smooth glad-handers, Mr. Zimmer is taciturn, even shy. He can, at once, seem soft-spoken and humble, yet fiercely pugnacious, focused and unafraid to go for the jugular. 
 "He's not slick and he's not smooth, but beneath that rough, sort of unhandled exterior is a really solid core," said Tom Wilson, executive director of the New Jersey Republican Party. "There have been a lot of quiet people who have very strong convictions, and I think Dick falls into that category."
Mr. Zimmer's willingness to fight has been evident this campaign season. While many people around the state, including some of his ardent supporters, have complained that his television campaign advertisements are too negative, he has been unrelenting, insisting that his attacks on his Democrat opponent, Representative Robert G. Torricelli, are fair and necessary. And rather than pulling the commercials, Mr. Zimmer has run more of them.
"I take full responsibility for all my ads," he said in an interview. "I do not believe it's negative for me to point out the truth."
For Mr. Zimmer, this is the reality of the political world he lives in. At age 52 and after 14 years in elective offices, he seems to have concluded that while the restrained and modest Dick Zimmer may get his reward in heaven, the aggressive and combative Dick Zimmer must prevail if he is to realize a reward on Election Day.
As his wife, Marfy Goodspeed, recalled him telling her last year, when he first decided to seek the Senate seat being vacated by Bill Bradley, "You know, this is going to be a year of hell."
 
The Student
An Early Certainty About the Future
Ms. Goodspeed worries, she said in an interview in the living room of their farmhouse, that the negative tone of the race will make "some people think Dick's a rotten guy, because they don't know him." Mr. Zimmer himself has expressed similar concerns about the way the public perceives him.
"People tend to forget that politicians are real people with families," he said. "What I am is a product of how I grew up and what my family has been through."
Richard Alan Zimmer was born on Aug. 16, 1944, in Newark, the second of two children. His father, William, a physician, died of a heart attack when Richard was 3, and for the next few years, his mother, Evelyn, supported the family by working as a clerk at the Sunshine Biscuits warehouse in Bloomfield. Mr. Zimmer still jokingly refers to the garden apartment where they lived as "the New Jersey equivalent of a log cabin."
When Mr. Zimmer was 12, his mother married Howard Rubin, a mailman who had three children of his own. They all moved to a house in suburban Glen Ridge, an upscale, overwhelmingly Protestant community of old, dignified homes.
This was the 1950's, and a Jewish family was unusual enough in Glen Ridge, much less one that was newly combined. "We were ahead of our times as a blended family," Mr. Zimmer's stepsister, Ilene Hyman, recently recalled.
To help make ends meet, Mr. Zimmer's mother sold encyclopedias, and after school Mr. Zimmer worked part time at a soda shop and a pants factory. Science was as much his passion as politics in those days, and according to his sisters, he always had some sort of science project brewing in his bedroom.
His stepfather, Mr. Rubin, said that as a teen-ager Mr. Zimmer would go to lectures and readings by science fiction writers. At dinner one night, he announced, "One day, we'll have a man on the moon,"' Mr. Rubin recalled. "I said, 'Dick, how the hell is he going to get back?' "
Known as Zeke to his classmates at Glen Ridge High School, Mr. Zimmer joined the chemistry and future physicians clubs, and became president of the Debate Club. He played bass drum in the marching band and acted in school plays.
As is clear in his senior class yearbook from 1962, where he is identified as "a future labor leader, politician and chemist," Mr. Zimmer was headed toward a life in public service. But based on quotes printed under his name in the yearbook, his political views were still evolving. "Workers of the World Unite," proclaims one. Another reads: "Never mind paying the check. This restaurant is a bulwark of reactionary conservatism."
He graduated at the top of his class, and gave the commencement address. But more significant about that day was what was happening to his family. Mrs. Zimmer, who was suffering from lymphoma, had become so ill that she had paramedics take her to the school auditorium on a stretcher. Several days later, she died.
Mr. Zimmer's sisters, too, have had bouts with cancer: all three have battled breast cancer.
Yet his opponent in the Senate race, Mr. Torricelli, has repeatedly criticized Mr. Zimmer's vote against a House proposal that would have made mammogram testing available to more women. Mr. Zimmer has defended the vote as part of his effort to hold down Federal spending.
And a stepsister, Susan Rubin, says Mr. Zimmer is "concerned" about people with cancer, adding, "He's lived through it."
Mr. Zimmer attended Yale University on a full academic scholarship. Shortly after his arrival, he became interested in attending law school and changed his major from chemistry to political science. He began reading the works of the economist Milton Friedman, who would win the Nobel Memorial Prize in Economic Science for his advocacy of free-market economics.
Even though Mr. Zimmer's family had always, as he said, "felt the Democrats were the party of the people," he began to reassess his own ideology. Concluding that he identified more with the Republican Party's philosophies of individual liberty and free enterprise, he became a Republican. More than 30 years later, Mr. Zimmer said he still finds himself explaining that decision at family gatherings.
While at Yale, he became smitten with a student attending Albertus Magnus College, a nearby Roman Catholic school for women. Marfy Goodspeed was the daughter of an engineer from Grand Rapids, Mich. They met on a blind date and that evening, he talked about his summer job surveying roads in Montana. "I thought, 'Oh, what an interesting guy,' " Ms. Goodspeed recalled.
The next summer, in 1965, he landed a job in the office of Senator Clifford Case, the last New Jersey Republican elected to the United States Senate. While Mr. Zimmer was working for Senator Case, "sometimes he'd say that being a senator is the best job in the world," Ms. Goodspeed said, adding, "But at 20, you don't think that's actually going to happen."
The Senate seat Mr. Zimmer is now seeking is the one once held by Senator Case.
The Zimmers married in 1966, during his senior year, and as Mr. Zimmer went on to Yale Law School, Ms. Goodspeed gave birth to their first son. They now have two: the older son, Carl, 30 , is an editor at Discover magazine, and the other, Ben, 25, is a graduate student at the University of Chicago.
 
The Budget Cutter
A Common Cause, A Fiscal Cause
After law school, Mr. Zimmer became an associate at the prominent New York law firm of Cravath, Swaine & Moore. He worked there from 1969 until 1975, when he became a general attorney at Johnson & Johnson, the pharmaceutical giant in New Brunswick, N.J. He remained there until he was elected to Congress in 1990.
Even before joining Johnson & Johnson, he became active in a new citizen lobby, New Jersey Common Cause, and as chairman of the group, led the successful lobbying campaign for the state's Sunshine Law, which opened government meetings to the public.
His fiscal conservatism was in full form by the early 1970's. He caught the attention of Thomas H. Kean, who was a State Assemblyman, for his Common Cause work lobbying successfully for campaign finance reform in New Jersey. Mr. Kean, in an interview, recalled that he complained to Mr. Zimmer in 1973 that the new campaign finance rules were too onerous.
"He said, 'No I don't think they are,' " Mr. Kean said. "So I said, 'Be the treasurer of my campaign and find out.' "
Mr. Zimmer accepted the challenge, and when Mr. Kean won re-election to the Assembly, Mr. Zimmer became a player in state politics.
In 1978 and again the next year, Mr. Zimmer ran for the State Assembly, promising to reduce the state budget. But the first time out, he withdrew early in the race due to a lack of money; the second time he was narrowly defeated. Two years later, he was elected, and was easily re-elected in 1983 and 1985. After State Senator Walter Foran died in 1987, Mr. Zimmer won a special election to replace him and was later elected to a full State Senate term.
His determination to cut spending and taxes in the State Legislature sometimes put him in conflict with his own party. He angered Mr. Kean, who was then Governor, when he voted against Mr. Kean's budget plan. "I don't think he's ever met a budget he liked," Mr. Kean said. "He always feels he can cut a budget a little bit."
In 1990, Mr. Zimmer was elected to Congress, representing New Jersey's 12th District, which cuts across the center of the state, from the Delaware River to the Atlantic shore. He has more than a half million constituents in Hunterdon, Mercer, Somerset, Monmouth and Middlesex counties.
In Washington, the self-proclaimed "fiscal hawk" sponsored legislation to phase out agricultural price support programs, persuaded the House leadership to phase out wool and mohair subsidies, and helped pass legislation to use unspent money from lawmakers' office budgets to reduce the Federal deficit. He led by example, returning $123,000 from his own office account in 1995. In 1996, when he joined House leaders in shutting down the Government, he put his own pay on hold during the shutdown.
Such actions have earned Mr. Zimmer high ratings from organizations like Citizens Against Government Waste and the National Taxpayers Union. But his fiscal restraint has come at a political price.
After first boasting that he "played a significant role" in helping House Speaker Newt Gingrich draft provisions of the Contract With America, Mr. Zimmer has spent this campaign trying to distance himself from the now-unpopular House Speaker. While Mr. Zimmer voted for most of the provisions in the contract, he now contends that his votes reflected "positions I had long before anybody thought of the term 'the Contract.' "
Mr. Torricelli says Mr. Zimmer has been overzealous in his pursuit of less Government spending and lower taxes. "People simply need to know the price of becoming this self-proclaimed deficit hawk," Mr. Torricelli said.
And Mr. Zimmer's wife confirms that he "really does pinch pennies." But, she said, "when something important comes along, he's got his priorities in order."
As a member of the House Ways and Means Committee, Mr. Zimmer helped write welfare reform legislation, and one of his most recognized acts as a member of Congress was his successful effort to enact a Federal "Megan's Law," requiring that paroled sex offenders notify the local authorities of their whereabouts. The earlier, New Jersey version was named after the 8-year-old girl from Hamilton who was raped and murdered in 1994. A convicted sex offender who lived across the street has been charged.
 
The Private Man
Budgeting Time For the Sunset
Campaigning and legislative duties have left little time for leisure, and most of Mr. Zimmer's reading during the campaign has been limited to newspapers and magazines. It has been several months since he has been fly-fishing, a hobby that he and his wife took up a few years ago.
And the farm work? Well, although he helps out, he never did much of the work anyway, his wife said. Moving to the farm was her idea.
"He had reservations," she said. "He wanted to live on an old, tree-lined street."
Even during busy weeks in the House, he tries to get back to the farm on weekends, and at a staff meeting in his Washington office one Monday morning, Mr. Zimmer sounded as if there was no better place in the world than the farm.
"I actually got to see the sun set with Marfy," he told his staff. In unison, they dreamily replied, "Ahhhh."
NEXT: A profile of Representative Robert G. Torricelli.

Zimmer, above, wielding a slide rule at age 16, after winning a science award and, right, as a 3-month-old with his mother and sister.

HEADLINE: A Saks Solo Effort Seen in Buying Barneys

BYLINE: By JENNIFER STEINHAUER 

 BODY:
Saks Fifth Avenue has significantly weakened its ties with the Isetan Company of Japan, the adversarial partner of Barneys New York, and is moving ahead on its own in its attempt to buy Barneys, people close to the situation said yesterday.
Some of these people said that the Neiman Marcus Group, which in the past had expressed a tepid interest in acquiring Barneys, had become more serious about a possible merger or acquisition with the upscale New York retailer 
 Dickson Concepts of a Hong Kong, which has stores in Asia and London, bid $350 million last month for the retailer, and is engaged in informal talks with the company, these people said.
Although Saks has not yet reached the talking stage with Barneys, it has signed the confidentiality agreements required for access to Barneys' financial records.
Saks, which went public with big growth plans in July, originally expressed its interest in Barneys through an agreement with Isetan, a Japanese investor group, to explore an acquisition together. That agreement provoked outrage from both Barneys and the creditors' committee, which thought that the two had gone behind their backs to maneuver a deal. Although Saks is still interested in working with Isetan, if that will facilitate the deal, any formal agreement to work together has expired. By signing the confidentiality agreement, Saks can now go about exploring merger or acquisition efforts with Barneys' blessing. Neither company would comment yesterday.
Barneys, which filed for Chapter 11 bankruptcy protection in January, is privately held, and for competitive reasons, it requires that those interested in looking at its financial information sign an agreement of silence.
The Neiman Marcus Group is also close to signing the agreement with Barneys' investment bank, the Blackstone Group.
"This is positive for both Barneys and the creditors that the two companies were able to put aside their differences and find a mutually satisfactory confidentiality agreement," said Lisa Stern, an analyst at M. J. Whitman.
People close to the situation, who spoke on condition on anonymity, said that Saks had told the creditors' committee that it would take at least a few months to do its own evaluation of Barneys.
Other potential investors have also signed confidentiality agreements, including LVMH Moet Hennessy-Louis Vuitton, the biggest luxury-goods vendor in the world, and Holt Renfrew, a Canadian-based retailer with a shopping clientele similar to that of Barneys. A few nonretail financial investors have also looked at the books, sniffing around but not really expressing a strong interest.
The rivals each have a vested interest in keeping others out of the deal. Saks does not want Neiman-Marcus -- already in New Jersey and White Plains -- stomping its fancy hooves about Manhattan. Neiman, which has watched Saks acquire new stores In Texas, would also like to limit Saks' growth.
In a deal with Barneys, each retailer would stand to benefit from economies of scales with vendors, in particular Saks, which is making a huge effort to bring in the cutting-edge designers whose clothes are sold by Barneys. Saks also has an aggressive private-label plan, which could benefit from Barneys' expertise.
Each retail company clearly plans to grow. Neiman Marcus recently offered shares of common stock. The proceeds will be used to repurchase the company's preferred stock -- around $416 million -- eliminating $27 million in dividends, which would help cash flow. Many analysts think it may be looking to grow through an acquisition. Neiman appears to have more cash on hand.
Each retailer runs the risk of running afoul of its existing businesses -- the Neiman Marcus Group owns Bergdorf Goodman -- if it does not seek to differentiate the Barney's stores from the mix. And Saks must be careful not to overextend its operations -- it is rolling out several stores of various sizes this year -- at the expense of current shareholders.
"The question remains, how many growth plans can Saks implement at once, given that they are fully leveraged," Ms. Stern said. "Neiman's may be in a better position to buy Barneys simply because it has a lot more liquidity. Barneys needs someone who can expand it internationally. They should be in Paris, London, Milan and so forth. That is where I see the growth in Barneys."
And while the question of the future of Barneys' current chief executives, Bob and Gene Pressman, has been called into question in case of a merger or acquisition, both Saks and Neiman Marcus would like to keep the brothers from running out and starting yet another competing business, taking their considerable merchandising skills with them.

HEADLINE: PARADE FOR THE YANKEES: THE CELEBRATION;
A Parade of Pride in Yankee Triumph

BYLINE: By N. R. KLEINFIELD 

 BODY:
With its lungs and its heart, New York City at its effulgent extreme saluted the triumphant Yankees yesterday in a rousing ticker tape parade up lower Broadway's Canyon of Heroes.
For several hours in the middle of a splendiferous autumn day, work stopped, play stopped and the dog had to wait. It was Yankees and nothing else. 
 Three days after one final foul fly ball plunked into the open glove of third baseman Charlie Hayes and the World Series finally belonged to the Yankees again, New Yorkers made it clear that they were not yet ready to let go of perhaps the most emotional October baseball the city has ever seen.
In a remarkable mass expression of civic pride combined with pure and unrestrained sports mania -- what many interpreted as yet one more indicator of a rebounding metropolis -- euphoric fans who had exhausted their lungs during the seat-squirming fall melodrama showed that they had still more voice left in them. Their earsplitting cheers reverberated off the skyscrapers that lined the parade path in a mighty roar of congratulations.
"I've been a Yankee fan since I was born, and I had to be part of history," said 11-year-old Eddie Padilla, who with a semitroubled conscience skipped school to watch the parade. "These guys made this my best year, and I wanted to tell them that by screaming as loud as I can."
On a brisk and windy day, under azure skies with unlimited blue, the city showed itself in all its extroversion. Screeching, passionate rooters dangled out of windows, scrambled into tree branches, settled on rooftops and choked the broad sidewalks into human gridlock, as lower Manhattan groaned under the weight of a crowd that Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani put at 3.5 million, an estimate probably borne more out of enthusiasm than precise methodology.
Joe Torre, the Yankee manager, who had endured the most profound emotional tribulations during the long season, told the crowd at a rally at City Hall after the parade that he had never seen anything like it.
"I've never been involved -- obviously -- never even close to being involved in anything like this parade today and this sea of humanity we see out here this afternoon," he said. "This is absolutely spectacular.
"What energy! You could have lit up the whole world today with the energy you put out today out here. Electricity is what it was all about."
Up the route of Lindbergh and MacArthur, of Van Cliburn and Nehru, of Earhart and the Apollo astronauts, the men in pinstripes, wearing their aura of success, wallowed in the delicious taste of it all atop blue-and-white floats that unhurriedly crept along a one-mile dream route from Battery Park to Chambers Street. All around them was magnificence, a vivid pageant to baseball superiority. Brass bands played, bagpipes wailed, majorettes strutted and a storm of paper snowed down on the players, coating the streets in manufactured white.
Ticker tape being an obsolete relic, people hurled everything from shredded paper to confetti to toilet tissue. In fact, several government agencies near City Hall dumped entire boxes of public and confidential records out the window, without troubling to shred them. Down came checks issued by the New York City Housing Authority and records of unemployment checks from the New York State Department of Social Services.
The hard-hurling bullpen artists and scampering infielders, the big power bats and the masterly starting pitchers, the gutsy players who held a city spellbound with their invigorating late-inning rallies, waved heartily to the clamorous throngs and applauded them back. The onlookers craned for peeks at their personal favorites. There was Jeter. There Pettitte. There Strawberry. There Williams.
Men in Yankee pinstripes had been down this route before, more times than anyone else, but not since 1978, when players named Jackson and Munson and Gossage wore the uniforms, and when all too many of the people on the streets had not yet learned what baseball was all about.
The gray financial district did not seem to mind the rackety intrusion of civic spirit, as people with suspect sliders and feeble swings who nonetheless play for even bigger sums of money gawked from windows and rooftops as the ballplayers inched past. A window washer, doing his job as scheduled before the Yankees chose to reorder the day, paused in his scrubbing to lift his fist in tribute.
A great many children clearly chose to study Yankees over fractions by bypassing school for a day. Louis Brandeis High School on the Upper West Side, a self-admitted haven of Yankee enthusiasts, reported that one in three students was not-so-mysteriously absent. While the school system said that elementary and junior highs kept their hold on students, it said that attendance at high schools tumbled to 75 percent from a normal 85 percent.
The Mayor permitted his son, Andrew, to take part of his school day off so he could ride on the float alongside his hero, Derek Jeter, the Yankees' rookie shortstop.
Many people came equipped with celebratory banners to proclaim their special interests: "Bronx Bombers Reborn," "They Did It From The Heart," "Andy Pettitte We Love You," "Kenny Rogers How About A Hug?" and more than one "Torre For President."
Some people arrived as early as the night before, tents in hand, to assure unobstructed views. Carol Lee Hauben and her son, Mike Hauben, put up their tent between Rector Street and Exchange Alley at midnight Monday. Mrs. Hauben said she slept in her car in a parking lot near the World Trade Center, but her son chose the tent. "He wanted to be on hallowed ground," she said.
"We're at the apex," said Mr. Hauben, 32. "It's a glorious day. If you didn't believe in destiny before this season, this would convince you."
Christeen Roth, 19, and her girlfriend Mercedes Lohmann, also 19, traveled from East Stroudsburg, Pa., to lower Broadway. Neither bothered sleeping the night before. Ms. Lohmann did not get off from her cashier's job until 10:30. Ms. Roth needed to study for a sociology test, and naturally had to watch the Yankees on David Letterman.
At 2:30 in the morning, they embarked for New York. Figuring parking would be challenging, they left their car in Dover, N.J., and took the PATH train the rest of the way. At 6:30 A.M., they found front-row spots before Trinity Church.
Ms. Roth: "I love the Yankees. I had to be right here."
Ms. Lohmann: "She talks about them all the time. Me, I'm not much of a fan. But I came to keep her out of trouble. All we had to eat were crackers."
Ms. Roth: "I did cut a macroeconomics class. That's not so good. I've got lit analysis at 5:40. I hope I can get back for it, but I don't know how except to pray."
Ms. Lohmann: "It feels like I've been here for a year."
At some inconvenience and considerable cost, Nicolas Richard, 32, saw the parade in settling a bet with his friend Tom Kelly that if the Yankees won the World Series he would show up at their celebration. Mr. Richard lives in Paris. His attendance subtracted 5,000 francs, about $1,000, from his bank account.
After the parade, Mayor Giuliani presented each ballplayer with a key to the city, a replica of the 1812 key to City Hall, at a ceremony on the steps of City Hall. He saluted the Yankees by saying: "Their victory is an inspiration for all of us. It's a metaphor for a city whose people work best under pressure. It's a metaphor for a city that is having a renaissance."
After he presented George M. Steinbrenner 3d, the Yankees' principal owner, with a proclamation declaring yesterday New York Yankee Day, Mr. Steinbrenner told the Mayor he was giving him the glittering World Series trophy to keep on display in City Hall.
One of the biggest ovations was for a guest named Joe DiMaggio, who rode in the parade alongside Gov. George E. Pataki and who managed to play for 9 world champion teams in 13 years. "I have never seen a ticker tape like this," he said, "and I think I've been in a few of them."
With the imprecision of crowd reckonings when the numbers explode into the millions, there was no easy way to weigh yesterday's outpouring against past tributes, though police estimates had 2.2 million people on the streets when the Mets took their magical trip in 1986 and 1.5 million in 1994 when the Rangers finally got around to capturing a Stanley Cup after 54 years.
In an estimate that seemed extravagant, the police concluded that 4.7 million people lined the route in 1991 for the Gulf War veterans.
The city said that the parade would cost more than $1 million, but that it expected to recover the expense from private sponsors. Anyone uninterested in a ball-and-bat game who needed to ride the subway yesterday faced what amounted to a daylong rush hour. Something approximating the entire population of a large city squeezed into the bottom of Manhattan yesterday, and most of them arrived underground.
Once it was over, they had to get out of there the same way they came.
The Transit Authority ran 16 more trains than normal, enough to carry about 30,000 people at a time. Still, trains were packed to capacity, and many of them sped past stations without stopping because the platforms were so crowded that there was no way to unload passengers.
Both the police and the Transit Authority, however, said that people were orderly.
"I've never seen this many people in my life," said Joseph E. Hofmann, the senior vice president in charge of subways, "and I can't believe how well everybody behaved."

Saks and Neiman Marcus Are Said to Vie for Barney's
Saks Fifth Avenue may make a solo bid for Barneys New York, people close to the situation said. Saks has significantly weakened its ties with the Isetan Company of Japan, Barney's adversarial partner.
Also, the Neiman Marcus Group, which had expressed a tepid interest in acquiring Barneys, has become more serious about a deal, some of these people said. [Page D2.]
 
TCI May Need a Satellite Deal
The F.C.C. reaffirmed a decision that bars Tele-Communications Inc. from transmitting a direct-broadcast satellite service to American homes from a Canadian-owned satellite. For TCI, the decision means that if it still wants to be a full-fledged player in satellite television, it may have to strike a deal with the News Corporation and MCI. [D7.]

Rupert Murdoch promoted Peter Chernin, left, the chairman of 20th Century Fox, to the newly created position of president of the News Corporation. He also named Mr. Chernin and Chase Carey, the chairman of the Fox Television Group, to be co-chief operating officers and board members of News Corporation. [D2.]
 
More Changes at America Online
America Online announced a pricing scheme, a management organization, a top manager and an accounting policy. [D4.]
 
Inquiry in McDonnell Douglas Deal
A Federal grand jury is investigating whether McDonnell Douglas or its Chinese partner in a $1.6 billion aircraft deal knowingly violated American export-control laws. [A1.]
 
Executive Shake-up at Microsoft
Microsoft said that Patty Stonesifer resigned as part of a shake-up that will centralize power in the hands of Paul Maritz, who has guided the company's operating systems business. [D4.]
 
Gates Gives Gift to Harvard
Bill Gates, a Harvard dropout, will donate $15 million to the university for computer science and engineering programs. [B9.]
 
Markets Rally on Economic Data
Signs of tame inflation ignited a bond market rally, but failed to impress the stock market. The yield on the 30-year Treasury bond fell to 6.68 percent. [D18.]
 
The Dow Jones industrial average rose 34.29 points, to 6,007.02. [D8.]
 
Earnings Hurt Donna Karan Stock
Donna Karan stock took a 17 percent tumble, closing down $3.25 a share, to $15.50, reflecting an announcement that the company's beauty division took and will continue to take a toll on profits. The company earned $13 million, or 52 cents a share, slightly below expectations. The designer must hope that the fashion mafia will take a kinder view of her spring clothing line than Wall Street did of her earnings. Market Place. [D8.]
 
Oil Supplies Climb Sharply
Supplies of heating oil and diesel fuel climbed sharply, continuing a turnaround. [D15.]
