HEADLINE: Growth in Downtown Brooklyn Is Still Looking Up, Planners Say

BYLINE: By THOMAS J. LUECK 

 BODY:
Just south of the Atlantic Avenue railway station, where city-owned urban renewal property sat vacant for 30 years, a new complex of oversized suburban-style stores is to open next week.
Six blocks away, on Adams Street, workers just broke ground for Brooklyn's first new hotel in 50 years. 
 Across from the hotel site, which is still just a hole in the ground, the huge Metrotech office complex, begun in 1986, covers 10 blocks of long-blighted property. That project continues to grow, with an eight-story headquarters for the Fire Department expected to be completed next summer.
So goes the rebuilding of downtown Brooklyn, which over the last 10 years has quietly transformed the aged urban core of New York City's most populous borough into what experts have begun calling a model for big-city downtowns across the nation. And the resurgence -- which has transcended the sluggish economy that has derailed development in most cities -- appears for now at least to be continuing in high gear.
"Few downtowns in America can claim this amount of investment and job creation," said the Regional Plan Association in a report to be released today, which calls Brooklyn's revitalization over the last decade "a sign of optimism for the entire country, a direction marker for the future."
Nonetheless, the report cautions that continued improvements could become more difficult for Brooklyn's commercial core, from the Brooklyn Bridge to the Brooklyn Academy of Music and from Brooklyn Heights to Park Slope. Among the obstacles, the report says, is the legacy of incoherent development patterns and failed renewal programs that left large swaths of rundown buildings and vacant lots adjacent to newly revitalized commercial strips and elegant 19th-century residential blocks. Moreover, the planning group says, the area is hindered by its lingering image as an "old inner city" and by a city government "that has not demonstrated that it recognizes the economic importance and potential of downtown Brooklyn."
"Brooklyn has come along much further in reality than in perception," said H. Claude Shostal, president of the Regional Plan Association, the largest nongovernmental planning group in New York, New Jersey and Connecticut. "But it is still not the Mecca for business and jobs that it should be."
The report by the planning group, which has had varying levels of influence over government planning decisions since the 1920's, has been anxiously awaited by many business people and city officials because of its source and timing. It lays out the group's second detailed analysis of downtown Brooklyn in 13 years: the first, in 1983, was credited by many for stimulating widespread interest, and ultimately business investment, in the neighborhood.
"The first report started things moving," said Howard Golden, the Brooklyn Borough President, whose office split the cost of the Regional Plan analysis with the Brooklyn Chamber of Commerce.
Mr. Golden said the 1983 report had persuaded the Koch administration to create city incentives, including tax abatements for business, to lure development to Brooklyn.
Although many of the city inducements remain in place, relations with City Hall have since cooled, particularly since Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani has made lower Manhattan a larger focus for economic development incentives. "We are back to being second-class citizens," Mr. Golden said.
Still, the scope of new construction, renovation and general sprucing up in downtown Brooklyn has been broad, involving business, universities and arts institutions. Recent projects include the 1987 renovation of the Majestic Theater at the Brooklyn Academy of Music, the 1989 restoration of Brooklyn's 150-year-old Borough Hall and an 11-story addition to the Brooklyn Law School that was completed in 1992.
"At the moment, this may be the most vital downtown area in the country," said Joshua Muss, the developer who broke ground this month on the new Adams Street hotel project. The project, the Marriott Renaissance Plaza, is to be completed in the summer of 1998 with 384 hotel rooms and a large section of corporate offices, already committed to an insurance company and the Kings County District Attorney's Office.
Mr. Muss said the economics of a new hotel were simple: there is already ample demand for rooms from business travelers in downtown Brooklyn to fill it up. And, he said, because development costs are far lower in the neighborhood, the Marriott will be able to charge less for its rooms -- under $150 a night -- than its competitors in Manhattan.
Other developers cite a similar combination of low costs and pent-up demand for the surge of building in Brooklyn.
"Brooklyn is probably the most under-retailed place in the country," said Bruce C. Ratner, a former New York City Commissioner of Consumer Affairs. He has emerged since the mid-1980's as the largest Brooklyn developer, building both Metrotech and the Atlantic Center, the new Atlantic Avenue retail complex of large, suburban-style "big box" outlets for Caldor's, Pathmark, the Sports Authority and other stores.
But despite such optimism among builders, the new Regional Plan report, like the 1983 study, reflects an uneven and far from complete pattern of urban revitalization.
While the earlier report made new office space a planning priority -- an objective clearly satisfied by Metrotech and several other new buildings -- it also recommended large-scale housing development on the blocks south of Brooklyn's main downtown shopping area, Fulton Mall. Those blocks remain a patchwork of vacant lots and dilapidated buildings.
And while the 1983 report recommended aggressive efforts to improve the tawdry appearance of some stores and attract at least one additional department store to Fulton Mall, investments in downtown retail development have largely bypassed the mall, moving instead to Metrotech, Atlantic Center and the residential areas of Cobble Hill, Fort Greene and Park Slope.
Now, in addition to pointing out several blocks or development sites that have languished for years -- blighted sections of Brooklyn's downtown waterfront, a stretch of Court Street near Borough Hall and other failures -- the Regional Plan report said that a new emphasis should be placed on improving the social environment and appearance of the neighborhood, and fostering more cooperation between its business, cultural and educational institutions.
In one recommendation, the study said efforts should be made to attract more people at night to Fulton Mall and downtown areas, in part by developing a cultural district, including new movie theaters, near the Brooklyn Academy of Music. It also recommended a University Center for students at downtown Brooklyn campuses.
To improve the appearance of downtown, the report said government officials and two business improvement districts already formed should clear illegal vendors from Fulton Mall, remove graffiti and promote old, partly vacant office buildings for use by nonprofit groups and small start-up businesses, or for conversion to housing.
And to create better coordination between business and educational institutions in the neighborhood, the report recommended creating a consortium of executives and university officials to develop new research and training programs, and to promote Brooklyn's reputation among students and business people.

HEADLINE: Metro Digest

 BODY:

NEW YORK
 
The Yankees, and Nothing Else
With its lungs and its heart, New York City saluted the triumphant Yankees in a rousing ticker tape parade up lower Broadway's Canyon of Heroes. For several hours in the middle of a splendiferous autumn day, work stopped, play stopped and the dog had to wait. It was Yankees and nothing else. [Page A1.]
 
It was difficult to detect whether the Yankees or their maniacal fans were more elated. Call it mutual elation on this day of celebration. [B6.]
 
Around the city, high school attendance slid from the usual 85 percent to 75 percent, or just over 12,000 revelers. [B7.]
 
The show of affection for the New York Yankees became a profoundly personal triumph for Mayor Giuliani, the culmination of some of his favorite themes. [B7.]
 
The Views From a Blimp
Paul Adams wasn't moved by the cheers that echoed through lower Manhattan, where praise and ticker tape were heaped upon the triumphant Yankees. He's British and admits to a certain stiff-upper-lip reserve, which may explain why he stayed above it all. Actually, he floated more than 1,000 feet above it. He flies a blimp. This city has a special beauty, he said, which is more than he could say about the Meadowlands, which was once considered a real possibility as the site of a new Yankee Stadium. About New York by David Gonzalez. [B1.]
 
Questions Raised on Tower Air
Federal safety officials have concluded that the pilot of a Tower Airlines plane that slid off a Kennedy airport runway last December made two critical mistakes as he tried to lift the plane off the ground, and they said the accident raised troubling questions about the airline. [B1.]
 
Rebuilding Downtown Brooklyn
For the last 10 years, the rebuilding of downtown Brooklyn has quietly transformed the aged urban core of New York City's most populous borough into what experts have begun calling a model for big city downtowns across the nation. [B1.]
 
Vallone Stumps for Proposal 2
For months, Peter F. Vallone, the Speaker of the City Council, has been stumping hard for Proposal 2, the referendum on next Tuesday's ballot that would extend the maximum time a New York City official can stay in office to 12 years from 8. [B4.]
 
Perjury Charges in Bomb Case
A taxi driver from San Antonio, Tex., has been charged with lying to a Federal grand jury about a trip to Pakistan with a man who was later convicted of conspiring in the 1993 bombing of the World Trade Center in Manhattan. [B2.]
 
Infant in Car Accident Dies
A baby who was removed from his dead mother's womb after she was killed by a car in Brooklyn died yesterday in a hospital, and the driver was charged with two counts of manslaughter. [B2.]
 
Insurers Rediscover Poor Areas
Insurance companies, which virtually wrote off the inner city in favor of the suburbs and wealthy pockets of cities, are now rediscovering poor and working-class urban neighborhoods. Business Day. [D1.]
 
NEW JERSEY
 
Strategies of a Senate Race
With polls showing President Clinton enjoying a strong lead in New Jersey, Robert G. Torricelli, the Democratic nominee for the United States Senate, is counting on the Clinton-Gore ticket to help him into office. Meanwhile, advisers to Mr. Torricelli's Republican opponent, Richard A. Zimmer, fear a Clinton landslide could sink their candidate. [A1.]
 
Representative Zimmer, in Paterson, emphasized the role he played in changing welfare. [B8.]
 
Representative Torricelli, in Newark, reiterated that he is a staunch advocate of gun control laws. [B8.]
 
The Contradictions of Zimmer
Buttoned-down and erudite, Richard A. Zimmer, the three-term Republican Congressman who is now running for the United States Senate, is a study in contradictions. Raised in a solidly Democratic household of meager means, he made his way in politics through devotion to the Republican Party. While he is an impassioned conservative on many issues, he is moderate on others. [B1.]
 
CONNECTICUT
 
Defense Suggests Cocaine Use
A toxicologist testified for the defense that the blood of the 16-year-old who said she was raped by Alex Kelly a decade ago showed that she had used cocaine and marijuana in the hours before the incident. The testimony given with the jury out of the courtroom was the first accusation that the accuser, now 26, had used cocaine. [B4.]

HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE RUNNING MATE;
Kemp Ruffled by Reports of Republican Pessimists and His Being a Defeatist

BYLINE: By JERRY GRAY 

DATELINE: APPLE VALLEY, Calif., Oct. 29

 BODY:
In the waning days of the Presidential campaign, Jack Kemp has found himself on the defensive, piqued by attacks from some Republican naysayers and smarting at questions about his loyalty to his running mate, Bob Dole.
Twice in the last two weeks, most recently on Monday in California, when Mr. Kemp has crossed paths with Mr. Dole, their meeting has occurred under the cloud of a report in a national publication suggesting that the Republican Vice-Presidential candidate has written off any chances of victory on Election Day. 
 Mr. Kemp's staff and the candidate himself have worked feverishly in recent days to put down such talk.
"He has campaigned his heart out for this campaign, for this candidate," said Alixe Glen, the campaign spokeswoman. "'He has energized the base, swung the swingable, especially the Reagan Democrats."
"He's been a great running mate," Ms. Glen said, rolling off Mr. Kemp's contributions to the ticket since Mr. Dole chose him in mid-August: 140 events in 28 states, 300 interviews with local news organizations, 17 appearances on national television programs and acting as the star attraction at fund-raising events that have raked in $2.8 million.
Mr. Kemp is indeed an indefatigable campaigner. And though there have been minor problems and some slight communication differences with Mr. Dole, he has remained remarkably disciplined to the programs and policies that the ticket leader has laid out.
In Los Angeles today, for example, Mr. Kemp was faithful to the script that Mr. Dole dictated, speaking against government affirmative action programs and accusing President Clinton of lying about Mr. Dole's agenda.
A senior member of Mr. Dole's campaign acknowledged that questions about Mr. Kemp's loyalty were distracting, but said that they had not caused any rift between the candidates and that Mr. Dole trusted his running mate completely.
"There is a strong sense that Kemp has run a very good campaign in behalf of the ticket," said John Buckley, the communications director for the Dole-Kemp campaign, "and nobody at the national headquarters of any standing would be critical of him."
The notion that Mr. Kemp has given up the fight is ridiculous, Mr. Buckley said.
Before Mr. Dole offered and Mr. Kemp accepted the No. 2 spot, the two men were longtime political rivals. Both were candidates for the Republican Presidential nomination in 1988, and in this year's primary season, Mr. Kemp incensed Mr. Dole with his 11th-hour endorsement of Steve Forbes. But, by all accounts, the two put their differences aside when Mr. Kemp joined the ticket.
Questions about Mr. Kemp's loyalty to Mr. Dole emerged last week when Newsweek magazine reported in its gossipy "Periscope" section that Mr. Kemp was telling Republican governors that "it's over."
That same day, the two men appeared together at an economic forum in Detroit. Campaign aides said the issue was resolved privately, with Mr. Kemp meeting with Mr. Dole to deny the report and to again pledge his fidelity. But Mr. Kemp awoke a week later, on Monday, to a front-page article in USA Today and a political column inside that revived the Newsweek account.
Aides said Mr. Kemp was angered by the report. And for the first time he went public with his frustrations.
In an interview on Monday in Sun City, Calif., he told reporters: "I challenge you to find a single human being on this earth -- including my wife, with whom I sleep every night, my children, my grandchildren -- there is not a single person on the face of the globe who has been told by Jack Kemp that this race is over."
The issue clearly rankles Mr. Kemp, who in recent days has stepped up his complaints about "sunshine patriots." He does not call names -- on or off the record. But the chorus of complaints about the Dole campaign has come from such prominent Republicans as Gov. Tommy G. Thompson of Wisconsin, the conservative Bill Kristol and even William J. Bennett, the former Education Secretary, who is one of Mr. Kemp's closest political allies.
"It's a really disappointing thing for Jack," Ms. Glen said. "There are the anonymous people who are doing this backstabbing and backbenching and there are people who have not been campaigning with Jack a single day."

HEADLINE: A NATO Expansion: Now, or Never?

 BODY:
To the Editor:
The judgment expressed in your Oct. 25 editorial that "there is no logic in rushing to expand an alliance that is still in search of a new purpose" deserves wider applause than it is likely to get. Unfortunately it conflicts with mainstream ideology. 
 As your editorial recalls, NATO was a creation of the cold war. Instead of preparing for the real challenges of the postwar era, such as the globalization of the economy, resources went into an unending effort to prepare for a military doomsday that was not in the cards.
We still misread the "lessons" of the cold war, of which we consider ourselves the victors. We helped our adversaries defeat themselves and they helped to exhaust us on the common road to nowhere. The absurd notion, rightly rejected by you, that "NATO, should take the lead in incorporating Europe's new democracies into the Continental community," epitomizes our tenuous hold on historic reality.
 
MARK PRICEMAN
Larchmont, N.Y., Oct. 26, 1996

HEADLINE: For Congress From Connecticut

 BODY:
Connecticut has long been fortunate in the generally high quality of its Congressional delegation, and once voters have chosen a House member, they tend to keep him or her in office a long time. All six Congressional races this year feature veteran incumbents. Here are our recommendations in three of the more competitive contests.
Fourth District (Fairfield County): During his first seven years in Congress, Christopher Shays, a Republican, was rightly celebrated for his commitment to political reform and progressive stands on social issues. But when his party took control of the House in 1994, Mr. Shays joined with Speaker Newt Gingrich to advance harsh elements of the G.O.P. agenda, including cutting Medicare excessively and ending welfare as an entitlement. 
 Still, Mr. Shays is not a Gingrich clone. He supported women's right to choose abortion, opposed repeal of the assault-weapons ban, championed a Congressional gift curb and helped defeat a phony campaign-finance reform bill that would have expanded the influence of wealthy individuals and special interests.
His Democratic challenger, William Finch, is a thoughtful Bridgeport City Councilman whom we could happily support under other circumstances. But Mr. Shays is a central figure among the moderate reformers on both sides of the aisle from whom we hope to hear more next year.
Fifth District (Danbury, Naugatuck Valley): The incumbent, Gary Franks, has been a reliable supporter of the G.O.P.'s assaults on the environment, gun control, abortion rights and funding for the arts. Having gained national notice as the first black Republican elected to the House since the Depression, Mr. Franks has become a popular speaker on the G.O.P. circuit for his willingness to blast affirmative-action programs. We greatly prefer his Democratic opponent, former State Senator James Maloney, whose balanced approach to social and economic issues seems more in tune with the needs of his district.
Sixth District (northwestern Connecticut): Nancy Johnson's independence and sensible approach to social problems has won our support over several campaigns. This year our feelings are more complicated. Our strong disagreement with some of her votes is balanced by our conviction that she is a valuable voice for moderation within a G.O.P. leadership that tilts far to the right. We believe the Medicare bill she helped develop was too harsh. As chairwoman of the Ethics Committee, she dawdled unconscionably before rallying the committee to appoint an independent counsel to conduct an impartial investigation of charges against Mr. Gingrich. We are hopeful that she will bring this inquiry to an honorable, credible conclusion.
The choice here is made doubly difficult because Mrs. Johnson's Democratic opponent, Charlotte Koskoff, has run a passionate and energetic campaign despite a shortage of campaign money and support from the Democratic organization. Even so, we support Mrs. Johnson. When Congress and the President finally get around to addressing explosive issues like entitlement reform, we would prefer to have thoughtful legislators on the G.O.P. side of the table.
House Republicans are badly in need of the measured leadership that we have come to associate with Mrs. Johnson at her best. We did not see enough of it in the 104th Congress. But she seems to have learned from her missteps.

CORRECTION-DATE: October 31, 1996, Thursday

 CORRECTION:
An editorial yesterday referred erroneously to Representative Gary Franks, Republican of Connecticut, as an opponent of abortion rights. Mr. Franks is pro-choice.

HEADLINE: Writing Policies In Cities Once Written Off;
Protest and Possible Profit Bring Back the Insurers

BYLINE: By JOSEPH B. TREASTER 

 BODY:
On a chill, gray morning seven months ago, State Farm raised its red and white banner in the heart of Harlem, on 125th Street, where shops like Ben & Jerry's and Capezio's alternate with boarded-up buildings, storefront churches, luncheonettes and used-furniture stores. It was the first time the company, the country's biggest insurer of homes and automobiles, had ventured into the neighborhood.
Most other big insurers had also given Harlem a wide berth. So when Linda Burchett, a former claims adjuster, opened the agency's doors, customers flocked in. 
 In three months, starting with no leads, she sold 50 homeowner policies. By now she has insured more than 100 homes and has sold scores of auto and life policies, becoming one of State Farm's top rookie agents.
Ms. Burchett, a small, engaging woman with two assistants, one of whom speaks Spanish, is at the leading edge of a movement: After years of pleading, lawsuits and political pressure from residents and advocacy groups, the insurance industry is rediscovering the inner city.
Companies that virtually wrote off the urban core in favor of the suburbs and wealthy pockets of cities -- where insurers say crime and the risk of fire are lower -- now say they see opportunity in Harlem and other communities populated mainly by poor and working-class families, many of them black or Hispanic.
Indeed, for some, business has been brisk. Agents in the Bronx and Brooklyn rank even higher in State Farm's national standings than Ms. Burchett does. Monica Williams, an Allstate manager, says sales have exceeded expectations at an office her company opened in the Bedford-Stuyvesant section of Brooklyn in August.
The refusal by insurers to sell policies in certain areas, a practice often called redlining, attracted national attention four years ago when California regulators discovered that nearly half the homes and businesses damaged in the riots in south Los Angeles were not insured.
Since then advocacy groups have mounted protests in New York, Chicago, St. Louis and more than two dozen other cities. They have marched and chanted in front of insurance offices and at a meeting of the National Association of Insurance Commissioners. They have instigated public hearings in which people have told stories of being unable to obtain insurance. And Democrats in Congress have pushed for legislation to make insurance more available and affordable in the inner city.
Posing as customers, members of the Association of Community Organizations for Reform Now, or ACORN, and the National Fair Housing Alliance found that agents were five times as likely to refuse to sell insurance to residents of poor urban neighborhoods as to those in higher-income, mainly white, areas. And when policies were offered, they were often twice as expensive as those available elsewhere.
Even as insurers open offices in urban areas from which they have long been absent, some of the disparities persist. In Harlem, State Farm charges $630 annually for a standard homeowner's policy on a $100,000 wood-frame house; if a house could be found for that price in the wealthy suburb of Scarsdale, N.Y., a policy would cost $309. In Philadelphia, the homeowner's unit of the Prudential Insurance Company of America charges $654 a year to insure a brick home of that value; in suburban Montgomery County the rate is $318.
Company officials say the price disparities reflect higher risks in the cities.
But regulators say such calculations may be skewed. Because so few policies have been sold in the inner city, said John Calagna, a spokesman for the New York State Insurance Department, there is no real sense of what the risk is. "If they wrote more policies in the inner city," Mr. Calagna said, "the companies would see that the losses in Harlem would be about the same as in Scarsdale."
ACORN, too, argues that insurers have long overstated the risk of urban coverage. But it says that whatever the risk may be, it can be lowered through education and the use of smoke detectors and anti-theft devices.
Several court battles have gone against insurers, and other cases have been settled through negotiation. Last year, the American Family Mutual Insurance Company of Madison, Wis., agreed to pay $14.5 million to settle a suit in Milwaukee. Plaintiffs' lawyers said the company's conduct was exemplified by a note from a supervisor to an agent to "quit writing all those blacks." And this summer, State Farm, which insures nearly one out of four homes in the country, agreed to sweeping changes in its national sales practices to resolve a discrimination complaint by the National Fair Housing Alliance in Toledo, Ohio.
In that agreement, State Farm promised to open more urban offices and said it would no longer consider the age and value of a home as barriers for issuing policies. Shanna L. Smith, executive director of the Fair Housing Alliance, said such practices had been widespread in the industry and had denied full coverage to many urban homeowners.
Other companies are changing their practices, as well. Allstate, beyond mirroring the steps taken by State Farm, will now sell insurance covering the full cost of replacing a home. Without such coverage, Ms. Smith said, people sometimes decide not to rebuild after a fire, contributing to the decline of cities.
Still, the industry's critics are not letting up. Some of the biggest companies are trying to fend off complaints to the Federal Department of Housing and Urban Development. And more lawsuits are pending, such as one in Missouri that accuses 23 insurers, including Allstate, Prudential and several subsidiaries of the Chubb Corporation, of discrimination in the marketing of policies in St. Louis and Kansas City.
While many companies acknowledge that city dwellers have often found it difficult to get coverage, they deny that they deliberately discriminated. It has been a question not of race or class, they say, but of economics. "When folks started migrating from the cities to the suburbs, we followed them," said Philip D'Achille, a senior vice president at Prudential.
As time went on, insurers shied away from the cities, executives say privately, for much the same reason that many now try to sell fewer policies in coastal areas. No matter what they charge, they say, they worry that huge claims could swamp them.
Most industrial states, including New York, tried to fill the void by establishing special insurance plans for property owners in poor urban areas. But these plans, called FAIR -- for Fair Access to Insurance Requirements -- provide only bare-bones coverage and cost at least 20 percent more than standard policies. Because of the cost and inferior coverage, advocates say, some people choose to go uninsured. Others are simply not aware of the plans.
Today, as they strive to sell more policies in the inner city, many insurers are teaming up with community groups -- including some of their harshest critics -- both to reach out to a ready pool of potential customers and to conduct seminars on protecting homes from fire and theft.
ACORN is working directly with Prudential in Philadelphia and with the Travelers/Aetna Property Casualty Corporation in Baltimore. Both companies have agreed to offer homeowners' policies at a discount to residents who join ACORN, attend a safety seminar and install security devices. Allstate and the Nationwide Mutual Insurance Company are also working with community groups in half a dozen cities.
Whether such efforts are necessary is not clear. Bruce Macdonald, president of the Roland B. Macdonald Agency in Boston, said he had been selling homeowners' policies from Aetna and a local company in the inner city for years, with losses generally no higher than for policies he sells in the suburbs.
"Many people in the city see their home as their entry to the middle class," Mr. Macdonald said. "They've worked very hard to make the down payment. They don't want losses. They install alarms and watch over each other's property. That's not always the case in the suburbs."
Many insurance executives say they see the inner city as a new marketing frontier. Travelers/Aetna officials, for example, say they began recruiting agents in half a dozen cities after studying Census Bureau projections showing that the fastest-growing parts of the population were black, Hispanic and Asian.
"You look at which market segments are growing," said Donald W. Davis, who is in charge of urban markets for Travelers, "and you say, 'Wow, here's an opportunity.' "
In the last year, State Farm has set up shop not only in three inner-city areas in New York but also in Cleveland, Atlanta and several other cities. Besides its office in Bedford-Stuyvesant, Allstate has opened agencies in inner-city parts of Detroit, Cleveland, Atlanta and -- as of tomorrow -- Dallas. Nationwide has gone into Chicago, San Antonio and Baltimore.
Still, insurers are moving at less than full speed. Most new urban offices are small -- often with one or two people -- and the vast majority of the 1,100 American companies that sell homeowner insurance are still on the sidelines. In its inner-city recruiting, Travelers/Aetna has added 18 agents to its nationwide sales force of 7,000.
The companies say they are merely being prudent, testing the waters before making big commitments.
Critics, however, say the answer can be found in simple economics. Even though the cities constitute new sales possibilities, one industry expert said, going after wealthier customers continues to make more sense financially. "The insurance companies don't really want to be in the inner city," said the expert, who insisted on not being identified, out of concern for damaging his relationships with insurers. "It takes the same amount of effort to sell a $50,000 policy as it does to sell one for $300,000. Agents work on commission. What would you rather have, the commission on the $50,000 sale or the one for $300,000?" -- though in New York City, even homes in poorer areas can cost that much.
Even at companies doing well in the cities, some executives say there is more money to be made in the suburbs. But earnings, they allow, are not everything.
"There is also this social obligation as a corporate citizen," said Leo J. Jordan, a vice president and senior lawyer for State Farm who negotiated with the National Fair Housing Alliance in the Toledo discrimination case. "The private sector cannot get away with purely economic considerations. You're certainly trying to make as much profit as you can. But the political and community pressure is on you to invest part of your assets and capital in less than promising markets. This is a major consideration."

CORRECTION-DATE: October 31, 1996, Thursday

A map in Business Day yesterday showing the number of homes insured through a last-resort insurance plan in each ZIP code area of New York City misidentified a neighborhood in Queens that has a high concentration of such homes. It is Far Rockaway; East Rockaway is in Nassau County.

HEADLINE: Baring Teeth In the Drug War

BYLINE: By Joshua Wolf Shenk; Joshua Wolf Shenk is a Washington correspondent for The Economist.

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
Bob Dole calls President Clinton's drug policies "soft" and "liberal," a betrayal of what he says is the successful legacy of Presidents Ronald Reagan and George Bush. In fact, the Clinton Administration has taken the Republican drug war to soaring new heights of Draconian ineffectiveness.
To see the distance we've traveled, consider the remarks of President Reagan in an Aug. 1, 1986, interview, published in his Presidential papers. When asked if he supported mandatory drug testing for Federal employees, Mr. Reagan replied, "I have great concerns." He said that, except for employees like air traffic controllers and Federal agents who carry guns, "I would rather see a voluntary program." 
 Should drug users go to jail? "No," he replied. "I think we should offer help for them." Should drug dealers be executed? Mr. Reagan said that while he favored it personally, such a proposal would "divide our ranks" and "would be counterproductive."
The contrast between these comments and President Clinton's positions are extraordinary. "Our message should be simple: No drugs or no driver's license," the President said in asking that all 16- and 17-year-olds be tested for drugs before getting a driver's license. The Administration has talked about cutting off welfare mothers and imprisoning parolees who fail drug tests.
On the campaign trail, Mr. Clinton has bragged that his 1994 crime package expands the death penalty for drug dealers. And his latest budget for anti-drug efforts is $15.1 billion, compared with $4.7 billion in 1988.
 That President Clinton is more severe on drugs than Mr. Reagan shows the narrow prism of ideas that makes up the drug war. When the get-tough policies don't work, invariably the response has been to get even tougher: longer mandatory prison sentences, broader interdiction efforts and more money for law enforcement. We heard such calls from both parties after the Department of Health and Human Services reported that marijuana use by teen-agers rose by 141 percent and that overall drug use nearly doubled between 1992 and 1995 despite big spending increases on interdiction and law enforcement.
The alternatives to the get-tough strategies, meanwhile, have never really been tried. During the 1992 Presidential campaign, Mr. Clinton promised drug treatment on demand. Yet while 67 percent of this year's anti-drug money is going to interdiction and law enforcement, Federal spending on treatment has increased only slightly in his Administration. And there has been little or no Federal support for programs that distribute clean needles, supply methadone to heroin addicts and provide counseling for addicts at a high risk of contracting AIDS and other diseases.
Still, Mr. Clinton is accused of being "soft" on drugs, and he responds as if he believes it. So the cycle continues, as politicians endorse harsh punishments and shortchange strategies that might make a real difference.

HEADLINE: Currents;
The (Quiet) Return Of the 'Barbarians'

BYLINE: By Timothy Jack Ward 

 BODY:
JUST a few years back, when it seemed furniture design couldn't get any nuttier, Neotu, the Paris-based gallery of art furniture, closed its SoHo branch, perhaps chastened by recession and the appetite for shabby comfort. The gallery is making a quiet comeback in a gentler venue: the walkup of its owners, Gerard Dalmon, left, and Pierre Staudenmeyer, at 409 West 44th Street. An appointment is recommended, but it is worth a visit to see new designs by Bonetti & Garouste and other once-intimidating "New Barbarians," which look just shy of cheery. Neotu, (212) 262-9250, will exhibit at the Seventh Regiment Armory modernism show, Nov. 13 through Nov. 17.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
British Raise an Interest Rate; Sterling Soars Against Dollar

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
The dollar ended mostly lower yesterday, falling to a 19-month low against the British pound.
Sterling surged after the Bank of England impressed the market by increasing interest rates. 
 In late trading in New York, the pound jumped to $1.6337, up nearly 2 percent from $1.6070 on Tuesday and the highest level since March 1995.
Earlier in the day, the British central bank raised its base lending rate a quarter-point, to 6 percent. It was the first such increase since February 1995 and increased the attractiveness of assets denominated in pounds.
The governor of the Bank of England, Eddie George, and the Chancellor of the Exchequer, Kenneth Clarke, said the move was intended to thwart inflationary pressure on the growing British economy.
And Jim Phoenix, head of foreign-exchange sales at CIBC Wood Gundy in New York, said: "The pound's strength has definitely been the dominant factor in the market today. I think it took people by surprise."
Although some expectations of a British rate increase had played a role in the pound's recent strength, many traders thought a move might not come until next year, perhaps not until after the next general election, which must be called by May.
In other trading in New York yesterday, the dollar fell to 113.94 Japanese yen from 114.34 yen on Tuesday. It finished slightly higher against the German mark, an indirect result of the mark's weakness against the pound. The dollar traded at 1.5109 marks, up from 1.5100 marks on Tuesday.
A notable exception in the dollar's mostly weaker showing yesterday was its rise against the Mexican peso, which dropped to the weakest level since Mexico's economic crisis nearly two years ago. The peso fell in New York to a rate of 8.00 to the dollar, compared with 7.93 to the dollar on Tuesday.
Traders said the weakness reflected anxiety about the stamina of Mexico's economic recovery and fear on the peso's future direction. Many investors were protectively shifting assets out of pesos and into dollars as a result. Putting further pressure on the peso was a demand for dollars by Mexican corporations and banks seeking to meet end-of-month obligations.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Home-Sales Report Sends Bonds Lower

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Treasury prices turned lower yesterday, hurt by a housing sales report that was stronger than expected.
The price of the 30-year Treasury bond fell 10/32, to 100 20/32. The bond's yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, rose to 6.70 percent from 6.68 percent on Monday. 
 Bond prices were bolstered in morning trading by a report showing a sharp slowdown in third-quarter gross domestic product. The growth rate of 2.2 percent was within market expectations, but it was still much slower than the 4.7 percent annual pace seen in the second quarter. The report showed moderate growth and tame inflation, suggesting that the Federal Reserve may not need to raise short-term interest rates later this year.
Carl Palash, chief economist at MCM MoneyWatch, pointed to the soft final sales and balanced inventory in the G.D.P. report.
"This combination points to continued modest growth in the fourth quarter," Mr. Palash said. "I think that the G.D.P.'s composition will shift in the fourth quarter and show stronger final sales and less inventory investment. Along with the recent rally, this will set the stage for stronger growth in early 1997. So this should keep the Fed on hold for the rest of the year, but with a risk that it will tighten next year."
But that report was soon overshadowed by one concerning new-home sales. The Commerce Department reported that new-home sales fell five-tenths of 1 percent in September to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 816,000. The report showed the resiliency of the housing market, since bond traders and analysts had been expecting a much sharper drop.
Mr. Palash said the figures in the home sales report were surprisingly robust and did not show the same kind of weakness that other housing data have shown.
"If these figures are correct," he said, "then the drag from residential construction should be over soon." Market expectations were for an annualized number of 750,000 to 775,000 units.
But the market's view of subdued growth was seen in yet another report yesterday, the Federal Reserve's survey of business conditions around the country. The report, known as the beige book, showed the economy continuing to grow at a moderate pace, but it was less upbeat than the beige book survey released on Sept. 17.
Mr. Palash said the beige book assessment indicated an uneven economy, but not one that was particularly weak. It still suggested that the economy was moving ahead at a moderate pace, something the market disapproved of since it was expecting a weaker report.
The market must still deal with some important data in the next two days, among them the figures on employment set for release on Friday. "I'm expecting a moderate 150,000 rise in payroll," Mr. Palash said. "And I believe the market will end the week in good or better shape."
Nancy Kimmelman, chief economist at Technical Data of Boston, noted that yesterday's data did not provide obvious confirmation to Tuesday's better-than-expected employment cost figures.
"Demand was weak," she said, "and granted there was no price pressure in G.D.P., but the beige book indicated no easing of price pressures. So we head into Friday searching for the magic bullet that's either going to confirm or deny this question about wage cost pressures. I think the market has gone too far and I think wage pressures are more serious than the employment cost index indicated."
Also yesterday, the Treasury announced the size of its quarterly refunding next week, and said it would sell $18.5 billion of 3-year notes on Tuesday; $10 billion of an already issued 10-year note (with 9 years and 11 months remaining) on Wednesday, and $10 billion of 30-year bonds on Thursday. In when-issued trading the 3-year maturity was being offered at a price to yield 5.96 percent, the 10-year at a price to yield 6.41 percent and the 30-year, 6.66 percent.

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Australia Is Set to Reduce Power of Unions

BYLINE: By The New York Times 

DATELINE: SYDNEY, Australia, Oct. 30

 BODY:
Australia is on the verge of a major overhaul of its restrictive industrial relations system next year after a compromise in Parliament. The overhaul reflects the coalition Government's drive to increase productivity and reduce union power.
The legislation, the Workplace Relations Bill, is a sweeping revision of a complex system of labor and industrial laws that have governed the workplace for some 90 years. The thrust of the measure gives employers greater flexibility to negotiate directly with employees on pay, hours and benefits. It also simplifies federal regulation of labor relations but not as much as the Government initially proposed. 
 Under the bill employees would be able to choose between individual nonunion contracts -- to be known as "workplace agreements" -- and so-called national industrial awards that set in concrete the terms and the conditions of employment, including the right to strike in a specific industry. Australia's system of labor awards is different from labor contracts in that the awards are handed down by an arbitration panel and govern general work rules in an industry. A labor contract generally goes a step further than an award by establishing a relationship between a company and a union.
Also under the bill, compulsory union membership would be abolished.
The legislation would undercut the industrial muscle of unions by encouraging workers and employers to forge individual contracts. At present almost 33 percent of workers are union members compared with about 15 percent in the United States.
The federal Government, a coalition of the Liberal and National Parties, Australia's major conservative parties, announced earlier this week that it had cleared the way for passage of the bill through the Senate, the upper house of the Parliament, after more than 50 hours of negotiations with a minor opposition party, the Democrats, which holds the balance of power in the Senate.
The new legislation is considered one of the most radical changes so far enacted by the conservative coalition Government, which came to power in March this year led by the Prime Minister, John Howard, after 13 years in opposition.
While the importance of collective bargaining has been undermined in the bill, it does invest greater power in Australia's premier workplace arbitration body, the Industrial Relations Commission, which oversees national union awards and industrial disputes.
The commission, which has the power of a federal court, can veto individual nonunion contracts between an employer and an employee if it finds that it would leave the worker worse off than traditional union awards.
The president of the Australian Council of Trade Unions, Jennie George, said the changes would make life more difficult for workers and unions.
Multinational companies and the Australian Chamber of Commerce and Industry welcomed the bill as a step for Australia, which has a reputation for having one of the world's most highly unionized work forces.
But while manufacturing, mining and automotive industry employers agreed that the bill could increase performance and productivity, they said that widespread use of individual contracts would be introduced slowly and only if there was agreement on both sides that it was better than collective bargaining. Peter Reith, Minister for Industrial Relations, refused to set any employment targets under the new system, saying the benchmark for its success would be whether it led to higher productivity. For most of the year, unemployment has been running at 8.7 percent.
The 59-page agreement was signed by the Government and the Australian Democrats after the Government conceded to 180 changes.
As a result of the Democrat-imposed alterations, the proposed changes are not nearly as tough as the Howard Government would have liked. The changes are expected to take effect early next year.
Chris Meallim, the employer relations manager for the Melbourne operations of Holden, the Australian subsidiary of the General Motors Corporation, said the company was still analyzing the legislation, but "on the surface, it should have a lot going for it." But Mr. Meallim said the auto maker would not switch to individual contracts immediately because it had a highly satisfactory bargaining agreement that runs until 1998.
A spokesman for the energy and mining giant, the Broken Hill Proprietary Company, endorsed the package, saying it represented "a step forward." A spokesman said: "Our principal concern has been with outcomes rather than processes. We feel this change could facilitate an environment focused on productivity and performance."
A spokesman for the British-Australian mining company, the RTZ CRA Group, said employers welcomed the "greater flexibility." However, he pointed out that many companies were already moving into collective bargaining agreements and even individual contracts. The legislation provides a federal framework and will make such procedures easier.
Trevor Cook, an official with Jackson Wells Communications, a large public relations firm with some union and nonunion employees, said the bill represented a "fundamental turning point" in Australia's industrial relations system. "Employees will be hesitant to leave collective arrangements at first," he said, "but in the better workplaces, as trust is built up, more and more will want remuneration arrangements which rewards them as individuals, for their unique contributions."

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: ADVERTISING;
Merrill Lynch tries on a new image by promoting a museum's show about Leonardo da Vinci.

BYLINE: By Glenn Collins 

 BODY:
MERRILL LYNCH is bullish on Leonardo. Specifically, Leonardo da Vinci and the scientific manuscript he bequeathed to the world, known as the Codex Leicester. Merrill Lynch & Company is spending $300,000 on an advertising campaign to publicize the new celebration of the Codex at the American Museum of Natural History in Manhattan. The exhibition opened Saturday and will end Jan. 1.
That advertising budget is more than Merrill Lynch, the world's largest securities company, has ever spent on a museum exhibition; indeed, the Codex show is the first scientific presentation Merrill has ever sponsored. 
 "We are trying to project a different image," said Paul W. Critchlow, a senior vice president at Merrill Lynch. "We want everyone to know that we're an intelligent company with intellectual capabilities. The Leonardo show exhibits the work of a Renaissance genius who understood forces that were shaping the world. And we feel that we do exactly that for our clients."
In other words, the classic "bullish on America" image was fine for the 1970's and 1980's, "but now we want to be regarded not as the thundering herd but as a sophisticated adviser," Mr. Critchlow said. "People are very busy these days, and they need Merrill to help steer them through the tremendous explosion of information."
As for the American Museum of Natural History, the Leonardo show is the first instance of a corporate sponsor's running its own advertising campaign for an exhibition. "As a museum, we have a limited budget, yet we wanted to reach out to the broadest possible audience," said Jeanne Collins, the museum's vice president for communications. "We're grateful to Merrill for making this outreach possible."
There are actually two campaigns. The first, mounted by the brokerage firm, was produced in association with Bozell Worldwide, a unit of Bozell, Jacobs, Kenyon & Eckhardt, and is an extension of Merrill Lynch's national corporate campaign. The targets include New York-area print outlets like New York magazine, as well as national publications.
Merrill Lynch is also advertising internationally in magazines like The Economist, hoping to attract tourists to the only scheduled public showing of the Codex Leicester in the United States.
The second advertising campaign, mounted by the museum, was designed by LaPlaca Cohen Advertising in Manhattan. According to Linda Boff, the museum's director for marketing and promotion, the campaign focuses on such publications as The New Yorker, The New York Review of Books and The New Republic; this campaign will continue until Christmas in an attempt to attract holiday visitors to the city.
Merrill Lynch is also helping to pay for a 30-second television spot celebrating the exhibition. ("Stand in the presence of genius," the voice-over says.) The television campaign will run until Nov. 13.
The Leonardo show will be promoted in Merrill Lynch's mailings to retail clients, who will be able to use their Visa cards to receive discounts on the museum's Leonardo merchandise. Gift shop items include everything from scientific toys and a CD-ROM presentation of the Codex Leicester to pop-up Leonardo books, Codex scarves, Codex ties and, yes, a Codex mouse pad.
"We even offer Leonardo notebooks to people inspired to do their own writing." said Rena Zurofsky, the museum's director for licensing and retailing.
One difficulty in creating a campaign for the Leonardo Codex "was that we didn't want to draw crowds that thought they'd be seeing drawings and paintings by Leonardo," said Michael LaPlaca, co-founder of LaPlaca Cohen. "We wanted people to know they were seeing manuscripts."
As for the television advertisements, "we're trying to get across a feeling of the drama and mystery of Leonardo da Vinci," said Andrew S. Clayman, creative director of Mediaworks in Manhattan, which put together the 30-second spot. "Leonardo was a person who could bring us into the light."
Certainly a $300,000 campaign would be a drop in the marketing bucket for a giant corporation, but that total "is unusually generous for a museum exhibition," Schuyler G. Chapin, New York City's Commissioner of Cultural Affairs, said. "This is an excellent example of public-private partnership, highlighting an exhibition that is a true rarity, cutting across science and the arts."
To be sure, there could be some irony in associating Merrill Lynch's newly intellectual corporate image with Leonardo. The Codex demonstrates that, despite his acknowledged genius, the master was often wrong. "The point is that Leonardo sought to understand the way things work," said Mr. Critchlow of Merrill Lynch, "and it is much better to make that effort than not to."

HEADLINE: China's Politics of Punishment

 BODY:
By sentencing the most prominent democratic activist of the Tiananmen Square generation to a new 11-year prison sentence yesterday, China's authoritarian Government once again demonstrated its intolerance of peaceful political dissent and its contempt for the United States. It is increasingly hard to see how Washington can move toward closer relations with a regime that so brazenly abuses human rights.
After high tensions with China over Taiwan earlier this year, the Clinton Administration has been trying to improve its relations with Beijing. If President Clinton wins re-election, Secretary of State Warren Christopher plans to use a late November stop in Beijing to arrange an exchange of state visits between Mr. Clinton and his Chinese counterpart, Jiang Zemin. 
 Those plans should be reconsidered. Yesterday's harsh sentencing of Wang Dan, which came just hours after a top State Department official completed talks in Beijing with Chinese leaders, was the latest move in a recent wave of repression.
Mr. Christopher will painfully recall that when he last visited Beijing, in 1994, China deliberately humiliated him by rounding up top democrats, including Wei Jingsheng, who had just met with State Department officials. There is no point in the Administration's again exposing its top diplomat, or the President himself, to such hostile behavior.
Wang Dan, who is 27, has already spent most of his young adulthood as a political prisoner. The new sentence means he will probably be incarcerated until he is nearly 40. Prosecutors absurdly argued that articles he wrote, including one for The New York Times, and a University of California correspondence course he took were part of an attempt to overthrow the Chinese Government.
Beijing seems to have designated Wang Dan for the same kind of lifetime persecution it has inflicted on Wei Jingsheng, founder of China's modern democracy movement. Mr. Wei served 14 1/2 years at hard labor for advocating political liberalization during the brief Democracy Wall era of the 1970's. Shortly after his release he was rearrested and is now serving a new 14-year sentence.
Earlier this month Beijing condemned Liu Xiaobo, a literary critic and Tiananmen Square veteran, to three years at hard labor for a petition criticizing President Jiang. Last month it sentenced another former student leader, Guo Haifeng, to seven years for "hooliganism."
China will not achieve political stability or an advanced economy by locking up those who call for open decision-making and liberty of thought. Nor will it develop warmer ties with American leaders by abusing the rights of its own people.

HEADLINE: For Congress From New Jersey

 BODY:
New Jersey voters face interesting choices in several races, between Democrats pledging to overturn the radical Republican agenda and Republicans who describe themselves as fiscal conservatives but who also support the environment, gun control and abortion rights. Here are our endorsements in some competitive races in the state.
Fifth District (parts of Bergen, Sussex, Passaic and Warren counties): Representative Marge Roukema, a Republican, has ably represented the area since 1981 and has been an advocate of the environment, the arts, women's right to choose abortion, banning assault weapons and the Family Leave Act. She also helped save Sterling Forest. But her record is marred by an almost xenophobic tone on immigration issues, particularly her advocacy of a bill making English the nation's official language. Her Democratic opponent, Bill Auer, has waged a worthy campaign against long odds, but we endorse Mrs. Roukema in the hope that she will moderate her views on immigration. 
 Seventh District (Somerset, Union, Middlesex and Essex counties): Representative Bob Franks is an energetic Republican elected in 1992. His Democratic opponent, Larry Lerner, is a businessman with a record of civic activities and human rights work. Mr. Franks backed the excessive cuts in Medicare, Medicaid and welfare spending in the last Congress but was one of the strongest champions of the arts on Capitol Hill. He supports gun control, abortion rights and the ban on Congressional gifts and has tirelessly advocated Federal help to dredge the Port of New York and New Jersey.
His record on environmental issues has also been good, with one conspicuous exception -- a vote to weaken the Clean Water Act in 1995. Mr. Franks argues that this was a tactical move to protect other parts of the bill -- like curbing sewage dumping and testing water quality at beaches -- and lay the groundwork for killing the anti-environmental provisions in committee. Mr. Franks also has a good record on the harbor-dredging projects that are important to the area's economy and environment. We believe his overall record as a moderate coalition-builder merits his re-election.
Eighth District (parts of Passaic and Essex counties): Representative William Martini, a freshman Republican, is in a tough race with his Democratic challenger, William J. Pascrell Jr., the Mayor of Paterson and a member of the State Assembly. Mr. Martini voted for the Republican agenda on spending cuts but, like some other colleagues, broke with his party on campaign-finance reform and gun control. He has been strong on the environment and worked to save Sterling Forest. Mr. Pascrell, however, a traditional Democrat who believes in government programs and the old alliance of labor and minorities, could provide valuable support for urban issues. On balance, we favor Mr. Pascrell as better suited for the district's broad needs.
Ninth District (parts of Bergen and Hudson counties): Both Kathleen Donovan, a Republican, and Steve Rothman, a Democrat, are vigorous moderates in their 40's running for the seat now held by Representative Robert Torricelli, who is running for Senate. Ms. Donovan, the first woman to head the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey, has pro-choice, pro-environment, pro-gun-control positions and is close to Gov. Christine Todd Whitman. She has the potential to build alliances with moderate Democrats and balance her party's rightward tilt. Mr. Rothman, a Surrogate's Court judge and former Mayor of Englewood, has an excellent future, but Ms. Donovan seems better positioned to work with moderates from both parties on the programs New Jersey needs.
12th District (central New Jersey): Michael Pappas, a conservative who opposes abortion and gun control, won a Republican primary election against two opponents who split the moderate vote. The district is now represented by the Republican Senate candidate, Richard Zimmer. The Democratic candidate, David Del Vecchio, the Mayor of Lambertville, is a fiscal conservative with an earnest manner and constructive views. Mr. Pappas, who declined to be interviewed by The Times, shows little promise of expanding beyond his narrow base. We endorse Mr. Del Vecchio.

HEADLINE: Caracas Journal;
Where Gas Guzzlers Live On, Kings of the Road

BYLINE: By DIANA JEAN SCHEMO 

DATELINE: CARACAS, Venezuela, Oct. 25

 BODY:
When Salomon Romero bought his Chevy Malibu 22 years ago, it must have been a beautiful moment.
Back in 1974, Venezuela was flush with oil money, and gasoline flowed as easy and cheap as water. Venezuelans on shopping sprees in Miami were nicknamed damedos, or give me two's. American cars -- big, sturdy and unabashedly gas-guzzling -- were the country's badge of opulence. 
 But if Mr. Romero imagined that many new cars would follow the Malibu, he was wrong. The moneyed days of the 1970's proved a brief flash of plenty that would disappear before the decade was out. What would be left, other than the memories, would be the cars that wouldn't die: armies of Caprices and Valiants and El Dorados chugging across Venezuela.
In all but the wealthiest neighborhoods or towns, streets are filled with the six- or eight-cylinder workhorses sold here by United States auto makers. For America's gas crisis was Venezuela's oil boom. While big cars became the dinosaurs of Detroit, here they were embraced.
These days, Mr. Romero uses wires, paste and screws to patch the bits of his car that give out, and finds ingenious uses for whatever is beyond repair. A metal brace fastened inside the door jamb and running the width of the car props up the front seat, while the air-conditioning vents hold pens and disposable razors. A switch, instead of a key, turns on the ignition.
There is something sad, like a socialite in a frayed dress, about the legions of old cars that were considered luxuries when they were new. They still roll across Venezuela, but their bodies are rusted bare in spots, their hoods held down by string. To cut down glare, many have a dark purple film over the windows, with only a mask-like space left clear across the windshield.
With the profusion of outsized cars, and the difficulty of affording a smaller, sporty model, has come junker mystique. Their drivers say that big cars are safer and more powerful, and that parts are cheaper. Besides, added Cruz Urbano, who drives a 1982 Chevrolet Caprice in Porto Ordaz, "only women like smaller cars."
Most prominent among the arguments for ample metal is that as long as there are so many big cars on the road, it would be suicidal to drive anything smaller.
"Those Japanese cars look like they're disposable," said Juan Fernandez Veiga, a car dealer here. "One little knock and they're finished."
Francisco Gomez Oseja, a 61-year-old taxi driver here, said the hills around Caracas required a strong car. "If you have a small four-cylinder you can't do it."
A few years ago, the Government subsidized the purchase of small, fuel-efficient taxis in Caracas, he said. "You don't even see those cars on the street anymore," Mr. Gomez said.
Manoel Areilo, 23, a taxi driver in the gold-mining town of Tumeremo, in Bolivar State, said that because of the size of his 1980 Impala he did not worry much about the broken seat belts.
"Nothing can happen to you," he said confidently. "We're inside the car." His odometer, at 83,499 kilometers (51,885 miles), was still spinning. Asked how many times it had started back at zero, the driver's hand went hypnotically round and round.
Despite their age, Mr. Areilo added, the cars are subject to rigorous annual inspections. "They even inspect the paint," he insisted. A raised eyebrow and a yank of the dangling seat belt sufficed for him to reconsider. "Really, they're just checking for stolen cars," Mr. Areilo admitted.
Alberto Fernandez, a taxi driver in Caracas, said passengers preferred big cars. "You really only need a taxi for three reasons," he said. "In an emergency, to carry things and to get to the airport. For all those times, it's better to have a big car."
Mr. Fernandez stood by an unusually neat taxi stand in the upper-middle-class neighborhood of La Florida, where a row of taxis, clean and polished, demonstrated the importance of fine maintenance. His was the 1979 gray Malibu.
"Look how many are still on the road," he said. "I'm lucky to have it." At that moment, three gleaming Malibus rolled slowly down the street.
"It's not the year of the car that matters, it's the person who owns it," Mr. Fernandez said.
But even the drivers of the biggest cars dream small. A Toyota Corolla is considered the luxury car of the moment, though few here can afford a new Japanese import. "I hear that it has good pickup, and takes curves really well," Mr. Urbano said.
The popularity of big cars, said German Perez, president of the Automotive Chamber of Venezuela, an industry trade group, is not esthetic or even muscular. "It doesn't have to do with cylinders or models," he said. "The problem is the economic crisis and the standard of living."
The owners of big old cars say they could never buy something new, or even smaller, with the price they would get.
"A small car is very expensive," said Alejandro Vasquez, a taxi driver whose 1982 Ford Zephyr ran out of gas outside the airport in Santa Elena de Uairen, on the border with Brazil. He watched as Javier Rodriguez, who drove a compact taxi, siphoned gas from his tank to the Zephyr. "It's a fighter," he said, shaking his head and smiling at his vast, lumbering car.
Mr. Veiga, the car dealer, noted that an old car in reasonable shape runs about $3,000, while the cheapest Fiat compact costs more than $8,000 new. There are no popular finance plans or reasonable interest rates for buying a new car. And gasoline, even with a major increase last spring, is still 10 cents a gallon here.
That is why there will always be a solution, however inspired or doomed, to an aging car's latest ailment. For old cars may choke and sputter, but seldom die.

GRAPHIC: Photo: After guzzling every drop of gas, Alejandro Vasquez's 1982 Ford Zephyr halted outside the airport at Santa Elena de Uairen, Venezuela. A fellow taxi driver came to Mr. Vasquez's aid, siphoning gas from his own tank. (John Maier Jr. for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: POLITICS;
In His Own Words

 BODY:

BOB DOLE
 
Speaking yesterday in Clarksville, Tenn.:
"I want to stress something, because a lot of people in the media who don't understand the economy very well report on it. And they've been reporting all these past months, Oh, this is a great economy; it's tough for Bob Dole because the economy is so good. 
 "Well, let me tell you: Today I'm afraid the truth about the Clinton economy is getting easier to see and easier to understand, because let me tell you what happened. This morning we learned that our economy is slow and getting slower and slower and slower because third-quarter growth has fallen to a paltry 2.2 percent. Two-point-two percent. It has dropped in half since the second quarter. Now this is a real economic slowdown. And I might say it's disastrous news for American workers and businesses and even worse news for low- and middle-income Americans who have been squeezed and squeezed and squeezed by lower wages and higher taxes in this Administration.
"I'm afraid the bad news doesn't end there. The evidence of a weak, limping economy is everywhere -- everywhere."

HEADLINE: Liberties;
Bal Harbour Dreamin'

BYLINE: By MAUREEN DOWD 

DATELINE: CLARKSVILLE, Tenn.

 BODY:
Bob Dole grumbles that The New York Times is not looking with favor on the idea of a Dole Presidency.
That is simply not true. There is nothing more entertaining than the thought of a Dole Presidency. 
 Admittedly, it is hard to picture, given the disconcerting fact that, as I travel on the final stretch of the Dole campaign through Tennessee to New Orleans, I seem to have more events on my schedule than he does on his.
But perhaps, after his little chat with Abe Lincoln yesterday in Washington, recalling the meditative stop of his mentor, Richard Nixon, who went to the Memorial during the Vietnam War, perhaps after he teams up today with former Presidents Bush and Ford the desultory Dole will somehow miraculously pull it out.
The benefits of a Dole upset are twofold. First, we would be spared another Harry Thomason Kmart Inaugural spectacle crowning the Sun King, this one sure to be even more self-congratulatory than the weeklong tack-o-rama that marked Bill Clinton's bare plurality four years ago.
We would also have the exquisite pleasure of watching the Dole transition, or, as it would informally be known, Guy With a Grudge Gets Even.
It would be a tanning transition, of course, conducted from a lounge chair by the pool at the Bal Harbour condo. Mr. Dole would need to get some sun before his swearing-in. At times it seems that he has worked harder at crisping than campaigning, and his decision to contest California and Florida only makes sense in terms of SPF strategy. He appears blissfully unaware that he is turning a very strange color, an orangy shade alarmingly reminiscent of Strom Thurmond's hair.
For fast-paced, bloody payback, the Dole transition would rival "Carrie," the movie in which a teen-age wallflower destroys all her rivals in one telekinetic blast at the prom.
Just as Mr. Dole's inverted campaign was more about what he would not do than what he would do, so the transition would be more about who would not get jobs than who would.
You can just see the President-elect broiling in the Florida sun, in white T-shirt and baseball cap, punishing all the Republican "friends" who deserted him and mocked his campaign, openly and behind his back.
Imagine Bob Dole barking staccato instructions at his transition chief, Bob Dole.
"O.K., Bob Dole, let's get crackin'. Jack Kemp. Told the Republican governors the Dole race was finished but the Kemp race was just beginning. Not. Not Dole finished, Kemp finished. Finished. Finished. Washed up. Bye-bye. New policy: Cut back bloated government. No more Vice President's office in White House. Let him shuffle off to Buffalo. Buffalo. Also: note to Secret Service. Next time he does that stupid quarterback pump, break his arm.
"D'Amato. Weasel. Thanks for delivering New York. Keep away. And don't call me Dolie anymore. Not Treasury Secretary. Rudy. Mayor Hot Dog. Appreciated that last-second endorsement during the Yankees game. Lotta people payin' attention. The check for New York highway funds is in the mail. In the mail. In the mail.
"Gaghhd! Colin Powell. Big help. Thinks he's better than Bob Dole. Not Secretary of State. How 'bout Ambassador t' Guam?
"Newt. Thanks for the big help on Medicare, crybaby. 'Bout time for special prosecutor. Get Jaworski.
"Steve Forbes. Rich brat. Almost killed poor Bob Dole spending millions on attack ads in New Hampshire. Put him in charge of the I.R.S. Then abolish the I.R.S.
"Pete Wilson. Did a better job as Nixon's gofer. Not Secretary of Defense. And that cheesehead traitor, Tommy Thompson. Not Secretary of Health and Human Whatever.
"That mealy-mouthed Benedict Arnold, Don Sipple. Not head of the White House political office. Told Newsweek we had no strategy. Wasn't he the strategist?
"That nitwit Scott Reed. Persuaded Bob Dole to beg Ross Perot to drop out. Drop out. Drop out. Fat chance. Fat chance. Fat chance. Brown-nosing whippersnapper doesn't even know how to nail down a banister. Not head of the R.N.C.
"Liberal New York Times, tried to get Bozo re-elected. Take away their White House press passes. Put 'em on the enemies list. Enemies. Enemies. Enemies. O.K., long list here, let's get crackin' . . ."

HEADLINE: Safeway Offers $1.6 Billion for the Rest of Vons

BYLINE: By CHARLES V. BAGLI 

 BODY:
Safeway Inc. said yesterday that it was offering to buy the two-thirds of the Vons Companies it did not already own in a $1.6 billion stock deal that would create a supermarket powerhouse on the West Coast.
Safeway, which operates 1,050 stores in California, Oregon, Washington and western Canada, currently owns 34.5 percent of Vons, a chain based in Arcadia, Calif. 
 The combined operations would have sales of more than $22.5 billion and 1,375 stores operating in 16 states, Washington, D.C., and Canada. The deal would cement Safeway's position as the No. 2 supermarket chain in the country, behind the Kroger Company of Cincinnati; Safeway is based in Pleasanton, Calif.
Vons, a leading food chain in Southern California, issued a terse statement yesterday, saying that "the proposal would be studied by a committee of outside directors of Vons, which will respond in due course."
The reaction by investors was more rapid. Shares of Vons rose 26 percent, or $11.219, to $54.344. Safeway stock fell $1.25, to $42.125. But based on Safeway's price of $41.75 a share on Tuesday, the deal valued Vons shares at about $56. Analysts generally regard Safeway as a well-run company where management has proved itself adept at improving the financial return on established operations.
"This is a good deal for Safeway," said George E. Thomson, an analyst at Prudential Securities Inc. "In a business where size counts a lot, they're significantly increasing their size in a demographically attractive market, Southern California, and they're doing it on a nondilutive basis."
Management at Vons has also undertaken a revamping effort that improved its profit margins in a very competitive but weak Southern California economy. Most analysts said that on its own, Vons stock price would have risen sharply over the next year, but that the Safeway bid would accelerate the process.
"In terms of merchandising or marketing, I don't know if there's a lot that Safeway brings to the table," said Edward F. Comeau, an analyst at Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette Inc. "Safeway can help with streamlining and cutting corporate overhead."
Safeway offered to issue 1.34 shares for each share of Vons stocks it did not already own. Safeway would also assume about $700 million in debt. In a letter to Vons management, Safeway asked the company to consider the offer and to reply within two weeks. "The merger of these two companies will allow us to create a truly great company," said Steven Burd, the chief executive of Safeway. "We will be able to utilize the best of both companies and improve operational practices, systems, category management, procurement and private label."
Mr. Burd said he anticipated a friendly merger because the companies shared a similar outlook and some of the same directors. Kohlberg Kravis Roberts & Company, the leveraged buyout firm that owns 52 percent of Safeway's stock, is critical to the transaction. The firm favors the deal and has 2 of the 12 seats on the board of Vons, as well as 5 seats on the 10-member board of Safeway. There are also two Safeway executives on the Vons board.
Mr. Burd emphasized that there was no overlap between Safeway and Vons stores, and therefore, no potential regulatory problems and no threat of job losses. In the last four years, he said, Safeway has added 8,000 employees, while reducing the number of stores to 1,050 from 1,121.
"Safeway has had a significant increase in jobs and employee hours at Safeway's stores and would anticipate having the same experience with Vons," Mr. Burd said in a letter to the Vons board. "A successful implementation of this growth strategy at Vons should mean a continued increase in jobs at Vons stores."
But the years after the 1986 leveraged buyout of Safeway by Kohlberg Kravis were not so peaceful. By 1990, some 63,000 managers and workers had lost their jobs through job cuts and store sales. Mr. Burd led a new management team in 1992 that turned around the company and greatly improved sales and profits. Analysts estimated that Safeway sales would be about $17.2 billion this year, compared with $14.87 billion in 1990. At the same time, operating margins rose to 5.1 percent from 3.6 percent.
But expansion has been the order of the day in food retailing, and Kohlberg Kravis has been in the thick of it. In March, Royal Ahold N.V., the huge Dutch grocery retailer that owns the Edwards and Giant chains in the United States, announced that it would acquire the well-regarded Stop and Shop supermarket chain for $1.8 billion in cash. The deal made Royal Ahold the biggest food retailer east of the Mississippi, and it made Kohlberg Kravis a fortune. The firm had bought Stop and Shop in 1988 for $1.45 billion.
