HEADLINE: Economic Scene;
On clear-cutting, economists can't see the forest or the trees.

BYLINE: By Peter Passell 

 BODY:
THE issue drawing many voters to the polls in Maine next week will not be taxes, Medicare or Presidential ethics, but clear-cutting: the practice of harvesting acres of timber at a time rather than selectively culling mature trees.
That should be no great surprise: Clear-cutting wood is hardly more controversial than clear-cutting corn in northern Maine where paper is king, but has become anathema to the L. L. Bean crowd in the south. What may come as a surprise is how little is really understood about the underlying economics. Or, for that matter, how uneasy economists are in dealing with any issue involving long-term environmental values. "After decades of debate, there is still drastic disagreement in how to weigh obligations to generations not yet born," notes Paul Portney, president of Resources for the Future in Washington. 
 Clear-cutting the Maine forest was initially an uncontroversial response to a natural phenomenon, an infestation of the spruce budworm in the 1970's that forced owners to use it or lose it. But necessity is still the mother of invention, and the industry quickly adopted gigantic machines that made clear-cutting the cheapest method of harvesting by far. And in 1989, responding to complaints that the practice was both unattractive and unsustainable, Maine effectively limited it to parcels under 35 acres.
It thus came as a shock when Maine's tiny Green Party managed to collect enough signatures to put an initiative barring all clear-cutting in the vast expanses of privately owned woods that lie outside organized towns. With the backing of the paper industry, Maine's political establishment quickly responded with an initiative of its own, one that would tighten restrictions on clear-cutting everywhere in the state and end it entirely on plots over 75 acres. Polls show this Compact for Maine Forests is likely to defeat both the total ban and an alternative no-ban initiative put on the ballot by a group of smaller land owners.
No shock there. The Compact group is selling limited clear-cutting as a job-saver that is compatible with sustainable mixed use of the forest. And the state backed the argument with a study estimating that the outright ban would reduce the fiber extracted from Maine's forests by 36 percent a year. Since only part of the loss would be made up from imports from other states, Maine's mills would be left with 19 percent less pulp to process. This, in turn, would destroy some 15,000 jobs.
But the study is deeply flawed, argues Spencer Phillips, an economist at the Wilderness Society in Washington (which takes no position on the ballot question). For one thing, it makes very strong assumptions about the difficulty of adjusting technology in the face of a ban on clear-cutting. For another, it assumes that people in the timber and paper products industries who initially lost their jobs would never work again. "Either they work in the mills or do nothing," he says.
That certainly has not happened in Oregon, however, which quickly recovered from the loss of forestry jobs after logging was restricted in 1991 to spare the habitat of the spotted owl. Nor, for that matter, has it happened in American industry as a whole, which has been re-engineering at a ferocious rate.
In any case, Mr. Phillips notes, Maine will lose forest products jobs whether or not it restricts clear-cutting. Extrapolating from the experience of the last two decades, he expects the state to lose about 750 forest jobs a year to labor-saving technological change. In an odd twist, the ban on clear-cutting may actually slow the brawn drain because it will inhibit capital-intensive harvesting methods and deter modernization of the paper mills. "Job losses are a red herring in this debate," he concludes.
The deeper problem here is how to think about management of the Great Northern Forest, which blankets northern New England and New York. There is no inherently natural use since virtually all the first-growth timber is gone and the mix of biological life has changed radically in the last two centuries. Nor is there even a clear sense of what uses are sustainable since ecological effects take many decades to play out. There is even a possibility that plant and animal diversity is buttressed by clear-cutting that creates patches of brushland.
The real monster in the box, though, is how to balance tangible benefits (timber, recreation, watershed protection) and intangible ones like species survival. Worse: how to weigh the interests of those living today against the interests of future generations. These questions are hardly new, but the stakes grow ever larger.

HEADLINE: Christopher to Go Ahead With Visit to China

BYLINE: By STEVEN ERLANGER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 30

 BODY:
Calling the battle for human rights and democracy in China a "long-term struggle," the Clinton Administration today condemned the harsh sentence meted out to a former student leader, Wang Dan, but said Secretary of State Warren Christopher's visit to China in mid-November would go ahead as scheduled.
American officials acknowledged that the timing of the verdict was embarrassing, coming only hours after a midlevel American delegation sent to prepare for Mr. Christopher's visit left Beijing after discussions that included human rights and Mr. Wang in particular. 
 Abuses of human rights in China, and Mr. Wang's case, will be an important topic for Mr. Christopher, and for President Clinton when he meets the Chinese President, Jiang Zemin, in Manila on Nov. 24.
But the senior American officials added that other issues were also important, including North Korean belligerence, nuclear nonproliferation and arms sales to Pakistan and Iran, Chinese concerns about an American-Japanese security treaty and continuing tensions between India and Pakistan.
The Administration is trying to build a relationship with China that will not be hostage to any particular issue, the officials said.
Denying that they were playing down human rights, the officials said they were looking ahead to a possible visit to Beijing this year by Vice President Al Gore, followed by an exchange of summit meetings between Mr. Jiang and Mr. Clinton, probably in 1997 and 1998.
Moreover, the United States is talking to the Chinese about a larger "package" on human rights that could include the release or medical parole of noted dissidents, prison visits to Chinese detainees by the International Committee of the Red Cross and a "more structured channel" to raise human rights concerns with Beijing.
In exchange, the United States might be willing to drop its co-sponsorship of an annual United Nations resolution condemning Chinese human rights practices. The United States has failed to win support for the resolution for the last seven years, and some American officials regard the exercise as increasingly sterile.
"But if we are going to back off on the resolution, we need real systemic progress on human rights," a senior American official said today. "If we get enough of a package to pass the red-face test, then some in the Administration would favor it."
This topic was among those discussed with the Chinese by the American officials who left Beijing just before the Wang verdict.
Beijing also cares deeply about the presidential visits themselves, a permanent renewal of the most-favored-nation trading status for China, making yearly or biannual renewals unnecessary, and Chinese membership on favorable terms in the World Trade Organization.
Stanley Roth, formerly Asia director of the National Security Council and now at the United States Institute for Peace, said that the Chinese also wanted stronger military-to-military relations, including access to some American technologies, and that most of these benefits "just won't be doable without some real improvement on human rights" and regional issues, like Taiwan.
Good relations "are not just about good feelings," he said. "Both countries have to benefit."
A senior American official said that the Chinese have been unresponsive on human rights issues no matter what the American strategy, a stand he attributed to political and economic uncertainty in China, where competing factions cannot show weakness on questions of national security and sovereignty.
"Human rights remains on our agenda," the official continued. "We try to approach this in a productive way and so far they're not responding. We know they want better relations, and we've made it clear the full scope of our relationship cannot be realized so long as we have this sorry performance on human rights."
Criticism of the American policy was muted. Bob Dole's campaign did not comment on the Wang case. Even Human Rights Watch/Asia, which has long urged a resolute response to human rights abuses in China, said the Wang verdict made it more vital that Mr. Christopher go to Beijing. Its Washington director, Mike Jendrzejczyk, said that human rights should be "a top issue on his agenda and that he make it clear that any possibility of a presidential summit is dependent on China making serious progress on human rights both in China and Tibet."
"The Chinese read the Administration as downplaying human rights in the relationship," Mr. Jendrzejczyk said.
James R. Lilley, a former Ambassador to China, said: "The major motive is to take a high-profile dissident and break him as a message to other dissidents that they will pay a high price. The second message is, 'Don't count on the Americans to help you.' And the security apparatus wants to tell Christopher that 'importuning is unwelcome, you Americans are living in a different world and we have support in Asia for our policies.' "
Michael D. McCurry, Mr. Clinton's spokesman, deplored the verdict today, saying, "We continue to stress that we believe that freedom of expression as recognized by the Declaration on Human Rights is the hallmark of any civil society." He was referring to a 1948 United Nations human rights accord.
Asked if Mr. Clinton could point to any demonstrable gains on human rights issues from his policy of constructive engagement with the Chinese, Mr. McCurry said:
"It's been very tough going in the area of human rights. There has not been progress that the President would see as sufficient. But the struggle for human rights, for freedom and for democracy is clearly one that is a long-term struggle when it comes to societies that are having a hard time adjusting to the realities of the world we now live in."

HEADLINE: COMPANY REPORTS;
Bethlehem Decides to Leave The Structural Steel Market

BYLINE: By CLAUDIA H. DEUTSCH 

 BODY:
The Bethlehem Steel Corporation, the last major steel producer to do battle with the minimills for the structural steel market, has conceded defeat. The company said yesterday it would sell four unprofitable units, including its structural steel mill.
If it cannot find buyers, the company said, it will close the operations, perhaps as soon as early next year. 
 Bethlehem will take a $375 million charge against fourth-quarter earnings to account for the dispositions and for a write-off of its coke operations as an "impaired asset" -- an accounting term for an asset that will never be worth its book value.
The earnings charges come at a bad time. Bethlehem's third-quarter earnings, also announced yesterday, were $11 million, less than a third of the $34 million it had in the comparable quarter last year. Revenues slipped 4.1 percent to $1.17 billion, from $1.22 billion for the third quarter of 1995. Low steel prices, soggy demand and a $15 million restructuring charge related to the sale of a coal mine combined to produce the lackluster results.
But Bethlehem's announcements did not faze analysts. The entire steel industry is suffering intense competition from imports and minimills, and Bethlehem's operating profits, about $21 a ton, are competitive.
Indeed, investors were untroubled by Bethlehem's decision to leave the market for "long steel" -- beams, angles and other shapes that minimills make more efficiently -- and concentrate again on its core business, flatrolled sheet steel. The company's stock closed unchanged on the New York Stock Exchange at $8.
"This is a positive move that is long overdue," Richard Aldrich, an analyst with Lehman Brothers, said.
Bethlehem did try hard to save its structural steel business, even after U.S. Steel, Inland and others gave up. Environmental and pension liabilities make it expensive to close steel plants. Moreover, the mills had a positive cash flow and, prior to the last recession, they "made a ton of money," said one steel expert who insisted on anonymity.
Last year Bethlehem closed a blast furnace in Bethlehem, Pa., and shipped steel to its structural mills from a more efficient arc furnace in Johnstown, Pa. It also closed the mill that made very big steel beams and confined itself to smaller products.
"They were jerry-rigging this to try to make it profitable," Mr. Aldrich said.
Curtis H. Barnette, Bethlehem's chairman, concedes the point. "We thought revised business plans and modernization efforts would help, but these businesses do not meet our expectations," he said during a conference call with reporters.
Now Bethlehem is selling what is left of the structural steel business. Also on the block are three businesses that together lost $7 million in the quarter: Beth Forge, which makes forged steel products; Centec Roll, which makes centrifugally cast steel; and Beth Ship, a ship repair yard in Sparrows Point, Md.
The four businesses employ 2,235 people, about 90 percent of whom are eligible for pension benefits. Mr. Barnette said pension liabilities would increase by $100 million.
He said he had hired Salomon Brothers and Chase Bank to seek buyers, and that some potential buyers had already expressed interest. Mr. Barnette said he would evaluate any offers early next year.
Even quick sales at high prices would not be a full solution for Bethlehem. A threatened strike against General Motors by the United Automobile Workers union would hurt Bethlehem, a major G.M. supplier. Moreover, flat roll capacity is about to rocket, as the Nucor Corporation, the LTV Corporation and a joint venture between Australia's Broken Hill Proprietary Company and Norstar Steel expect to have new minimills producing within the next year.

HEADLINE: Job Shifting Is Big Issue In G.M. Talks

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER 

DATELINE: JANESVILLE, Wis., Oct. 30

 BODY:
The showdown between the United Automobile Workers union and the General Motors Corporation this autumn is often described as a struggle over corporate downsizing and job security, but as striking workers here are quick to volunteer, the dispute is also about whether corporations owe easier jobs to older workers.
Previous labor agreements already make it very expensive for Detroit's auto makers to lay off workers, so that most workers are effectively guaranteed jobs until retirement. In place of layoffs, G.M. has not been hiring replacements for the many retirees among its 215,000 U.A.W. workers, instead asking more overtime of the remaining workers and hiring outside contractors to do less-skilled jobs. 
 But many of the less-skilled jobs are considered more pleasant than assembly work, and are sought-after positions for U.A.W. workers. The U.A.W. is now trying to brake the use of overtime and outside contractors by forcing the company to hire more workers under a new three-year labor contract being negotiated in Detroit.
To put more pressure on G.M., the U.A.W. had workers walk out here and at an important truck parts factory in Indianapolis on Tuesday evening.
Claude Lewis, a 51-year-old picketer in Janesville who has worked for G.M. since 1968, described how he still spends 50 hours a week lifting 30-pound suspension parts into truck frames. He is angry that there are no longer many easy jobs cleaning the floors even for workers like him with considerable seniority, and even angrier that G.M. now wants to bring in an outside janitorial service instead.
"When I started here, I figured after 25 years I'd get a sweeper's job," Mr. Lewis said between puffs on a Marlboro cigarette, his black parka zippered up against the freezing prairie wind. "But now, with the outsourcing, I'll finish out my years on the line."
Yet to analysts, the continued presence of even a few U.A.W. workers earning $17 an hour to clean factories is precisely what is still wrong with G.M. "It's not a U.A.W. kind of job," because little skill is needed, Ronald A. Glantz, an analyst with Dean Witter, said.
G.M. officials declined to comment.
About 4,800 workers at the assembly plant here and 2,750 workers at a metal-stamping plant in Indianapolis walked out on Tuesday evening. The strikes are the latest gambit by the U.A.W. to force G.M. to guarantee that its employment of U.A.W. workers will stay at 95 percent or more of current levels for the next three years.
While the Ford Motor Company and the Chrysler Corporation have already accepted this provision, G.M. is resisting. The company uses more labor to produce each car or truck than any other auto maker in North America, partly because G.M. makes a higher proportion of its parts in-house.
But as Mr. Lewis indicated, the dispute over the 95 percent guarantee is only partly about job security. G.M. offered in September to guarantee that none of its current workers will lose their jobs, an offer that the U.A.W. declined. With roughly 5 percent of G.M.'s aging workers already retiring each year, such a provision would allow the company to reduce the staffing of its factories sharply over three years.
Tom Long, a 50-year-old picketer, said that when General Motors began building its mammoth Chevrolet and GMC Suburbans here in 1990, they moved down the assembly line at the rate of 44 an hour. Now the rate is 62 an hour, and there are fewer workers doing more tasks, he said.
"Our big concern here is getting some more people," he said, while echoing Mr. Lewis's worry about the janitorial work.
Mr. Lewis said that the company had installed a mechanical hoist to help him move the heavy suspension parts but that he could not use it quickly enough to keep pace with the medium-duty truck assembly line where he works.
"I couldn't keep up so I don't use the hoist, I use my hands," he said, his shoulders hunched against the wind and a baseball cap pulled down over his forehead.
The factory here, two hours' drive northwest of Chicago, covers many city blocks with a sprawling hodgepodge of old brick buildings and new steel warehouses, all topped with a forest of chimneys. The factory stands near the edge of this small town, just a mile from cornfields now browned by autumn.
Talks in Detroit to settle the strikes and produce a new national labor contract for G.M. made little progress today. Some U.A.W. leaders spent much of the day instead at a quarterly meeting of the union's board, people close to the union said. The long-planned board meeting was scheduled to last through Friday, and the union's local officials at G.M. factories were scheduled to hold a conference on Saturday in Detroit.
G.M.'s profits run as high as $12,000 apiece on the Suburbans, and $6,000 each on the Tahoe and Yukon sport utility vehicles also assembled here, Mr. Glantz of Dean Witter said. He estimated that each day that the factory is closed will reduce G.M.'s after-tax profits by about $7 million.
The total cost of the latest strikes could soar to as much as $50 million a day, Mr. Glantz added, if the strike in Indianapolis lasts long enough to force G.M. to close seven other assembly plants that make sport utility vehicles or pickup trucks and that rely on the Indianapolis factory for truck doors, hoods and roofs.
Thomas Klipstine, a spokesman for G.M., said this evening that one of these factories, a pickup truck assembly plant in Fort Wayne, Ind., that employs 2,250 workers, would close for lack of parts later tonight.
Stephen Girsky, an analyst with Morgan Stanley, said that the next fight between G.M. and the U.A.W. would likely be over whether laid-off workers at the seven other assembly plants are eligible for state unemployment benefits, which would tend to drive up G.M. unemployment insurance costs.

HEADLINE: Medicare Patients in H.M.O.'s Win a Case

BYLINE: By ROBERT PEAR 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 30

 BODY:
In a major victory for consumers, a Federal judge has expanded the rights of Medicare patients in health maintenance organizations, saying they are entitled to immediate hearings whenever they are denied medical services.
The judge, Alfredo C. Marquez of the Federal District Court in Tucson, Ariz., rejected the Government's argument that it was not legally responsible for the actions of H.M.O.'s that have Federal contracts to care for Medicare patients. 
 Four million of the 38 million Mediare beneficiaries are in H.M.O.'s, and the number is rapidly growing. Republicans and Democrats alike look to such health plans as a way to control Medicare costs. The decision may contribute to this trend, assuring elderly people that they can join H.M.O.'s without losing the rights available to patients in the traditional Medicare program.
Judge Marquez said that when H.M.O.'s denied services to Medicare patients, they were acting for the Government, so their decisions amounted to Government action, and beneficiaries were entitled to "due process of law," including full notice of adverse decisions and a meaningful opportunity to challenge the denial of care.
The Clinton Administration and the H.M.O. industry had argued that H.M.O.'s serving Medicare patients were private entities and that the Federal Government should not be held responsible for their decisions.
"The decisions of the H.M.O.'s are not Government action," the Secretary of Health and Human Services, Donna E. Shalala, asserted in court papers. "Such medical decisions are not made by Government officials, and the Government has neither a role nor an interest in the decisions made by any H.M.O."
The Justice Department said Medicare patients in H.M.O.'s already had far more rights and protections than non-Medicare patients in the same H.M.O.'s. In addition, it said, no court has the power to second-guess the Secretary's decisions about whether to take enforcement action against an H.M.O.
Darrel J. Grinstead, chief counsel of the Federal Health Care Financing Administration, which runs Medicare, declined to comment on today's decision. Other Federal officials refused to say whether the Government would appeal.
Judge Marquez ruled that "H.M.O. denials of Medicare services are state action" because "the Government pays for covered services" and regulates the activities of H.M.O.'s serving Medicare patients. So, he said, beneficiaries are entitled to due process of law.
Medicaid officials in New York and many other states are prodding low-income people to join H.M.O.'s. While Medicaid was not mentioned by Judge Marquez, his analysis will be useful to Medicaid recipients challenging denial of services by H.M.O.'s. Several such challenges have been filed by Medicaid patients in Tennessee and other states.
Karen M. Ignagni, president of the American Association of Health Plans, which represents H.M.O.'s, offered today to work with the Government to help Medicare patients exercise their rights to file appeals and grievances.
In his ruling, Judge Marquez declared: "Existing procedures followed by H.M.O.'s fail to secure minimum due process for Medicare beneficiaries. Notice and informal hearing requirements set forth by statute and regulations are all but ignored."
The lawsuit, a nationwide class action, was filed in 1993 by Medicare beneficiaries who said they had been denied medically necessary services like emergency care, home health care, skilled nursing care and physical therapy. In many cases, the services had been recommended by doctors, but denied by H.M.O.'s.
Patients in the traditional fee-for-service Medicare program can also challenge decisions denying coverage for particular services. But they usually receive care before filing claims, whereas H.M.O. patients may be denied care if the H.M.O. decides that a particular service is not covered. Economists say that H.M.O.'s often have a financial incentive to limit the amount of care, whereas doctors paid through the standard Medicare program have incentive to provide extra services.
Judge Marquez reviewed many cases in which H.M.O.'s had been asked to reconsider decisions denying care. "H.M.O. reconsideration approximates a 'rubber stamp' of the initial denial," he said. "This has grave consequences because an H.M.O. denial may mean the enrollee will go without medically necessary service."
While campaigning for re-election in Florida last month, President Clinton expressed concern about changes in the health care industry that threaten the quality of care, and he said he would appoint a commission to recommend ways of protecting consumers.
Sally Hart Wilson, a lawyer at the Center for Medicare Advocacy who represented patients in the class-action lawsuit, said, "The position taken by the Government in its legal briefs in this case seems to be at odds with the Administration's recent statements about how it wants to protect the quality of care for people in H.M.O.'s around the country."
In papers filed with Judge Marquez in March, the Administration said the Federal Medicare law "does not require an impartial review procedure for Medicare beneficiaries enrolled in H.M.O.'s before a termination or denial of their H.M.O. services."
Judge Marquez found defects in many of the notices sent to Medicare patients by H.M.O.'s. "Not one notice provided the specific basis for coverage denial," he said.
In many cases, he added, the H.M.O. "hides the ball" and fails to inform Medicare patients that they have "a right to present additional evidence to the H.M.O. for reconsideration." This omission violates Federal regulations, he said.

HEADLINE: ECONOMIC GROWTH RETREATS TO 2.2%

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 30

 BODY:
In one of the last major indicators of the country's economic performance before the Presidential election, the Commerce Department today reported a sharp slowdown in economic growth in the third quarter of the year, but no sign of a revival of even modest inflation.
Today's news, that the growth rate fell to 2.2 percent in the third quarter, from 4.7 percent in the second, did not come as a surprise. Most economists had expected that the economy would cool off considerably. But they differ on whether this is the beginning of a longer downturn or a brief slowing of an expansion that can be sustained well into next year. 
 Both sides in that debate could pick out evidence from numbers reported today to support their arguments: Growth in personal spending by consumers slowed to a near standstill, but companies continued to buy equipment at a remarkable pace, a sign that they expect continued good times.
There was little question, however, that the combination of slower growth and continued low inflation wiped out the fears prevalent three months ago that the Federal Reserve would be forced to raise short-term interest rates.
And the figures put the economy on a path to grow 2.8 percent for the year, by most estimates -- well ahead of what both Democrats and Republicans expected early in the year.
Not surprisingly, both campaigns seized on today's numbers to bolster their sharply conflicting assessments of the nation's economic health. Commerce Secretary Mickey Kantor, carefully plucking out figures on the growth of the private economy, which exclude government spending, argued that the economy was growing faster than "in the so-called go-go 80's."
"We're in the midst of a balanced, sustained economic expansion that is delivering the goods for the American people," he said.
But Bob Dole, campaigning in Tennessee, referred to an economy that "is barely afloat," and said, "If this is a recovery, I can hardly wait for the recession." His staff compared today's numbers with those of the first quarter of 1993 -- when Mr. Clinton was inaugurated and when the economy was climbing out of recession with growth at a rate of more than 4 percent -- to support its argument that Mr. Clinton had been a lucky bystander to good times rather than a contributor to the economy's success.
Despite the evidence of slowdown, there was good news for wage earners. The Federal Reserve, in a separate report, said that it was finally seeing "some proliferation" of wage increases around the country -- something that has been oddly absent throughout the last few years -- in a variety of jobs.
Still, that news followed a report on Tuesday by the Labor Department that growth in wages and benefits had slowed in the third quarter. The Employment Cost Index, the broadest and probably most accurate compensation gauge, climbed just six-tenths of 1 percent in the quarter, the lowest reading in a year.
In any case, the Fed said in its report today -- the beige book that looks at regional economic conditions -- that few of the increases it found were being translated into higher prices for goods, and that inflation remained well under control.
"All the news today was very good from the point of view of the central bank," Allen Sinai, the Lehman Brothers economist, said today, referring to the indications that the Federal Reserve had stepped around a potential landmine this fall by deciding not to raise interest rates.
The biggest surprise in the figures was the paltry growth in consumer spending: a mere four-tenths of a percent at an annual rate. In the first half of the year the rate was closer to 3.5 percent. Mr. Kantor and the chairman of the Council of Economic Advisers, Joseph E. Stiglitz, portrayed that as an odd blip, and said they expected a sharp recovery of spending in the fourth quarter. Many economists agree, but there is hardly unanimity on the issue.
Inventories also increased sharply in the third quarter, suggesting that some of the G.D.P. gains were attributable to goods that were being produced but not yet consumed.
Buying habits historically wax and wane, so it is difficult to determine how much the spending slowdown really means. But the caution of consumers was in sharp contrast to the continued enthusiasm of American businesses, which spent 19 percent more than a year ago on equipment, largely computers, and built factories at a pace suggestive of a far more healthy expansion.
"This is one of the signs that the underlying fundamentals are very good," Mr. Sinai said. "When you talk to company executives, they seem confident. And they are acting that way."
One area that saw a significant downturn was exports, which until now have spurred the economy. Exports were up only six-tenths of a percent. Some of that slowdown is understandable: the United States economy has grown far faster than Europe's or Japan's, meaning that the country is in a position to take in more goods while demand for American exports lags. But it is an inconvenient turn of events for the the Clinton Administration, which has made the export boom a centerpiece of its economic message.
While the gross domestic product is the most widely used measure of the economy's health, its political utility -- for either side -- is probably minimal this late in a campaign. Moreover, ordinary workers rarely directly feel the ups and downs of the growth rate, while unemployment numbers, which come out later this week, strike far closer to home. Inflation rates are also quick to make an impact across the country, and an inflation gauge in the report on growth showed inflation of only 1.9 percent at an annual rate.
Data included in the gross domestic product for the third quarter of 1996 follow, with all dollar amounts in billions at seasonally adjusted annual rates. Percentage changes are from the previous quarter at seasonally adjusted annual rates.

 A roundup of regional economic conditions in recent months for the Federal Reserve's 12 districts, according to the quarterly beige book report issued yesterday by the Fed.
 
Minneapolis -- Construction was strong, with highway work leading the way. Scarcity of skilled workers pushed wages up.
 
Chicago -- Retail sales improved in September, but results were mixed in October. Demand for skilled workers remained strong.
 
Cleveland -- Employment agencies reported strong demand for temporary workers, but that skilled workers were hard to find. Retailers reported mediocre sales.
 
Boston -- Retailers and manufacturers reported solid growth. Some manufacturers raised prices, though overall materials prices changed little.
 
New York -- Sales at large retailers slowed in September, but quickened in October. Banks' willingness to lend to consumers increased.
 
Philadelphia -- Manufacturing gains slowed since the summer. Employers had trouble finding qualified workers.
 
Richmond -- Retailers reported higher sales. Durable goods and textiles manufacturing slowed last month.
 
Atlanta -- Retailers said sales were rising with inventories on target. Builders said construction fell below last year's level.
 
St. Louis -- Companies said inventories were at or below desired levels. Some companies reported higher costs for raw materials.
 
Kansas City -- Retailers reported slightly higher sales. Housing starts showed a bit and labor markets were still light.
 
Dallas -- Slower home-building activity led to some layoffs and slumping demand for raw materials. Retail and auto sales wer sluggish.
 
San Francisco -- Manufacturing picked up in recent weeks, led by aircraft production and demand for electronic parts. Banks reported increased delinquencies but strong loan demand.

HEADLINE: Stock Market Has a 'Treading Water Day'

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
Technology shares regained some ground, but most stocks edged lower yesterday as mixed economic data heightened anxiety before tomorrow's important monthly employment report.
The Dow Jones industrial average meandered through the day, falling 13.79 points, to 5,993.23, after surrendering an early 21-point gain. 
 Broader measures also retreated after bonds gave back an opening advance that briefly brought long-term interest rates toward their lowest levels in nearly seven months.
But the technology-laden Nasdaq market posted modest gains as investors looked for bargains after several days of profit taking in leading computer-related shares.
Stocks started the session higher, tracking the bond market, which extended Tuesday's rally after the Commerce Department reported that economic growth slowed sharply in the third quarter as consumer spending fell to its weakest pace in five years.
But bonds slipped back near opening levels, pulling stocks lower, after a subsequent report showed stronger-than-expected activity in home sales toward the end of the third quarter.
Later in the day, the Federal Reserve released a report on a periodic survey of regional business conditions, known as the beige book. The survey found few indications of price increases, but it showed upward pressure on wages because of tight labor markets in some occupations.
The day's conflicting signals on the economy left many investors wondering what tomorrow's reading on October payroll and wage levels will reveal. "This was just a treading water day," said Ned Riley, the chief investment officer for the Bank of Boston, calling the week's remaining data "critical to the direction of the markets."
Higher inflation or interest rates would make the set payments on existing bonds less attractive, forcing down prices to improve the yield. As bond prices rose yesterday morning, the yield on the 30-year Treasury fell from Tuesday's 6.68 percent to 6.64 percent. But by late yesterday afternoon, the yield rose to 6.70 percent.
Advancing issues barely outpaced decliners on the New York Stock Exchange, where volume was 438.71 million shares, down from 443.9 million shares on Tuesday.
The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.60 point, to 700.90, and the New York Stock Exchange composite index fell 0.41 point, to 372.72.
But the Nasdaq composite index rose 3.18 points, to 1,206.23, as several technology bellwethers rebounded. Intel rose 2 3/8, to 106 3/4, and Dell Computer rose 2 13/16, to 78 15/16.
The session's best performers were banks and other financial services stocks, which enjoy a bigger profit margin on the money they lend when interest rates are lower.
In New York Stock Exchange trading, Nationsbank rose 1 5/8, to 94 1/8; Chase Manhattan rose 7/8, to 85 3/8, and Citicorp rose 7/8, to 98. Among the Dow industrials, American Express rose 1/2, to 46 1/4.
Retail companies were weaker. Wal-Mart Stores fell 1 1/8, to 26 1/4; Sears, Roebuck dropped 1, to 47 1/2, and Woolworth dropped 1/2, to 21.
 Imation climbed 4 7/8, to 27 5/8, after the company posted its first profit since being spun off in July from Minnesota Mining and Manufacturing.
 CompUSA tumbled for a second day, falling 6, to 45 5/8. The computer retailer doubled its profits for its first quarter, which ended Sept. 28, beating forecasts, but investors continued to worry about sluggish computer sales in this quarter. Comp-USA said the retail side of its business was somewhat weak, confirming recent market sentiment, the Dow Jones News Service reported.
Michaels Stores fell 1 29/32, to 10, after the company said it would report a wider-than-expected loss for its third quarter.

HEADLINE: POLITICS: POLITICAL MEMO;
Behind Dole's California Strategy: A Bid to Save His Campaign

BYLINE: By B. DRUMMOND AYRES Jr. 

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, Oct. 30

 BODY:
Until a month ago, the conventional political wisdom among Republican leaders was that Bob Dole could win the Presidency on Nov. 5 without carrying California and its biggest-in-the-nation haul of 54 Electoral College votes. All he had to do, the reasoning went, was run well elsewhere, particularly in Republican strongholds in the South and the Midwest.
Then, suddenly, Mr. Dole diverted major campaign resources from the South and the Midwest and made an all-out effort for California, a state where President Clinton has held one of his biggest two-digit leads for months and, to boot, is a sometimes loopy place that seems a bad political fit for a straitlaced Republican septuagenarian. 
 So why the puzzling shift?
Theories abound -- that Mr. Dole made a bad political judgment of historic proportion, that he needs California's big money to keep financially afloat until Election Day, that he has secretly given up on winning the Presidency and will instead go down fighting to re-elect Republicans to Congress, especially California's crucial 26-member Republican contingent in the House.
But the true explanation, Dole campaign officials say, is much more straightforward and practical.
As they tell it, the Dole shift to California is a last-ditch "Hail Mary" effort to get some momentum going in a place so politically important that the rest of the country will be forced to take note and, it is hoped, give Mr. Dole a second look.
If the big California money flows in because of that effort and if California's House Republicans get a lift, as well as local and state Republican candidates, so much the better, the officials say. But the main goal of the strategy, they insist, is to salvage the Dole campaign nationally.
"You do what you have to do," said Ken Khachigian, the coordinator of the Dole effort in California and the main architect of the new strategy.
Before the shift, Mr. Khachigian noted, nothing was happening in the Midwest or South to convince voters that the former Senator from Kansas was on a victory roll. Rather, he recalled, Mr. Dole was trailing by double digits in many states in those regions, and officials in the Dole camp were debating whether to abandon California entirely.
"We had to do something dramatic," Mr. Khachigian said. "It's admittedly a long, unusual shot. But if we can do it in California, we can do it anywhere, and people will see that. And if all this seems too big a stretch, keep in mind that California is a state with a history of come-from-behind campaigns, most notably Pete Wilson getting re-elected Governor two years ago by better than 10 points after being more than 20 points back."
In fact, two of Mr. Wilson's favorite campaign issues -- illegal immigration and affirmative action -- are being heavily employed by Mr. Dole as he campaigns the length of California. Given the race, sex and economic factors involved in those issues, they are as controversial now as they were when Mr. Wilson used them. Some of the controversy is within the Dole camp itself.
The Dole strategy has caught the nation's attention, to be sure. But thus far polls indicate that Mr. Dole has made little progress in catching up with Mr. Clinton in California, certainly not the kind of progress that Mr. Khachigian said would cause voters elsewhere to conclude that Mr. Dole deserves a new look.
Most voter surveys here still show the President in the lead by 15 percentage points or more, with Mr. Dole barely holding his own in some historically Republican strongholds like Orange County, south of Los Angeles, where he must win big to offset heavy Democratic majorities in Los Angeles. While voters tell pollsters that they are deeply concerned about illegal immigration and affirmative action programs, these issues do not seem to be helping Mr. Dole very much, and may be costing him the support of some women and Hispanic voters who might otherwise have been part of his base.
"There's really not much movement showing for Dole despite his big effort and his hot issues," said Mark Di Camillo, a director of the Field Poll, a nonpartisan California survey organization. "Californians as a whole don't seem to be buying it."
Mr. Clinton's California strategist, Bill Carrick, calls the new Dole strategy "the last refuge of a losing candidate, a strategy straight from the 'Failed Campaign Playbook.' "
"I've been in this business long enough to see variations on this theme before, including losing efforts out here by Democratic candidates like Walter Mondale and Michael Dukakis," Mr. Carrick added. "You end up not only dropping California, but also losing other places that you might have won had you stayed put or shifted resources to those places. Pour it on, Bob."
The new strategy was formulated in early October after Dole campaign officials, notably Mr. Khachigian, took a look at polls from various states around the country and concluded that, among major states, California offered as much chance for improvement as any. Not only was it the biggest prize, but there were also the hot immigration and affirmative action issues to exploit and a long history in the state of using hot issues to turn elections around at the last minute.
Mr. Khachigian drew up a plan that called for a massive special infusion of $8 million or so in campaign money, most of it for a television advertising blitz. He also called on Mr. Dole, his wife, Elizabeth, and his running mate, Jack Kemp, to commit to 20 or so campaign visits to California by Nov. 5, either together or individually.
In the days leading up to the second Dole-Clinton debate, in San Diego on Oct. 16, Mr. Khachigian held a series of meetings in Southern California with Mr. Dole and other top Republican officials, and persuaded them to adopt the new plan.
There was some debate about how much emphasis to put on affirmative action and immigration, said several people familiar with what happened at the meetings, and Mr. Dole and Mr. Kemp themselves have chosen to emphasize those issues differently. But Mr. Khachigian said the differences were never so great as to threaten adoption of the plan.
Since the new plan went into effect, Mr. Dole has hammered long and hard on the immigration issue, but he has only begun to hit on affirmative action. He is not particularly comfortable with the latter issue, some campaign aides say, viewing it as a two-edge political sword.
Mr. Kemp has touched only lightly on both issues in most of his stops, raising the ire of a number of Dole partisans.
The Republican leader who is perhaps happiest with the new Dole strategy is Governor Wilson, never particularly close to the former Senator. Well before last summer's convention in San Diego, Mr. Wilson began urging Mr. Dole to concentrate on California and the immigration and affirmative action issues, arguing that he had personal experience with the results that surely would follow.

HEADLINE: POLITICS;
In His Own Words

 BODY:

BILL CLINTON
 
Speaking yesterday at Eastern Michigan University in Ypsilanti:
"In every community, in every state, the face of our businesses are changing. Every day it looks more and more like the people in this room: men and women, people of color. Increasingly, Americans with disabilities are becoming business owners. More and more people are finding ways to make the free enterprise system work for them. That supports stronger families, more stable communities, and a much, much stronger America with a brighter future. 
 "Today we received some more good news: our economy is continuing to grow steady and strong, with an annual growth rate of nearly 3 percent. Real incomes for American workers, after being stagnant for virtually 20 years, are rising at nearly 5 percent, with no inflation . . . In the past three months alone, business investment has risen at 18.9 percent, and the rate of investment now is the strongest since President Kennedy was in office over 30 years ago.
"There is an extreme increase in the expectation that we can keep our free enterprise system growing, flourishing and growing stronger, with rising incomes, more businesses and more jobs. And we cannot turn back on that."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Economic Growth Slowed To 2.2% in Third Quarter
In one of the last major economic indicators before the Presidential election, the Government reported a slowdown in the growth rate in the third quarter, to 2.2 percent from 4.7 percent in the second quarter. Economists -- and, not surprisingly, the opposing camps in the campaign -- differ on whether this is the beginning of a longer downturn or a brief slowing of an expansion that can be sustained well into 1997. [Page A1.]
 
Treasury bond prices rose initially on the news of weakening growth, but fell back after a report of stronger-than-expected housing sales. The 30-year bond's yield edged up to 6.70 percent from 6.68 percent on Tuesday. [D17.]
 
Technology shares regained some ground, sending the Nasdaq up 3.18 points, to 1,206.23. But most stocks slipped, with the Dow Jones industrial average falling 13.79, to 5,993.23, as the mixed economic data heightened anxiety before tomorrow's employment report. [D10.]
 
Bay Networks Appoints New Chief
Bay Networks landed a new chief executive, naming a longtime Intel executive who was instrumental in persuading I.B.M. to adopt Intel's microprocessor for its PC's. [D7.]
 
An Offer for Carter-Wallace
Marvin Davis, the billionaire investor, announced an $835 million bid for Carter-Wallace, the maker of Trojan condoms and Arrid deodorant. [D2.]
 
Bethlehem Quitting Structural Steel
Bethlehem Steel, the last major steel producer to battle the minimills for the structural steel market, conceded defeat. It said that it would sell four unprofitable units, including its structural steel mill, and that it would close the operations if no buyer was found. [D2.]
 
Fed Lowers Banking Barriers
The Fed voted to knock down some of the walls that separate banks from securities affiliates, allowing companies like J. P. Morgan and Bankers Trust greater freedom to market and develop investment products. [D2.]
 
Medicare Patients Win in Court
A Federal judge in Tucson, Ariz., expanded the rights of Medicare patients in H.M.O.'s, saying they are entitled to immediate hearings when they are denied medical services. [B15.]

A Well-Promoted Public Offering
The difference between a small company selling for pennies a share and one selling for $9 a share seems to be promotion -- at least in the case of a hot initial public offering for Chem International, a small maker of vitamins. Floyd Norris: Market Place. [D10.]
 
An Expanding Luxury Empire
In airport duty-free shops and on fancy shopping boulevards, the products of LVMH Moet Hennessy-Louis Vuitton are easy to find. Having acquired many of the most expensive, most luxurious brands of Champagnes, perfume, fashion and luggage, Bernard Arnault, who controls the company, is trying to gain additional outlets with his bid for DFS Group. [D9.]
 
Lotus to Offer Web Software
I.B.M.'s Lotus Development unit said it would strip key technology out of its Notes software and sell it as a separate product called Weblicator to enhance the ability of computer users to organize and exchange data over the Internet. [D7.]

SECTION: Section B; Page 12; Column 1; National Desk 

LENGTH: 943 words

HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE PLAYERS;
For One Dole Aide, Optimism Is Unavoidable

BYLINE: By JAMES BENNET 

DATELINE: DENVER, Oct. 30

 BODY:
Bob Dole trails in the polls; former advisers and fellow Republicans are flinging eggs at the campaign; aides are throwing up their hands while the campaign's messages shift by the day, and its schedule, it seems, by the hour. And Mr. Dole has taken to attacking the press.
These might seem like lonely days for Mr. Dole's press secretary, Nelson (Lens Cap) Warfield, so named by camera crews for his habit of blocking their shots of Mr. Dole when he is asked impertinent questions. 
 But if he is feeling low, Mr. Warfield shows little sign of it. "I just am not down," he said over a vodka and tonic on Sunday night before two days of campaigning from San Diego to Denver. "I mean, I haven't ordered my office furniture yet, but I think we're going to win this thing. You can't be around Bob Dole and not have that sense of optimism."
Mr. Warfield noted that Mr. Dole, having won 27 primaries in a row, is "on a winning streak." Further, he said, gathering speed, "one of the reasons I have confidence we'll win the race is there are still people who haven't tuned in."
Press secretaries are closest to a candidate and the most attuned to his thinking and moods, traveling at his side for months. Yet alone among the campaign staff, press secretaries cannot hide behind closed doors.
Republicans and Democrats who have been press secretaries to candidates behind in the polls say that it can be agony to spin in what reporters treat as the grave.
"It's so painful at the end," said Paul Costello, the press secretary to Kitty Dukakis during Michael S. Dukakis's Democratic campaign in 1988. "Every question, especially for the spouse, is akin to: 'Why are you married to such a loser?' "
Marlin Fitzwater, who worked as Presidential press secretary for Ronald Reagan and George Bush before losing in 1992, said reporters were on the alert for any hint of weakness. "I always had the idea that the White House press corps was willing to read frustration on the faces of baggage handlers if you let them," Mr. Fitzwater said.
At times, as Mr. Warfield labors to construct a favorable image of his candidate from seemingly dismal details, he can seem as resourceful as MacGyver, the television hero forever getting out of scrapes with things like paper clips and chocolate bars for tools.
Preparing to fly here from California on the Dole plane, reporters pressed Mr. Warfield for explanations as to why the campaign was deciding at the last minute where to pop in next. "The kind of corporate mentality that seems to be evident in our opponent's camp is not evident here," he responded, bracing himself against a bulkhead. "We're trying to be a very aggressive, very flexible campaign, and if that means scheduling on a very tight basis, that's what we'll do."
At other times, Mr. Warfield, a baby-faced 36-year-old, seems to be struggling to build a life raft from a Clorox bottle washed up on a beach. On Sunday, for example, a reporter pointed out that The Los Angeles Times had had no front-page article Mr. Dole's California bus trip the previous day.
This, Mr. Warfield rapidly explained, was a good sign. The campaign, he said, had become "so prevalent in California that we're commonplace and don't make the front page of the L.A. Times." But he finally allowed himself a grin. "I don't know what you're laughing about," he said.
Mr. Warfield has ruffled more than a few feathers among the reporters traveling with Mr. Dole. Some accuse him of playing favorites, a charge he denies. Others say he appears rarely and, when he does, vanishes too soon. Several reporters said they were amused at how visible Mr. Warfield became after a reporter turned up this week to profile him.
Many of the reporters on the plane have voiced a familiar complaint on the campaign trail, saying they wish that Mr. Dole were more available to them and that he has not held a full-blown news conference since March.
Indeed, even Mr. Dole's assessment of Mr. Warfield and their relationship had to be relayed through Mr. Warfield. "I trust him," Mr. Warfield said Mr. Dole said. "When he goes to the back of the plane, I don't worry about what he's saying."
For trying to sustain Mr. Dole's optimistic message and for standing by his man, Mr. Warfield wins high marks from Republicans who are now critical of the campaign.
But the feuding among Dole aides is becoming notorious, and at times even Mr. Warfield sounds frustrated with his colleagues. Told by telephone today that John Buckley, the communications director, had praised his work, Mr. Warfield remarked, "What'd he tell you off the record?" He added hastily: "Just trying to keep it light."
Mr. Dole himself, when asked on Sunday by a CNN correspondent, Candy Crowley, whether he was well served by his staff, did not answer yes. "My view is you don't look back and say, 'Well, it is their fault, their fault, their fault,' " he said. "I have been around long enough to know it is going to be the candidate's fault. If I lose, the rest of them are going to be home free."
All year, Mr. Warfield, known for acid asides and pop-culture references, has been waging a battle of witticisms with Joe Lockhart, the Clinton campaign press secretary. Tired of seeing the cigarette-suited Butt Man at Dole rallies, for example, Mr. Warfield threatened to have Joint Man dog Mr. Clinton.
Mr. Warfield has drawn admiring comments from his opponent. When he declared that a recent New York Times poll showing a substantial Clinton lead might have "oversampled Indonesian billionaires," Mr. Lockhart told a reporter, "Tell Nelson that we enjoyed that Indonesia line. It was a very good line."

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 6; National Desk 

LENGTH: 1404 words

HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE REPUBLICAN;
DOLE ASSERTS DATA ON ECONOMY SHOW RECESSION IS NEAR

BYLINE: By ADAM NAGOURNEY 

DATELINE: NEW ORLEANS, Oct. 30

 BODY:
After a midmorning visit for prayer and reflection at the Lincoln Memorial in Washington, Bob Dole headed south today and seized on news of sluggish economic growth to warn that a recession was waiting over the Election Day horizon.
"If this is a recovery, I can hardly wait for the recession -- if this is the recovery," Mr. Dole declared, his voice booming off the hard walls of a stuffy state university gym this afternoon in central Tennessee before he flew on to Louisiana.
Mr. Dole said today's economic figures, which showed that growth of the Gross Domestic Product slowed to 2.2 percent in the third quarter, proved his assertion that President Clinton's claim of an economic recovery was false and that the economy was in fact faltering because of Mr. Clinton's policies, particularly the tax increase of 1993.
"So all this false and empty talk about the so-called Clinton recovery comes to an end," Mr. Dole told his audience of mostly students, who filled about half the gym floor at Austin Peay State University in Clarksville. "It ended today. With today's announcement, take off the mask -- it's not Halloween -- take off the mask! The economy is not good."
Mr. Clinton, for his part, focused on the future today as he began a final, 15-state swing. [Page B13.]
Although Mr. Dole expressed public alarm about the implications of the economic figures -- "The evidence of a weak economy is everywhere. Everywhere," he warned -- it was a comment on the state of the campaign that his political strategists could fairly be described as delighted with the economic news.
Mr. Dole's entire day pivoted on the economic report, which was released at 8:30 A.M. by the Commerce Department. The decision to push ahead with a renewed attack on the economy, an issue Mr. Dole has raised sporadically, was contingent on how bad those numbers were, Mr. Dole's aides said.
The assertions that President Clinton's economic plan was slowing the economy provoked an unusually sharp response from Laura D'Andrea Tyson, the chairwoman of the National Economic Council at the White House.
"It was the Republicans who predicted in 1993 that the President's economic plan would lead to the economy going in the tank, that it would lead to an increase in the unemployment rate, would lead to an increase in the deficit, and some of them said it would lead to a recession," Ms. Tyson said.
"I think the predictive abilities of some of the members of the Republican side of the aisle in the Congress on the economy is questionable."
Mr. Dole's decision to turn to the economy came as he embarked on a two-day trip through the South, a part of the country that a Republican Presidential candidate would normally not need to visit this late in a race. It was another reminder of the difficult position Mr. Dole finds himself in the last week before the election. Tonight, after the stops in Tennessee and Louisiana, he continued on to Florida, a state no Democratic Presidential candidate has carried in 20 years.
On the way from his home to National Airport in Washington this morning, Mr. Dole diverted his motorcade for an unscheduled pilgrimage to the Lincoln Memorial. The visit was evocative of the time that President Richard M. Nixon, who is a seminal figure in Mr. Dole's life, walked up the same marble steps of the memorial during his own difficult passage -- in Mr. Nixon's case, at the height of protests against the Vietnam War in 1970.
Aides to Mr. Dole said he and his wife, Elizabeth, who is now introducing her husband at most of the rallies, stayed for about five minutes, but did not go inside. Mr. Dole, recounting the experience at his Tennessee appearance, said he had turned to his wife in the car to say he wanted to visit the memorial.
"I need a little inspiration," Mr. Dole said, recounting the back-seat conversation. "I need to reflect about America and about the Republican Party and maybe we have a word or two of silent prayers. And so we did."
Mr. Dole's audience listened in silence as he recounted the visit. Then he declared loudly, "We are going to win this election, Mr. Lincoln." His audience rose to its feet, and Mr. Dole was treated to some of the longest applause he has heard in this campaign.
It turned out not to be the only time today that Mr. Dole offered some insights into his emotions. In an interview taped with David Frost this afternoon for broadcast on Friday night on PBS, Mr. Dole responded to the question of what he might do if he failed to defeat Mr. Clinton on Tuesday.
"If it happens -- I'm not suggesting it's going to happen -- but if it should happen, I'll probably find work," Mr. Dole said. "I'll work with those with disabilities, and do other things fulfilling. But I'm not going to stop and go off and say it's all over."
In his speech in Tennessee, Mr. Dole forcefully blamed the media for the fact that his own economic plan, which includes a 15 percent across-the-board tax cut, had not lifted his campaign, at least according to polls. Mr. Dole, who has taken to criticizing newspapers over the past week, widened his net today, and reeled in network newscasters as well.
"How can we expect the people of the United States to understand our plan when anchormen have already made up their -- or anchorwomen -- have already made up their minds?" Mr. Dole said.
"They've already decided it's not a good idea. So they tell you it's not a good idea. I didn't know that was their job. I thought it was to report the plan, report the plan."
Newspapers were not spared. Mr. Dole argued to Mr. Frost that the endorsement of Mr. Clinton by two newspapers in particular -- The New York Times on Sunday and The Washington Post today -- should be taken as a testimonial for him, rather than for the President.
"When you have The New York Times and The Washington Post, in effect, apologizing for endorsing Clinton, I think they have some serious reservations," Mr. Dole said. "They say, 'Well, maybe he'll straighten these things out in his second term.' "
He continued: "But when America's great newspapers have to sort of choke up before they can say something positive, I think that's an indication. They are liberal papers. If they feel that way, once the American people understand it fully, they are going to be outraged."
A former political colleague, former Senator Howard H. Baker Jr. of Tennessee, said he welcomed Mr. Dole's focus on how the news media was covering his campaign.
"It's an extension of the candidate's personality," said Mr. Baker, a Republican. "It fits Dole. He does it well. He makes the point well. The audience responds to it well."
Mr. Dole's audience in Tennessee was enthusiastic as Mr. Dole addressed both the economic figures and ethical problems with Mr. Clinton's Administration. Referring to the White House security officer implicated in the unauthorized inspection of F.B.I. records, Mr. Dole shouted, "Nobody knows who hired Craig Livingstone!"
"Hillary!" came the shout from at least two corners of the crowd.
Mr. Dole considered his response. "I guess you got the word first," he said. "I didn't know."
In New Orleans this evening, Mr. Dole, in attacking Mr. Clinton, drew the most vivid contrasts yet between the current White House and the Watergate scandal of the Nixon years, during which Mr. Dole was the chairman of the Republican National Committee. In recounting the story, Mr. Dole inadvertently inserted Mr. Clinton into the familiar tableau of Mr. Nixon's last day in office.
"As a Republican, I remember Watergate," Mr. Dole said. "I remember what it did to our party. I remember President Clinton leaving on a helicopter. I remember what happens when things get out of hand at the White house."
The dispute between Mr. Dole and Ms. Tyson over the health of the economy comes after a campaign in which Mr. Dole has repeatedly tried to argue that appearances to the contrary, the economy is in bad shape. He said the figures today, along with others he cited -- a decline in consumer confidence, and slow growth in exports -- proved his assertion that the economy was "barely afloat."
"Now this is a real economic slowdown," Mr. Dole said "And I might say it's disastrous news for American workers and businesses and even worse news for low- and moderate-income Americans who have been squeezed and squeezed and squeezed by lower wages and higher taxes in this Administration."

SECTION: Section A; Page 28; Column 5; Editorial Desk 

LENGTH: 210 words

HEADLINE: 5 Days to Go: What Do We Want? Or Deserve?;
Hey, Big Spender

 BODY:
To the Editor:
After reading many of your editorials over the last four years criticizing President Clinton's lack of full disclosure on many issues, I was surprised that you fell into the same trap in your endorsement (editorial, Oct. 27). 
 For example, you say that Mr. Clinton "stood up to the spendthrifts in his own party" in driving toward a balanced budget. I don't remember a call for a balanced budget at the start of his term; what I do recall was a move to push through a "stimulus package" to revive the economy because his predecessors, he contended, had neglected to tell him how bad things really were.
A bigger spending package is not a drive toward a balanced budget. You credit him with a victory for free trade, yet you forget to mention that it was Republican support that won the day.
Mr. Clinton failed miserably in winning the support of the Democratic leadership. And had those leaders had their way, there would be no free trade agreements now. You credit Mr. Clinton with heading in the right direction on health care, yet you fail to mention that the American people, though in support of the overall goal to make it a better system, were overwhelming opposed to his methods.
 
GEORGE COSTA
Chicago, Oct. 28, 1996

SECTION: Section A; Page 28; Column 6; Editorial Desk 

LENGTH: 199 words

HEADLINE: 5 Days to Go: What Do We Want? Or Deserve?;
Education and Earth

 BODY:
To the Editor:
I commend your well-reasoned editorial (Oct. 27) endorsing President Clinton for a second term. In your reflective attention to such areas as the economy, international trade, foreign policy and health care, you fail to touch on one area that will determine the health and longevity of American society and the human experiment: the education of our children. 
 Our national security depends far more on how our children and grandchildren are learning in school and in the home than on the bloated Pentagon budgets on which both Congress and the President heap further billions in scarce resources.
It is as if we cannot sort out our real national priorities. On the same plane with the education of our children and the support and encouragement of capable, caring teachers and principals, we must put protecting the environment or, as you note, reverence for the earth. That means giving more attention to such interrelated issues as population, consumption, loss of habitat, growing toxification of the planet, alternative energy strategies, farming without pesticides, loss of topsoil, overfishing, and so on.
 
SCOTT MCVAY
Princeton, N.J., Oct. 28, 1996
